Science is rooted in the will to truth. With the will to truth it stands or falls.
Lower the standard even slightly and science becomes diseased at the core.
Not only science, but man.The will to truth, pure and unadulterated, is among
the essential conditions of his existence; if the standard is compromised he easily becomes a kind of tragic caricature of himself.
Max Wertheimer

FOREWORD: HOW TO RECONSTRUCT
DECONSTRUCTIONS
Alex Kozulin

The book in front of us is provocative, one may say deliberately provocative. The
authors basically claim that almost all that we have read about Lev Vygotsky and
his theory is a result of hero worshipping resulting in the cult of Vygotsky. The
aim of the authors is to debunk this cult and weed out numerous historical and
textual mistakes associated with the “Vygotskian narrative.” By pursuing this goal
the authors turn their book into something not only provocative, but also potentially dangerous to themselves: their attempt to deconstruct the “Vygotskian narrative” could easily backfire. If Vygotskian theory is no more than the product of
hero worshipping, then readers might start wondering what prompted the authors
to spend practically their entire careers studying this theory instead of abandoning
it altogether?
By now it should already be clear that by opening this book the reader enters a
highly contested territory. As to how to proceed in such a territory, the best advice is
to learn … from the authors. As the authors do not take any historical fact, opinion,
or connection for granted, then the reader should also read this book with a critical
eye. Moreover, while the authors’ goal was to deconstruct the existing “Vygotskian
narrative,” the reader may actually use this book to construct a Vygotskian narrative
of their own on a higher conceptual level.
The main tool brought by the authors to the job of deconstructing theVygotskian
cult is a very rich and detailed social and ideological analysis of the relevant periods
of Soviet history. The authors have identified and described the following factors
of Soviet life as crucial for understanding the mechanics of Soviet academic life
and its reflection in various texts: centralization and control, clique mentality, ritualism, the gap between theory and practice, intellectual isolationism, cultism, and
hagiographies. By articulating these factors the authors have greatly enriched our
understanding of the contexts in which Soviet psychologists, educators, and clinicians actually worked.

Foreword

xiii

There is no doubt that the Soviet system was highly centralized, with central
government in Moscow deciding practically every aspect of everyday life, economy,
education, and science. The case of psychology, however, is a special one. While
every reasonably big city in the Soviet Union had its own School of Medicine and
Institute of Technology, thus providing a modicum of diversity, for practically the
entire period described in this book there were only two places in Russia where
psychology could be studied – Moscow and Leningrad (St. Petersburg). It is not
surprising, therefore, that just by occupying the position of Head of the Department
(or School) of Psychology at Moscow University, people like Sergei Rubinstein or
Alexei Leontiev could exercise unprecedented control over the ways in which academic research was conducted and books or articles were selected for publication.
The world of academic psychology was very small and as a result very crowded.
In this congested academic environment each group of researchers felt almost by
definition threatened by other groups. One may guess that the formation and maintenance of scientific cliques, vividly described by the authors, had a lot to do with
this absence of academic “space.”The considerable gap between theory and practice,
with psychology since the mid-1930s confined to the academic sphere, only added
to this congestion.
Two additional aspects, intellectual isolationism and cultism, deserve a more
detailed discussion. For quite obvious political reasons a free exchange of psychological ideas with Western colleagues was practically impossible during the
Stalin era and often mortally dangerous for anyone attempting such a desperate
action. In this sense, in the period from the mid-1930s to the mid-1950s, Soviet
psychology was indeed intellectually isolated. It is also true that in the immediate post-World War II period, in parallel to a supreme cult of Stalin a system of
lower-level cults was created, so that in each area of life a “great man” was selected
as an emblematic representative of Russian science, art, industry, etc. For a while
Ivan Pavlov was selected for such a role in the field of behavioral sciences. The
question, however, remains to what extent this atmosphere of isolationism and
cultism was relevant to Vygotskian hero worshipping, which definitely was not
created in the 1940s.
The readers may engage in their own revisionist interpretation of the above
factors. Such a “revision of revisionist history” may actually lead to a higher level
of conceptual understanding. For example, it might be important to explore to
what extent centralization and control in academic areas was rooted in a pattern
of Russian intellectual life much older and deeper than “Stalinist science.” There
are, very roughly, two opposite models of intellectual life – a centralized French
model, with Paris and its universities serving as an uncontested intellectual center
situated in close proximity to the state bureaucracy, and a decentralized German
and American model that allowed first-rate universities and publishing houses to
grow in small towns with much less connection to state bureaucracy. Even before
the Communist Revolution Russia had adopted the French centralized model;
Russian academic and intellectual life has always been concentrated in Moscow and
St. Petersburg and depended on state bureaucracy. In this sense, “Stalinist science”

xiv

Foreword

just strengthened the pre-existing model that apparently continues to dominate in
Russia even after the fall of Communism.
In what concerns the existence of various warring clans and cliques within Soviet
psychology, this phenomenon can be interpreted as an exaggerated version of a
quite normal, one would even say positive phenomenon – the tradition of scientific
schools. As one can speak about a school of Gestalt psychology, Freudian psychoanalysis, or Piagetian epistemology, it is possible to talk about the followers of Ivan
Pavlov forming a school that was very different from that of Vladimir Bekhterev,
or a school of Alexei Leontiev as distinct from that of Daniel Elkonin. Scientific
schools, seen less and less nowadays, reflected not only the “mentor–novice” model
of the transmission of knowledge, but also an intellectual commitment to a certain
set of theoretical principles that should be defended against competing approaches.
While positive in itself, this scientific school mentality could easily regress to clannishness when the only chance for a successful academic career is associated with
the patronage of the powerful head of “your” school. The conflicting narratives
of Soviet psychologists belonging to different schools or cliques sometimes create
a very confusing picture. As demonstrated in Chapter 2, even during Vygotsky’s
lifetime his “school” was far from monolithic. Later on different constellations of
Vygotsky’s colleagues and students started forming their own separate groups.To an
outside observer all these groups could appear to be “Vygotskian,” but this did not
prevent them from competing with each other or presenting their own group as the
only genuine followers of Vygotsky’s approach.
While deconstructing and reconstructing various conflicting narratives, the readers should keep in mind that many of these narratives remained unrecorded. Even in
such “establishment” research groups as those of Alexander Luria or Daniel Elkonin,
much of the discussion occurred during informal seminars conducted in the
living-room or kitchen of their apartments and was never listed among the official
university courses. The impact of these seminars on the next generation of scholars
was very significant, but only a fraction of the discussions later appeared in print
and often in a form highly distorted by the authors’ self-censorship. One may thus
speak about two coexistent intellectual traditions: The written one embodied in the
official corpus of texts that appeared in the journal Voprosy Psikhologii (Problems of
Psychology) and various psychology monographs, and the oral one, based on informal seminars conducted by various research groups. Sometimes the gap between
these two streams was so wide that one could easily imagine them taking place in
two different countries. Many of the informal seminars were based on materials
(foreign books, old articles, current research protocols) available only to the group
leader and unavailable in any library.
In addition to the intricacies of Soviet intellectual life, the authors provide
us with an informative case study of the relationships between Vygotskians and
their Western colleagues; more specifically with the school of Gestalt psychology.
German Gestalt psychologist Kurt Koffka was not only knowledgeable about the
work of the Vygotsky–Luria group, but actually participated in their expedition
to Central Asia in the early 1930s. From the material presented in Chapter 8 it

Foreword

xv

becomes clear that the Vygotskians and Koffka sought and found very different
results in this expedition. Koffka, who adhered to the main theoretical premises of
the Gestalt school, was interested in discovering the universal principles of human
perception. For him, the expedition to Central Asia provided an opportunity to
identify those elements of perception that are not affected by cultural differences.
The goal of the Vygotsky–Luria group was very different. They wanted to use the
unique situation in Central Asia as a basis for a “quasi-historical” experiment. They
reasoned that people of the same ethnic origin and initial lifestyle may display
cognitive processes characteristic of different historical periods depending on their
current status – either still preserving a traditional lifestyle and pre-literate culture,
or already being involved in more modern occupations and lifestyle, or not only
acquiring modern occupation but also receiving a formal education via the literacy courses for adults. The study included a broad range of cognitive processes,
perception being just one of them. Koffka focused specifically on the case of visual
illusions and claimed that the majority of participants, irrespective of their current
occupational and educational status, demonstrate such illusions that confirm Gestalt
claims of the universality of some perceptual patterns. The Vygotsky–Luria group
evaluated the entire range of cognitive functions and concluded that occupational
and educational status is decisive in shaping cognitive processes, including in some
cases visual illusions. The above story, if properly reconstructed, may lead us beyond
the question of the relationships between the Gestalt school and Vygotskians and
show how “the same” findings acquire a very different meaning depending on the
overall theoretical perspective of various researchers.
Readers will also benefit from a detailed discussion about the publication and
translation problems associated with Vygotsky’s texts. One may identify three
types of such problems: the first is related to the fact that even during his lifetime Vygotsky was unable or unwilling to publish some of his major works, such
as his book Psychology of art. As a result, even though these works were published
later, we will never know if they appeared in the form that their author wished.
In the posthumous publications organized by Vygotsky’s followers, various of
Vygotsky’s texts were “glued” together, sometimes certain portions of the text or
references were deleted, and sometimes the texts were “adjusted,” apparently for
easier understanding by the reader. The last point becomes crucial when translations are considered, and this is a second problem. Vygotsky’s texts are not easy
even in Russian, and they definitely do not render themselves easier in translation. As mentioned elsewhere in this book, German Gestalt psychologists who
immigrated to the USA in the 1930s were quite aware that the American audience was not ready for their theoretical discourse as it was formulated in German.
So they “adjusted” this discourse in the English texts prepared for their new
audience. It is not surprising, therefore, that American followers of Vygotsky also
considered it necessary to “adjust” his discourse to what they considered acceptable to readers at that time (the 1960s and 1970s). The third, and much deeper,
problem that leads us beyond the Vygotskian story is the fact that in the age of
Google Translate we tend to forget that there is a considerable gap between the

xvi

Foreword

semantic fields of different languages. When Vygotsky wrote about “obuchenie”
(learning/teaching) his Russian readers placed this concept in a very different
semantic and associative field than either “teaching” or “learning” conjured in
the mind of a native English-speaker. So, in this sense, only complete bilingualists
may have an opportunity to understand with some certainty what Vygotsky had
in mind when he wrote about “smysl” and what Bruner understood when he
commented about Vygotsky’s use of the concept of “sense.”
So, when the reader closes this book I hope they may feel that not everything
has been “lost in translation” and that there is much more to Vygotsky’s theory than
“hero worshipping” or the clannishness of his followers. The reader of this book
holds some very rich material, including some only recently uncovered archival
documents; what the reader may do with this material is to go beyond deconstruction and construct their own Vygotskian narratives.

This page intentionally left blank

PART I

Contexts and people

This page intentionally left blank

1
THE ARCHETYPE OF SOVIET
PSYCHOLOGY
From the Stalinism of the 1930s to the
“Stalinist science” of our time
Anton Yasnitsky

Russian psychology emerged against the rich philosophical, artistic, and scientific
background of Russian culture of the fin de siècle and was institutionalized as a
scientific discipline virtually at the same time as academic psychology took root in
the West. Yet, in the first decades of its development psychology in Russia can be
best described as a more or less successful adaptation of Western tradition. Perhaps
the only original contribution of Russian scholars to international psychological
scholarship was their teaching on the “conditional reflexes” that was developed by
two major research groups associated with Ivan Pavlov and Vladimir Bekhterev.
The state of the art in psychology in Imperial Russia (i.e. before 1917) is, perhaps,
best understood within a continuum between two extremes that do not belong to
the domain of scientific research proper, yet largely determine its existence in the
concrete historical, social, and cultural context. The two extremes are, on the one
hand, philosophy, and, on the other hand, human industry, broadly understood as
a range of social activities geared towards the attainment of material gains. Given
that psychology as a scientific discipline is the inseparable unity of theory and
empirical studies – laboratory-experimental (in vitro) and applied (in vivo) – the
continuum can be structured into several strata that may be represented as “philosophy – general psychological theory – empirical research – social practice”
(see Table 1.1).
Psychology in late Imperial Russia manifested itself as three fairly distinct disciplines and related kinds of social agency.
First: Speculative academic psychology, typically based in university departments
of philosophy and extremely critical towards any empirical psychology whatsoever.
In fact, despite proclaimed interest in “psychology” the advocates of this approach
were professional philosophers or philosophizing intellectuals in a wide range of
other intellectual/practical spheres from social and human sciences to non-scientific
enterprises such as the arts, theology, or psychiatric practice.

4

Anton Yasnitsky

TABLE 1.1 Structural components of a scientific discipline in social context

1. Philosophy
2. General (psychological) theory
3. Empirical research:
• laboratory-experimental
• applied
4. Human industry and social practice:
• military
• medicine and • education and • industry
affairs and
health care
child-rearing
proper and
defense
labor

• propaganda and
public relations

Second: Empirical psychology that widely used the methods of laboratory
experiment of a natural-scientific type for the investigation of the so-called “lower”
psychological processes and at the same time frequently allowed for theoretical
speculations on explanations of the “higher” psychological processes and phenomena – the “spiritual,” superindividual, cultural, or naturally organismic ones – that
the advocates of this approach did not believe are accessible by the methods of
“purely scientific,” positivist quantitative research. Therefore, the two typical frameworks for these speculations were vitalism or spiritualism that postulated the primacy of “life” and “spirit” as fundamental explanatory principles of these theories.
Third: Applied psychology that primarily aimed at solving practical tasks outside
the academic domain proper and was directly related to social practices, predominantly in the spheres of education and health care. Occasionally, the representatives of
this strand could occupy university chairs and be involved in traditional academia,
but regardless of their possible participation in theoretical discussions and laboratory studies, their definite priority was transformative agency in some segment or
another of social practice.
Therefore, although Russian psychology on the threshold of 1917 seems to have
been well represented by a few intellectuals in a wide range of national institutions
and organizations, in fact there were very fragmented and often rival psychologies
of three types, not to mention competitive groupings, cliques, and clans within
each of these types. The gap between the three “psychologies” was enormous and
required a truly revolutionary shift in the professional consciousness, self-identity,
and social practice of psychologists. Russian psychology as a unified scientific discipline with original conceptions and innovative methods for their implementation
owes virtually all its achievements to the Soviet era and, primarily, to the turbulent
period of the 1920s–1930s, when the foundations of “Soviet psychology” as we
know it today were laid.

Psychoneurological disciplines in the Soviet Union in the 1920s
The revolutions in February and October of 1917 dramatically changed the entire
structure of Russian society and gave access to power, administration, and social
advancement to large sections of the population of the country, such as the Jewish

The archetype of Soviet psychology

5

minority in the Russian Empire, who had fairly limited opportunities for education
or state-funded positions before the October uprising and the Bolsheviks’ ascent
to power. Quite characteristic in this respect are the careers of Lev Vygotsky and
Alexander Luria, for whom access to university education before the revolution
would mean the need to overcome the 3 percent Jewish quota and, furthermore, the
random ballot during the candidate selection process, in contrast to the open and
freely available governmental positions of various kinds possible after the Bolshevik
revolution. Bolsheviks had a high regard for science and technology as the vehicles
of social modernization and progressive transformation of national industry and the
economy (Kojevnikov, 2004; Krementsov, 1997). Besides, the issue of official recognition of the USSR was one of the national priorities of the new state, and the
impact of “cultural diplomacy” – including international scientific exchange – on
the reputation of the country and propaganda for the Soviet project of radical social
transformation can hardly be overestimated (David-Fox, 2012). The example of the
only Russian Nobel Prize winner as of 1917, Ivan Pavlov, is quite telling: not only
did the Bolsheviks persuade him not to leave the country after the revolution, they
also provided him with favorable conditions for living and working that eventually
turned him from profound criticism of the new regime to moderately enthusiastic
support for Bolshevik rule (Todes, 1995, 2014).
The decade of the 1920s can without any exaggeration be described as a period
of intensive growth – even flourishing – for Russian psychology. It was then
that the new generation of young intellectuals and activists emerged, inspired by
Nietzschean ideas of radical revaluation of the entire social structure including the
promise of a new science of man and its decisive role in revolutionary transformation of the world. Thus, one of the key tasks of the post-revolutionary era was
utopian “remolding of man,” the creation of a new type of people, who will master
their nature and uncover the yet unknown potential of human beings.1 These ideas
were grounded in the pervasive post-revolutionary belief in the possibility of virtually unlimited personal growth and an active, creative attitude to the world. This
is why the public discourse of Soviet science, fiction, and media often returns to the
topics of the specifically human characteristics that distinguish homo sapiens from
other species, the nature of humans and the ways to overcome it, and the specific
non-natural, i.e. cultural, laws of human development that are not inherited and
genetically predetermined, but are formed by means of society, art, and culture.
By the mid-1920s a four-level structure of the social organization of Soviet
psychology and allied sciences formed. This structure tied together philosophy
and practice and comprised: (1) Marxist philosophy and dialectical materialism as
the philosophical foundation of general psychology; (2) general psychology as a
high-level scientific theory; (3) applied psychological disciplines (for instance, pedology or psychotechnics, roughly equivalent to child studies and industrial and
vocational psychology, respectively); and (4) social institutes and practices (medicine, education, industry, etc.) (see Table 1.2; for convenience experimental psychology of the laboratory type is not represented). Some of these theoretical and
applied disciplines originated in the West and were imported by Russian scholars

6

Anton Yasnitsky

TABLE 1.2 Disciplines of Soviet psychology in the 1920s

Philosophy
General (psychological) theory:
psychology, reflexology, reactology
Applied empirical studies:
psychotherapy, defectology, pedology, psychohygiene, psychotechnics
Human industry and social practice:
• military affairs • propaganda and
• medicine and • education and • industry
health care

proper and
and defense

labor

and practitioners from abroad before the Bolshevik revolution of 1917, primarily from Western Europe and North America. These include pedology (alternatively,
referred to as “pedagogical psychology” or “experimental pedagogy”), psychotechnics,
psychotherapy, and psychohygiene (in North America known as mental hygiene).2 On
the other hand, a few original Soviet disciplinary “brands” were created such as
the reflexology of V. M. Bekhterev (originally, Bekhterev’s “objective psychology” of
the pre-revolutionary period that, at its creator’s insistence, radically departed from
the original and quickly changed its “brand-name” after the Bolshevik revolution)
(Bekhterev, 1925, 1933). Other examples are K. N. Kornilov’s reactology and defectology, understood as a complex scientific-practical agency towards overcoming children’s “defectiveness”: physical, mental and psychological, and social alike.
According to the Bolsheviks’ requirement of the practicality of science, it was
practice that would become the criterion of truth and usefulness – quite in
agreement with the postulates of Marxism, which, in fact, was the second of the
Bolsheviks’ requirements of science. Indeed, the requirement of practicality could
be interpreted as a direct challenge to philosophy’s ambition to provide a theoretical viewpoint, yet it was certainly an extension of Marx’s ideas on the role of
philosophy in the radical and revolutionary transformation of the world as opposed
to its traditional understanding as a purely theoretical and speculative enterprise.
The role psychology was to play in this social transformation was very special and
highly important. Psychology was to find the means for the normative remolding of
the “old man” of the capitalist past and educating the “new man” of Communism.
These methods would be subsequently implemented in large-scale social projects
and would lead to the creation of the improved and advanced people of the future.
These utopian and idealistic plans were also supported by most immediate and
utterly practical goals of concrete social reality. The country was recovering after
the Civil War (1918–22) and the actual tasks of the day were to modernize the
national – predominantly agricultural – economy, overcome child homelessness and
illiteracy, reanimate the system of schools and higher education, reform health care,
and provide professional reorientation and training to thousands of new workers.3
Therefore, it was not abstract, theoretical interest, but the urgent demands of social
practice that determined the rapid development of applied psychoneurological disciplines grounded in the actual concrete tasks of the establishment of a new society.

The archetype of Soviet psychology

7

The agency of the Soviet psychologists in the 1920s, therefore, was not confined
within the borders of psychology as an isolated discipline. Lavishly supported by
the Bolshevik leadership, work was done on creating a pragmatic interdisciplinary
synthesis of scientific-practical interventions and a higher-level theoretical paradigm
that would encompass all human sciences and bring about a universal framework in
which disciplines at different levels and from various spheres of application would
work together as a complex, based on a shared methodological and philosophical
foundation. The development of all these disciplines and social movements was
rooted in a wide range of scientific societies and civil organizations. Hundreds of
new research institutes were founded in the 1920s. In 1928 a few new scientific
journals were launched such as Psychotechnics and Psychophysiology of Labor (in 1932
renamed Soviet Psychotechnics), Psychology, Pedology, and Problems of Defectology.
As a result of all these social transformations, a range of new scientific schools
and research groups in human sciences emerged in the Soviet Union by the end of
the 1920s. Soviet psychology triumphantly entered international psychological science at the IX International Congress of Psychology held at Yale University in early
September, 1929, where Soviet scholars were represented by a record-breaking third
largest non-American delegation (after those of Great Britain and Germany). This
scientific event was followed by major international congresses held in the Soviet
Union – the VII International Congress of Applied Psychology, or Psychotechnics,
(Moscow, 1931) and the XV International Physiological Congress (Leningrad and
Moscow, 1935) – that solidified the success of Soviet human and biomedical sciences. Characteristically, Carl Murchison’s Psychologies of 1930 for the first time
contained a whole new section titled “Russian Psychologies,” which included three
chapters that featured Ivan Pavlov’s theory of higher nervous activity (Pavlov, 1930),4
Vladimir Bekhterev’s reflexology (Schniermann, 1930), and the “dialectical-materialist
psychology” of Konstantin Kornilov (Kornilov, 1930). These were accompanied by
a number of translated publications of Soviet scholars that came out in the West
mostly in the second half of the1920s and first half of 1930s.5
Subsequently, a number of Western scholars visited the Soviet Union in order to
familiarize themselves with the state of the art in contemporary Soviet psychology.
These trips were reflected in a series of Western publications that present a relatively
mixed, but generally quite favorable image of the success of the Soviet project in the
field of the human sciences, including psychology.6

Soviet psychology as a “Stalinist science” from the 1930s to
our time
The end of the 1920s in the Soviet Union saw the start of major social, economic,
and cultural transformations that the leader of the country, Joseph Stalin, characterized as the “Great Break” (Velikii Perelom). The literature on Stalin’s Great Break
and its impact on virtually all spheres of social life in the USSR is enormous,7 but
in the context of this discussion we are primarily interested in its effect on science

8

Anton Yasnitsky

and particularly on the psychological and allied sciences. For our current purposes
it suffices to point out that as a consequence of the “Great Break” and the accompanying social processes a new model of science and, furthermore, the model of
the interrelations between science, the state, and the ruling Communist Party was
established in the Soviet Union by the end of the 1930s. The reader is thus directed
to the classic book by Nikolai Krementsov that provides an excellent and seemingly exhaustive discussion of the topic that the author referred to as “Stalinist science” (Krementsov, 1997). The phrase does not appear unquestionable and is used
here interchangeably with another phrase with essentially the same meaning: the
“Stalinist model” of science. While Krementsov’s book will provide a rich source of
thought-provoking input to the interested reader, several points need to be clarified
and explicated here in order to shed some light on the nature of the tectonic shift
of this period and those specific changes that Soviet science had to undergo in its
transformation in agreement with the “Stalinist model.”
First: The rationale of the Great Break as it is related to scientific research and
national science is perhaps best understood as the Party and state demand for the
accountability of all subordinate structures of society, which primarily required
establishing control over these structures and social institutes. Indeed, as far as the
social and human sciences are concerned, the preceding decade of the 1920s is
characterized by the state-sponsored but relatively free establishment and proliferation of numerous revolutionary and experimental projects in science and social
practice. In contrast, the decade of the 1930s was characterized by the introduction
of planning in virtually all spheres of social life, primarily the economy of the Soviet
Union, now to develop according to a series of Five-Year Plans. Therefore, the
whole rhythm of social life changed during this decade, and even scholars were now
to create their annual plans of research and submit them for inspection to the supervisory agencies. Another consequence of the major transformation as it is directly
related to the psychological and allied sciences was the beginning of the process of
inter-disciplinary struggle for survival under the scrutiny of the organs of state and
Party control. Indeed, as it turned out by the beginning of the 1930s, quite a few
of the new scientific disciplines of the post-revolutionary era of the 1920s considerably overlapped in their topics, theories, and methods of research and were more
often than not represented by pretty much the same set of spokesmen and activists.
A characteristic example of such overlap would be the interplay between such disciplines as psychology, pedology, psychotechnics, and defectology that appeared to represent
just different faces of the same entity. For fairly obvious reasons such inexplicable
fragmentation could by no means satisfy the administrators who were appointed to
supervise the progress of scientific research in the Soviet Union in the early 1930s.
Second: The decade of the 1930s and the overarching “Stalinist model” of science gave birth to a number of specific rituals that, according to a new social
contract, were understood and followed by all parties involved as the new system of communications between science and its only grant-making agency in the
Soviet Union – the state and its supreme guiding force, the Communist Party of
the Union of Soviet Socialist Republics. The rituals can be divided into closely

The archetype of Soviet psychology

9

interrelated behavioral and rhetorical ones and included a number of techniques
and performances such as celebrating a wide range of jubilees and reporting progress on achievements on these occasions, writing letters to the leaders of the state,
public discussions, including public criticism and self-criticism, and many others.
In the sphere of ritualistic discursive practices the most notable examples include
the penetration of the political newspeak of the Bolshevik fashion in the oral and
written discourse of Soviet science to the extent of the virtually total disappearance
of the difference between the two, and the emergence of the standard of “nomadic
quotes” and mandatory references that would typically be expected on the very first
pages of publications as a token of loyalty and alignment with the goals of the sponsors of science and, at the same time, the political leadership of the state.
Third: As a result of all these transformations of the decade of the 1930s a new
model of national science, the “Stalinist model,” is best described as the hybrid
of state, Party, and science, according to which the borderline between the three
entities in most instances was extremely blurred and hardly distinguishable. The
main technology and the instrument of this merger was the custom of nomenklatura appointments described at considerable length in the classic book by Voslensky
(1984) and succinctly discussed in its relation to Soviet science by Krementsov:
The main instrument of party personnel policy in general was the system
of nomenklatura. Nomenklatura was, literally, a list of posts that could not be
occupied or vacated without permission from the appropriate party committee. All party committees, from the Central Committee to the smallest one
in the countryside, established personnel departments, whose main function
was to approve candidates for appointment to any post included in their own
nomenklatura. Initially devised for the personnel of party organs and agencies, the system was expanded in the early 1930s into the scientific community. The nomenklatura system was strictly hierarchical – the higher the post,
the higher the party committee controlling its personnel. The posts of president, vice-president, and scientific secretary of such central institutions as the
USSR Academy of Sciences and VASKhNIL were in the nomenklatura of the
Politburo.The posts of institute director and editor-in-chief of a journal were
in the nomenklatura of the Central Committee Secretariat. The position of
laboratory head belonged to the nomenklatura of the regional party committee. Even the post of librarian in a scientific institute was in the nomenklatura
of the local party committee.
(Krementsov, 1997, pp. 40–41)
Therefore, here the loop closes: The custom of nomenklatura was, on the one hand,
the main and apparently the most efficient instrument of the top-down Party–state
control over science, and, on the other hand, inevitably dictated the necessity and
utmost importance of rituals for the bottom-up processes that allowed scholars to
manipulate their bosses in the government and utilize their goals and aspirations – as
distant from their personal, career, and research goals as they might have been – in

10

Anton Yasnitsky

order to benefit from the ever-changing agenda of the increasingly authoritarian
supervisory agencies in power.
As a result of numerous and somewhat chaotic processes in the turbulent 1930s,
by the middle of the decade the understanding had developed in the leadership of
the country that the multitude of “revolutionary” disciplines in the human sciences
was fairly redundant and, therefore, had to be reduced in favor of a few – apparently
the most conventional – disciplines and related social practices. It seems that among
the most important criteria that played the decisive role in making the decision was
the discipline’s representation and recognition at the international level and Soviet
scholars’ participation in similar international societies, associations, publications,
etc. of the kind that would bear exactly the same name as the one Soviet spokesmen
for their discipline would advocate. It appears that on close inspection there was
but one scientific discipline that would stand any possible criticism from this perspective, whereas the rest of the disciplinary newcomers of the post-revolutionary
era had to go and to give way to more conventional projects, i.e. more traditional
and well-established in international scholarly networks. Thus, as a result of natural
selection and the disciplinary purge of the 1930s all those fashionable disciplinary
projects of the 1920s such as pedology, psychotechnics, defectology, reactology, reflexology,
and the like were to unconditionally merge with the traditional disciplines and
social practices.The social practices that benefitted from this merger were primarily
education (and the directly related “science of pedagogy”) and medicine, encompassing former defectologists, psychotherapists, psychohygienists, and some pedologists (i.e. typically those professionals who originally had a medical education or
were inclined to get re-educated, like, for instance, both Vygotsky and Luria). As
for the winner in the inter-disciplinary struggle for survival – the winner was …
psychology, perfectly forged in the late 1930s along with the “Stalinist model” in the
making. Thus, after a brief interruption of most social activities caused by another
of Stalin’s social projects of the 1930s – the Stalinist Great Terror of 1936–8 that
considerably confused the system of the interrelationship within informal personal
networks of Soviet scholars and their Party/state bosses and patrons, some of them
outlawed and persecuted in purges – Soviet psychology re-emerged as one of the
leading human sciences that was seemingly supposed to be the bridge that would
provide the link between inevitably overly abstract and essentially speculative philosophy and the “real” empirical sciences and social practices related to humans
and their activities in concrete social-historical contexts. As a result, it turns out
that by the end of the 1930s the multitude and diversity of theories, disciplines,
and approaches dramatically decreased and the dominant model of the 1920s that
included four levels within the continuum “philosophy–general (psychological)
theory–applied sciences–social practices” was reduced to two level hybrids, namely,
the hybrid of “philosophy–general psychological theory” and, on the other hand,
“applied psychologies–social practices,” which in effect meant total failure to bridge
the gap between theory and practice and a return to the age-old problems of the
late Imperial period of psychology’s development. Thus, in other words, theoretical
psychology remained speculative and hardly usable in empirical research, whereas

The archetype of Soviet psychology

11

TABLE 1.3 The structure of psychoneurological and human sciences in the USSR in

the 1920s and 1930s

1920s: four levels of human sciences
1. Marxist philosophy
2. General sciences
3. Applied sciences
4. Social practices

End of 1930s: two hybrid levels of
psychological sciences
1. Marxist philosophy and general science
2. Social practice and applied sciences

applied studies returned to the original pre-revolutionary state of being fully subordinated to practice and virtually fully isolated from any theoretical thinking whatsoever. For an illustration of this situation see Table 1.3.
In sum, the start of the great disciplinary cleansing was announced in 1936 with
the publication of the Communist Party’s notorious “pedological decree” that in
effect banned or considerably limited the area of application of a number of revolutionary projects of the preceding decade. Thus, Soviet psychology as the main winner of the fight was forged in 1936.This was followed by a series of other important
achievements that included a range of officially endorsed textbooks in psychology that came out in 1938–41, the establishment of the Institutes of Psychology
in Soviet Georgia (under the auspices of the Academy of Sciences of GSSR, in
1941) and in Soviet Ukraine (in 1945), important and most prestigious national
scientific awards granted to psychologists (e.g. the award to S. L. Rubinstein of
the Stalin Prize in 1941), and first appointments of psychologists to the top of the
social scientific hierarchy, the Academy of Sciences of the USSR (Rubinstein and
Kravkov as Corresponding Members, in the 1940s). Finally, the last stroke was to
follow: In 1946 psychology was introduced in public school curricula as a mandatory subject to be taught all over the Soviet Union. This event logically concludes
the ten-year period that can be legitimately referred as the Golden Age of Soviet
Psychology of 1936–46.
Yet, the last phase of the formation of the discipline of Soviet psychology according to the “Stalinist model” followed in the late 1940s/early 1950s with the evolution of this model as such. Following the notorious “Lysenko trials” (1948) and
the subsequent “Pavlovization” of the humanities (from 1950) and in relation to
other infamous political campaigns of late Stalinism such as the “doctors’ trials,” the
xenophobic search for national roots and Russian priority in virtually all spheres of
human activity or the anti-Semitic fight against “rootless cosmopolitanism,” Soviet
psychology eventually took its shape as a “Stalinist science,” conceived as such in the
late 1930s and solidified in the early 1950s. Incidentally, it was virtually at this very
time that the two first Departments of Psychology were founded in Moscow and
Leningrad (in 1943–4) that started preparing new cadres for the new discipline, and
their exclusive monopoly in the professional reproduction of specialists in this field
would be preserved for several decades to come. Thus, the first wave of Candidate

12

Anton Yasnitsky

of Sciences dissertations was defended right during late Stalinism’s outbreak of
neo-imperial nationalism and xenophobia, anti-Semitic campaigns, and extreme
isolationism. All these factors, it seems, were to play a crucial role in the subsequent
fate of the entire discipline in the country. The reason for that is pretty clear: it was
this generation – who entered university in the second half of the 1940s, graduated
and then wrote and defended their scholarly dissertations by the mid-1950s – that
fully assimilated the “Stalinist model”: they were brought up in it, and massively
implemented it when they finally came to power in the scientific administration
of psychology in the USSR as a result of demographic processes and the change
of generations. Thus, it makes perfect sense to talk about two generations of Soviet
psychologists and dramatic differences between the two. Let us first focus on the
older generation of scholars and scientific administrators of psychology.
Despite numerous differences between all these people, at a certain high level
of generalization a number of similarities between them can be discerned. These
similar features, then, can be put together in order to make up a vague and pretty
impersonal “cumulative portrait” of this generation. Thus, the representative of this
group of scholars was typically born into a university-educated family within the
intelligentsia and was educated in traditional gimnazia in pre-revolutionary time,
and therefore had a pretty good knowledge of at least two foreign languages, French
and German; this was followed by university education in one of the Russian universities in Moscow, St. Petersburg (renamed Petrograd during World War I), Kazan’,
or abroad, most typically in Germany. The group of the representatives of this generation, thus, was born, roughly, in the 1890s, brought up in the classical intellectual
and cultural traditions of late Imperial Russia; they enthusiastically took part in the
Bolshevik project of radical social transformation and successfully passed through
cleansings and post-Revolutionary “social selection” of various sorts.
This generation of intellectuals remained for a while in the somewhat ambiguous position of Bolshevik sympathizers, and at the same time as representatives of
the older – politically suspect and presumably disloyal – pre-Revolutionary intelligentsia of the “bourgeois” cultural tradition. The crucial turn took place in 1936
that inaugurated the birth of a distinctly Soviet intelligentsia:
In one of many drastic reversals of the Stalin period, a social category that
had been relegated to the dustbin of history experienced an unexpected
revival. In 1936, the dictator declared that a reformed and loyal “toiling intelligentsia” had emerged, a “stratum” (prosloika) with a rightful place in socialist
society alongside the workers and collective farmers. While perhaps dubious
in its Marxist credentials, Stalin’s re-establishment of intelligentsia as a social
category was both constitutive and reflective of the social system that took
shape in the 1930s. By legitimizing the place of educated professionals under
socialism in doctrinal terms, Stalin’s “toiling intelligentsia” signaled a wider
integration of educated elites in the Soviet order … Just as importantly, the
“toiling intelligentsia” provided the possibility of rehabilitation to the remaining representatives of the pre-revolutionary educated stratum, people who

The archetype of Soviet psychology

13

had been considered class enemies or marginalized as “bourgeois specialists” in the preceding years. Of course, such assimilation came at a price: the
Stalinist system made professional thinkers of all sorts more dependent on
state power and agendas than ever before. Relentless demands from above for
unquestioning service and “party-mindedness” accompanied state-employed
intellectuals’ newfound prominence and (relative) social privilege. Indeed, the
very construct of the “toiling intelligentsia” underscored intellectuals’ reliance
on serving the state, as it defined membership in the group according to formal educational achievement and occupational criteria (the performance of
“mental labor”), both of which the party-state monopolized.
(Tromly, 2014, pp. 4–5)
Subsequently, the small circle of the “chosen ones” of the “generation of the 1890s”
occupied all the highest positions in the scientific administration of Soviet psychology in the 1940s, when psychology as a scientific discipline got access to the
top of the scientific hierarchy: the Academy of Sciences of the USSR. It should
be noted, though, that, according to the “Stalinist model” of science, virtually all
administrative power and control over national psychology in the 1940s was in the
hands of one person, Sergei Leonidovich Rubinstein (1889–1960), the first psychologist elected as a Corresponding Member of the Academy of Sciences, the first
psychologist awarded the Stalin Prize, and who simultaneously occupied positions
as Director of the Institute of Psychology in Moscow (in 1942–5), Head of the
Department of Psychology at Moscow State University (1943–9), and Head of the
Sector of Psychology and Deputy Director of the Institute of Philosophy under the
auspices of the Academy of Sciences of the USSR (1945–9). The tumultuous social
processes of late Stalinism undermined Rubinstein’s unquestionable authority and
brought his power to an end: in 1949 Rubinstein, like many other Jews in the Soviet
Union at that time, turned out to be a “rootless cosmopolitan” and was released
from all his supervisory and administrative positions. And yet, the high concentration of power and influence within a narrow circle of individuals – highly characteristic of the “Stalinist model” – was preserved even after Rubinstein’s demise.
Thus, for instance, the renowned Swiss psychologist Jean Piaget, who visited the
Soviet Union in the mid-1950s, immediately after the visit published a report of the
trip, where he reminisced about his meetings with the “Great Five” that comprised
official top-rank Soviet scholars Rubinstein, Teplov, Smirnov, Leontiev, and Luria
(Piaget, 1956), of whom, according to anecdotal evidence from another source, only
Teplov and Smirnov had real power throughout the 1950s, at least (Shchedrovitskii,
2001). Piaget, who personally met these academicians – all these the representatives
of the “generation of the 1890s” – recalled the nice and free exchanges they had
had in his native French during his trip to Moscow and was apparently impressed
by the fairly Western manners of his Soviet peers and their familiarity with recent
trends in international psychological science. The sharper and more striking contrast is between these representatives of the “generation of the 1890s” and the new
generation of their students and followers, the psychologists of the next generation

14

Anton Yasnitsky

to come and reign. Despite the vulnerability of such a generalization, let us sketch
another “group portrait”: that of the representative of the “new generation.”
The representative of the “new generation” of Soviet psychologists is a person who was born within the period of, roughly, the second half of the 1920s/
first half of the 1930s. The childhood of this individual took place at the time
of early Stalinism, and he received his high-school education during World War
II, not unusually, in remote eastern areas of the Soviet Union, far from the front
line, where families were relocated during the war. Unlike the “generation of the
1890s,” who were typically educated in classical, elitist, German-style gymnasiums
of the late Imperial tradition, the representatives of the “new generation” spent their
high-school years in a fairly “democratic” and “affirmative-action” Soviet educational system. Furthermore, the wartime period was characterized by deprivation
and continuous disruption of the learning process caused by the lack of qualified –
especially male – teachers, the relatively low status of school studies during the
war, and particularly the poor quality of foreign-language instruction. Then, these
individuals obtained their university education in the post-World War II period,
when their undergraduate and graduate studies typically coincided with the social
processes of late Stalinism, characterized by a distinctly neo-imperial, xenophobic, Russian nationalist, anti-Semitic, demonstratively patriotic, and, to large extent,
isolationist rhetoric that in more or less apparent forms continued throughout the
entire decade of the 1950s. The representative of the “new generation” of Soviet
psychologists would typically receive a university education at one of the two existing departments of psychology (in Moscow or in Leningrad). The person normally
graduated in 1950s, often joined the Communist Party of the Soviet Union at the
end of the 1950s/early 1960s, defended Candidate and then Doctoral dissertations
in the 1950s–1970s, and made a fairly successful scientific career.To a certain degree
this description fits quite a few representatives of the “new generation,” some of
whom were to replace their teachers and predecessors of the pre-revolutionary
generation in the administrative leadership of their discipline. The new group of
academic bosses eventually came to power and got control over virtually the entire
discipline of psychology – if not in the whole multi-national country, then definitely in its largest part, the Russian Soviet Federative Socialist Republic (RSFSR).
Quite in line with the requirements of the “Stalinist model” the circle of the new
leaders is quite limited, and does not exceed a dozen names of varying importance.
It was this generation, whose formative years passed during the period of
Stalinism of the 1930s–1950s, that gradually occupied all key positions of power and
authority, their most triumphant achievement being, in 1971, the establishment of
the Institute of Psychology at the Academy of Sciences of the Soviet Union under
the leadership of its founder and director Boris Fedorovich Lomov (1927–89) and
his associates. This “new generation” – that came to power in the 1960s–1970s and
remained in control over the course of development of Soviet psychology until the
early 2000s – was to ultimately accomplish the “archetype of Soviet psychology,”
in other words to implement the “Stalinist model” of psychological science in a
wide range of educational establishments, research institutions, and social practices

The archetype of Soviet psychology

15

throughout the entire country – long after the mastermind of this model, the “Great
Leader and Teacher” Joseph Stalin, had died.
Interestingly, Stephen Toulmin in his famous article of 1978 on the scientific legacy of Vygotsky and Luria, characteristically titled “The Mozart of psychology,” also
mentions the “generation gap” of some twenty years between 1935 and 1955 and
points out the profound negative effect this gap had on the development of psychology in the Soviet Union in general, and, specifically, on Vygotsky’s legacy in the
Soviet Union. In particular, Toulmin argues that despite the fact that “half a dozen
of his students survived the Second World War, and took up important positions
after it”, still, because of this very “gap” that was filled with psychological studies of
a reductionist kind:
In fact, some informed American observers consider that his ideas are likely
to be developed and exploited more effectively in the US during the years
ahead – by such people as John Flavell of Stanford and Ann Brown of the
University of Illinois – than in the Soviet Union. By some intellectual version
of Gresham’s Law, just as Western psychology is beginning to escape from its
earlier positivism, Russian academic psychology is, ironically, beginning to
look more like the American experimental psychology of the intervening
forty years!
(Toulmin, 1978)
The gap between the two scholarly cultures and traditions in Soviet psychology – as
it was realized in the scientific work of the two generations of the 1890s and the
1920s – is as conspicuous as it is detrimental. Furthermore, there is every reason
to believe that many – virtually all – idiosyncratic characteristics of the “archetype
of Soviet psychology” have been preserved in the group mentality and, thus, social
behaviors of contemporary Russian psychologists, the self-proclaimed heirs of the
“great,” “unique,” and “self-sufficient” Russian psychology.

Archetype of contemporary Russian psychology (1990s–2010s):
a sketch for a portrait
We have got used to thinking about the so-called Soviet shestidesiatniki – despite the
considerable vagueness and imprecision of this expression – as the representatives
of the Soviet scientific-engineering and artistic-humanities intelligentsia socially
active in the 1960s and onwards and charged with the ideas of social transformation, modernization, and progress. Recent studies in the culture and tradition of
the Soviet Union of the twentieth century tend to interpret the ideology of this
group within the Soviet intelligentsia of the 1960s as being rooted in the early
Soviet, so to say, “pre-Stalinist” period of the 1920s that served them as the source
for cultural self-identification as the spiritual heirs of the utopian romanticism of the
post-revolutionary era. No doubt, there were many individuals among the Soviet
psychologists of the “new generation” of the 1960s who would perfectly fit this

16

Anton Yasnitsky

description. And yet, there is also no doubt that it was a group of the representatives of this very generation who implemented in social practice the conservative
“Stalinist model” of science in the Soviet Union pretty much in the same fashion as
it was designed and institutionalized during the period of late Stalinism. Different
scientific disciplines within the somewhat abstract and overly general “Soviet science” apparently demonstrated different patterns and group behaviors of their
agents, and therefore this “Stalinist model” was not necessarily implemented in all
disciplines without exception. Still, the “Stalinist model” can be clearly seen in the
institutional organization and social practices of psychology as a scientific discipline
throughout its postwar history.
As we now know, both Imperial Russia’s pre-revolutionary psychology and
Soviet psychology of the 1920s and 1930s were intrinsically connected with foreign scholarship and followed international academic standards (Yasnitsky, 2011a,
2012b, 2012c), which, furthermore, appears to have considerably contributed to its
survival in the process of “natural selection” of experimental revolutionary disciplinary projects in the humans sciences – such as the exotic and, therefore, extinct
“reflexology,” “reactology,” “defectology,” “psychotechnics,” etc. – during the era of
“disciplinary cleansing” of the mid-1930s. At the same time, Soviet psychology and
its ideological heir – the science of psychology in contemporary Russia – is conspicuously different from the cumulative image of “Western science.” What distinguishes the two is the whole set of idiosyncratic features that do not seem to be well
understood in the West up to the present – even by the relatively numerous group
of Western admirers of Soviet and Russian psychology. It is this set of distinctive features of Russian psychology in its social habitat that is discussed in this chapter and
is referred to as “the archetype” of Soviet psychology. In order to better understand
what exactly is meant by “archetype” here, let us summarize the major distinctive
features of the “archetype of Russian psychology,” formerly “Soviet psychology,”
that by the mid-1950s was consolidated as “Marxist,” “ideological,” “militant,” and
“advanced” science (Bogdanchikov, 2006). These are the characteristics that appear
to represent the values and attitudes, therefore, to motivate the behavior patterns of
the majority of individual agents of Russian psychology, and, finally, to determine
its form and contents throughout several decades of the history of this scientific discipline and related social practice.

(1)

Centralization and control

The highest form of Party–state control over science and at the same time its
supreme institutional embodiment was the Academy of Sciences of the USSR,
originally founded by decree of the Russian tsar Peter I in 1724. In the course
of its historical development the Academy underwent a series of transformations
and was renamed a few times, but only as a result of the organizational reforms
of the mid-1930s did the Academy get those enormous and unprecedented rights
and power that characterize the highly centralized “Stalinist model” of scientific

The archetype of Soviet psychology

17

organization and management and that this organization, renamed the Russian
Academy of Science after the collapse of the Soviet Union in 1991, retained until
its latest reform in 2013:
The main peculiarity of the Academy of Sciences of USSR that was established by the new Ustav [i.e. the Statute of the Academy of Sciences of the
USSR] in 1935 was that the Academy ceased being autonomous from the
state scientific establishment as it was founded and had existed for 200 years.
Personified by its members – and the leadership of the Academy and the
administrative bodies the Academy members would elect – the Academy
turned into a full-fledged social institution, in fact, a Narkomat [i.e. the
Ministry] of non-industrially significant science that would supervise the
scientific and auxiliary establishments affiliated with the Academy, determine their research agendas and distribute between these establishments
the budget that was granted from the state to the Academy as a whole.
The Members of the Academy acquired the status of special high-rank
state scholars with well above the average remuneration and professional
privileges unprecedented in the history of world science … Like other
Narkomats, the Academy of Sciences was directly subordinated to the head
of the government of the state. Only the highest governmental officials
were entitled to interfere in planning research agendas of the Academy’s
research institutes and in internal regulations of the Academy (albeit only
by means of changes in the Statutes of the Academy) … A contemporary
reader well-versed in political intrigues does understand that in 1935 a new
social stratum comprising individuals highly loyal – if not in earnest, then
according to the new social contract – to authority was created within
the hierarchy of numerous nomenklatura strata of Soviet society. Between
now and then virtually everything has changed. Late Soviet Union elites
degenerated and provoked the collapse of the regime that promoted them,
and, in turn, they disappeared with the collapse of the regime. And only the
Full and Corresponding Members of the contemporary Russian Academy
of Sciences (RAN) – along with the Members of two other “state-owned”
Academies, those of Agricultural and the Medical Sciences – whose social
roots essentially are in the nomenklatura system of the Soviet Union, position
the existing order as the allegedly natural and necessary structural organization of any science at the national and federal level. In fact, though, they are
just a forlorn relic of the era that has gone.
(Khromov, 2002)
Similarly, the system of state control over science has preserved the Higher
Attestation Commission (VAK), established in 1932 (and in effect launched in
1934), the national government agency that oversees the awarding of advanced
academic degrees, including those of Candidate and Doctor of Sciences. A high
level of centralization of power in the administration of science is implemented not

18

Anton Yasnitsky

only at the federal level, but also at the lower level – within each specific discipline or geographical locale. As a rule, quite in agreement with the “Stalinist model,”
power is concentrated within the hands of one or a very limited number of individuals. Traditionally, administrative control over psychology in the Soviet Union
was granted to the representatives of Moscow or Leningrad (after 1991 renamed St.
Petersburg) universities or individuals closely related with them. This tradition has
been largely preserved until now. Thus, for instance, the Dean of the Department of
Psychology of Moscow State University concurrently occupies a number of the key
administrative positions that allow him direct control over a considerable segment
of academic and applied psychology in the administrative, research, publication, and
supervisory domains of this discipline as a social institution.8

(2)

Cliquism and patronage

The pervasiveness of cliques and patronage in contemporary Russian science, specifically psychology, is manifested in the intellectual and organizational separation
of different cliques of scholars in conjunction with the authoritarianism of scientific administrators (who share several key appointments of different kinds) and the
servility and personal loyalty of the subordinate personnel. The consequence of this
is the more or less definite “clique self-identification” of psychologists and demarcation of the borderlines between “ours” and “others.” In his reminiscences of the
early 1980s Georgii Shchedrovitskii rendered a very characteristic description of
this phenomenon that he once heard from P. A. Shevarev, a psychologist of the old
school of the “pre-revolutionary generation.” Shevarev was talking about one particular group of Soviet psychologists, but it seems this characteristic applies to the
whole social system and intergroup dynamics in Soviet and contemporary Russian
psychology in general: “I do not like the students of Vygotskii. They are like black
market traders.9 This is a group for whom there are no truths, what they know is
only ‘ours’ or ‘not ours’, that is, somebody who does belong to the group or an
alien.” Further, Shchedrovitskii commented in these words:
I should say, the old man was absolutely right, and I had many occasions to
make sure that his judgment was correct, that is, that the group of Vygotsky’s
students lives and has always lived first and foremost following the principle
[of distinguishing] the “own” or the “alien,” and this is what determined their
relationships. The problem of the contents, the truth, has always receded into
the background. It did not cease altogether, but it has always been secondary.
The preservation of friendly relationships within the group and their struggle
against the others – this is what became their second nature. This is how they
were brought up, and it has always been a determining element of their group
culture. Furthermore, they never considered such factionalism as immoral
… I would not really dare to accuse these people now, and I do not want
everything I am saying on this matter to be interpreted as personal negative

The archetype of Soviet psychology

19

judgment – I am far from that. Furthermore, I do not even know if I can
express and am entitled to a judgment on such issues. This is a very delicate
matter. I only make a snapshot of this situation as a fact of the lifestyle and
culture of the people that they assimilated in those, apparently very complex,
perhaps life-threatening circumstances, in which they would turn out at some
points. But I would like to use this example in order to demonstrate the difference between the people of the preceding generation, who were brought
up in a totally different culture, on the basis of very different principles,
and the people of the new generation, who were brought up already in the
1920s–1930s. Theirs is a different morality, different logic, for them political
correctness, political interrelations, labeling “ours–the other” became dominant in principle and started determining their behavior. Perhaps we should
keep it in mind that they have long been under pressure and that they had
to live as a narrow group who defended themselves from offensives from all
sides, and therefore, when they reached a few positions of power, they used
this power in the spirit that they were brought up in. At this point, I only
underline the difference between the cultures of the two generations that
came one after the other – this is what must be known and must be understood. I am far from the position of accusation, but at the same time I think
that such a situation – and this is what I discovered on the basis of my personal
encounters with all these people, regular collaborative work with them, and
conflicts that ensued as a result – made them in principle incapable of scientific work. One cannot be a scholar, a thinker, with such values, such culture.
This is what is important, in my opinion.
(Shchedrovitskii, 2001, pp. 50–51)
An interesting manifestation of cliquism and its special variation is the characteristically Soviet nepotism in science. The relatively high social status of intellectuals
in the Soviet Union was finally established as the cultural norm in the post-World
War II period and, as a result, “along with improved standards of living came hereditary continuity. The Stalin-era professional strata – both the old holdovers and the
many more newcomers – reproduced their social position by passing on educational
achievement to their children, taking advantage of the end of forced social mobility”
(Tromly, 2014, pp. 8–9).The notable outcome of such “hereditary continuity” is the
phenomenon of distinctly Soviet “scientific dynasties” that in the case of psychology
comprise the continuity of up to four subsequent generations of psychologists, the
most remarkable example being the extended family of the descendants of Lev
Vygotsky.

(3)

Ritualism

The Soviet science of the “Stalinist model” is characterized by considerable ritualism that encompasses a wide range of scholars’ behaviors and practices and spreads

20

Anton Yasnitsky

from the top to the very bottom of the social pyramid. Thus, for instance, administrative control and supervision over the agency of Russian scholars is up even to
the present day performed by the administrative bodies that inherited the idiosyncratic Bolshevik newspeak phraseology and preserved it in their official titles such
as “Presidium,” “Sovet,” “Bureau,” and “Sekretariat.” Quite often this phraseology
is indicative of the preservation of institutions virtually in the same form as they
were introduced during the Stalinist era. One might counter this observation with
the entirely legitimate remark that bureaucratization of state control over science is
characteristic not only of the “Stalinist model” and not only in the Soviet Union or
in its legal and spiritual heir, the Russian Federation.
And yet, ritualism of the Soviet type – unlike that of industrially developed countries – is supported not only through the administrative apparatus or the phraseology
used in naming these organizations, but also through the actual functioning of these
supervisory and administrative bodies in concrete social contexts. Thus, the ritualism of scientific bureaucracy of the “Stalinist model” reveals itself in a wide range
of “carnivalesque” phenomena and events such as various jubilees of prominent
individuals, scientific groups, administrative units, and organizations that, according
to age-old Soviet tradition, keep being celebrated every ten or even, not unusually,
every five years in the form of a variety of celebratory events such as public meetings, round tables, banquets, jubilee conferences, etc., and are frequently inaugurated
in special publications, for instance edited volumes, special journal issues, conference
proceedings, etc. dedicated to these celebratory events. Frequently these and many
other publications of the kind are accompanied with obligatory number of “group
references” (ironically also referred to as “mass graves”) and “nomadic quotations,”
mandatory from the standpoint of the social norms, scientific micropolitics, and
diplomatic positioning of specific publications. It is likely that the practice and
extent of this technique differs depending on the specific scientific discipline and
the genre of academic writing, but, as it appears in informal and unsystematic observations of Russian scientific discourse, this practice is really widely spread among
Russian psychologists.
Finally, ritualism of the Soviet kind is also implemented in the form of idiosyncratic discourse, which does not allow characterizing its functional style as scientific
speech proper, but rather suggests that this is a hybrid between scientific style proper
and a journalist-propagandist style. Numerous examples of such hybrid discourse
include a number of linguistic and stylistic peculiarities such as (a) subjective valuative statements; (b) emotionally marked phraseology; (c) binary synonymic and
antonymic oppositions for denoting identical phenomena and processes depending
on whether the context of discussion is critical or laudatory; (d) argumentation
ad hominem; (e) differentiated attribution of historical figures in psychology, when
certain persons are referred to by their first name and patronymic in an abbreviated form; furthermore, in specific instances the last name of those individuals may
be dropped (compare the following examples: “S. Freud,” “A. A. Smirnov,” or “K.
Lewin” as opposed to “Lev Semenovich [Vygotskii]” or, more rarely, “Aleksandr
Romanovich [Luria],” etc.). All these stylistic and semantic peculiarities of Soviet

The archetype of Soviet psychology

21

scientific discourse most likely originate in the discourse of Bolshevik bureaucracy
of the 1920s, but in the most conspicuous forms they appear to have solidified in
the Soviet “scientific newspeak” of late Stalinism during the early Cold War period
(Gerovitch, 2002).

(4)

Gap between theory and practice

The gap between theory and practice, characteristic of late Imperial Russian psychology, was not overcome in the 1920s, despite the considerable efforts of a few
Soviet scholars, and was further aggravated during the 1930s – primarily because
of considerable ideological and political influences on Soviet psychological science.
The critique of this gap “from inside” is quite characteristic of the state of the art
in postwar Soviet psychology; of particular value is an indication of the ideological
nature of this gap:
Generally, in Leontiev’s conception – as well as in the speculations of many
other psychologists – the issue of the analysis of distinctly psychological process of development of needs – that is, the process of their transition into
qualitatively new forms – has remained beyond the scope of his discussion.
He attempts to solve this problem in the abstract-theoretical plane, with the
use of the arguments of istmat [i.e. “historical materialism”] in those instances
when he has not enough concrete psychological data. And this can be easily
explained given that there are only a few experimental studies in this field of
knowledge in which he could have grounded his work.
(Bozhovich, 1972, p. 14)
The history of Soviet psychology has passed through a series of periods of dominant theoretical frameworks, mandatory for all national research in this field. These
are the period of “Pavlovization” (i.e. aligning all psychological research with the
teaching of Ivan Pavlov, Nobel Prize winner in medicine and physiology) in the
1950s/early 1960s, followed by the period of “activity approach” (1960s/early
1970s), and then “systemic approach” (mid-1970s/1980s), the last two periods primarily associated with the names of A. N. Leontiev and B. F. Lomov, respectively.
All these periods were understood as variations of “Marxist psychology,” which, in
turn, came to an end in the late 1980s and was finally decisively and demonstratively
denounced, damned, condemned, and ridiculed only after the political collapse of
the Soviet Union in 1991 and the demise of the Communist Party of the Soviet
Union. Interestingly enough, all these fairly radical twists and turns of the “mandatory theoretical line” had minimal (if any) bearing on the course of empirical
research in psychological sciences in the Soviet Union. Indeed, the change of theoretical terminology and, therefore, the set of mandatory “nomadic quotes” on the
first pages of publications had virtually no impact on the actual studies. Therefore,
psychological theory in the Soviet Union of the postwar period is best understood

22

Anton Yasnitsky

as mainly belonging to the rhetorical space and the context of social legitimization
of empirical research rather than as the source of ideas and the springboard for
further experimentation. More often than not this conclusion fully applies to the
legacy of Vygotsky, whose writings in certain contexts are used as merely a source
of highly superficial and uncritical – not rarely, self-contradictory – quotations and
references, primarily for the purposes of social positioning and as a token of conformity to the academic standard of publication. Overall, the age-old gap between
theory and practice – demonstrated by great numbers of atheoretical empirical and,
in contrast, highly speculative theoretical publications in contemporary Russian
psychology – has been preserved to the present.

(5)

Intellectual and linguistic isolationism

Recent studies of the alleged “isolationism” of Soviet psychology have clearly demonstrated that the development of this scientific discipline during the 1920s and
1930s – despite brief xenophobic campaigns in society and science – was accompanied by intensive international exchanges and active cooperation between Soviet
psychologists and their peers abroad (for a discussion of one of the most prominent
cases in the history of transnational collaboration and cooperation see Chapter 9).10
In contrast, strong isolationist tendencies in national scientific policy drastically
increased during the period of late Stalinism and the early Cold War (i.e. in the late
1940s and early 1950s). Although this period was relatively short, its impact on postwar intellectual isolationism was considerable. Indeed, in the mid-1950s an official
Psychological Society of USSR was authorized at the highest level of the country’s
political and state leadership and was formed and affiliated with the International
Society of Psychological Sciences in 1957. By this time, Soviet psychologists – more
precisely, mainly a narrow circle of loyal official representatives of the export version of “Soviet psychology” who comprised renowned scholars and high-ranking
administrators of science and their associates – quite regularly traveled abroad to
scientific and organizational events all over the world. Rank and file researchers, though, also had, albeit somewhat limited, access to international scholarship
through a number of channels. Thus, for instance, the record-breaking attendance
of roughly 4,000 domestic and international scholars from forty-three different
countries – including those of the “capitalist West” and the Soviet “Eastern bloc”
alike – was registered at the XVIII International Congress of Psychology that was
triumphantly held in the midst of the Cold War in Moscow in 1966 (Rosenzweig,
Holtzman, Sabourin, & Bélanger, 2000).
Nevertheless, as paradoxical as it might appear, it was during the post-World War
II period that Soviet psychology increasingly turned into an isolationist, demonstratively “Soviet science” characterized by (a) the lack of reading knowledge in foreign
languages in the majority of the agents of the new population of Soviet researchers,
resulting in (b) their ignorance of the state of the art in contemporary scholarly
research in the West (rare exceptions such as Alexander Luria and his collaborators

The archetype of Soviet psychology

23

only prove the general rule), and (c) relatively small numbers of foreign studies
cited in publications within Soviet Union and, conversely, of Soviet publications
cited abroad. One of the most direct consequences of this situation was the fact
that Soviet psychologists largely failed to acquire the skills of statistical analysis of
experimental data and, more generally, did not catch up with the developing culture
of quantitative research and, even broader, the standards of research presentation in
the West.11
This description mainly applies to the situation in contemporary psychology
in Russia. Russian psychologists today, according to the new official priorities of
national scientific policy of the Russian Federation, quite often have strong budgetary and career incentives to present their research abroad – specifically, to publish
their studies in foreign languages in top-rank scholarly journals in the West – but
in the vast majority of cases for some reason or another are incapable of independent preparation of their scholarly materials for publication in accordance with the
standards of Western academic publishing.
Besides, the evidence of scientific intellectual isolationism is often demonstrated
