by the claims of “self-reliant,” “unique,” “exceptional” Russian psychology and by
the search for the special “Russian way” in science. One of the most remarkable and
radical – and astonishing – examples of the isolationism of the kind is the so-called
“Christian” or even, more precisely, “Russian Orthodox psychology” that is actively
developed, advocated for, and promoted by quite a number of contemporary psychologists and psychotherapists in the Russian Federation nowadays, including the
leadership of the Psychology Department of Moscow State University, traditionally considered one of the oldest and most prestigious psychological schools in the
country.

(6)

Cultism and hagiographies

The principle of nomenklatura appointments not only was essential for the whole
structure of Soviet society, but is also highly characteristic of the entire framework
of Soviet psychology as a discipline and social institution. From the early 1990s,
after the collapse of the Soviet Union and the elimination of the Communist Party’s
administrative apparatus of control over professional appointments, it is hardly
appropriate to refer to the nomenklatura proper, in its original sense. This change,
however, brought about greater independence for scientific-administrative cliques,
randomly freed from Party control from above.Thus, the void was largely filled with
the clique principle of professional appointments.
These large-scale social and historical processes had a direct bearing on the historiography of Soviet and Russian psychology as a powerful mechanism that was
used in rebuilding the new ideology to fill the lacuna of “loyalty to the Party.”
Thus, the new ideology’s motto can be described as “loyalty to the tradition,” where
by tradition is meant a primitive version of an “intellectual history of science,”
fairly idealized, seemingly depoliticized, and clearly separated from its original

24

Anton Yasnitsky

socio-historical context. According to the logic of administrators of science after the
collapse of the Soviet Union, it is faithfulness to the tradition that serves as the criterion for a future employee’s evaluation and as a guiding principle of professional
selection. In practice, however, this results in even further separation between the
cliques and in hiring of one’s “own” graduates (i.e. selecting loyal “ours” and rejecting the suspicious “others” and the “aliens”), which further leads to the decrease of
intellectual cross-pollination and competition between different intellectual traditions and, at the same time, to an increase of intellectual degeneration and corruption in the system of academic psychology in the country.
Therefore, the historiography of Soviet and Russian psychology in contemporary Russia serves an important social function of constructing an ideology that, in
turn, is used as a foundation in the processes of policy-making, including reproduction of scientific elites, especially in the human and social sciences. In practice,
over the past few decades we observe an increase of the hagiographic tradition
in historiography, manifested in the proliferation of various cults of “Founding
Fathers” and their “scientific schools,” the traditions of “sacred names” of “Our
Teachers” and their loyal “brothers-in-arms” and devoted students. Among the
most important instruments and techniques for creating cultist hagiographies in
the historiography of science – such as the historiography of Russian and Soviet
psychology – and, therefore, the criteria for detecting such hagiographies in scientific discourse are:
(a) heroic depiction of the historical past;
(b) emotionality and ethical valuation of discussion, quite often involving propagandist and militarist phraseology;
(c) binary polarization of historical characters and protagonists into “the forces of
good” and “the forces of evil” and depiction of historical processes as the struggle between the two forces;
(d) demonization of the “evil” and the victimization of the “good,” who, in fact,
quite in the tradition of medieval Lives of the saints (i.e. hagiographies proper) are
presented as “martyrs” and “innocent victims” of persecutions.
Some examples of uncritical and propagandist constructing of the historical past are
presented by the cults of G. I. Chelpanov, G. G. Shpet, the “cult of the Psychological
Institute” (in Moscow) or the “cult of the Department of Psychology of Moscow
State University.” The latter is frequently supported by numerous publications –
books, collected volumes, and journal articles – on the so-called “activity theory,”
most directly associated with the names of Aleksei N. Leontiev and his clique as
the core group of Vygotsky’s students and intellectual heirs. These and many other
similar but less significant “cults of the scientists,” regardless of how little related to
each other they might appear, follow essentially the same structural model and use
virtually identical rhetorical tools of an idiosyncratic victim-heroic narrative about
a “Great Man” or a “Great Institution,” depending on the propagandist and career
purposes of their proponents and advocates.12

The archetype of Soviet psychology

25

Revisionist Vygotskian narrative rewritten against the
background of the history of “Stalinist science”
And yet, the most famous “cult” in the history of Soviet and Russian psychology
that spread virtually all over the globe is arguably the “Vygotsky cult,” forcefully
promoted in scholarly literature in contemporary Russia (and Eastern Europe) and
abroad – primarily in North America and, perhaps less prominently, in Western
Europe, Australia, and South America.13 Quite curiously, the cult emerged as a result
of diverse, complex domestic and international activities across state borders and scientific disciplines. Still, the domestic context of the “Vygotsky cult” as it developed
in its natural environment of the “Stalinist model” of science in the Soviet Union
seems to have largely determined the many problems that are now faced by the
revisionist movement in Vygotsky studies. Many of these are addressed in this book.
First, a cult requires a figure of a solitary Founding Father on the very top of
the social and intellectual pyramid constituted by academic worshippers of the cult.
Therefore, the tradition of a “Great Man” history is countered here by a holistic
approach that takes into consideration the whole group of scholars behind a theory or an idea, albeit never leaving out of focus individual agents of history. Most
important from this perspective are the collaborative projects of the Vygotsky–Luria
circle (see Chapter 2), the extended team of German-American psychologists,
the proponents of Gestalt psychology, and the overlap between these two major
groups of scholars that are discussed here as a Soviet-German-American project of
“cultural-historical Gestalt psychology” (see Chapters 6, 8, and 9).
Second, the cliquism, hagiography, and ritualism of the “Stalinist model” and its
contemporary Russian and Western derivatives requires the reign of authority, mystery, and miracle. None of these might possibly impress the revisionists, whose chief
method is critical, meticulous, and rigorous analysis of the most reliable empirical
data. Particularly illustrative of this approach and of this kind of scholarship are
textological and historical reconstructions of actual Vygotsky texts, published and
unpublished, and analysis of their contemporary relevance and theoretical importance, such as in Chapters 4, 5, and 6.
Third, provincialism and isolationism is solidly based on keeping the borders of
various sorts. The new scholarship presented here is quite different from the traditional in its focus on transnational and cross-disciplinary processes, which is generally characteristic of many new revisionist works, but is particularly explicit in comparative histories of the development of “Vygotskian tradition” in psychological
and allied sciences in the USSR (Chapters 2, 3), and in the West (Chapter 7). These
are followed by the histories of a truly transnational Soviet-German-American scientific project that developed in the interwar period in the Soviet Union, Western
Europe, North America, and even, to smaller extent, the Near East and Central Asia
(Chapters 8 and, particularly, 9).
Fourth, cultist traditions in science need foundational myths – histories of their
origin, legends about heroes, and the veneration of victims and sacred objects. The
critical scholarship represented by the Vygotskian revisionism tolerates neither the

26

Anton Yasnitsky

“heroes” nor the “martyrs.” Thus, for instance, an attempt at “devictimization” and
overcoming the “sacrificial cult of Vygotsky” is made in Chapter 3 of this book.
Another, similar effort at “deheroization” is undertaken in Chapter 8, which scrutinizes a heroic story of Luria’s expedition to Central Asia that has always been among
the foundational myths of the whole field of “Vygotskian” studies worldwide.
Fifth, this book is not merely about historical, textological, and theoretical
research. In other words, the revisionist project not only generates critical studies
that reveal histories or revaluate commonly held ideas, but also provides ideas for
future cutting-edge research in human sciences. By virtue of the combination of
“critical” and “constructive” strands in Vygotskian revisionism the new scholarship
bridges the gap between theory and practice, and sorts out the most valuable in
the diverse and fairly inconsistent Vygotsky legacy, still potentially of interest in a
contemporary context (Chapter 4), suggests some ideas to international brokers
and academic translators (Yasnitsky, 2012d), and presents a conceptual and terminological framework for talking about the most important topics in the last period
of Vygotsky’s scholarship: the topics of consciousness, sense, and meaning (see the
Epilogue).
All this new scholarship seems to be instrumental in helping us overcome the old
“Stalinist” and cultist model of science that is still dominant in Russian and Western
“Vygotskiana” alike and in helping us move forward, to new heights, into the future.
Or, on the contrary, the revisionism may be said to direct us back, to the past of the
revolutionary pre-Stalinist Bolshevik project of radical social and cultural reconstruction. Both options are good and, regardless of the phraseology and metaphors
that we might use, words as such change virtually nothing. After all, real action still
remains the criterion of the truth, even in such a seemingly highly abstract and theoretical enterprise as scientific research.

2
UNITY IN DIVERSITY
The Vygotsky–Luria circle as an informal
personal network of scholars
Anton Yasnitsky

During his lifetime Lev Vygotsky (1896–1934) failed to establish himself as a leader
of an institutionalized scientific school in the human sciences associated with
his name, unlike, for instance, his contemporaries and compatriots Ivan Pavlov
(1849–1936) or Vladimir Bekhterev (1857–1927). Both Pavlov and Bekhterev not
only founded scientific theories and rival research schools, but also set up institutionalized “factories” for massive production of scientific knowledge (Todes, 2002,
2014). However, Vygotsky was fairly well known, and for his contemporaries the
name of Vygotsky was associated with his theory of human development that was
at different times variously referred to as “instrumental,” or “cultural” psychology, or
a “theory of cultural development,” or a “theory of cultural development of higher
mental (psychical) functions” (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991). This terminological
diversity and fluidity reflects the constant search for the adequate descriptors for the
original research program and theoretical innovation of integrative human developmental science introduced by Vygotsky and his associates in a wide range of fields
and disciplines. This chapter explores the dense network of scholars associated with
Vygotsky during his lifetime, the interpersonal and professional interrelationships
of Vygotsky and his group, and the dynamics of group change in the interwar
period (1924–41).

The “school of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria” narrative
The first written contributions to the emergent historiography of Vygotsky’s
psychology (that perhaps tell us more about their authors than the subject of their
papers) appeared as early as in the mid-1930s, in obituaries and memorial speeches
written or delivered by his closest collaborators soon after Vygotsky’s death (A. N.
Leontiev, 1934; A. R. Luria, 1935a, 1935b). However, the core of the now widely

28

Anton Yasnitsky

disseminated narrative about “the school of Vygotsky” was not formed until the
period from mid-1970 to the early 1980s.This is when a pile of publications on the
history of the “school” came out, including a series of memoirs, public presentations, and interviews with the protagonists of the history of Vygotskian psychology
in the Soviet Union (A. N. Leontiev, 1986; A. N. Leontiev & Leontiev, 2005; A. N.
Leontiev & Luria, 1976; A. R. Luria, 1979, 1982) as well as a number of publications by their followers and students (Davydov & Radzikhovskii, 1980, 1981,
1985; Radzikhovskii, 1979). Quite a few of these appeared around the time of the
death of Alexander Luria (in 1977) and Aleksei N. Leontiev (in 1979) (Elkonin,
1983, 1984; Galperin, 1983, 1984; Zaporozhets & Elkonin, 1979), which might
account for the laudatory and elevated rhetorical style of these publications, and
the sometimes uncritical and rather heroic depiction of the protagonists.Two edited
memorial volumes on the scientific legacy of the two scholars are especially notable as the two foundational and comprehensive collections of articles on the life
and scientific legacy of Luria and Leontiev authored by their former colleagues
and students (Khomskaya, Tsvetkova, & Zeigarnik, 1982; Zaporozhets, Zinchenko,
Ovchinnikova, & Tikhomirov, 1983).
The stream of these and related publications formed the solid corpus of what we might refer to as the “canonical” account about the “school
of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria” that has several key narrative elements. The core of
the narrative is the story about the 1924 meeting in Moscow of the three founding
fathers, Lev Vygotsky, Alexander Luria, and Aleksei Leontiev, and the formation of
the first alliance of the troika (“the three”). Then, a second alliance was formed by
the five first-generation students of “the triumvirate,” named the piaterka (“the five”
in Russian), which included Alexander Zaporozhets, Lidia Bozhovich, Roza Levina,
Natalia Morozova, and Lia Slavina. According to this canonical narrative, the group
of these eight individuals constituted the “school of Vygotsky.” Typically, this narrative does not provide us with any information about the later fate of the piaterka, but
we are told that at the end of 1931 a group of Vygotsky’s followers that included
a part of the troika (Luria and Leontiev) and a part of the piaterka (Zaporozhets
and Bozhovich) left Moscow for Kharkov, then capital of Soviet Ukraine, to start
their work in a new organizational unit, the Sector of Psychology at the Ukrainian
Psychoneurological Academy (UPNA). In Kharkov, the Muscovites met with a
group of local scholars, including Pietr Gal’perin, Pietr Zinchenko,Vladimir Asnin,
Ksenia Khomenko, and Grigorii Lukov, who formed an alliance that later came to
be known as the Kharkov group or the Kharkov school of psychology. According
to this narrative, Luria headed the Psychology Sektor (Section) at UPNA, whereas a
position as Head of the Otdel (Department) of Child and Genetic Psychology was
offered to Leontiev. However, Luria reportedly soon returned to Moscow; and after
his departure Leontiev became the Head of the Psychology Section and supervised
virtually all administrative and research work from then on. A series of recent publications added the name of Mark Lebedinskii as one of those scholars invited from
Moscow to Kharkov, as well as other minor details, but the structure of the narrative,
the sequence, and the interpretation of the main events remain basically identical

Vygotsky–Luria circle 29

across the vast majority of “traditional” publications on this topic (cf. Ivanova, 1995;
A. A. Leontiev, 1983; A. A. Leontiev & Leontiev, 2013; A. A. Leontiev, Leontiev, &
Sokolova, 2005; Sokolova, 2007, 2011).
The history of troika da piaterka (“the three and the five,” in Russian) and the
interpretation of the history of Vygotskian psychology along the lines of A. N.
Leontiev’s so-called “activity theory” is not the only interpretation of the history
of the Vygotskian research project after Vygotsky’s death. A number of researchers
questioned the validity of the construct “the school of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria”
from time to time, and doubted the legitimacy of the claims of the continuity
of Vygotsky’s original framework in the research program of activity-oriented
psychological studies. For instance, Luchkov and Pevzner (the latter, as it happened, another of the first generation of Vygotsky’s students) argued that “we
have every reason to doubt the appropriateness of the phrase the activity theory
of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria, as well as the juxtaposition of these three names with
respect to this theory. Futhermore, in the school of Leontiev, it has recently become
usual to talk, generally, about the school of Vygotsky –Luria–Leontiev … which is also
illegitimate, in our opinion” (Luchkov & Pevzner, 1981).
However, the full-scale revision of the troika da piaterka version of the history
of the Vygotsky school started around the end of the 1980s/early 1990s and seems
to have coincided with the processes of perestroika in the Soviet Union. The traditional history of Vygotskian psychology evoked much criticism from authors
who questioned Leontiev’s role as leader of the school of Vygotskian scholars
and challenged the claim that Leontiev’s “activity theory” was a direct continuation of Vygotsky’s theory of cultural development. The critique of the “official”
historiography can often be recognized by the authors’ insistence on the split
between Vygotsky on the one hand and Leontiev and the Kharkov group that
reportedly departed from Vygotsky’s research program on the other. Another characteristic feature of the “revisionist” historiography of Vygotskian psychology is
the authors’ insistence on the rupture in Vygotsky’s theory development in the
works of his students and followers – despite recognizing the continued influence
of Vygotsky on his students (Kozulin, 1984, 1986, 1990; Orlov, 2003; Van der Veer
& Valsiner, 1991). Some scholars even discuss Leontiev’s and Luria’s personal betrayal
of Vygotsky and “his case” that allegedly took place sometime around the end of
1933, approximately half a year before Vygotsky’s death (Van der Veer & Valsiner,
1991;Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996). However, despite considerable criticism of the
“school of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria” narrative, scholars in favor of the traditionalist interpretation of the history of Vygotskian psychology are still fairly active and
the troika discourse is quite notable in various publications to date.

The Vygotsky–Luria circle as informal personal network
Notably, the vast majority of these publications follow the tradition of “Great
Men” histories, or, in other words, represent “hagiographic” historiography that is

30

Anton Yasnitsky

characterized by distinctly celebratory (or, otherwise, accusatory and denunciatory)
accounts of the history of ideas against the background of the personal life-stories
of a few protagonists, typically, Vygotsky, Leontiev, or Luria. In contrast, the present account is inspired by the idea that, “remarkably, Vygotsky’s approach de facto
embodies, in its real life history, the very theoretical principles central to it, such
as the inseparability of knowledge and action, theory and practice, and the collaborative nature of cognition” (Stetsenko & Arievitch, 2004, p. 58). This account
of the Vygotsky–Luria circle contrasts with the traditional narrative of the school
of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria by investigating the “cultural-historical school as a
collaborative, multi-generational, value-laden, and ideologically-driven investigative
project that stretched far beyond the confines of science in its traditional mentalist
guise” (Stetsenko, 2003, p. 96). Recent literature in Vygotsky studies increasingly
stresses essentially the collective, dialogic, and collaborative nature of the “Vygotsky
project” that was born in and existed through lively discussions with a number of
people, who
formed the so-called Vygotsky Circle, which included several brilliant women,
and they carried out research projects collectively … [T]he ubiquitously collaborative nature of Vygotsky’s project in general must be emphasized, especially because it has often been underestimated or even ignored in previous
accounts of his heritage.
(Stetsenko, 2004, pp. 502–503)
Thus, this account follows the lead of those researchers who underline the
importance of informal personal networks for understanding the processes of science development. Mark B. Adams, who introduced the notion of informal networks in the discourse of the historiography of science, clarifies:
Here, I am not referring to anything arcane or technical – not to the ‘networks’ of the sociologist, dynamist, or social studies theoretician, much less
the computer specialist – but to the looser, more evocative meaning the word
has come to have in everyday language, one familiar to every kind of historian: personal networks. A personal network is a much looser, less coherent ‘structure’ than either an institution or a discipline. Developed out of
extended family, old school ties, mutual experience, hobbies, private passions,
and shared interests, such networks involve ramifying contacts that are multiple and complex – as are all free associations that underline civic society.
They can also form ‘nodes’ or ‘ganglia’ where various networks interface and
new connections are made – sometimes in the form of informal circles, private societies, clubs, salons, soirées, and the like, sometimes in more organized forms, ranging from things we might call ‘movements’ to interest groups,
political organizations, and even ‘mafias’. And some of these, in turn, might
eventually gain further structure as would-be disciplines or proto-institutions.
(M. B. Adams, 2001, p. 261)

Vygotsky–Luria circle 31

The informal personal network of Vygotsky included a lot of individuals, many
of whom are not necessarily related to his scientific enterprise. Tracking down the
agency of a network of such individuals is obviously a very ambitious goal.Therefore,
this chapter focuses on a smaller subset of the larger network: the people who either
directly collaborated with Vygotsky or made a considerable impact on him through
personal contacts, discussions, and, possibly, correspondence.This “node” of the network of thinkers and scholars is discussed here as the Vygotsky circle of those who –
willingly or unwillingly – contributed to Vygotsky’s research program and whose
impact on Vygotskian psychology can be discerned. Apparently, in many instances
at the same time the reverse impact of Vygotsky on these individuals and their
research agenda is even more notable. And still, even with its focus on a relatively
smaller group of Vygotsky’s collaborators, this study is almost necessarily somewhat
limited and fragmentary – much as the available data are limited and fragmentary.
Therefore, this chapter presents a systematic – yet somewhat superficial – attempt at
reconstructing the composition of the Vygotsky circle, the range of scholarly activities of its participants, and the dynamics of intergroup change within this group
of scholars. Given Vygotsky’s closest association and collaboration with Alexander
Luria (1902–77) that lasted throughout his entire career in Moscow in 1924–34,
it seems even more appropriate to designate the hub of the larger “Vygotsky network” as the “Vygotsky–Luria circle,” and therefore both expressions will be used
here interchangeably.
According to the traditional historiographical narrative, Vygotsky’s psychology
was first developed by the members of Vygotsky’s troika. In contrast, it appears that
we have no compelling evidence of the troika, the trio of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria,
during the last decade of Vygotsky’s lifetime (1924–34), and it is more appropriate to single out the duo of Luria and Vygotsky and their numerous personal
and professional connections and interrelations. There are a number of reasons for
selecting the duo of Luria–Vygotsky as the intellectual and organizational core of
a team developing cultural-historical theory in the 1920s–30s, and if the number
of publications alone is indicative, then from the sheer number of collaborative
publications it is clear that Vygotsky and Luria were closely connected indeed.
For instance, in the mid-1920s they wrote two introductions to the Russian translations of psychological books (Vygotskii & Luria, 1925, 1926); then, they participated in preparing educational materials and handbooks (Artemov, Bernstein,
Vygotskii, Dobrynin, & Luria, 1927; Artemov, Vygotskii, Dobrynin, & Luria,
1927); co-authored a paper on egocentric speech that Luria presented at the IX
International Congress of Psychology held at Yale University, which Vygotsky was
unable to attend (Vygotsky & Luria, 1930), a book (Vygotskii & Luria, 1930), and
a book chapter that was to appear in Murchison’s Handbook of child psychology in
1930 (Vygotsky & Luria, 1930/1994). Finally, their collaborative book was written
at the end of the 1920s, but remained unpublished for several decades.1 In contrast,
the only paper that came out under the names of both Vygotsky and Leontiev
was the introduction to the book on psychology of memory written by the latter
under the supervision of the former (A. N. Leontiev, 1931). It is also interesting

32

Anton Yasnitsky

how contemporaries – mostly outsiders and critics – perceived and attributed
Vygotsky’s group and theory. Thus, for instance, Talankin in his speech given in
June of 1931 in Kharkov attacks the group of Vygotsky and Luria and calls for a
Marxist critique of their psychologica theory (Talankin, 1931, 2000). Around the
same time, another critique of the “theory of cultural development” came from
Feofanov, for whom it is clearly associated with just two individuals: Vygotsky and
Luria (Feofanov, 1932, 2000). Somewhat later, in 1934, Razmyslov severely criticized Vygotsky’s and Luria’s cultural-historical theory of psychology (Razmyslov,
1934, 2000). Finally, in 1936 an author that identified (or rather did not identify)
himself with the just two letters G. F. – which presumably stand for philosophy
professor Filipp Georgiev (A. A. Leontiev et al., 2005) – also mentions the school
of Vygotsky and Luria in the context of a critical discussion in the light of the
Party decree of July, 1936 (F. G., 1936). Judging from their personal correspondence and the facts of their biographies the connections between Vygotsky and
Luria remained very close until the very last days of Vygotsky’s life. For instance,
in the fall of 1931 Vygotsky and Luria resumed their studies in medicine: they
both got accepted as students in the Medical Department in Kharkov, and were
spending much time together preparing for their exams (E. A. Luria, 1994). At the
same time they were involved in intensive research in clinical settings conducted in
parallel in Kharkov and Moscow (Vygotskii, 2004;Vygotsky, 2007).
This circle of scholars as a unit of analysis comprises only those individuals
who were related to both its leaders and excludes people occasionally involved in
the episodic independent projects of the two protagonists. The data used in this
study include mainly scientific publications of the interwar period and, to a somewhat smaller extent, archival data and materials of correspondence between the
members of the Vygotsky circle and some second-hand sources, such as postwar
research overviews and memoirs. The development of the Vygotsky circle – rather,
Vygotsky–Luria circle – is traced here throughout the period 1924–41 and has been
divided into several phases.

Phase one (1924–1927): prehistory of the Vygotsky–Luria circle
The first phase of Vygotsky’s circle starts with Vygotsky’s move from Gomel’ to
Moscow, and is characterized by his initial activities at the Institute of Psychology
and in various defectological institutions. During this period he made his only foreign trip, in the summer of 1925, to a conference in London on the problems of
the deaf (Van der Veer & Zavershneva, 2011). After his return from that European
trip, in the fall of 1925,Vygotsky was hospitalized with tuberculosis and spent several months of 1925–6 in hospital. At the end of 1925, on the basis of his unpublished dissertation The psychology of art he was awarded a doctoral degree without
defense, in absentia.Virtually all of 1926 Vygotsky spent in hospital or, subsequently,
at home recovering from the disease in a state of temporary professional disability,
officially recognized as an invalid. During this period Vygotsky was also writing his

Vygotsky–Luria circle 33

Sakharov
Leontiev

Varshava

Zankov

Lebedinskii
Vygotsky—Luria

Solov’ev

Averbukh

Daniushevskii

Bernstein
Gellerstein

FIGURE 2.1

The pre-history of the Vygotsky circle in 1924–1927

methodological work on the crisis in psychology, but never finished the oeuvre and
abandoned it without even attempting its publication as such (Zavershneva, 2012b;
Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b).
Aleksander Luria seems to have been instrumental in inviting Vygotsky to
Moscow and very soon the new scientific alliance of Vygotsky and Luria was
formed. The two closely collaborated and as early as the mid-1920s published several co-authored pieces. Apart from Luria,Vygotsky’s major academic contacts and
collaborators during this time were his Moscow colleagues and graduate students
Leonid Zankov, Ivan Solov’ev, Leonid Sakharov, and Boris Varshava, and relatively
short-term collaborators at the Moscow Institute of Psychology, most notably
Nikolai Bernstein.
Not much is known yet about the professional interconnections between
Bernstein and the Vygotsky–Luria project, but on the basis of an account in
Bernstein’s biography (Feigenberg, 2004), we may assume, at least, fairly strong personal ties between them (for a discussion of Bernstein’s likely involvement with
the “cultural-historical Gestalt psychology” project of Luria, Vygotsky, and their
German-American peers see Chapter 9). These ties were later reinforced during
World War II when a group of Vygotsky’s associates launched a series of clinical
studies in wartime hospitals on the rehabilitation of motor skills in the wounded
and speech disorders of patients with brain lesions. This group included, among
others, Solomon Gellerstein, which might also be a possible – yet unverified – indicator of his affinity to the Vygotsky–Luria group in the 1920–30s.
Another major area of Vygotsky’s activity was defectological work, which was
done chiefly in contact with Daniushevskii, his acquaintance from Gomel’, who

34

Anton Yasnitsky

occupied a high administrative position in the Narkompros of the RSFSR (later
renamed the Ministry of Education of the RSFSR) and was most likely instrumental in helping Vygotsky get a middle-level governmental position as supervisor of
the defectological sector within this administrative body in charge of the social and
legal protection of children and adolescents (in Russian: sotsial’no-pravovaia okhrana
nesovershennoletnikh, SPON).
In turn, Luria and Leontiev were continuing their investigation of affect by using
Luria’s combined motor method, a research project initiated by Luria in which
Vygotsky did not seem to have been particularly interested or to have participated –
at least, not during his first years in Moscow. Mark Lebedinskii, a medical doctor, was another important Luria collaborator, who implemented Luria’s research
method in clinical settings treating patients with nervous and psychiatric disorders.
Another of Luria’s associates was Roza Averbukh, a medical doctor from Kazan’
and, like Luria, a former member of Kazan’ Psychoanalytic Society, who left Kazan’
for Moscow and there joined the Russian Psychoanalytic Society. It was during this
period of the mid-1920s that Luria occupied the position of the secretary of this
Society and regularly reported on the Society’s activities in his numerous publications, in German, that came out in the West in the 1920s.
Among Vygotsky’s main research interests in Moscow in 1924–7 was the investigation of dominant reactions in collaboration with his students Zankov, Solov’ev,
Sakharov, and, episodically, Gagaeva (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, pp. 33, 128), and
defectological work – primarily of an administrative and propagandist nature – with
Daniushevskii, Zankov, and others. Also, during this period Vygotsky initiated his
relatively well-known research on concept formation in collaboration with his student Leonid Sakharov (1900–28) (Sakharov, 1928, 1930, 1994). Sakharov’s research
was most likely completed by the end of 1927: according to van derVeer andValsiner,
on January 1, 1928 Sakharov presented his findings at the Pedological Congress in
Moscow, and on May 10, 1928 he died under unknown circumstances, possibly
committing suicide (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, p. 96). Another of Vygotsky’s
collaborators during his first years in Moscow was Boris Varshava (1900–27), whose
premature and tragic death in July 1927 Vygotsky mourned in the introduction
to their only collaborative publication, a Psychological dictionary that came out, after
considerable delay, in 1931. In retrospect, Vygotsky’s words about Varshava, written
on the occasion of the publication of their collaborative work, seem to be quite
characteristic of his own life and scientific legacy:
Varshava was just at the beginning of his path as a psychologist-researcher
and a writer. He made only first steps on this road that was interrupted by
his death. But these first steps left no doubt to anybody who knew him as to
what kind of road was waiting for him. This road was one of creative scientific
work and a struggle for the complete reconstruction of psychological science
on a new methodological foundation, and his choice for this road was very
early and decisive.
(Vygotskii, 1931a, p. 3)

Vygotsky–Luria circle 35

We tend to measure the extent of the loss of those who worked along with
us by the extent of what they achieved, accomplished, completed. And this is
correct. But the opposite is correct too: to measure by what remains unrealized.This measure will be great if we apply it to the death of Boris Efimovich
Varshava. What he could have achieved is incomparably more than what he
has achieved. The measure of what he was destined to carry out and what is
left unrealized is greater than the measure of his achievements. And this is the
true meaning of the tragedy of his death.
(Vygotskii, 1931a, pp. 5–6)
The projects undertaken by many of Vygotsky’s colleagues and collaborators in
the years 1924–7 do not leave the impression of being interrelated pieces of a unified research program. Indeed, this period of Vygotsky’s thought development is
characterized by a search for a research methodology and a theoretical foundation.
However, by the end of this period several disparate lines converged and became
increasingly intertwined, leading to the emergence of new research alliances and
new teams of collaborators.

Phase two (1927–1931): Vygotsky–Luria circle formation
The larger Vygotsky and Luria circle includes all their co-workers and likeminded individuals of that time, and their acquaintances; the exchange of ideas
in a circle normally takes place through more or less informal communications
and personal discussions (e.g. Vygotsky’s and Luria’s “internal conferences” and
their extensive domestic and international correspondence). The periphery of the
circle, however, is formed by the individuals who cannot fully qualify as students
or collaborators of either Vygotsky or Luria, but whose work and ideas must
have been very important for the development of Vygotsky’s theory and whose
influence on Vygotskian psychology – despite the lack of any formal records such
as scholarly references to published works or co-authored papers – must have
been pervasive and profound. In-depth analysis of the interrelations between
the Vygotsky–Luria group and these peripheral members of the circle remains
beyond the scope of this discussion. Yet, a digression is needed in order to illustrate the complexity and the multitude of the professional connections of this
scientific alliance, if only to delimit the “inner circle” of Vygotsky and Luria.
A number of individuals are of great importance for the complete reconstruction of Vygotsky’s “peripheral circle” of his “invisible college.” Among these
are Soviet intellectuals and researchers Nikolai Bernstein, Solomon Gellerstein,
Sergei Rubinstein, Dimitry Uznadze (alias: Usnadze) and German-American
psychologists Kurt Koffka and Kurt Lewin to mention but a few (for the discussion of Lewin’s influence and Soviet-German-American transnational communications and collaborative projects see Chapters 8 and 9). Let us consider just
one example: Sergei Eisenstein.2

Child studies
FIGURE 2.2

The Vygotsky–Luria circle in 1927–1931

Sergei Eisenstein (1898–1948), a revolutionary Soviet film director and film
theorist, first met Luria sometime around the end of 1925/beginning of 1926,
soon after the theatrical release of Eisenstein’s Bronenosets Potemkin (The battleship
Potemkin) in order to discuss psychological problems of the “theory and psychology
of expressiveness” that were of great interest to him at that time. Luria most likely
introduced Vygotsky and Eisenstein to each other, and the three eventually became
close friends (E. A. Luria, 1994, p. 121). In the late 1920s, Eisenstein – along with
Vygotsky and Luria – participated in psychological studies on human movement
under hypnosis (in December, 1928) (E. A. Luria, 1994, p. 122) and the investigation of the phenomenal memory of the famous mnemonist Shereshevskii and in his
notes of 1937 thus reflected on this case:
He [Shereshevskii] presents a unique example of a man who – along with
the absolutely full and perfect development of a normal human being – preserved as well the entire set of early mechanisms of perception. Over the
years, professors Vygotsky and Luria were doing a wide range of psychological
observations and experiments with him. In turn, I managed to have a series
of most interesting discussions with him about the problem that interested
me: synaesthesia that was represented in him in utmost completeness (in 1928
and in 1933).
(Eisenstein, 2000, p. 385)

Vygotsky–Luria circle 37

In the 1950s, Shereshevskii recalled the voices of Vygotsky and Eisenstein and
described Vygotsky’s “crumbly, yellow voice” and Eisenstein’s voice as an “entire
composition, a bouquet … listening to him, it was as though a flame with fibers protruding from it was advancing right toward me” (record of November, 1951) (A. R.
Luria, 1987, p. 24). The discovery of the only surviving manuscript of Vygotsky’s
doctoral dissertation Psikhologiia iskusstva (The psychology of art) in Eisenstein’s personal archives attests to the fact that the contacts between these three individuals in the 1920s, before Eisenstein’s trip to Europe and North America (August,
1929–May, 1932), were remarkably intense. In the early 1930s, after Eisenstein’s
return to the Soviet Union, another research project was conceived: the film director and theorist Sergei Eisenstein, the philologist academician Nikolai Marr, and
the two psychologists Lev Vygotsky and Alexander Luria planned to systematically
analyze the “problems of the nascent language of cinematography.” However, the
untimely death of Vygotsky (in June, 1934) and Marr (in December, 1934) put an
end to this ongoing research (Eisenstein, 2002a, p. 136). Still, there is evidence that
the group produced – or, at least, planned to produce – several scholarly papers
that would present their findings to a Western audience. Thus, according to the
announcements of 1932–3 in the section “Some Forthcoming Articles” of the journal Character and Personality, several papers by Russian authors were scheduled to
come out, specifically the articles by Vygotsky (two papers titled “Comparative
psychology of the child” and “Strong memory and the structure of personality”),
Luria’s “Self-analysis and social conduct,” and Eisenstein’s “The dynamics of facial
expression (an experimental study in cinematography).” For unknown reasons, publication never took place, and we do not know the whereabouts of these manuscripts or even if they were ever submitted to the journal.The connections between
the three of them seem to have been fairly strong, so even after Vygotsky’s death
and Luria’s relatively short – intensive albeit intermittent – tenure in Kharkov’s
Ukrainian Psychoneurological Academy in 1931–4, personal and professional contacts between Eisenstein and Luria continued.The evidence for that can be found in
Eisenstein’s diaries, for instance, the entry of December 29, 1938 about the schizophrenic case of “Tioma” that Luria demonstrated to Eisenstein on December 26,
1938 (Eisenstein, 2004, p. 672) or his reference to the experiments with Luria in
his diary entry of July 24, 1940 (Eisenstein, 2002b, p. 431) (for further discussion of
the Eisenstein–Vygotsky–Luria intellectual interdependence see Bulgakowa, 2014).
And yet, despite the great importance of the peripheral interconnections with a
wide range of intellectuals at home and worldwide, the work of the “inner circle”
no doubt was even more important for the development of the original contribution of this group to theoretical and empirical psychology. From the end of the
1920s, for a number of organizational, social, political, professional, and – last but not
the least – personal reasons, research in the “inner circle(s)” of Vygotsky and Luria
was conducted by several relatively independent groups. The extent of independence and isolation of these groups varied.
This period was quite productive for Vygotsky, Luria, and their group’s experimental research. Thus, Vygotsky continued the studies on concept formation that

38

Anton Yasnitsky

had been interrupted by Leonid Sakharov’s sudden death in May, 1928. Vygotsky’s
occasional collaborators Kotelova and Pashkovskaia undertook this research, and the
study was completed by the end of 1929. As follows from the final footnote to the
posthumous publication of Sakharov’s paper that was read several months before
his death at the Pedological Congress in Moscow, the study had been completed
by the time of this publication, and a monograph was being prepared for the press
(Sakharov, 1930, 1994). The monograph, though, was never published, and we do
not know for sure if it was ever actually written.
Also during the late 1920s, Luria and Vygotsky initiated research in clinical
settings, typically in collaboration with medical specialists (neurologists, psychiatrists, etc.), such as Mark Lebedinskii, Roza Averbukh, Lia Geshelina, or Marina
Eidinova. This research was typically conducted under the auspices of the G. I.
Rossolimo Clinic of Nervous Diseases of the First Moscow University and the
Department of Clinical Psychology at the Academy of Communist Education
(AKV) (Lebedinsky & Luria, 1929; A. R. Luria, 1929b, 1931a, 1932c).The alliance
of Luria with Lebedinskii seems to have been instrumental in sustained clinical
research in the Vygotsky circle from the end of the 1920s onwards. Also, it was
during this period that Lebedinskii conducted major research on the development of “higher motor functions” in children that was published as a book in
1931 (Lebedinskii, 1931).
Another group of “Vygotskians” was formed during the period and included
the five students of the Department of Pedology at the Second Moscow University
(1925–30), later known as the piaterka (“the five”) of Aleksander Zaporozhets, Lidia
Bozhovich, Roza Levina, Natalia Morozova, and Lia Slavina.This group, along with
the troika (“the three”) of their teachers at the University – Vygotsky, Luria, and
Leontiev – was involved in a series of experimental studies conducted under the
aegis of either the Second Moscow University or the Psychological Laboratory
at the Academy of Communist Education (AKV), such as: “The development of
memory” (Leontiev), “Motor skills development in children” (Zaporozhets), “The
role of sign-mediated [znakovyi] operations in the reaction of choice” (i.e. the development of complex choices in small children) (Morozova), “The planning role of
speech” (R. Levina), and “The development of imitation in children” (Bozhovich
and Slavina). At the same time, the whole group was collaboratively involved in
studies using the “method of pictograms,” when children of different ages were
asked to invent pictures that would help them memorize a set of abstract words
(A. R. Luria, 1979, pp. 46–51; Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996, p. 104). After their
graduation in 1930, the members of the piaterka were appointed to mandatory jobs
in different geographic locations across the Soviet Union (the so-called raspredelenie),
and ceased to exist as a unity or a research team (see, e.g., the biography of one of
the members of the group, Bozhovich, in Gutkina, 2008).
From Vygotsky’s and Luria’s overviews of the experimental studies done by their
group by the beginning of the 1930s (Vigotski, 1930; Vygotskii, 1930b; Vygotsky
& Luria, 1994), we also know about the studies on “active” (i.e. voluntary) remembering and attention (studies by Zankov, Solov’ev, and Veresotskaia), visual thinking

Vygotsky–Luria circle 39

(Geshelina), the transition of external to internal speech (Leontiev, Shein), as well
as clinical studies on patients with oligophrenia (Zankov, Leontiev, Bozhovich, and
Slavina), aphasia (Luria, Slavina, Bozhovich, and Morozova), and hysteria (Eidinova
and Zaporozhets). In his postwar publications, Luria also noted that a study on
hysteria and the role of “hypoboulic” (i.e. unconscious) processes was initiated
by Vygotsky in collaboration with Averbukh and Eidinova (A. R. Luria, 1960,
pp. 434–435). Quite a few of these studies were never published and remain virtually unknown.
It was also during this period that a group of former students of Kurt Lewin, a
German psychologist of the Gestalt school in Berlin, returned to the Soviet Union
(in 1930–1) and joined the Vygotsky–Luria group. These included Gita Birenbaum,
Nina Kaulina, and Bluma Zeigarnik, who started their work in clinical settings of
the Institute of Higher Nervous Activity in Moscow and notably contributed to
the convergence of Lev Vygotsky’s, Alexander Luria’s, and Kurt Lewin’s ideas (for
further discussion see Chapters 8 and 9).

Phase three (1931–1934): Vygotsky–Luria circle and the beginning
of specialization and separation (Moscow–Kharkov–Leningrad)
During the period 1931–4, several groups of “Vygotskians” were working in parallel
in a number of institutions in three cities of the Soviet Union: Moscow, Kharkov,
and Leningrad. However, the internal unity of this geographically and thematically
extended research project was guaranteed by Vygotsky’s and Luria’s role as mediators between these groups and as coordinators of a whole range of studies of normal
development, developmental pathology, and clinical research.
Until 1931,Vygotsky’s and Luria’s collaborators were working in a number of educational and research institutions in Moscow, but the end of the year was the turning
point in the history of the groups, when a few of Vygotsky’s former colleagues left
Moscow for Kharkov (the capital of Soviet Ukraine in 1919–34) to head research
at the Psychology Sector of the newly formed Ukrainian Psychoneurological
Academy (UPNA). This group of ‘Kharkovites’, formed by the local psychologists
(e.g. Gal’perin, Zinchenko, Asnin, Rozenblium, and others) and the newcomers
from Moscow (Luria, Leontiev, Lebedinskii, Bozhovich, Zaporozhets, and his wife
Ginevskaia), is commonly referred to as the “Kharkov school of psychology” and
is best known for their work on the foundations of what is often called Leontiev’s
“activity theory” (Yasnitsky, 2008, 2009a;Yasnitsky & Ferrari, 2008a, 2008b).
Vygotsky also might have planned to move to Kharkov at some point and –
along with Luria – even started his studies at the extramural department at the local
Medical Institute, but his move to Kharkov never took place, and instead, in the fall
of 1931, he accepted an invitation to lecture part-time in Leningrad at the Herzen
State Pedagogical Institute. This is where a third research center was formed. From
1931 until his death, Vygotsky frequently commuted to Leningrad to lecture and
supervise the research of the Leningrad group of his collaborators, which included

Daniil El’konin, Mira Levina (not to be confused with Roza Levina, from Moscow),
Zhozefina Shif, Tatiana Konnikova, Frida Fradkina, and several others.
In Moscow,Vygotskian studies continued in the field of defectology under the
auspices of the Moscow Experimental Defectological Institute (EDI). This group
included long-time Vygotsky collaborators Zankov and Solov’ev and their colleagues, such as medical doctors, educationalists, and defectologists Vera Schmidt,
Maria Pevzner, Rakhil’ Boskis, Roza Levina, Natalia Morozova, Esfir’ Bein, Ksenia
Veresotskaia, Marina Eidinova, Lia Geshelina, and Zhozefina Shif (the latter moved
from Leningrad to Moscow in the early 1930s).
Another line of Vygotskian research in Moscow in the early 1930s was conducted by Kurt Lewin’s former Berlin students Bluma Zeigarnik, Gita Birenbaum,
and Nina Kaulina, who returned to the Soviet Union in 1930–1 and started collaborating with Vygotsky’s group on psychological research in clinical settings
(Birenbaum and Zeigarnik) and in the field of child studies (Kaulina: in VIEM,
from the mid-1930s). In 1931–2 Birenbaum and Zeigarnik worked at the Clinical
Department of the Institute for Research in Higher Nervous Activity (IVND), and
later moved to the All-Union Institute of Experimental Medicine (VIEM) in 1933.
Until Vygotsky’s last days they worked in VIEM under his (and Luria’s) supervision and, according to their publications, collaborated with a group of researchers at the local psychiatric clinic, mostly medical doctors – psychiatrists and neurologists – that included N. V. Samukhin, E. S. Kaganovskaia, and A. M. Dubinin.

Vygotsky–Luria circle 41

The work of the Moscow group of clinical psychologists was paralleled by clinical
research in Kharkov, at the Department of Clinical Psychology of the Ukrainian
Psychoneurological Academy (UPNA) (Luria, Lebedinskii, Gal’perin, Zaporozhets,
Bassin, Kozis, Margolis, and Voloshin).
During the last months of his life, in the spring of 1934,Vygotsky was hectically
working to organize a psychological department – hypothetically, a department of
clinical psychology – at VIEM (Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996, pp. 129, 318–319),
most likely with the Psychological Sector of the UPNA in Kharkov as a model for
this new organizational unit. According to Vygotsky’s archival documents, he was
going to form another research team and invite a number of individuals, including
Solov’ev, Zankov,Veresotskaia, R. Levina, Slavina, and Shif (Vygodskaya & Lifanova,
1996, p. 129). Unfortunately, this project was never realized: in May, 1934, due to a
throat hemorrhage that was caused by chronic tuberculosis,Vygotsky was prescribed
bed rest until his hospitalization and death in June, 1934. This is the situation that
Alexander Luria described in his speech at the L. S.Vygotsky memorial meeting in
Moscow at the Dom Uchenykh (House of Scientists) on January 6, 1935:
This death was even more tragic given that Lev Semenovich died amongst
everybody’s devotion and love, for the first time in his life being on the verge
of bringing all his plans to life and gathering the organized group of researchers he had been dreaming about all his life, and who could undertake the
realization of everything that was hidden in this brain of a genius.
(A. R. Luria, 2003a, p. 275)

Phase four (1934–1936): the circles of Vygotskians and
disintegration of the original research program
After Vygotsky’s death, several groups – or, for that matter, several circles – of
researchers evolved. The two major circles were formed by Vygotsky’s former associates at the Experimental Defectological Institute (renamed Scientific-Practical
Institute for Special Schools and Children’s Houses of the Narkompros of the RSFSR
in the mid-1930s) and scholars of the so-called Kharkov group (Kharkov school)
that remained virtually unchanged from the preceding period.
In 1934 both Luria (in March) and Leontiev (at the end of the year) returned
to Moscow from Kharkov. Well before he officially quit as the Head of Department
of Psychology in Kharkov, Luria was hired by the Medico-Biological Institute (in
March, 1935 renamed Medico-Genetic Institute), where he took charge of the
Psychological Department and, following the earlier work of Lebedinskii and other
researchers affiliated with the institute, such as Anna Mirenova,Viktor Kolbanovskii,
and Faina Yudovich (Lebedinskii, 1932; Mirenova, 1932, 1934; Mirenova &
Kolbanovskii, 1934; Mirenva [sic], 1935), headed this team of researchers and
launched a series of experimental studies with twins. Some of these studies were
published in the 1930s (A. R. Luria, 1936; A. R. Luria & Mirenova, 1936a, 1936b;

42

FIGURE 2.4

The circles of Vygotskians in 1934–1936

A. R. Luria, Mirenova, & Morozova, 1936), yet others came out only several decades later (A. R. Luria, 1948, 1962, 2002; A. R. Luria & Yudovich, 1956, 1959). This
research on the interrelation between genetic and cultural factors in development
of twins made visible impact on Vygotsky’s thought in the last year of his life as
evidenced by his increasing involvement with the problems of the role of inheritance and environment in the child’s development in his 1933–4 “pedological”
lectures and public presentations, published posthumously (Vygotskii, 1934b, 1935b,
1996, 2001).
From October, 1934, Luria was also serving as the Head of the Department
of Clinical Psychology at VIEM. It is not quite clear yet if Luria was officially
employed at the Defectological Institute, but, by his own account, he collaborated
with researchers from this institute, many of whom had been the old-time members of the Vygotsky–Luria circle. Thus, Luria’s “inner circle” and research team of
that time included Birenbaum, Zeigarnik (Clinical Psychology Department of the
All-Union Institute of Experimental Medicine), Mirenova, Morozova, and Yudovich
(Department of Psychology of the Medico-Genetic Institute), as well as R. Levina,
Boskis, and S. Ya. Rabinovich (all from the Defectological Institute) (Luria, 1937).
Leontiev also left Kharkov and in October, 1934, like Luria, was hired by VIEM,
where he headed the Laboratory of Genetic (i.e. Developmental) Psychology.Around
this time, Leontiev also became a Professor at the Higher Communist Institute
of Enlightenment (in Russian: Vysshii Kommunisticheskii Institut Prosvescheniia,

Vygotsky–Luria circle 43

VKIP). Formally, he also remained employed by the Ukrainian Psychoneurological
Academy in Kharkov (until December, 1936) and the Kharkov State Pedagogical
Institute (until December 1, 1937), and occasionally traveled to Kharkov to supervise the work of his colleagues (Leontiev, Leontiev, & Sokolova, 2005, pp. 59, 63).
The work of the group of Kharkov scholars was discussed at length in recent publications on the development of Vygotskian psychology in the “Kharkov school”
(Yasnitsky & Ferrari, 2008a, 2008b). During this period the Leningrad group of
developmental psychologists from the Herzen State Pedagogical Institute, led by
El’konin, established first personal and professional contacts with the Kharkov
group and became increasingly close to the Kharkov developmentalists. An interesting research project, very highly reminiscent of Kurt Lewin’s “topological psychology,” was conducted in Gorky Central Park of Culture and Leisure in Moscow
in 1935 under the supervision of Leontiev and his colleague from the Kharkov
Institute of Labor A. I. Rozenblium. Another former student of Vygotsky’s – Lidia
Bozhovich – also participated (A. N. Leontiev & Rozenblium, 1999). This was the
only distinctly “topological” study conducted by the associates of Vygotsky. Such
an “anomaly” does not strike as inexplicably mysterious given that among the participants of the study was Nina Kaulina, a former Berlin student of the founder of
“topological psychology,” Kurt Lewin (Lewin, 1936) (for discussion see Chapter 9).
Both Bozhovich and Kaulina were Leontiev’s subordinates in the Department of
Genetic Psychology at VIEM that he headed at that time.
This period may well be referred to as the brief “Golden Age” of Vygotskian
psychology in the interwar period (Yasnitsky, 2012e).Thus, after a dramatic decline
in the number of Vygotsky’s publications in 1932–3 (e.g. just two publications
in 1933: a scholarly paper on thinking in schizophrenia and a two-page-long
introduction to a book), a pile of Vygotsky’s books came out posthumously in
1934–6, including his Thinking and speech (1934), Foundations of paedology (two editions: Moscow: Second Moscow State University, 1934 and Leningrad: Herzen
State Pedagogical Institute, 1935), Mental development of children in the process of
learning (1935), The mentally retarded child (1935, co-edited by Vygotsky and
Daniushevskii), and Diagnostics of [human] development and paedological clinical treatment of abnormal childhood development (1936).
All these book publications became possible due to the concerted effort of
Vygotsky’s students and collaborators, the former members of the Vygotsky–Luria
circle. Thus, for instance, Thinking and speech was prepared for posthumous publication by Kolbanovskii, Zankov, and Shif (Kolbanovskii, 1934, p. v). According to
Aleksei N. Leontiev’s recently published memoirs of the early 1970s, the participation of Kolbanovskii in this publication project was not incidental: Kolbanovskii,
who was never in any formal way associated with Vygotsky, still, reportedly, considered himself as Vygotsky’s associate and follower (A. N. Leontiev & Yaroshevskii,
2013). Indeed, the first postwar publication with Vygotsky’s name in the very title
was the paper by Kolbanovskii, who was the first in the decade of the 1950s to
openly and most energetically advocate Vygotsky and his methodological standpoint (Kolbanovskii, 1956). This fact not only corroborates the testimony of

44

Anton Yasnitsky

Leontiev in his reminiscences of the early 1970s, but also suggests that Kolbanovskii
might well have been instrumental in the organization of the first postwar publication of Vygotsky’s works, the volume of 1956 (for a discussion of the complexities
and the history of domestic publication and non-publication of Vygotsky’s works
see Chapters 3 and 4).
Another posthumous volume was published in 1935, also by Zankov and
Shif, in collaboration with El’konin (Vygotskii, 1935d). The latter book presents a remarkable collection of Vygotsky’s works of the 1930s on the problems of the interrelation between learning and development. It was in this
book that perhaps the best known of Vygotsky’s theoretical innovations, the
“zone of proximal development,” was discussed at considerable length. Some
of these texts that came out in English translation four decades later (Vygotsky,
1978) were instrumental in the launch of the “Vygotsky boom” among North
American educators and psychologists (Cole, 2004). Also in 1935, a volume of
collected defectological papers by Vygotsky, Zankov, and Solov’ev-Elpidinskii
came out in Moscow under the supervision of the director of the Defectological
Institute, Daniushevskii (Vygotskii & Daniushevskii, 1935). A handbook on the
foundations of pedology was published in Leningrad under the editorship of
Mira Levina of the Herzen Leningrad Pedagogical Institute (Vygotskii, 1935b),
and yet another pedological booklet was prepared for publication in 1935 and
published the following year in Moscow by Roza Levina of the Experimental
Defectological Institute (Vygotskii, 1936).
Finally, during this period Vygotsky’s students conducted research on normal
and retarded child development and the degradation of mental functions (for an
overview see Yasnitsky, 2008;Yasnitsky & Ferrari, 2008a). Several works in the fields
of defectology and clinical psychology were published with explicit acknowledgement of Vygotsky’s supervision or collaboration (Birenbaum, 1934; Birenbaum
& Zeigarnik, 1935; Kaganovskaia & Zeigarnik, 1935; Samukhin, Birenbaum, &
Vygotskii, 1934; Zeigarnik, 1934; Zeigarnik & Birenbaum, 1935), and several books
by former Vygotsky students came out with a dedication to the memory of their
late teacher (Levina, 1936; Shif, 1935; Zankov, 1935).

Phase ﬁve (1936–1941): the beginning of the
“Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria school”
The notorious decree of the Communist Party on “pedological perversions” officially banned studies in pedology in July, 1936. In addition, in the summer of
1936 a campaign against Western (i.e. bourgeois) influences in Soviet science and
a patriotic struggle with “kowtowing to the West” was launched. This campaign
specifically targeted Soviet scholars who published their work abroad (the so-called
“Luzin affair”). Finally, the summer of 1936 is notorious as the beginning of major
political trials that initiated the “Great Terror” (alternatively: the “Great Purge”) in
the Soviet Union. All these processes contributed to social unrest and led to the

Vygotsky–Luria circle 45

Clinical &
Medical

LebeZaporozhets dinskii

Child studies
FIGURE 2.5

The circles of Vygotskians in 1936–1941

period of instability in the established system of interrelations between scholars and
their Bolshevik patrons in power.
Thus, by the end of 1936, Luria – a professor of psychology with a record
of international publications – resigned from all his academic and research positions in order to “disappear” in the relative quietness of an internship at the N. N.
Burdenko Clinic of Neurosurgery in Moscow that he qualified for as a recent
Medical Department graduate (graduated in 1937), in isolation from social pressure. This internship (1937–9) probably helped him survive the years of the Great
Terror.Then, in 1939, he joined the Institute of Experimental Medicine in Moscow
where he – yet again – headed the Laboratory of Experimental Psychology (E. A.
Luria, 1994, p. 89). Indeed, the two years 1936–7 of Stalin’s Great Terror can be
best described as a period of the utmost instability, anxiety, and disorientation. The
second half of the 1930s, though, is typically notoriously misunderstood and its significance is underplayed by historians of Soviet psychology.Yet, this period seems to
be particularly important for the development of the entire course of Soviet psychology. Let us see why this is so. The careers of two protagonists, Aleksei Leontiev
and Alexander Luria, are particularly interesting in this respect and deserve a closer
analysis.
On the basis of fairly fragmentary evidence available to date we know that both
Leontiev and Luria were very active and had made quite impressive careers by
their mid-thirties when the disastrous July 4, 1936 decree prohibiting pedology was
issued. A series of discussions of the decree ensued. Criticism of Vygotsky and his

46

Anton Yasnitsky

associates, one of the leaders of pedology at the time, concluded with a notorious
booklet signed with the name of Eva Rudneva, and titled Pedological perversions of
Vygotsky (Rudneva, 1937). This publication, which came out in January of 1937,
virtually replicated the title of the Communist Party decree and constituted the
major offensive against Vygotsky and his followers. There is circumstantial evidence
to the effect that both Leontiev and Luria were likely to have been forced to quit
their jobs around 1936–7. We can also assume that Luria had to temporarily flee
from Moscow in order to find a relative refuge in the Caucasus area. From the publication of Luria’s correspondence with Max Wertheimer we know, for example,
that his letter to Wertheimer of September, 1936 was sent with a return address in
Teberda (Caucasus region) (King & Wertheimer, 2005). Likewise, it was in Tbilisi,
Soviet Georgia, under uncertain circumstances, that Luria defended his doctoral
dissertation, allegedly, in 1937. Perhaps, resigning from their professorships and fleeing from the capital were not the only social strategies for physical and professional
survival used by Luria and Leontiev. Thus, we know about a paper by Leontiev
on “The teaching about environment in pedological works of L.S. Vygotsky (a
critical investigation)” that was most likely the basis for an oral presentation that
he made some time during the turbulent years of 1936–7 (A. N. Leontiev, 1998,
2005). Such a presentation was likely to have taken place in 1937 when, after a
considerable break, Leontiev returned to the Institute of Psychology. This paper
was originally located in the archives of the Institute and was not published until
the late 1990s, when it was accidentally discovered by the well-known Russian
scholar Irina Ravich-Shcherbo. Employed as a strategy of survival, Leontiev’s public critique of Vygotsky might have served the purpose of distancing himself from
Vygotsky and his affiliation with pedology. Another strategy that both Leontiev and
Luria must have applied was to establish personal connections with decision-makers
and key individuals in power at that time; in the absence of any official or archival
documents, we can only speculate about such possible connections. For example,
Alexander Luria’s father, Roman Al’bertovich Luria, was one of the most successful medical professionals of the time, and the personal doctor to quite a few of the
elite inhabitants of the Moscow Kremlin, including the Prosecutor General of the
USSR Andrei Vyshinskii (E. A. Luria, 1994). Another of Alexander Luria’s possible connections with the Communist Party leadership was Lev Sheinin, an aide
to Vyshinskii in the late 1930s, with whom Luria collaborated in the mid-1920s
during his studies on the traces of affective reactions in suspected criminals. This
research resulted in the publication of Luria’s first major English book The nature
of human conflicts (A. R. Luria, 1930c, 1932c). It is still unclear who exactly helped
Luria and Leontiev establish personal connections with key figures in power, but
it is fairly apparent that both scholars were successful in building such connections
with powerful patrons of the time.
Thus, it is not coincidental that it was in 1939 that both Luria and Leontiev
were appointed to supervisory positions in research and educational institutions in
Moscow and Leningrad. A contemporary historian of Soviet science, culture, and
society, Nikolai Krementsov, points out that

Vygotsky–Luria circle 47

by 1939, Soviet scientists understood perfectly well the principles of operation of the Stalinist science system and had learned to use that system to their
own advantage.They knew that the real power was concentrated in the highest party bodies – the Central Committee and its Secretariat – and they petitioned party bosses in numerous letters. For their part, the party bosses read
scientists’ petitions and relayed them with their own remarks and notes to the
lower level of the party hierarchy “for consideration” or “for implementation,” and sometimes “for archiving”.These second-hand echelon bureaucrats
prepared concrete decisions and sent them back to the top for approval. The
behaviour of both the top officials and their subordinates was shaped by their
own interests and agendas, and thus by considerations external to the scientific questions raised in the petition.
(Krementsov, 1997, p. 80)
It is clear that from the end of the 1930s onwards only those individuals who
thoroughly understood the real meaning of Soviet science policy and the internal
mechanics of decision-making in the country could make scientific careers in the
Soviet Union.
In 1940 Leontiev defended his dissertation on the “Genesis of the psyche,” largely
based on empirical research on the origin of sensation conducted in Kharkov and
Moscow in the mid-1930s under his supervision. Along with S. L. Rubinstein and
B. M. Teplov, an opponent at Leontiev’s doctoral dissertation defense was Leon
Orbeli, a great Pavlovian scholar and one of the most influential figures in Soviet
scientific hierarchy before World War II. By 1940 Orbeli, considered Pavlov’s scientific heir after his death in 1936, was a Member of the Academy of Sciences of
the USSR (from 1935), the Head of the Institute of Physiology of the Academy
of Sciences of the USSR (from 1936), and Director of the I. P. Pavlov Institute of
Evolutionary Physiology and Pathology of Higher Nervous Activity (from 1939).
Furthermore, in 1942 Orbeli was appointed Vice-President of the Soviet Academy
of Sciences and in 1943 Head of the Military Medical Academy in Leningrad. It
seems that connections with Orbeli as well as with a number of other prominent
scholars (and, most likely, Soviet officials) of the highest rank in the hierarchy of the
Soviet scientific establishment was one of the key factors that facilitated Leontiev’s
future success as one of the leaders of Marxist Soviet psychology. Furthermore, as a
token of recognition of their trustworthiness and loyalty, both Luria and Leontiev
were invited to contribute to the most prestigious Bol’shaia Sovetskaia Entsiklopediia
(Great Soviet Encyclopaedia), particularly, to co-author such important articles
as Psikhologiia (Psychology) and Rech’ (Speech) (A. N. Leontiev & Luria, 1941;
A. R. Luria & Leontiev, 1940). In addition, they contributed an independent article
each: both Luria’s Psikhoanaliz (Psychoanalysis) (A. R. Luria, 1940) and Leontiev’s
Psikhika (Psyche) (A. N. Leontiev, 1940) came out in 1940. These publications of
Luria and Leontiev in this multi-volume edition – perhaps the most prestigious
edition of the time – marked the inclusion of their authors in the Soviet scientific
nomenklatura of the highest rank. Hence, not surprisingly, during the war Luria and

48

Anton Yasnitsky

Leontiev were appointed the heads of military hospitals in the Ural region. Fairly
soon thereafter, both joined the Communist Party of the Soviet Union (Luria in
1943, Leontiev in 1948), a move that should most likely be interpreted as a precondition of future social success and major career advancement.
From the end of the 1930s, therefore, the alliance of Luria and Leontiev formed
the new organizational center of Vygotskian psychology. Also, this is the origin of
the “school of Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria” and the “Kharkov school of psychology.”
The latter term was probably first introduced in 1938, but would not get actively
used in historiographical and psychological discourse until the late 1970s (Yasnitsky,
2009a). As the new center, Luria and Leontiev served as mediators between different groups of scholars who continued the Vygotskian line of research in the
country. For instance, the remains of the Leningrad group and the Kharkov group
received considerable support from Leontiev around the mid-1930s when they
established personal and professional ties between the two groups in Kharkov and
in Leningrad (for instance, Leontiev served as an official supervisor of the dissertation of El’konin, from Leningrad, at his defense in 1935). The ties between the
Leningrad and Kharkov groups especially strengthened after 1939 when Leontiev –
like Vygotsky in 1931 – started commuting between Moscow and Leningrad,
where he spent ten days of each month lecturing and heading the Department of
Psychology at the Leningrad Krupskaia Pedagogical Institute (A. A. Leontiev et al.,
2005, p. 74).Thus, during this period Leontiev’s brokerage between different groups
of scholars was somewhat limited to Soviet scholars, who mostly worked in the field
of child studies. In turn, Luria was instrumental in establishing international and
cross-disciplinary communications between clinical and defectological researchers
that, in turn, considerably contributed to his own studies on the brain and the
then-nascent discipline of neuropsychology (see, e.g., A. R. Luria, 1937).
Relatively little is known about the research of the former members of the
Vygotsky–Luria circle after 1936. There was no a single specialized psychological journal in the country at that time: the journals Psikhologiia (Psychology)
and Pedologiia (Pedology) were closed down in 1932, Sovetskaia Psikhotekhnika
(Soviet Psychotechnics) in 1934. In 1936, Nevropatologiia, Psikhiatriia i Psikhogigiena
(Neuropathology, psychiatry, and mental hygiene) was renamed and shortened to
just Nevropatologiia i Psikhiatriia (Neuropathology and psychiatry), which clearly left
out “psikhogigiena” (i.e. mental hygiene). Still, this Moscow journal, along with
the journal Sovetskaia Psikhonevrologiia (Soviet psychoneurology) that was published
in Kharkov, was accepting studies by clinical psychologists and defectologists, and
several studies by former Vygotskians such as Zeigarnik, Lebedinskii, Boskis, Levina,
and their associates were published there (Boskis & Levina, 1936, 2006; Dubinin
& Zeigarnik, 1940; Lebedinskii, 1936, 1938, 1940). Another major outlet for psychologists during that period was the journal Sovetskaia Pedagogika (Soviet pedagogy,
founded in 1937) that published several papers by defectologists, developmentalists,
and educational psychologists formerly associated with Vygotsky (Boskis & Levina,
1938; Bozhovich, 1937, 1940; A. N. Leontiev, 1937; Levina, 1940; Slavina, 1939).
In addition, Soviet scholars’ publications abroad in foreign languages were getting

Vygotsky–Luria circle 49

increasingly rare during the second half of 1930s, but still – quite contrary to popular opinion – were not impossible for researchers in the Soviet Union (Yasnitsky,
2011c). For a few examples of such foreign publications by Soviet psychologists in
the late 1930s see the works of the participants of the Vygotsky–Luria circle that
came out in 1936–7 in three languages – German, English, and French – in Austria,
the United States, and France, respectively (Lebedinsky, 1936a, 1936b; A. R. Luria,
1936, 1937).
Quite a few studies came out in various Scientific notes of institutes such as
the Herzen State Pedagogical Institute in Leningrad and the Kharkov State
Pedagogical Institute. Finally, several books written by the former members of
the Vygotsky–Luria group were published in a range of subfields of psychological
research.These include clinical psychology (Lebedinskii, 1941), but are mainly concerned with defectology (Boskis, 1939; Daniushevskii & Zankov, 1941; Korsunskaia
& Morozova, 1939; Pevzner, 1941; Zankov, 1939; Zankov & Daniushevskii, 1940;
Zankov & Solov’ev, 1940).
All these studies present an interesting yet unexplored development of what we
might refer to as the “Vygotsky–Luria–Lewin project” of the mid-1930s. However,
one should certainly keep in mind that in many instances due to self-censorship –
whether legitimate and necessary or not quite – the authors afforded only indirect, camouflaged references to Vygotsky or, for that matter, Kurt Lewin after 1936,
which certainly complicates the task of historical and theoretical reconstruction
(for a discussion of censorship issues in relation to Vygotsky’s and, for that matter,
Lewin’s and, more broadly, Gestaltist scholarship see Chapters 3 and 9, respectively).

3
DECONSTRUCTING VYGOTSKY’S
VICTIMIZATION NARRATIVE
A re-examination of the “Stalinist suppression”
of Vygotskian theory
Jennifer Fraser and Anton Yasnitsky

Although many facets of Lev Vygotsky’s (1896–1934) life have attracted considerable attention from historians of science, perhaps the most commonly acknowledged feature of his personal narrative was that he was actively chastised by the
Stalinist government, with his work being placed under an enforced publication ban
that would persist for a period of almost twenty years after his death. References
to this infamous ban pervade contemporary accounts of Vygotsky – almost all of
them emphasizing that in the years from 1936 to 1956, it was forbidden to either
discuss or disseminate any of Vygotsky’s works within the Soviet Union. According
to these narratives, it was only after Stalin’s death in 1953 that Vygotsky’s ideas were
able to resurface in Russia, as a network of his collaborators and associates began to
circulate his central tenets during the late 1950s, which is allegedly due to the first
post-Stalinist era publication of Vygotsky’s works in 1956.
In the most general sense, the entire corpus of literature that to a varying extent
focuses on Lev Vygotsky and his scientific legacy can be referred to as “Vygotsky
studies.” Hardly an independent field of knowledge, these “Vygotsky studies” are
informed by and feed back to several somewhat more canonical scholarly traditions
such as psychology and education, the history of the Soviet Union and Russia, and
the history of human sciences.There are notable differences between all these disciplinary subfields, and we are fully aware of the dangers of oversimplification, yet for
simplicity’s sake these will be further referred to as “psychology” and “history,” and
their agents will be described as “psychologists” and “historians” respectively.Thus, it
might be instructive to sketch an outline of the early history of the “Vygotsky ban”
narrative as it is presented in various publications on Vygotsky in the West and in
the East. The history of the reception and the social construction of the life story of
Vygotsky and his legacy has not been written yet, and such endeavor is definitely
beyond the scope of this chapter.Yet, we believe that some tentative generalizations

Deconstructing Vygotsky’s victimization narrative

51

are possible even at this early stage and that this study contributes to understanding
the “Vygotsky myth” as it circulates in the contemporary international context.

Deconstructing the narrative of the “Vygotsky ban”
Presumably the first reference to the “Vygotsky ban” can be found as early as in
the first major post-World War II English-language publication of Vygotsky’s work.
This is Jerome S. Bruner’s Introduction to Vygotsky’s book Thinking and speech,
which came out under the title Thought and language in 1962 (Vygotsky, 1962).
Twenty years later, in his autobiography, Bruner described the circumstances of his
participation in this publication project: “Then in 1961, after his official ‘rehabilitation’ in Russia and a great deal of backing and filing diplomatically to obtain
rights, his Thought and language was translated into English by my colleague Eugenia
Hanfmann. She asked me to write a preface” (Bruner, 1983, p. 139). Then this
Introduction, signed by its author in July 1961 in Cambridge, Massachusetts –
among other fairly curious statements of interest to a historian of science – presents
the following paragraph:
For an English-speaking audience it avails little to trace the ideological course
of Vygotsky’s work through the groundswells and storms of psychology in
