account that what we know about scholarly genres does not necessarily apply in
Vygotsky’s case. Thus, for instance, several of his editorial introductions and book
reviews contained in-depth analyses of the author’s work and a critical assessment
that betrayed Vygotsky’s theoretical views at the time of writing. The introductions
to the Russian translations of books by Wolfgang Köhler, Jean Piaget, and Kurt
Koffka form an illustrative example (Vygotskii, 1930a, 1932b, 1934c). So, it would
be unwise to use such a formal criterion to exclude writings as being theoretically
useless. On the other hand, genres of scientific writing that would seem to be very
relevant need not be so. A significant segment of Vygotsky’s journal articles, for
example, would hardly be considered as “research papers” by the standards of our
time: indeed, as a social activist and middle-level governmental employee,Vygotsky
would write programmatic, administrative, review or polemical articles, which was
not infrequent in the academic press in the Soviet Union of the 1920s–30s, but
is quite unusual for us now. These articles typically did not present any empirical
studies and hardly touched upon in-depth theoretical issues, but addressed matters
of scientific policy or philosophical foundations of Marxist scholarship or reported

Vygotsky the published

79

the progress of scholarly activity in a specific locale or organization. For obvious
reasons, such publications are mainly of historical interest. More or less the same
holds for the genre of books. Vygotsky published an impressive number of books
and book chapters during a relatively short period but not all of them were theoretically relevant. In fact, a notable number of these book publications were written for
non-academic audiences (e.g. general readers lacking basic undergraduate education
or minimally trained professionals with traditional agrarian or working-class backgrounds). Such texts often presented compilations and overviews of contemporary
Western (mostly European) studies in the humanities and social sciences and were
never meant to circulate in the academic context as original scholarly contributions.
Thus, the attempt to determine Vygotsky’s core writings on the basis of formal
criteria is bound to fail. The issue is decidedly more complicated than that. Even
Vygotsky’s most famous book, Thinking and speech (1934), can be used to illustrate
this state of affairs.

The case of Thinking and speech (1934)
This book is traditionally positioned and widely discussed as the summit of Vygotsky’s
theorizing, his claim to fame and the “swan song” of the untimely deceased scholar,
who passed away in his late thirties. Indeed, according to different sources, the last
additions to the book were made during the last months of the author’s life, and the
final release of the volume took place roughly half a year after his death. The question is, though, whether all this allows us to characterize Thinking and speech as the
main contribution of the author to psychological theory. The answer is: hardly so.
There are a number of reasons why we feel justified to make this claim.
First, as we can see, this book was partially based on articles and chapters that
Vygotsky published earlier and, therefore, roughly half of the book might qualify as
a compilation rather than as a monograph (see Table 4.1). Furthermore, the diversity
of the genres that contributed to the composition of the book yet again reminds
us about the danger of relying on formal criteria in the assessment of scholarly
production.
Second, this example also illustrates the danger of oversimplification of chronology (see our discussion of Lifanova’s bibliography above). Indeed, the formal
release of the book in 1934 does not qualify it as the source that was created in
1934 or even within a reasonably short time before that. As we see, the earliest text
incorporated in Thinking and speech was written sometime in 1928, which creates a
serious challenge for our assessment of the intellectual integrity of the whole book,
given Vygotsky’s criticism of his ideas of the 1920s, which he expressed in the 1930s
on a number of occasions.
Finally, as far as this specific book is concerned and, specifically, with respect
to the claim that it represents the peak of Vygotsky’s thinking: despite the fact
that it was the last book in his lifetime oeuvre, it definitely did not represent his
ultimate views and insights. For, as we know, it was already in 1932 that Vygotsky

conceived the whole book project, planned the study of his graduate student
Zhozefina Shif discussed in the penultimate chapter, publicly presented the overview of the volume, and even reported it as “in press.” Which means that the
topics that were clearly of utmost interest to Vygotsky during the last two years
of his life (i.e. in 1932–4) – such as the wide range of clinical and neuropsychological issues or his growing fascination with German-American psychologist
Kurt Lewin’s scholarship (see Chapters 2 and 9) – are hardly presented in this
volume at any length.
In sum, none of the formal criteria of scientific production assessment, such
as its release data, the genre of scholarly writing, or even reputation and international fame can serve as a sufficiently solid foundation in our analysis. Overreliance
on each of these taken alone potentially leads us to a flawed analysis and inadequate conclusions. Instead, a complex approach grounded on a combination of
various factors and criteria is needed. And even this does not free us from mistakes.
Therefore, expert opinion and professional interpretation of the various data is crucial. Needless to mention, this does not guarantee success in our assessment of the
intellectual legacy, either.Yet, it is the best we can do.

Vygotsky’s self-assessment
In this situation, before we ultimately give our own opinion and interpretation of
the data, it might be not a bad idea to consult Vygotsky himself and find out his

Vygotsky the published

81

opinion about his scientific works. However, this seems hardly possible considering the fact that the author has not been with us for the last eighty years. And yet,
hope never dies and we still can get a glimpse of the author’s self-assessment of his
contribution to science. Interviewing the author is not an option under the circumstances, but we certainly can consult his published works in order to check what
he considered valuable and to which of his published works he most often referred.
Unfortunately, this method is hardly helpful in the present situation. The reason
is that, as we can see in Figure A1 (Appendix B), the decline of the author’s publications in the most interesting period of the early 1930s considerably limits the actual
material we could use as data. Even the publications that we have refer back to studies that the author did not necessarily consider as his most important, valuable, or
representative of his theory in its most advanced form. Keeping in mind this general
limitation of using data of the kind, we need a better source of information than the
author’s self- and cross-references.
Luckily, we do have some useful material. First, as we know, the Soviet Union
was a highly bureaucratic state that created tons of all kinds of documents, most of
which are still faithfully kept in the state and institutional archives of the Russian
Federation. A normal job-selection procedure in the Soviet Union included filling out a questionnaire or writing an essay according to a given plan providing
the future employer with personal information of various kinds. Of relevance to
our current quest is the fact that in academia quite a few of such forms and essays
required informing a future boss about one’s publications, if any. It is clear that the
applicant, were he or she a scientific worker like Vygotsky, would completely realize
that the whole procedure did not exactly qualify as an “objective self-assessment of
the personal contribution to international scholarship,” and that he or she would
mention the published works that would present him best under these specific
circumstances and in this particular moment of time – i.e. given the current, ever
rapidly changing (particularly in the 1930s), political situation in the Soviet Union.
Nevertheless, we consider such data to have considerable value, if used critically, cum
grano salis.
Unfortunately, the “archival revolution” in Vygotskian scholarship is still
in its infancy. That is, we do have several of the relevant documents, but much
more remains unexplored: highly interesting material is currently preserved in
archives and somebody just needs to go there to collect the data. In any case, as
of now we have copies of three documents that present Vygotsky’s self-created
auto-bibliographies, all created at different moments and on different occasions during the 1930s. First, there is the handwritten document, dated March 13, 1931, that
Vygotsky produced upon the request of the administration of the State Institute of
Child and Adolescent Health Protection (Gosudarstvennyi Institut Okhrany Zdorov’ia
Detei i Podrostkov imeni 10-letiia Oktiabr’skoi Revoliutsii) in Moscow, as a part of his
application for the job of Vice-Director of the Institute (in charge of scientific
research).1 Second, we used the bibliography of Vygotsky’s works that was published in English in a volume of 1932 under the editorship of American scholar and
scientific entrepreneur Carl Murchison (Murchison, 1932).We have every reason to

82

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

believe that no one other than Vygotsky authored this bibliography and that it was
finished not later than early 1931. Third, there is another handwritten document
that was created on January 14, 1933 – i.e. a year and a half before his death on June
11, 1934 – under doubtful conditions and for an unknown recipient. This document, entitled “Nauchno-pedagogicheskaia biografiia,” was published in 1996 by
Vygotsky’s daughter in the Russian journal Voprosy Psikhologii (Vygodskaya, 1996)
and is currently available online.2 The bibliographies in these three documents vary
in terms of their length and the selected publications. Thus, the first bibliography is
the shortest (five items only) and includes just books published in the Soviet Union.
In contrast, the second and the third are notably longer and include books, unpublished manuscripts, and scholarly papers, both published in Russian in the USSR
and published abroad in foreign languages.

Vygotsky’s autobiographic ofﬁcial documents and publications
In our analysis of these materials we followed two rules of thumb. First, the more
frequently a publication is mentioned by the author in these three documents, the
more important to him it should be considered. Therefore, in our selection we
eliminated those publications that Vygotsky mentioned only once in any of these
documents. And, on the other hand, those that he mentioned in all three were
considered as unquestionably important for the author. Second, the later documents were given some preference in our assessment.Thus, in our opinion, the third
document (signed on January 14, 1934) has more weight as a source of information
about the most advanced stages of Vygotsky’s work, although, unfortunately, it still
does not cover the period of the last year and a half of his creative work. All these
considerations and limitations should be kept in mind.
As a result, four items that occur in all three documents occupy the top of the list
of Vygotsky’s publications. These are:
(1)
(2)
(3)
(4)

Pedagogical psychology (1926)
Pedology of the school age (1928)
Pedology of the adolescent (1930, 1931)
Studies in the history of behavior (1930).

These four titles were published in 1926–31, but, as our earlier study demonstrates
(Yasnitsky, 2011b), written not later than the end of 1930. As to the third item on
this list, Pedology of the adolescent, it is worth mentioning that in different documents
Vygotsky indicates different years of publication: 1930 (in document three) and
1931 (in documents one and two). Given that this item came out as a three-volume
series in 1929, 1930, and 1931, it is somewhat difficult to identify which of these
Vygotsky meant to identify on his list. We even do not discount the possibility that
Vygotsky made a mistake in indicating the year of publication or meant to indicate
the whole three-volume series. However, as we know, entities should not be multiplied without necessity, therefore, in our analysis we stick to the data as it is and

Vygotsky the published

83

assume that Vygotsky would range the volumes of this edition according to their
importance: volumes two (1930) and three (1931) as relatively more valuable scholarly contributions and volume 1 (1929) as relatively less significant.
Then, we created the auxiliary list of publications that occur in two of the three
documents and include not only books but also a few scientific papers:
(1) The psychology of art (book manuscript, 1925–6)
(2) Consciousness as a problem for the psychology of behavior (chapter, 1925)
(3) Principles of social education of deaf and dumb children in Russia (conference
paper, 1925, in English)
(4) The problem of dominant reactions (chapter, 1926)
(5) The problem of the cultural development of the child (article, 1929, in English)
(6) Imagination and creativity in childhood (book, 1930).
This list, yet again, comprises pieces that were created mostly in the 1920s and
not later than early 1930. Interestingly, it contains one unpublished manuscript,
The psychology of art, which was contracted for publication as early as in 1926
(a photocopy of the contract was published in Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996), but
was eventually denied publication during Vygotsky’s lifetime – under circumstances
which remain unclear, but, as we tend to believe, for censorship reasons (for discussion of censorship issues see Chapter 3). Also notable is the inclusion of two
foreign-language publications: a journal article of 1929 and a conference paper of
1925, both in English.
A last remark on these three documents is in place. It is interesting to note that
the third list contains a number of items that could have been mentioned in one
of the earlier bibliographies (such as in document two), but in fact were not. On
the other hand, quite a number of those publications that occur in the second
bibliography are not mentioned in the later bibliography of January 14, 1933. In
our cautious interpretation, this may be evidence of Vygotsky’s reassessment of
his scientific work that started somewhere mid-1929 and continued throughout
the whole period of the early 1930s until his death in June, 1934. Thus, we would
not be surprised to eventually come across a very different version of “the best of
Vygotsky” in one of the author’s documents created around 1933–4, when and if
such an archival document is discovered.

Vygotsky’s private documents and correspondence
In addition to these autobiographies and auto-bibliographies, another important
source of information is Vygotsky’s private correspondence. This is particularly
rich with first-hand information about Vygotsky’s opinions, preferences, thoughts
and doubts; information that can hardly be found in official publications or formal
presentations. Fortunately, the correspondence and personal notes have been published not just in Russian (Vygotskii, 2004), but are also available to the interested
reader in English and German translation (Rückriem, 2009;Vygotsky, 2007).3 These

84

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

materials we have also checked for indications of Vygotsky’s self-assessment of his
scientific legacy and scholarly publications.
Characteristically,Vygotsky’s correspondence and private documents contain little in terms of positive assessment of his published work. However, there is a wealth
of critical assessment and also several self-critical remarks and statements that provide insight into how Vygotsky would range his work in terms of its importance
and contribution to international scholarship. Besides, a number of other, indirect and seemingly tangential, remarks and statements also provide us with essential
information.
A good example is Vygotsky’s correspondence about a book jointly planned and
written with Luria. In a letter to Luria dated July 26, 1927, he wrote:
I was called to GIZ to sign a contract concerning the ‘ape’ … It looks like
the date will be December 1. What remained was to sign the contract at the
finance department, which I will do immediately. Once that is done, they will
give me the money – 175 roubles. I feel boundless joy about this commission. It is an opportunity to present the outlines of psychology in light of the
concepts of culture and the superman.
(Vygotskii, 2004, p. 11)
In this fragment Vygotsky refers to a book that was eventually published under
the title Studies on the psychology of behavior: Ape, primitive, child. Vygotsky seems to
indicate the deadline for manuscript submission: December 1, presumably 1927.
The book, however, was considerably delayed and did not come out until 1930.
By that time, Vygotsky’s enthusiasm about this project had considerably decreased.
Thus, in a letter to Leontiev of July 23, 1929, he wrote:
I am correcting the 2nd part of the ‘ape’. Goodness, the 1st part is written
wholly in the spirit of the Freudians (not even according to Freud, but according to Vera Schmidt (her materials), Melanie Klein, and other second-rate
stars). And then the impenetrable Piaget, who is absolutized beyond all measures; and over and above all that, tools and signs are mixed together, etc. etc.
This is not Aleksandr Romanovich’s personal fault, but that of the entire “era”
of our thought. We must ruthlessly put an end to that. What is not yet clear
from our view, how it should be transformed so as to become an organic
component of our theory – all that should not be allowed to be part of the
system. Let us wait then … And, therefore, we should structure the same
external organizational regime so that the errors of the ‘monkey’ become
impossible.
(Vygotskii, 2004, p. 18)
The same letter gives us an idea of what Vygotsky thought about some of his
own work, but does not provide specific references to any particular commitments

Vygotsky the published

85

and obligations: “For the moment I am at the dacha. I am busy with profitable
[khlebnye] and therefore useless tasks until August 1” (Vygotskii, 2004, p. 19).
In 1930 a collected volume of papers with the overviews of contemporary
strands in Western psychology was published. Vygotsky authored two chapters for
the volume, one on Gestalt theory that he referred to as “Structural psychology”
(Strukturnaia psikhologiia, in Russian) and the other about the then popular theory of eidetic perception developed by the German psychologist Erich Rudolf
Jaensch (1883–1940), who, as a matter of fact, would later join the National Socialist
German Workers’ Party (NSDAP, the Nazi Party). Vygotsky seems to have been in
correspondence with Jaensch, as follows from one of his letters. Vygotsky’s assessment of these two chapters of 1930 seems to be generally applicable to much of the
work that he did at that time. Consider a fragment of his letter of December 27,
1930, to Vladimir Vagner:
The book (anthology) by Fingert et al. has come out; I have not yet received
and read it, but have heard bad opinions about it: It had been planned as
a large, broad anthology, comprising all directions and branches of modern
psychology in a popular presentation. I wrote two essays – extremely basic
and popular.
(Rückriem, 2009, p. 250)
Vygotsky’s critical attitude to some of his publications was not incidental and was
partly rooted in the deep crisis in his theoretical thinking that he experienced after
1929. In a certain sense, we can describe it as a Copernican revolution and major
shift in his theorizing during which he realized that the most foundational concepts
and terms that he had been using until 1929 were no longer satisfactory and valid.
One of the foundational notions that underwent radical transformation during that
period was that of “psychological function.” We know of at least one context in
which Vygotsky announced a dramatic change in the entire conceptual framework
and, thus, the research goals of his team. In a talk that he gave to his colleagues on
October 9, 1930,Vygotsky stated:
In the process of development, and in the historical development in particular, it is not so much the functions that change (these we mistakenly studied before). Their structure and the system of their development remain the
same. What is changed and modified are rather the relationships, the links
between the functions. New constellations emerge which were unknown in
the preceding stage.
(Vygotsky, 1997b, p. 92)
This theoretical crisis continued in 1931 and no apparent solution was found to
the numerous problems that Vygotsky was fruitlessly struggling with. Thus, in a letter to Luria, dated June 12, 1931,Vygotsky wrote:

86

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

I am still beset with thousands of petty chores. The fruitlessness of what I do
greatly distresses me. My scientific thinking is going off into the realm of fantasy, and I cannot think things through in a realistic way to the end. Nothing
is going right: I am doing the wrong things, writing the wrong things, saying
the wrong things. A fundamental reorganization is called for – and this time
I am going to carry it out.
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 36)
This dissatisfaction with the state of the art of his work continued and is evident
even by the end of 1932. This is how Vygotsky described this situation in the personal notes that he prepared for his presentation of December, 1932, a year and a
half before his death:
1. The necessity of a new stage of inquiry does not stem from the fact that a
new thought has occurred to me or a new idea has caught my interest, but
from the necessity of developing the research itself – new facts prod me into
searching for new and more intricate explanations. The narrowness, bias, and
schematism of the old mindset led us to a wrong assessment of the essential
principles, which we mistook for the secondary ones: interfunctional connections. We focused attention on the sign (on the tool) to the detriment of
the operation with it, representing it as something simple, which goes through
three phases: magical – external – internal. But the knot is external and the
teenager’s diary is external. Hence we have a sea of poorly explained facts and
a desire to delve more deeply into the facts, i.e., to evaluate them theoretically
in a different way. 2. The higher and lower functions are not constructed in
two tiers: their number and names do not match. But our previous understanding was not right either [, according to which] a higher function is the
mastery of the lower ([e.g.] voluntary attention is the subordination to it of
involuntary attention) because this means exactly – in two tiers.
(Vygotsky’s record titled “Symposium, December 4, 1932,”
see Zavershneva, 2010b, pp. 41–42; see also Chapter 5 and,
specifically, Chapter 6)
Yet, the situation was not as desperate as it might seem. Indeed, the early 1930s –
despite Vygotsky’s considerable dissatisfaction with the theory – opened up promising new avenues for empirical research and, quite possibly, new theoretical insights
and groundbreaking discoveries.
As we have seen, in his bibliography of January 13, 1933,Vygotsky included his
paper on schizophrenia that came out in English in the United States in 1932. This
paper was published through the mediation and translation of the local scholar and
medical doctor of Russian descent Jacob Kasanin, who by his admission had visited
the Soviet Union in 1930, where he met Vygotsky and had first-hand acquaintance with his work (on Kasanin and his role in the dissemination of Vygotsky’s
ideas in the West see also Chapter 7). On several occasions Vygotsky refers to his

Vygotsky the published

87

correspondence with Kasanin about his paper. Thus, in his letter of June 1, 1931,
he wrote to Luria: “I received a letter from Kasanin that my article is on the way”
(Vygotskii, 2004, p. 24). Indeed, the original paper that Vygotsky refers to in this
letter was apparently penned sometime in 1931 in order to eventually come out in
Russian in 1932 (Vygotskii, 1932a). This paper was imported by Kasanin into the
United States and was published there two years after its publication in Russian
(Vygotsky, 1934).
In the same letter we come across a reference to Vygotsky’s book: “I was terribly
happy about your words on my book. I know it is bad, but considering your pessimism (‘We haven’t done anything’) I am glad that you see the path with its broader
perspectives” (Vygotskii, 2004, p. 25).There is not much clarity about exactly which
book Vygotsky is referring to in this letter, but given that we know of only one book
that Vygotsky published in 1931, it might be a reference to volume 3 of his Pedology
of the adolescent. Perhaps this negative self-assessment is related to the over-delayed
Psychological dictionary (1931), co-authored with his associate BorisVarshava (1900–27)
and probably completed in the late 1920s. Alternatively, what Vygotsky meant by a
“book” was perhaps yet another unpublished manuscript that we now know as one
of his “lost works” (see the discussion of Vygotsky’s “lost work” below).
Yet, the topic of schizophrenia appeared to be of increasing interest to Vygotsky
and again and again it emerged in his personal correspondence.Thus, a year later, on
June 26, 1932,Vygotsky wrote to Luria: “I finished a lecture on schizophrenia and
would like to speak to you about several things in this context” (Vygotskii, 2004,
p. 35). Then, less than a month later,Vygotsky yet again returned to this disquieting
and exciting topic of interest. On July 13, 1932, he wrote to Luria: “I have written
a lecture on schizophrenia; I will tell you more about it in a few days” (Vygotskii,
2004, p. 36). And, indeed, this lecture and paper, finished in the summer of 1932, was
subsequently published in 1933 under the same title as his preceding “schizophrenia
paper,” which was very confusing. This persistent reference to the same mental disease indicates the whole new world of exciting research that Vygotsky switched to
in 1932–4: research on pathology in clinical settings.
In one of Vygotsky’s letters of 1933 we came across a most curious discussion of
an anticipated publication that, unfortunately, was never to materialize. Thus, in his
letter of November 21, 1933,Vygotsky wrote to Luria:
Finally, about the series: If it actually and periodically is to be published (at
all costs from issue to issue), we must take it into our own hands with full
responsibility. I have already: (1) the classification of aphasias, (2) Birenbaum
and Vygotsky: aphasia and dementia, (3) Birenbaum and Zeigarnik: agnosia, (4) Vygotsky: written speech in brain lesions, (5) Vygotsky: grammatical dysfunction, etc. etc. ohne Zahl, as our patient answers when asked about
the number of fingers she has on one hand. By the middle of December
I will have prepared my first article and we will prepare three or four others
in advance. A general title for all papers, as in Gestalttheorie, Lewin, etc., is
a must …

88

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

The title for the series (options):
1. psychological [crossed out] Investigation of the higher psychological
functions in development and disintegration;
2. investigations in clinical psychology;
3. experimental and psychological [the last word crossed out] clinical investigations in psychopathology;
4. investigation of thinking and speech in pathological dysfunctions;
5. psychological investigation of nervous and mental diseases;
6. psychological clinic of nervous and mental diseases.
(Vygotskii, 2004, p. xx)
The whole projected series of publications clearly indicates very intensive
research conducted by Vygotsky along with his Moscow (Birenbaum and Zeigarnik)
and Kharkov (Luria, Lebedinskii, and their associates at the Department of Clinical
Psychology) teams in 1932–4. Not much of this work has been published to date
and what was published appears of considerable interest not only to Vygotsky and
his collaborators at that time, but also to contemporary scholars who are generally unfamiliar with this work (Birenbaum, 1934; Birenbaum & Zeigarnik, 1935;
Kaganovskaia & Zeigarnik, 1935; Samukhin, 1934, 1935; Samukhin, Birenbaum, &
Vygotskii, 1934; Zeigarnik, 1934; Zeigarnik & Birenbaum, 1935).
Finally, the summative assessment of Vygotsky’s general contribution to psychology and specifically the theory of consciousness that he was most intensely
engaged with during the last two years of his life can be found in one of the
personal notes discovered among his archival documents. This is how Zavershneva
presents this quotation in her English publication of 2010:
The series “Manuscript Progress” includes an entry that apparently can be
regarded as the last one he made before leaving for the hospital. The entry
“Conference (Zaks)” [Konferentsiia (Zaks)] begins with someone else’s
words: Vygotsky quotes some science functionary who asks why he needs
a big auditorium, what he is going to talk about; then come fragmentary
notes from a symposium (a discussion of the clinical cases of Kogan and
Zaks), and then the text changes direction, as it no longer runs lengthwise
down the page but instead runs across it. It is a very personal entry that
indeed runs at right angles to the entire text, above it. It reflects the mood
with which Vygotsky said goodbye to what had constituted the purpose of
his life:
NB! Pro domo suo
This is the final thing I have done in psychology – and I will like Moses die
at the summit, having glimpsed the promised land but without setting foot on
it. Farewell, dear creations. The rest is silence.
(Zavershneva, 2010b, p. 58; see also Chapter 5)

Vygotsky the published

89

In order to shed light on this – fairly pessimistic and somewhat cryptic –
self-assessment of Vygotsky’s contribution to psychology we suggest comparing it
with the statements that Vygotsky made in the first and the last chapters of his last,
posthumously published, book Thinking and speech, where he equally pessimistically
asserted:
We perfectly well understand the inevitable imperfection of that first step in
the new direction that we tried to make in this work. But we see its justification in the fact that it, in our firm belief, moves us ahead in the investigation
of thinking and speech in comparison with that state of this problem that had
formed in psychology when we began our work. It uncovers the problem of
thinking and speech as the key problem of all psychology of man and directly
leads the researcher to a new psychological theory of consciousness. However,
we touch upon this problem only in the few concluding words of our work
and interrupt the investigation at its very threshold.4
(Vygotskii, 1934a, p. 3, in our translation from Russian)
This statement is mirrored in and further clarified by the concluding words on the
last page of the book, where Vygotsky clearly stated:
In conclusion of our investigation, we cannot but dwell in a few words upon
those perspectives that unfold beyond its threshold. Our investigation leads us
closely to the threshold of another, even vaster, even deeper, even more grandiose problem than the problem of thinking – the problem of consciousness.5
(Vygotskii, 1934a, p. 318, in our translation from Russian; for a
discussion of Vygotsky’s theory of consciousness see Zavershneva, 2014)
The similarity and parallels between these fragments and their imagery are as
striking as they are obvious: Written in the same period, they provide us with
Vygotsky’s perception of his work as unfinished, abandoned at the “threshold” of
the ultimate goal of his life. Thus, the history of Vygotsky’s (and his collaborators’)
theory of consciousness is the re-enactment of the old biblical plot, when the hero,
who brought his people from slavery to the Promised Land, has to die without ever
setting foot on the new terrain. And although the settings and guiding metaphors
in these two stories – the one of Vygotsky (and his research team) and the other
of Moses (and “his people”) – are quite different, in both cases the mission was not
entirely accomplished and the hero tragically dies.

“The lost works”
In order to identify the “best of the best” of Vygotsky, that is, to assess the relative importance of Vygotsky’s works we made the decision to “interview the
author” and analyzed his self-bibliographies, correspondence, and personal notes

90

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

for evidence of Vygotsky’s self-evaluation. We believe this method of research is
productive, although not universally applicable. Of course, someone might argue
that the author did not necessarily mention all the works that he considered most
important or valuable. The reason for this might be Vygotsky’s fear of criticism
and censorship that might eventually become a threat to his career or even lead to
political persecutions and criminal charges. Indeed, this was not an unlikely scenario in Stalinist Russia, although, at the same time, we believe that the horrors of
the so-called “repressed science” (repressirovannaia nauka) have been grossly overestimated by Western scholars of the Cold War era and their intellectual heirs in
the Soviet Union from the late 1980s and until now (see discussion of these issues
in Chapters 1 and 3). In any case, we are certainly aware of the phenomenon of
“self-censorship” in Soviet culture and science and need to analyze our findings
from this perspective, too.
We have carried out an additional investigation of the sources that we will call
here “the lost work” of Vygotsky. The reference is to those bibliographic items that
were either censored at some point in the history of Soviet censorship or were
mentioned in a source but never saw the light of day. Here we have something
that may well be interpreted as a layer of “unknown,” “hidden,” “prohibited,” or
“banned” works – works that are yet potentially utterly important for contemporary
scholarship. A list of the “lost works” of Vygotsky can be found in Appendix A. On
the basis of our analysis of this material we came to a few conclusions.
First, the list contains an incredible number of bibliographic entries that are
referred to in some source but were never published – at least not under the given
titles. Furthermore, this category includes a wealth of items that were reported as
“in press” (in several instances, in non-existent publishing houses), but no trace of
either subsequent publications or archival manuscripts were found (for discussion
of Vygotsky’s archival materials see Chapters 5 and 6). This leads us to the tentative conclusion that these manuscripts either never existed or were lost or even
“secretly borrowed” from the archive by Vygotsky’s anonymous admirers. Another
option is that some manuscripts did exist at some point but were renamed – quite
possibly, several times; for a historical precedent see the discussion of alternative
titles of Vygotsky’s manuscript “(Historical) Meaning of the crisis in psychology” in
(Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012b). Possibly, some of these manuscripts were reworked
and included in some other – unpublished or eventually published – works. For
example, we have certain reasons to believe that the text that was published under
the title The history of the development of higher psychological functions in the Soviet
edition of 1983 was constructed by the Soviet editors in the early 1980s on the
basis of two unrelated manuscripts of Vygotsky. In fact, this title never occurred
in a single one of Vygotsky’s texts or documents and a manuscript of that name is
conspicuously absent in Vygotsky’s archive. Most likely the text was compiled from
the manuscripts The investigation of higher psychological functions (1930) and (Outlines
of the) history of the cultural development of the normal and abnormal child (1928–9),
which were mechanically merged and published under one, artificially created title
(Vygotskii, 1983).

Vygotsky the published

91

Second, in several instances Vygotsky did not finish his work on certain manuscripts and abandoned them. These texts are clearly distinct from those that he did
finish and intended to publish. Perhaps the most notable feature of Vygotsky’s unfinished (and most often abandoned) works is the absence of full references in the body
of the text and the lack of a bibliography after the text.These are the case in his (The
historical) meaning of the psychological crisis (1926–7) or his variously titled manuscript
on Spinoza and the psychology of the emotions of 1932–3.This is also characteristic
of the last chapter of Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech in the original edition of 1934,
which was notably “rectified” in the “corrected” versions of the text of the later editions. These unfinished manuscripts stand in sharp contrast to the published works
of Vygotsky or, for that matter, his contemporaries and even to those unpublished
works that Vygotsky did finish and prepared for publication. An example of the latter
case is the book manuscript Psychology of art, which was, as we saw before, contracted
for publication but never published during Vygotsky’s lifetime.The argumentation in
this manuscript – as we know it from Soviet publications of the 1960s that came out
under the editorship of a non-psychologist, the philologist Vyacheslav Vs. Ivanov –
was most meticulously supported with numerous references and concluded with a
list of over 160 quoted sources (Vygotskii, 1965, 1968, 2008).
We know that in several instances there was nothing fatal or tragic in Vygotsky
abandoning his unfinished writings: thus, for instance, it was recently demonstrated
that virtually the entire set of key ideas of Vygotsky’s unfinished manuscript on
the crisis in psychology was eventually published during the author’s lifetime. The
ideas appeared in scholarly articles that were more consistent, coherent, and unambiguous than in the unfinished manuscript (Zavershneva, 2012b). There were also
instances – not unusual for Vygotsky – when already published segments of works
were later republished by their author (see our discussion of the case of Vygotsky’s
Thinking and speech, above).
Third, one might argue that the absence of Vygotsky’s references to his unpublished works was due to (self-)censorship and that they still are of considerable value
regardless of whether Vygotsky referred to them or not. Having examined our data,
we would disagree with such an interpretation. The example of The psychology of
art – Vygotsky’s doctoral dissertation manuscript that in all likelihood was banned
from publication for political reasons (such as references to “politically incorrect”
figures like Leon Trotsky) – is quite telling. The title does occur on Vygotsky’s own
list of his most important works. In contrast, a number of other “lost work” titles did
not deserve Vygotsky’s praise as important – yet unpublished – works. Perhaps these
works do not deserve our praise, either.

Conclusion: Vygotsky’s foundational works
The time has now come to connect the loose threads and put all the pieces of the
puzzle together. In order to single out Vygotsky’s main works we used the materials of his authorial bibliographies and focused on books that occurred no less than

92

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

twice in the three documents that we studied.That criterion resulted in the following list, ordered chronologically:
(1)
(2)
(3)
(4)
(5)

The psychology of art; unpublished manuscript, 1925–6
Pedagogical psychology; textbook, 1926
The pedology of school age; textbook, 1928
Imagination and creativity in children: A psychological essay; popular booklet, 1930
Studies on the history of behavior: Ape, primitive, child; book, 1930 (in collaboration
with Luria)
(6) Pedology of the adolescent; textbook in three volumes, 1929, 1930, and 1931.
These publications, according to Vygotsky, constituted the core of his published
works and appear to represent the main tenets of his theory. Yet, we would like to
comment on this list, as important as it is.
First, it is interesting to note that none of these works was included in the
Russian and English six-volume collection of Vygotsky’s works, with the exception
of part of the third volume of the Pedology of the adolescent. This seems to reveal a
quite remarkable discrepancy between the author’s representation of his scientific
legacy and the image of the “Vygotskian legacy” as it was constructed after World
War II by his “intellectual heirs”: the publishers, editors, and censors of his works –
typically, the very same people at the same time.
Second, as we know, not all Vygotsky’s publications were of equal scholarly value.
Thus, by Vygotsky’s own admission, several of them were written just to earn some
money (“khlebnye zakazy”). These were written in the popular science genre and
often based on secondary – most typically, Western – sources rather than original
research. This is the case for items four and, partially (i.e. the first half of the book),
five on the list.
Third, another series of Vygotsky’s publications was written for teaching purposes and addressed the lowest academic level, the undergraduate audience (see also
above).Therefore, these books also hardly qualify as solid theoretical treatises.This is
true of items of two, three, and six.
Finally, the list comprises works that were written not later than the end of 1930,
and, therefore, might illustrate Vygotsky’s ideas of his “instrumental” – mechanistic
and fairly reductionist – thinking of the 1920s but not the major shift that his thinking underwent in the tumultuous 1930s.
For all these reasons, we need to augment the above list with other works: the
publications that came out after 1931 and, where possible, the studies that Vygotsky
and the members of the “Vygotsky circle” conducted during Vygotsky’s “holistic
period” of the 1930s. This task is quite challenging, given the dramatic decrease in
publications of this period, Vygotsky’s self-criticism, and the apparently unfinished
nature of his project of the theory of consciousness (see discussion in Zavershneva,
2014).Therefore, the best we have is a series of posthumous publications of 1934–6 –
apparently, not authorized by their author for publication, but still, in our estimate,
not so profoundly and shamelessly edited (and distorted) as the Soviet publications

Vygotsky the published

93

of the 1950s–80s. The materials are somewhat fragmented and further research will
be needed to reconstruct the overall image of Vygotsky’s “holistic period.” This
reconstruction will chiefly be done on the basis of several books that came out after
Vygotsky’s death under the editorship of his associates V. N. Kolbanovskii, Zh. I.
Shif, L. V. Zankov, D. B. El’konin, M. A. Levina, and I. I. Daniushevskii. These titles
include:
(1)
(2)
(3)
(4)

Thinking and speech; book, 1934
Foundations of pedology; textbook, 1934/5
Mental development of children in the process of learning and instruction; book, 1935
The mentally retarded child; book, 1935.

Vygotsky mentioned none of these publications as being among his best
published works (the only exception is Thinking and speech, which occurs in his
auto-bibliography of January 1933), and for quite obvious reasons: they were posthumous editions prepared and submitted for publication after the author’s death on
June 11, 1934. For one reason or another they can be criticized for lack of scholarly
integrity or as not being sufficiently “scientific”: thus, the list includes a publication
based on shorthand notes of Vygotsky’s undergraduate lectures (item 2) or edited
collections of works written on different occasions at different periods of Vygotsky’s
life (items (3) and (4), and, as we have already demonstrated, item (1) – Thinking and
speech – also fit this description). And still, these are the publications that give us an
idea about how and in what direction Vygotsky’s thought developed during the last
years of his life.Yet again, with the exception of the first item, none of these sources
was included in the six-volume Russian or English edition of Vygotsky’s Collected
works or fully republished during the Cold War/Soviet period.
Finally, the list of Vygotsky’s books of the 1930s needs to be augmented with
various smaller publications by the members of Vygotsky’s circle who worked
on the problems of speech impairment and other dysfunctions caused by trauma,
neurological and infectious diseases, brain lesions, and psychiatric disorders. An
important direction of their theoretical work was the deliberate synthesis of certain
of Vygotsky’s ideas on mental development with the ideas of Gestalt psychologists,
perhaps most notably those of Kurt Lewin and his associates. For references to specific publications and the overview of these scientific interdependencies and transnational developments see Chapters 2 and 9.
All this creates a fairly new, not so familiar image of Vygotsky and his scientific
legacy. Readers – particularly those readers who have always considered themselves
as experts in “Vygotskian psychology” – might feel uneasy and startled by the terra
incognita and the new pathways that it suggests. If so, we would consider our mission
accomplished. In fact, this is what the “revisionist revolution” in Vygotsky studies
is all about.

5
VYGOTSKY THE UNPUBLISHED
An overview of the personal archive
(1912–1934)
Ekaterina Zavershneva

The main reasons to study Vygotsky’s personal archive
In this chapter we will present the results of our study of Vygotsky’s personal
papers in the family archive. It has been argued repeatedly that Vygotsky’s personal
archive should be studied scientifically (e.g. Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, p. 9).
The main reason is that this source has never been subjected to systematic historical scrutiny. Even during the preparation of the Russian edition of Vygotsky’s
Collected works in six volumes in the early 1980s, no systematic ordering of the
archive took place, no inventory of the documents was made, and the manuscripts
published in these volumes were not studied in any detail. Another reason to
begin our study of the archive in 2006 was the unreliability of the text of the
Collected works, which until now remains the basis for republication of his works
in Russian and other languages. It is well known that the policy of active editorial interference in the original text led to a large number of unjustified factual
and semantic changes (and even replacements), which continue to be reprinted
in new editions. The level of historical analysis of Vygotsky’s works also leaves
something to be desired: for example, the dates of writing of manuscripts, such
as The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis, Defect and compensation, and The
problem of consciousness, are given incorrectly. Also, the text itself of the writings in
the Collected works and in other Soviet writings is almost never subjected to critical analysis. A positive exception is Ivanov’s edition of The psychology of art (see
Vygotskii, 1968). Apart from that, the investigation of the personal archive should
involve the historically most interesting part: the notebooks, scientific diaries, and
scattered notes, which until the early 2000s remained practically untouched. As
it turned out, it was precisely the study of Vygotsky’s personal notes that formed
the missing link that allowed us to view the creation of a great number of works,

Vygotsky the unpublished 95

such as Thinking and speech, A theory of the emotions, and Pedology of the adolescent in
a new light. It proved possible to establish the date of writing of manuscripts more
exactly and to uncover several of Vygotsky’s plans for future books and investigations that never materialized.
When we began the ordering of the archival materials we had two major
goals: (1) to search for hitherto unknown writings, and (2) to reconstruct the
original form of previously published texts. It seemed obvious that the archive would reveal many new facts about Vygotsky’s life, but even the preliminary results far surpassed our expectations. We were able to find unknown
manuscripts (several of them from the Gomel period of his life), we could
complete the bibliography of his works, and we managed to bring order to the
personal notes: from the early years (1912–16) to the notes written in the final
months of his life (1933–4). More than two dozen previously unknown shorthand versions of lectures were found. These included the lectures The problem
of perception (April 25, 1934) and The main problems of the preschool age (May 7,
1934), which Vygotsky gave one and a half months before his death and which
were not mentioned in existing bibliographies. There is also a rare but excellently preserved copy of an issue of Veresk, the journal that the young Vygotsky
edited in Gomel. At present Vygotsky’s very first text, dedicated to the book of
Ecclesiastes, and entitled The tragicomedy of strivings (1912), is ready for publication. We also found drafts of an analysis of Shakespeare’s Hamlet, theater and
book reviews from the Gomel period, the manuscripts of the talks at the Second
All-Russian Congress of Psychoneurology and the 2nd SPON1 Congress in
1924 – with these talks the young researcher from Gomel entered psychological
science (Vygotskii, 2013; Zavershneva, 2013), and much more. In addition, we
reconstructed the authentic text of The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis, from which the references to ‘inconvenient’ authors (e.g. Trotsky, Bukharin,
and Radek) had been removed (Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b). Finally,
we reconstructed the idea of the monograph Foundations of defectology and deciphered and systematized practically all of Vygotsky’s notes. The present chapter
succinctly presents the results of the investigations carried out in this part of the
personal archive.
The unequivocal scientific value of the archival notes is not just that they reveal
ideas and plans that were not realized, but they also help us to follow the process of
Vygotsky’s thinking. Most interesting are not so much his chains of reasoning but
the leaps of thought, the sudden flashes of insight, the ruptures in the argumentation. More than elsewhere, in these notes one feels Vygotsky’s direct presence as a
colorful and open person and at the same time a deeply tragic figure, who realized
that the time allotted to him was short and the task immense; a task that he planned
to solve if not alone, then with a small group of kindred spirits. This task was the
creation of a general psychological theory in which all preceding approaches would
be “superseded” (to use a Hegelian term Vygotsky often used), which, in its turn,
would allow us to overcome the crisis in psychology.

96

Ekaterina Zavershneva

General characteristics of the personal notes
Outside appearance and degree of preservation
It must be noted that the archive has survived to date thanks to the Vygotsky family
and, notably,Vygotsky’s wife Roza Noevna and his eldest daughter Gita L’vovna.The
personal notes are generally very well preserved, although somewhat less well than
the bigger manuscripts.This is because they were not meant to be read by other persons and were jotted down on whatever was at hand, such as pieces of paper, cards,
the backs of forms, off-prints, and even newspaper pages. All documents are small –
the result of Vygotsky’s predilection for miniature notebooks and the acute shortage
of paper in those times. As a rule,Vygotsky has written on both sides of the paper –
mostly in no particular order, so that the order of the notes must be reconstructed
when preparing texts for publication – in his small but clear handwriting, which
changed markedly toward the end of his life. In the years 1933 and 1934, Vygotsky
frequently resorted to abbreviations, the text lost most of its vowels, and sometimes
a phrase or quotation was indicated with just the first letters of the words. In the
notes from 1934, Vygotsky’s handwriting becomes unsteady and the paper shows
tiny brown specks, which presumably reflect the fact that the author had just a few
months to live and died of a hemorrhage caused by chronic tuberculosis of the lungs.
Unsorted, the notes filled approximately five to six voluminous boxes, filled to
the brim with small leaflets.The notes were scattered over the boxes and painstaking
work was required to reconstruct the complete texts from the separate fragments.
Several documents contain sketches that are hard to read, because they are partially
erased or faded by sunlight (part of the notes were kept outside the archive under
inappropriate conditions). However, there was little that could not be reconstructed,
not counting the cases when we did not manage to find the missing pages in the
notes. Several records remain unstudied, however, because reading them requires the
use of ultraviolet light or handwriting expertise.

Special characteristics of the work with Vygotsky’s notes
The same circumstances that made Vygotsky’s work difficult now substantially
help the investigator of the archive. Had these fragments been typed on simple
white paper, it would have been extremely difficult to assemble the complete texts,
let alone to establish their date of writing. However, each piece of paper in the
archive has its own profile, its own individuality. A first sorting of the documents
according to the type of paper immediately revealed several basic groups of documents, within which it was easier to find one’s way. Subsequently, within each group,
subgroups could be distinguished on the basis of both their content and formal
characteristics, to wit:
(1) the type and format of the paper;
(2) the handwriting characteristics (slope, roundness, extent of abbreviation, etc.);

Vygotsky the unpublished 97

(3) the numbering of the pages or statements (the position on the page, brackets,
dots, number type, etc.), type of ink or pencil;
(4) persistent quotations, expressions characteristic of a specific period, repetitive
aphorisms or sayings, frequently used foreign terms;
(5) mention of names of collaborators, colleagues, students; references to events
known from the literature about Vygotsky; polemics with the major scientific
opponents of a specific period;
(6) the theme of the notes and their link with the central themes of the given
period as reflected in Vygotsky’s published and unpublished texts; references to
his own writings;
(7) the link of the major themes of the notes to material both within a given group
and with other groups.
Both the formal characteristics and the content characteristics (with the exception of point (3), of course) were not just used to assemble the texts, but also to
establish their date of writing, because Vygotsky rarely put a date on such notes.
Within one group, texts from which the date of writing was firmly established
formed a reference point for the work with the other texts; comparison of similar
text fragments yielded approximate dates. Material with printed dates (for example,
library cards, invitations, bills) provided a lower time boundary, just like quotations
from works with a known date of writing or publication.
The theoretical foundation for the work with the documents was a detailed periodization of Vygotsky’s scientific life that we created. It describes the hard core of
his scientific research program (the main principles) and the related ideas (auxiliary
hypotheses, research methods, etc.), the problematic propositions, the vulnerable spots
in his conception, a list of anomalies (i.e. facts which require another explanation)
caused by progressive shifts in the scientific research program, and also an indication
of Vygotsky’s main opponents with whom he had oral or written discussions that
were very important for the development of his thinking. These key moments were
used as chronological markers, but in a number of cases it turned out that the generally accepted view on the appearance of some theme in Vygotsky’s work, based on
his published writings, itself needed correction. In particular, the archival documents
showed that the cultural-historical theory in the proper sense of the word originated
in mid-1926 (notebook from Zakhar’ino hospital) and that the so-called “principle
of the semantic structure of consciousness” was introduced no later than mid-1932.

The most important archival documents: series of notes,
exercise books and notebooks, separate notes, letters
The notes cover the greater part of Vygotsky’s life, but the various periods are
represented unevenly: most materials belong to the years 1931–4. The year 1932
is particularly well represented – both quantitatively and qualitatively. This was the
year of a turning point, a huge step forward from the system principle, formulated
toward the end of 1930 in the talk On psychological systems (Vygotsky, 1997b), to the

98

Ekaterina Zavershneva

hypothesis of the semantic structure of consciousness. Also well represented are the
final two years of Vygotsky’s life, when he worked with incredible energy and was
full of plans for the future.
As said before, the division into groups was carried out on the basis of a series of
criteria. It primarily served the practical function of systematizing the archive and
also allowed us to preserve the chronological order in the exposition of a theme.
A separate investigation should be dedicated to the links between the groups, the
historical continuity, and the dynamics in the treatment of certain key problems
that return in all periods of Vygotsky’s work. However, these tasks go beyond the
boundaries of this chapter and we will limit ourselves to the preliminary description of the notes and the most important documents that substantially complete or
change our ideas about Vygotsky’s biography.
The titles of the notes cited below are based on their first lines; the shorthand
abbreviations (leaving out letters in words) have been deciphered, the spelling and
punctuation of the original have been changed in accordance with contemporary
norms. Editorial insertions are given in square brackets; illegible words are given
in angle brackets, and a provisional interpretation is provided. No emphasis in the
quotations has been added.

Notes from the years 1912–1930
Exercise books and notebooks
Exercise books 1–3 revealed little-known aspects of Vygotsky’s work from the years
1912–15.
1. The tragicomedy of strivings. This is an exercise book, labeled 1912, that contains the draft of a talk dedicated to the interpretation of the book of Ecclesiastes.
Presumably, it is the detailed plan for a talk that the gymnasium student Lev Vygotsky
planned to give during one of the sessions of the circle for the study of Hebrew history and the philosophy of history (for a description of that circle, see Feigenberg,
2000, pp. 7–11).Vygotsky set himself the task to present a philosophical and psychological analysis of Ecclesiastes as an “eternal symbol” of the history that repeats itself
in the spiritual quest of each person, a sort of existential Odyssey. He distinguished
three periods in the life of Ecclesiastes, pointed out their connection, and wrote that
the main tragedy of human life is felt as the impossibility of achieving the plenitude of being. However, each of us is forced to live under the circumstances of a
fragmented, polar world, with its irreconcilable contradictions: “we ask, and know
in advance that we will not get it; we knock the door of knowledge that is forever
closed, knowing in advance that it will not be opened; we seek, knowing in advance
that we shall never find,”2 writes the 16-year-old author. And, nevertheless, the book
of Ecclesiastes, a complete mystery, shows that an answer can be found: despite the
fact that “no one has as yet unraveled life” we may bring ourselves to accept it if we
do not search for the temporary but for the eternal, with its inaccessible plenitude

Vygotsky the unpublished 99

and integrity, which is projected on the surface of the world as a struggle between
contrasts.
The text was written in the old spelling and among the cited authors we find
Belinsky, Dostoevsky, Steinthal, and others. The tone and content (the tragedy of
existence in a disrupted world, the longing for the absolute) resonate with the
Tragedy of Hamlet.
2. Exercise books with sketches (drafts) for a large text, in which the historical
fate of the Jewish people is discussed and the possibility of the creation of the state
of Israel.Vygotsky criticized the ideas of radical anti-Semite and pro-Jewish political movements (in particular, nationalism, autonomism, and so on). The document
is dated 1915 and written in the old spelling. No less than half of the text consists
of elaborate quotations from the works of philosophers, politicians, journalists, and
writers who in their works touched on the Jewish question (e.g. Judah Halevi,
Chaim Bialik, Nikolai Berdiaev, Semen Dubnov, and Daniil Pasmanik). Most of
the text is rather abstract. However, the end of the fifth exercise book contains a
quite striking fragment about the tragedy of the Jewish people during World War I,
which was at its height when Vygotsky wrote these passages, as if he apprehended
the future Holocaust. The Jewish people bear the stamp of a tragic fate and at the
same time of divine providence, he remarked. On the last page of the fifth exercise
book,Vygotsky glued a clipping from the newspaper Russkoe Slovo:
Finally, I shall present a small episode from this war, which in light of the great
events is lifted to the height of a general symbol:
Brother against brother.
In one of the last battles near the river San a Jewish soldier killed an Austrian
rifleman with a blow of his bayonet and was himself wounded on the spot.
When he was brought to the field hospital he refused to leave the wagon.
He asked the nurses and the doctor not to touch him and refused to answer
questions. The rabbi was called and the dying man told him: “When I hit the
enemy in the chest with my bayonet, he fell and cried: ‘Shma Yisrael!’ (‘Hear,
O Israel!’) – the words that the Jews pronounce when they die or are in terrible danger. I then decided that, although I fought as my duty and military
conscience demanded, I could no longer live” (Russ. Sl.).
In this symbol-fact all sides of Jewish history emerge as if in a focal point,
are refracted, and glow in light of the great events. Two Jews (“whoever says
‘Shma Yisrael’ is a Jew”, says the Talmud, and from the desperation of the one
and the pain of the other it is clear that both are Jews; here Jewry lives in this
pain) act in this insoluble tragedy as blind tools of dark forces – one is killed,
the other driven crazy – and from the grave and from insanity (from outside
the usual) they shout the meaning of this tragic senselessness.
Later this episode found its way into one of Vygotsky’s articles for the journal
Novyi Put’, under the title “Thoughts and Moods: Some Lines about Hanukkah”

100

Ekaterina Zavershneva

(Vygotskii, 1916), and several of its ideas were included in a series of articles about
the Jewish question and the fate of Jewry in Russia (Zavershneva, 2012a).
3. An undated exercise book belonging to a larger study of Dostoevsky begins
on page 33 and has no conclusion (the missing pages of the manuscript were not
found). The paper is the same as that of the Tragicomedy of strivings and the spelling is old (thus, the notes may date from the years 1914–15). Its major theme is
Dostoevsky’s attitude to the Jewish question and the way Jews are depicted in his
novels.Vygotsky rejected the common opinion that Dostoevsky shared the ‘everyday anti-Semitism’ of his time, pointed out that Dostoevsky penetrated deeply
into the essence of the issue, and argued that his view can only be regarded as
national extremism after a first and superficial study.Vygotsky analyzed the scene of
Svidrigailov’s suicide in Crime and punishment and wrote:
After Svidrigailov’s nightmare, why exactly was it a Jew who proved to be
the ‘official witness’ of his suicide? Was it accidentally? Or a continuation of
the nightmare? … There is a hidden, inexplicable, symbolic meaning in this
scene, whose reality borders on the fantastic … a stroke of genius of incredible
force gives the whole scene its strange appearance and fills it with symbolic
meaning.
In any case this stroke is unforgettable: the real, and at the same time fantastic, nightmarish, delirious scene, flooded with a strange, enigmatic twilight,
gets lost in a thick milky fog, and a strange, unlikely (and at the same time real)
“small person, muffled in a grey military coat and wearing a copper Achilles
helmet” merges with this fog and becomes inseparable from it, and the sorrow
of the dying night merges with the “age-old peevish sorrow” on his face, and
we hear, and hear, and hear this sorrowful, anxious, frightened … “This is not
the right place!” and his mournful Jewish pupils become wider and wider …
Vygotsky showed that Dostoevsky took all the typical everyday viewpoints
about the Jewish personality and appearance to create a symbolic character, who
lacks all accidental features and who has some mystic, marked relationship with fate
and death. Unfortunately, the manuscript contains only fragments from the second
part and it is almost impossible to guess its conclusions.We may assume that the text
is a preliminary version of a little-known article, called The Jews and the Jewish question in Dostoevsky’s writings, which was first published in the Israeli newspaper Vesti
in 1997, and subsequently in a volume with several of Vygotsky’s early writings
(Feigenberg, 2000). According to Bela Kotik-Friedgut (personal communication),
the manuscript of this article is in the archive of Semen Dobkin, one of Vygotsky’s
friends in Gomel.
4. A notebook dated summer 1925. This tiny gilt-edged booklet contains
Vygotsky’s diary of a trip abroad. It is about Vygotsky’s visit to a London congress
dedicated to the problems of deaf-mute children, the only foreign trip he ever
made. A large part of the notebook contains notes of a personal character. Judging
by the notes in this notebook, the foreign trip became one of the major events in
Vygotsky’s life:

Vygotsky the unpublished 101

In a foreign country, in the fresh air (an airplane) at sea – you feel a strong
aloofness of everything. A reconsideration of your whole life, a soul-searching.
Suddenly you look at your whole life from aside, as in the minute of
death … In my destiny, this is the sign of enormous future ordeals. Am
I afraid? Of course, I am afraid, I feel terror, but I keep it in check … My
journey is astounding and its purport is much more important and larger than
just its work aspect.
However, the personal was tightly interwoven with the non-personal; Vygotsky
made a quite striking statement in his notebook in which he connected his fate
with that of the new Russia:
In essence, Russia is the first country in the world. The Revolution is our
supreme cause. Just 1 person in this room knows the secrets of the genuine
education of d-m [deaf-mutes, EZ] – and that person is me. Not because I am
more educated than the others, but because I was sent by Russia and I speak
on behalf of the Revolution.
These words spoken to himself, in a notebook for private use that miraculously
survived, confirm that his attitude toward the events in his country was not for
show (let alone that he was following instructions), but formed part of a deeply felt
personal conviction.
The notebook also contains information about the sights that Vygotsky saw in
England as well as addresses and telephone numbers of colleagues from various
European countries, excerpts from library catalogues, impressions from visits to the
British Museum, and so on.
This notebook was the most difficult to work with; its pages were greasy, the
pencil faded and some fragments probably cannot be reconstructed. Nevertheless,
on the basis of the pages that could be read (some 80 percent of the notebook) and a
cross-validation with the congress events,Vygotsky’s stay in London could be almost
exactly reconstructed (Van der Veer & Zavershneva, 2011).
5. The notebook from Zakhar’ino hospital from the first half of 1926. It is a
voluminous text with valuable notes that throw light on one of the periods in
Vygotsky’s life that is most hard to analyze: the origin of the cultural-historical
theory in the strict sense of the term. As far as we know, the notebook contains the
earliest formulation of the theory of sign mediation. It also has notes concerning
the problem of consciousness and the psychophysical problem, additions to The
psychology of art, the first draft of the manuscript The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis, a plan for the unwritten monograph Zoön politikon, in which Vygotsky
planned to give a sketch of a theory of consciousness based on the idea of the social
origin of consciousness, and propositions about the leading role of speech in the
genesis of consciousness.
The notebook begins with a note that adds to The psychology of art and the
Tragedy of Hamlet (the analysis of several fables, new propositions and citations) and
discusses Alfred Adler’s idea of supercompensation:

102

Ekaterina Zavershneva

This is a central set of ideas, which I share in the psychology of art, but it does
not come from Freud or from the formalists. In essence, it is a dialectical principle of a philosophical, epistemic nature.3
However, the principal themes of the notebook are the relationship between thought
and word (in which Aleksandr Potebnia’s influence is notable4), word meaning (a
theme that Vygotsky developed further only in the 1930s!), consciousness as an
interiorized system of social relationships (Marx is frequently quoted), consciousness as an internal dialogue (Vygotsky refers to Lev Shcherba5 and, remarkably,
not to Mikhail Bakhtin6). We shall give one fragment of the notebook, in which
Vygotsky writes about the leading role of speech in the development of consciousness and plans a series of papers (or chapters of a book) about thinking and speech:
2. The difference between the vocal and the speech reaction is not in their
symbolic nature, which they share: the conditional vocal and speech stimuli
are both symbolic, the unconditional are both nonsymbolic. The difference
is not about the relationship between things. The word is not a relationship between the sound and the object to which it refers. It is a relationship
between the speaker and the listener, a relationship between people, directed
toward the object; it is an interpsychic reaction, which establishes the unity
of two organisms in one direction toward the object. Linguistics makes the
word into a fetish; the psychologist reveals that behind the apparent relationships between things are relationships between people (cf. Marx’s commodity
fetishism). It signifies the unity of the reaction of 2 persons or 2 reactions, not
of two stimuli.
3. Consciousness = speech in oneself … deliberate speech is always a dialogue (Shcherba). Consciousness is a dialogue with oneself. The fact that the
child first listens and understands and only later acquires verbal consciousness
already shows that 1. Consciousness originates in experience, 2. Speaking
with himself = acting consciously, the child places himself in the position of
the other, relates to himself as to the other, imitates the other who speaks to
him, replaces the other in the relationship to himself …
5. The meaning of word [sic; in English in the original, EZ] is not the
object which it replaces but a dialogue (the function of listening is speaking
in oneself); the relationship between people is speech; between objects it is a
symbol; between each of the speakers and the word (the thing) it is empathy.
Each article must end with the statement of a problem for the next one.
Epigraphs to the whole book: form and appearance according to Marx, I –
Zoön politikon,7 II – Freud word and consciousness, III – Lipps – empathy
makes organisms social. The title of the whole book is Zoön politikon: The key
to the psychology of man.
At the end of the notebook we find statements that were developed further
in The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis, in particular about the indirect

Vygotsky the unpublished 103

method on which scientific psychology must rely. The handwriting is at times
inconsistent, illegible; notes of a personal nature testify to the fact that Vygotsky had
not yet recovered from a severe attack of tuberculosis. The breakthrough toward a
new theory literally took place on the verge of death and the end of the notebook
coincides with the discharge from the hospital:
In the half year spent in this home, where death was as everyday and common
as the morning breakfast and the doctor’s round, I absorbed so many impressions of death that I am inclined to death just like a tired person is inclined
to sleep.
Before departure
May 18, 1926
However, fatigue and torpidity gave way to new enthusiasm.Very many themes
are addressed in the notebook and the formulation of the new conception became
increasingly clear:
Thus, the differences of the word: it is an artificially created stimulus (cf. technique), it is a tool of behavior; it presupposes two subjects and one object.
Verbal behavior differs from nonverbal behavior just like labor differs from
the adaptation of animals.
In this citation we find both the psychological question of the specific characteristics of man and the future scheme of the instrumental act, which was later
precisely described in the talk The instrumental method (Vygotsky, 1997c). It is factual
proof that Vygotsky formulated the basis of his cultural-historical approach already
mid-1926. It was only in December 1927/January 1928 that this theory became
public in his talk The development of the difficult child and its study, presented at the
1st All-Union Pedology Congress. Of course, we can find the pre-requisites of this
approach in all of Vygotsky’s early writings but only in 1926 were they combined
and thereby became a hard core of the conception, i.e. a series of postulates, upon
which the whole scientific research program rests that subsequently became refined
and formulated anew. The system principle was added in 1930 and the principle
of the semantic structure of consciousness in 1932. We suggest the following as a
criterion for the birth of the cultural-historical conception in the strict sense of
the term: the synthesis of two ideas that until 1926 were not clearly connected
(i.e. the principle of sign mediation, which in the continuation was dramatically
changed and receded to the background of the conception, and the idea of the
cultural development of the mind). In the past, it was assumed that 1927 was the
critical, transitional year: Vygotsky’s correspondence with his colleagues gave rise
to the idea that the experimental investigations with the new method of the double
stimulation were carried out in the summer of 1927 but did not reach the press.
Now we know that the theoretical basis for these investigations was formulated one
year earlier.

104

Ekaterina Zavershneva

The notebook has been published in the internet journal Gefter;8 a detailed analysis can be found in Zavershneva (2012b).

Various notes
1. A series of notes about the Jewish question on the pages of an exercise book. It
primarily contains excerpts from other authors (Merezhkovsky, Smirnova-Rosset,
Dostoevsky), lists of Hebraisms in Russian, plans for various articles (“The Problem
of the New Jewry,” “The Jewish Socialist Movement,” and so on). Dates from
approximately 1914–17, old spelling.
2. Several poems written by Vygotsky in various years (1915, 1921). Written in
the form of syllabic quatrains, stylistically close to the poetry of Symbolism.
3. A series of notes about The psychology of art (no date) on narrow strips of
paper. Preparatory texts for chapters on the proverb, the saying, and the riddle, lists
of Russian proverbs (presumably not later than 1925).
4. A short note, called N.B. Will (The central idea), written on the upper part of
stationery from the Osborne Hotel (2, Gordon Place, London, WC1). It is difficult
to give an exact date for the fragment. As mentioned, Vygotsky was in London in
1925, but this does not mean that the note is from that year as he was in the habit
of collecting pieces of paper (with or without text) and writing on them later on.
Several of the terms in this note (dominant, subdominant) we meet in his writings
of 1924–5, whereas the idea of the social organization of brain centers was elaborated only in the last years of his life. Nevertheless, we cannot exclude 1925 as the
date of writing, despite the fact that an exact formulation of the idea of the “transfer inward of social relationships” appears only in 1926 (all in all, we hypothesize
that the note was written around 1927–8). The note shows that Vygotsky began
thinking about the theme of volition much earlier than is evident from his publications. The abbreviated aphorism natura parendo vincitur (the translation of the
complete text is “nature to be commanded must be obeyed”) belongs to Francis
Bacon and was frequently used in the notes from 1928; for example, in the notes
3.04.1928 and NB! Lightnings of Spinozian thought (Vygotsky, 2010). This is the
first part of the note.
NB.
The will (the central idea) must not be derived from the mutual subordination and regulation of centers (cortex, subcortex) and processes (dominant, subdominant), but from social relationships that are transferred inward
and embodied in the activity of the centers when they make use of natural,
organic subordination (superseded category, executive mechanism, parendo
vincitur). What are these relationships?
Commands of subordination.
Cf. Blondel: the mechanisms of will and maximal obedience coincide.
[In the left margin with pencil; EZ] Nietzsche: the affect of commanding.

Vygotsky the unpublished 105

5. A similar theme is dealt with in another document, called The instrumental
method and will, presumably written in 1928–9. Here we see the typical formulations
characteristic of this period and similar to the statements in his talk The instrumental
method, which was characterized by a rather rigid and partly mechanistic interpretation of cultural development, discarded by Vygotsky after 1930. The instrumental
act here functions as Archimedes’ fulcrum to move the world.
NB! 1. Why is the I.M. [Instrumental Method; EZ] important for education,
for the abnormal child, etc.?
In the instrumental function of sign use we find the root of all culture (=
higher intellectual development). Whoever is incapable of the instrumental
function, is incapable of arithmetic, language, writing, mnemotechnics, etc.,
etc. Let us call this X, this function, ‘will’, i.e. intellect that has reached such [a
level of] development that it is turned to itself.
Сf. Spinoza. Int. et vol. iden.9
This note is primarily valuable because it contains an explicit definition of will
that is based on processes of thought, moreover, thought that for the first time can
consider itself (reflect). It is clear that other processes as well – attention, in particular, but even practical action – will have a volitional and intellectual nature.Volition
and thinking are placed at the center of the psychological system, which also rests
