upon the almighty sign operation. Another archival note (On giftedness, presumably
1929–30) reflects Vygotsky’s youthful optimism in aphoristic form: “Everything can
be replaced – Binet: this guarantees unlimited development.” But within two or
three years his interpretation of the process of development changed considerably.
We can only master what we have,Vygotsky wrote in Thinking and speech (Vygotsky,
1934, p. 227). Obviously, the mind is not infinitely malleable by outside forces, the
sign operation builds on a foundation that is somehow prepared, and, having rooted,
proceeds differently depending on that foundation. As is well known, when around
1933–4 Vygotsky created his conception of the cooperation of nature and culture,
heredity and environment, he introduced the notion of the zone of proximal development, which provides a measure of what can be potentially reached with the help
of the instrumental act.
6. A note about the name of his own conception is particularly interesting,
because until now we have had no idea whatsoever about Vygotsky’s preferences for
a name. In a document that may have been written in 1930 we read:
NB! The name, the designation we lack. It must not be a signboard (intuitivism). Not instrumental, not cultural, not significative, not constructive, etc.
Not just to avoid confusion with other theories, but also because of inner
vagueness: for example, the idea of the analogy with the instrument = just the
wood, more essential is the dissimilarity. Culture: but where does culture itself
come from (it is not primordial, but this is hidden). Thus:

106

Ekaterina Zavershneva

1.

as a designation of the method
method of double stimulation;
2. for the theory as a whole
(a) psychology of the higher functions, i.e.
(b) historical psychology or
(c) the historical theory of the higher psychological functions.
Because the central concept for us is the concept of the
higher function:
it contains the theory
(a) about its development, (b) about its psychological nature, (c) about
its method of investigation.

Having discussed the name,Vygotsky opted for variant 2с but we should not forget that here he was characterizing his approach in the version of the late 1920s, i.e.
before the radical changes. Already by the fall of 1930, the concept of “higher psychological function” ceased to be a guiding instrument, a leading concept, although
it remained part of the conceptual framework.

Letters
The letters from Bykhovskii, Shklovskii, Ehrenburg, Ivanovskii, Kornilov and Luria
found in the archive also belong to the period 1912–29.The most interesting documents are two letters by Kornilov, at that moment a well-known scholar and director of the Institute of Experimental Psychology, to Assistant Professor Vygotsky,
whom he had hired some time before. Kornilov openly expressed his delight with
The psychology of art, which Vygotsky had submitted as his doctoral thesis. One of
the letters Vygotsky received at Zakhar’ino hospital in April, 1926. We will present
some fragments from the other letter, dated October 12, 1925:
If I had not read your dissertation (and I only just finished it), I would address
you with the usual “Most respected”, but now my whole being longs to call
you “Dear Lev Semenovich” and this is why …
Your application of the principle of monophasic energy expenditure in the
domain of emotions is exceptionally good, I had not thought about such an
interpretation of the application of the principle.10 But what especially surprised me is that you and I simultaneously raised the question of the explosive
reactions.While you arrived at these explosive reactions from purely theoretical considerations about art, I myself, as you know, arrived at this question
purely experimentally, as a direct continuation of the investigation of reactions of a more complex order …
It was this coincidence of results, using completely different methods, that
surprised me so much: it is the best proof of the ideological affinity between
us. And for the first time I clearly realized that if we were to succeed in working together for a few more years, we would no doubt form an ideologically

Vygotsky the unpublished 107

exceptionally close-knit collective. And somehow I thought that no one other
than you, with your creative mind, might organically grow into this collective, while at the same time preserving all of your rich individuality. This is
why it is so extremely painful to me to realize that you are temporarily absent
from our ranks, due to your disease. Let us hope that it will be temporary and
that you will soon recover. I regret it very much that we cannot organize a
public defense of your dissertation to have the opportunity to tell the others about its exceptional value. But I think that only now, when your disease
has exacerbated, will we get all formalities through the board and the other
echelons. When you have regained your strength, we will, independent of all
formalities, organize a public discussion of your work. We’ll catch up with
what needs to be done, and until that time will give you the possibility to use
all your rights connected with your work, which is your unconditional right.
I studied your work for a week, learning from you how to deal with the
aesthetic reaction, how to treat it from our position.You did a tremendous job
and I am extremely glad that I have such a strong comrade to work with, and
this joy gave me the right to call you “dear”, which, I hope, you will not mind.
My deepest and sincerest regards,
K. Kornilov
P.S. No answer whatsoever is required; you must avoid all strain. We will
talk later.
It should be mentioned that we have another document specifying the conditions of the written defense of The psychology of art as a doctoral thesis. The rest is
self-evident.

Notes from 1930 to 1934
Before we proceed to a description of the documents, we will give a brief sketch of
the last part of Vygotsky’s scientific life, because all notes cited below are intimately
connected and in the notes we can trace leading ideas that have the principle of the
semantic structure of consciousness as their basis.
The system principle, advanced toward the end of 1930 (Vygotsky, 1997b),
and also the inner logic of the development of the idea of sign mediation, led to
the appearance of the new theory of consciousness as a dynamic semantic system
in 1932. All previous achievements were reconsidered in light of this new view.
Vygotsky submitted himself and his co-workers to sharp criticism, increased the
number of ‘internal conferences’ for his closest colleagues, intensified the polemic
with Leontiev, created separate research groups, and at the same time the new principle turned out to be incredibly fertile.Vygotsky began working in several important areas at the same time (psychopathology and clinical psychology, child, developmental, and educational psychology, the problem of thinking and speech, the
theory of emotions, neuropsychology); even at the internal conferences he did not

108

Ekaterina Zavershneva

manage to share all his new insights, and several of his ideas did not appear in print
but remained in the private notes (“Previously I had nothing to say. And now more
than I can,” from the note The present state of the psychological theory of emotion). These
shifts are also reflected in the archival documents. From the notes quoted below,
series 1 (Green ink) contains documents of a transitional period, when, in particular, the principle of the systemic structure of consciousness was elaborated. Series 2
(Spinoza) is dedicated to one of the unsolved key problems of Vygotsky’s conception – the theory of the higher emotions. Series 3 and 4 (Library cards, The French article) form one whole and date from 1932. They contain propositions for the further
development of the cultural-historical approach and show the rapid progress toward
a new theory of consciousness (cf. Zavershneva, 2014).
In 1932 the ultimate goal, already formulated in the 1920s – the creation of
a general psychological theory – came within reach. Vygotsky sketched plans for
a book dedicated to the problem of consciousness, which he did not manage to
write (Vygotskii, 2007). From 1931 to 1932 he found a first-class opponent in Kurt
Lewin, perhaps Vygotsky’s most serious opponent of all, a person of equal strength,
striving for the same goal, and because of that more like an ally than an opponent.
First from a distance, but then also at a personal level, they developed a relationship
of open discussion and even friendship, which is also apparent from Lewin’s short
visit to the USSR (the polemic with Lewin is reflected in the series 5–9; see below).
Among the opponents of the second rank were Jean Piaget and William Stern (by
1932 Vygotsky had already found the major arguments against their theories), the
psychophysiologists Kurt Goldstein and Henry Head, and others. Sigmund Freud
was also a strong figure in this circle of opponents, but he rarely left the shadows, rarely left the inner dialogue for open polemics. For that reason the remarks
about him in the personal notes are even more valuable (cf. The conference on research
themes and the notebook Anomalous development). Among the factual ‘anomalies’ that
allowed the theory to progress were first of all Vygotsky’s own studies of thinking,
Walter Cannon’s experiments, studies in the field of clinical psychology, in particular the ‘enigma’ of schizophrenia, which according to Vygotsky, constituted a loss
of the person’s semantic network, the Shereshevskii phenomenon – in his personal
notes Vygotsky pointed out that this phenomenon was scientifically most important
(cf. A. R. Luria, 1968), and so on.The theoretical shift was caused by the movement
in two directions – the study of the development and loss of conceptual thinking
(Vygotsky developed the principle of the unity of the laws of development and disintegration of the mind).
Emotions, in particular, formed a problematic field for the new theory; almost
simultaneously with Thinking and speech, his A theory of the emotions was being written (1931–3) (Vygotsky, 1999), which, however, remained unfinished. Vygotsky
was looking for the unit of the synthesis of intellect and affect and it finally
appeared in his last works on developmental psychology: the emotional experience (Russian: perezhivanie; German: Erlebnis) was introduced as the unit of the
internal and the external, the person and their environment. We see that Vygotsky
gradually completed the “upper floors” of his theory, approaching comprehension

Vygotsky the unpublished 109

of the concept of personality, which was also present in his early work. But he was
also busy with strengthening the foundation – the central theme of the last period
of his life was elaborated along the lines of the idea of the systemic localization
of the higher psychological functions. Discarding the subdivision into lower and
higher psychological functions (see below) and trying to create a unified theory of
mind,Vygotsky suspected this split, this unsurmounted dualism, also in his own new
conception. The psychophysical problem got a central place in his notes. He kept
proposing new solutions, but his early death – or, perhaps, the irredeemable internal
problems of his theory – prevented him from realizing his ambitions.

Series of documents
1. Green ink. The series dates from approximately 1929–30 and the notes are in
green ink. Most of them are theoretical: Vygotsky reflects on genuine concepts,
polemicizes with Hegel and Henri Bergson; actively quotes not just Marx but also
Lenin, basically their arguments against Plato, Hegel, and other authors considered idealist. We also come across authors seldom mentioned in Vygotsky’s writings,
such as Ernst Cassirer, Boris Stolpner, and Nikolai Marr.11 The quoted fragments
from Lenin (“analysis in practical action”) also appear in Pedology of the adolescent
(Vygotskii, 1931d). The notes in this series are very similar to the spirit of that
book, which Vygotsky considered to be an important step in his scientific development. The basic theme of his observations is that freedom of thought rests upon
genuine concepts. We should not forget that among the major anomalies requiring
explanation – which would also allow the appearance of a new view on the nature
of mind – were the experimental results about the development of word meanings found with the Vygotsky–Sakharov method in the late 1920s. Memory and
attention could easily be described in terms of the instrumental act but thinking
was a different matter. The reflections about what is behind the sign operation in
this domain subsequently led to the idea of word meaning. In the Green ink series
we see the genesis of new ideas in elaborate reflections, in still very unclear moves,
repetitions, and doubts, and yet toward the end of 1934 from these doubts arose the
seventh chapter of Thinking and speech with its law of the “equivalence of concepts”
(Vygotskii, 1934a, pp. 242–243). It should be mentioned that this theme remained
present in Vygotsky’s private notes until the very last months of his life; the most
important note of the series, Everything is historical, the modern theory of concepts as well,
is also dedicated to it. We present two excerpts from this text, both dealing with
philosophical aspects of thinking and directly leading to the formulation of the law
of equivalence of concepts; the first one touches upon the freedom of thought, the
second is about the problem of the general and the unique in the word.
Thus: the central idea: In the simplest generalization, in the most elementary
general idea is a certain piece of fantasy = freedom. In the concept is freedom.
The rational analogue of the idealist theory of concepts (they are not things
but ideas) is the theory about the freedom from the thing in the concept (=

110

Ekaterina Zavershneva

in the idea of the thing). This is the genuine (and great!) meaning of Plato
and all idealism … Lenin contra Hegel (?) and I contra Bergson: why cannot the separate be named? … If the word might be subsumed under one
concept, Hegel would be right, but the possibility to say: the round card table
that stands there etc., i.e. to include the object in an overlapping network of
concepts, of which it forms the center, allows us to call the word unique.
Another important note in the series, On the problem of systemic investigation,
was probably written in 1930. It is interesting because it reveals the polemic with
Leontiev about the issue of practical intellect and the relationship between thinking
and speech. In the document Vygotsky discussed the outline of the experimental
study of the analogues of complexive thinking12 in practical actions; the outline is
accompanied by a drawing of the experimental device (a disk with weights, suspended at various distances from the center).We may surmise that this outline illustrated a study by Bozhovich,13 which she began in 1929 and which lasted for some
three years;14 apparently, the documents from different archives – Bozhovich’s and
Vygotsky’s – refer to the same events.
A further note, dedicated to giftedness and cultural age, and mentioned above,
also demands our attention. It is joined by a separate text about youth as a cultural
phenomenon. In this note appears the still indistinct term “the zone of proximal
possibilities,” which obviously anticipated the idea of the zone of proximal development that appeared two to three years later.
2. Spinoza. This series is dedicated to the (unwritten) second part of the fundamental work A theory of the emotions (1931–3). The notes were primarily written
with a simple pencil on small pieces of paper and also on narrow strips of paper
of approximately 3 cm. They are hard to read because of the many abbreviations
and, in fact, resemble inner speech. The texts contain many deletions, illegible parts;
some parts are lost. On the whole it can be said that Vygotsky had a rough idea of
the theory of the higher emotions, i.e. a vague image of the desired result, but didn’t
know yet how to achieve it.
The central theme of the series is the theory of the higher emotions, which
could only be developed after the solution of the psychophysical problem, according to Vygotsky. He assumed that Spinoza’s Ethics contained a number of postulates
that could be helpful but needed rethinking:
Spinoza’s greatest idea, which liberates all of psychology, is not the idea of
the absolute power of the soul over the body (Spinoza rejects it) but that of
its relative power (which Spinoza accepts), i.e. the relationship between soul
and body (life and intellect) is not absolute and immutable but changeable,
relative. Inde15 – this is not the postulate but the problem (i.e. Spinoza made
the problem the postulate, and the postulate the problem). Inde – the real
possibility of psychology (a special form of movement-development) of the
change of the contribution of the soul to the life of the body, of intellect /
of life.

Vygotsky the unpublished 111

This is real, this is accessible, this moves.
Everything in Spinoza’s theory struggles with mechanical causality, with
immutability (there is no development), with incoherency, with parallelism
and demands to transcend its limits.
This is … the strength and the weakness of Spinoza: strength because he created what leads to this; weakness because it is not yet there.
(from the note Spinoza’s greatest idea)
In the document The end of the preface a note was discovered that reveals Vygotsky’s
personal assessment of A theory of the emotions, plus reflections about to whom this
book should be dedicated:
The book of all my life,
Is badly written, but its thoughts are mine –
What is of the evil of the day will become obsolete
(I am writing it not at the heights of my spirit, but overwhelmed by the evil
of the day.16 Something will remain for the future.
The seed are my children and students, those to whom I would dedicate
it if I would value its prospects higher. But the past, in whose power I am, is
stronger. That is why <labor>).
________________________
Maybe dedicate it to Roza? Alive?
[The next clause outlined; EZ] The greatness of what is mortal and
perishable.
The idea of dedicating A theory of the emotions to Roza Vygodskaya replaced
the idea of a dedication to his father Semen L’vovich Vygodskii, who had died in
October 1931 (another note of the Spinoza series contains the dedication ‘SLV’).
Vygotsky was of the opinion that Spinoza managed to formulate the idea of man
from the ‘peak’ viewpoint. He planned to finish A theory of the emotions using this
idea: “This idea can be leading for the psychology of man as a science. It is a real
idea, because it corresponds with the object, because it shows – using Shakespeare’s
words – man in the fullest sense of the word.17 Thereby it shows the psychology
of man its true object. Ecce homo18” (Vygotsky’s note L.S.V. A theory of the emotions).
The series also contains reflections about The ethics, marginalia to it, and schematic sketches for the second part of A theory of the emotions (cf.Vygotsky, 2010).
3. Library cards. This is the truly “golden” series of the family archive; it contains
extremely important documents that reveal the inner dynamics of the transition
toward a new viewpoint on the nature of consciousness. The notes are made on
the backs of library cards (typographically set in 1931); several of them are dated
by Vygotsky. The series contains notes made during internal conferences, notes
about colleagues (including Leontiev), reflections about the psychophysical problem, about the expedition to Central Asia, about the nature of schizophrenia and

112

Ekaterina Zavershneva

other pathological phenomena (aphasia, in particular), a polemic with Goldstein and
Head, and statements about child development, but its core is the idea of the semantic structure of consciousness and the relation between thought and word.
The note Symposium, December 4, 1932 begins with a radical revision of the previous achievements:
1. The need for a new stage in the investigation does not arise because a
new idea came up, because a new idea became interesting, but because the
development of the investigations required it – new facts lead us to the search
for new, more complex explanations … We focused our attention on the
sign (tool) to the detriment of the development of the operation with the
sign, presenting it as something simple that goes through 3 stages: magical,
external, internal. But the knot is external and the diary of the adolescent is
external. Thus we have lots of inadequately explained facts and want to dive
deeper into the facts, i.e. to theoretically re-interpret them.
2. The higher and lower functions are not built in 2 floors: their number and names do not coincide. But neither our previous understanding: the
higher function is the mastery of the lower one (voluntary attention is the
subordination of involuntary attention), for this also means 2 floors.
Vygotsky pointed out that his main goal was the search for a method that allows
the study of the dynamics of consciousness and he called this method “semic analysis.”19 The colleagues wondered whether in so doing he would not replace the
study of the whole mind by the study of just one part of it – the verbalized part.
Vygotsky countered: “A.N. [Leontiev, EZ] is wrong: The verbal and nonverbal
experiment do not coincide with the studies of speech and action and the concepts
of pure speech. This is the problem, but the first thing is: the research method. Ergo,
the place of A.N.’s own work is also wrong.” This was the beginning of a difference
of opinion between him and Leontiev and Vygotsky’s further notes abound with
the word “contra” in those places where he discusses Leontiev’s propositions. They
did not agree in their theoretical assessment of the processes involved in the transition from speech to thought: Leontiev suggested the metaphor of “spectral analysis,” Vygotsky rejected it. Speech, he said, participates as actively in this transition
as thought itself; we should not present it as a picture, a cast, a sort of death-mask
made of the living process of thinking. The role of speech is not just to fixate the
thought or what remained of it after the translation of the thought into a tangible
form; the word is not just the main carrier of the thought, its canvas, but also the
carrier of consciousness as a whole. Leontiev insisted on the investigation of the
practical action as the central theme, but Vygotsky did not agree: the dynamics
of the action derive from the dynamics of meaning and in itself, separate from
the ‘peak’ dimension of meanings, cannot be analyzed. The polemic between them
became increasingly pungent, as is evident in Vygotsky’s notebook of October 1932
(cf.Vygotskii, 2007), and also in the excerpts from other documents (for further discussion see, in particular, Chapter 6). We will not analyze this polemic in any detail

Vygotsky the unpublished 113

but it remains a fact that Leontiev from the early 1930s distanced himself from the
study of consciousness as proposed by Vygotsky, although he continued participating in the work of the Vygotsky–Luria extended group, carried out experiments,
and participated in the discussions during their symposia.
Another part of the document Symposium, December 4, 1932, contains Vygotsky’s
answer to an objection raised by Bozhovich. To a considerable degree it was also an
answer to Leontiev (“speech is not a discovery”). The fragment is remarkable: not
only is the question of the relationship between thinking and speech raised in a
manner that we do not even encounter in the monograph of the same name (when
does thinking become thinking in the proper sense of the word?), but also provided
is a new formulation of the psychophysical problem (cf. Zavershneva, 2010c):
Lidia Il’inichna.
1. From without–inward: but the question is the method: how can we in a
mediated way study the inner hidden processes. [The next two statements are
vertically marked on the left with the remark: “this is wrong”; EZ] Thinking
reveals itself in speech and action. Why is it that action and speech are essential, where is the criterion of essential disclosures?
[The statement is outlined; EZ] This is totally wrong: this is why we differ
from all older psychology – speech is not a discovery.20
2. Is thinking a cloud?21 Or does it become thinking, when pours out into
speech. Thinking <gn.> is its [own] action? How does the inner psychological relate to behavior – the psychophysical problem. This is also the problem of thinking and speech
[Statement outlined; EZ] This is terribly important:
speech / thinking as psycho / physical problem.22
Other notes in the series also emphasize the special role of speech in the development of consciousness and are directly linked with the talk The problem of consciousness (Vygotsky, 1997d):
Speech and consciousness: first we thought: speech and thinking, then: through
intellectualization – speech to memory, attention, perception. I.e., we spread
speech all over consciousness. More important: to infer it from the change
it causes in consciousness. The first word is a change of consciousness long
before the change of thinking: prise de conscience,23 cf. the problem of the first
questions (in aphasia, schizophrenia and other pathological changes).
In other documents from 1932 Vygotsky pointed out that the child’s first orientation is semantic, the first questions are about the sense and not about the meaning
of the surrounding world (Consciousness without the word, The first questions: Origin of
the object world, etc.).
Vygotsky described the complex dynamics of meanings and their inner contradictions, and provided an interesting philosophical treatment of the relationship

114

Ekaterina Zavershneva

between thought and word; one sees a hidden dialogue with Potebnia, whose book
Thought and language exerted a considerable influence on the young Vygotsky:
Two paradoxes of thinking:
1. Why thinking ≠ associative reproduction
2. Why thinking ≠ logical figures Barbara[,] Celares.24
Because it proceeds in the struggle against tendencies with semantic fields
and in the consciousness of these fields (cf. inner speech – creates the fields –
external speech proceeds through these fields).
The new viewpoint suggested by Vygotsky also led to progress in the domain of
psychopathology (and neuropsychology, which at the time did not exist as a separate
branch of psychology). Leontiev remembered that a talk about consciousness that
Vygotsky gave at an internal conference toward the end of 1932 provided the general picture of the future theory: “all psychology was reduced to word meaning.”25
Apart from that, Leontiev said, “aphasia, schizophrenia, statements about localization
(neuropsychology), etc. also crept in” (A. N. Leontiev & Leontiev, 2005, p. 374).26
And indeed,Vygotsky not only became a student for the third time, studying medicine in the Kharkov Medical Institute but, according to his notebooks, he also
actively worked in the clinic until the last month of his life. His paper about schizophrenia was published in 1932 (Vygotskii, 1932a)27 and no less than a dozen notes
are dedicated to this theme.We will give an excerpt from the note Toward the problem
of sch [schizophrenia; EZ]: “what we observe in the disease is the life of a consciousness torn from its vital roots: i.e. the shipwreck of consciousness, of its heights, as if
it struck a submarine cliff in the depths.”
4. The article in French. The notes were made on the back of a French paper on
child psychology. The series contains another plan for a book about the problem
of consciousness, a protocol of the discussion during a symposium (with the participation of Leontiev, Zaporozhets,28 Luria, and Bozhovich), observations of his
youngest daughter Asya, and a separate document with remarks for Leontiev. The
book plan belongs among the more complete ones; apart from its basic claims
(about the principles and methods of the new theory), it contains the titles of
chapters about the freedom of man, about the dynamic of meanings (a separate
chapter is dedicated to an analysis of Velimir Khlebnikov’s poetry29), about the
psychophysical problem, and about the unconscious. Inserted is a separate note
testifying of the fact that Vygotsky was constantly thinking of his discussion with
Leontiev: “How can comrade Leontiev be fertilized – by the light of the problem of consciousness?” In the note NB! A.N., on the left part of p. 17 there is a
bracketed section with six exclamation marks: “The way to study meaning is the
central question of our investigation. The plan30 looks backward, does not make a
decisive step forward to the new stage of work – to semic analysis. What does the
meaning of action mean?”
Linked to these two series are the documents Comments to A.R. Luria’s talk,
Toward the question of the historical current in psychology, and notebooks dated 1932.

Vygotsky the unpublished 115

5. Geographical maps. The notes on the back of leaflets with a picture of the earth’s
hemispheres date from 1932/3. They include comments on the propositions by
Leontiev and Luria, on Lewin’s theory, and also descriptions of clinical cases (The
trip to the headquarters, The lost mail), which Vygotsky personally observed. He often
returned to the analysis of these cases in other notes.
6. Publishing house ONTI. Notes on the backside of a publisher’s form, part of
the notes dated 1933. It is with this series that the debate begins with Lewin about
the role of thinking in personality development and about the unity of two types of
dynamics: the fleeting dynamics of the semantic field and the more rigid dynamics
of action. Among the published works about this theme was The problem of mental
retardation (Vygotskii, 1935c), which contains a number of formulations that are
verbatim repetitions of the notes.
The document K. Lewin. 3.05.33 consists of two parts: the first is a criticism, the
second, entitled, Lewin. Lectures, contains an exposition of several claims of Lewinian
psychology. We assume that the second part is a summary of the lectures that Lewin
gave during his two-week stay in Moscow in the spring of 1933 (see Van der Veer
& Valsiner, 1991, pp. 287–288, and Chapter 9). In his note Vygotsky remarked that
the child’s ability to ‘leave the field’ is underestimated, an ability which Lewin considered to be the distinguishing characteristic of man. This is what Vygotsky wrote:
1.

2.

Why do concepts liberate action?
2 problems: (1) the relation of thinking to the real plane
(2) shifts in the plane of thinking …
Thinking allows us to overcome the forces.
Why does thinking acquire ever more meaning over the years?
Concepts are genuine relations. (numbers) are relations of systems.
Most important in thinking is freedom: ich kann was ich will.31 From there it is
transferred to the action.
But freedom is born in the thought.
Thinking provides the way out of the vector field.

The series also contains preparatory notes for the last chapters of Thinking and
speech (the functions of speech, their differentiation in ontogeny, the dynamic of the
transition from thought to word, the nature of inner speech, etc.) and descriptions
of clinical cases.
7. Aerial photo and Histology. The notes are on the back of sheets of paper with,
respectively, a picture of the earth’s surface and a picture of the cell structure, of
equal size; apparently the series date from the same period (roughly 1933–4). The
documents of this series are dedicated to the continuing discussion with Lewin
and to psychopathological and neuropsychological matters (aphasia, agnosia, the
post-encephalitic syndrome, schizophrenia, mental fatigue); various notes contain
an analysis of Pötzl’s views and a criticism of the Jackson–Ribot law. The series is
joined by the shorthand protocol of a conference on Wolpert’s agnosia (simultagnosia); the main presentation was by Luria, the shorthand report begins at p. 18,

116

Ekaterina Zavershneva

preserved is just Vygotsky’s talk, in which he discusses the key tenets of the theory
of the systemic localization of the higher functions. In the document About complete aphasia, we find a remark about the problem of the concept of color (exactly
which abstract features can create it? What is the similarity between color perception and the perception of words, what is hidden behind the obvious difference
between the two processes?) and color agnosia. Vygotsky phrases the question as a
neuropsychological one:
Are alexia and color agnosia analogous to object agnosia? The difference is
tremendous: perception of an object + perception of a symbol (<the printed>
word) + perception of a quality, which is most difficult to generalize (color)
are different things. Why does alexia go hand in glove with color agnosia?
Whoever solves this psychologically, unravels one of the hardest mysteries.
The color concept is one of the brain-twisters that arises when one thinks about
the law of the equivalence of concepts, which elucidates the central note of the
series About the extent of commonality of concepts.32 Vygotsky polished this law for a
number of years, criticizing his own investigations of the development of word
meaning. He now believed his introduction of the idea of a network of concepts
was ‘artificial’ and tried to comprehend its application to the system of the real, living language:
For a long time we (vainly) searched for a (reliable) symptom to qualify the
structure of generalization in real word meanings (syncretic wholes, complexes,
etc.) and thereby for a bridge from the experiment to real concepts: apparently, it can be found in the extent of commonality, because this (the relationship of commonality) is the most fundamental and essential feature of every
meaning (to be a meaning = to stand in certain relationships of commonality
with other meanings, i.e. to have a specific extent of commonality), which
can be the most reliable criterion for the structure of generalization of real
concepts: the relationship between Ober- and Unterbegriff.33
See also the fragment from a document called Lewin’s fourth dimension:
We must study the little known properties of the complex, the pre-concepts,
etc. In each of these domains of thought there is another relation to the object
and another act to grasp the object in thought, i.e. 2 fundamental features,
characterizing the concept. Inde the nature of these concepts and all their
properties are different.
8. The psychology of art. The notes on the cards with the title The psychology of
art, which were printed to be glued on folders with the text of the dissertation,
belong to 1933–4 (it was not exceptional for Vygotsky to use material that was ten
years old; sometimes the notes were written on the back of sheets with texts in the

Vygotsky the unpublished 117

spelling used before the reform of 1918). It is a short series, but one of its documents
(NB! About Zaks and Kogan. Intellect and dynamics) is unique, because it contains
not just a critique of Lewin’s theory and an analysis of complex cases (the patients
Kogan and Zaks; no first names were given34), but also the outlines of a dynamic
theory of consciousness. We will present fragments in which one can detect statements close to activity theory, a reinterpretation of Lewin’s theory, an aphoristic formulation of the criterion for the unity of intellect and affect, and a sharp criticism
of his own approach:
1. Consciousness determines life (form), but itself it originates in life and
forms an aspect of it: ergo life determines life through consciousness. As soon
as we separated thinking from life (from the dynamics) – and regarded it as a
psychic concept and not a psychological one, we closed the door to any clarification and explanation of its major property: to determine the form of life
and behavior, to act, to exert influence …
3. The degree of development of concepts is the degree of transformation
of the dynamics (the affect) of the real action into the dynamics of thinking …
6.The mistake of our analysis of Z. and K. is that the analysis is carried out
in 2 planes: the external field and the semantic field – there is no unity, but
parallelism and correspondence.
We must have a unified plan: because the dynamics is of the flüssig type,35
to the extent it is in the action, the action involves thinking. The freedom of
dynamics in the real field and the action is an exact measure of the participation of thinking in behavior.
7. The criterion of the unity of intellect and affect is the principal fundamental relation to the situation – in the sense of connectedness and freedom: this is the genuine unit of affect-intellect. The degree of distancing of
generalization from the object determines the degree of transformation of the
affect of a thing into the affect of concept.
Later these claims were elaborated in the paper The problem of mental retardation,
published posthumously (Vygotskii, 1935c).
The series also contains excerpts from Friedrich Engels and Edward Sapir (1921),
a polemic with Leontiev, reflections about the motivation of thought, etc.
9. Manuscript progress. This series of notes, made in the last months of Vygotsky’s
life, shows how productive he was despite his rapidly deteriorating health.The notes
were written on library cards: the handwriting had greatly changed (uneven, large),
and vowels were only retained when absolutely essential for understanding: all this
gives the impression of urgency. Vygotsky evidently felt his death was near (cf. his
last note below). It is a voluminous series: among its themes are a unified viewpoint on the dynamic of consciousness, the problem of the relation between affect,
intellect, and will, the periodization of child development, the social situation of
development, the relation of teaching and development, the problem of aphasia
and agnosia, etc. Several conferences (in November and December, 1933) were

118

Ekaterina Zavershneva

dedicated to the theme of child play and toys; as usual, a number of notes are about
the observation of patients and experimental studies in the field of neuropsychology.
The polemic with Lewin grew into a genuine dialogue;Vygotsky was convinced
that dynamic analysis might become a valuable instrument in the work of the
psychologist, including clinical work (entry: Sehr wichtig.36 The unity of intellect and
affect) (cf.Vygotskii, 1935c):
The unity of affect and intellect (is correct) – we must find the unit [of
analysis].37
But the fluid dynamics apparently does not exist at all outside thinking
(this is what their unity is about); ergo to the extent that it is found in the
dynamics of the field it was introduced there by thinking. Not like this: there
is a dynamics of 2 sorts (fluid, free, and stark38 connected) independent of the
intellect (the elements: hydrogen) and there are two sorts of activities (thinking and real activity) independent of the dynamics … But like that: 2 unities
of dynamic activity exist: thinking and real activity. Both have their dynamic
aspect, i.e. a dynamic system sui generis,39 of a certain type and sort. Outside
activity the 2 types of activity do not exist in abstracto.This is fundamental and
important … To say that thinking is disturbed is to say that dynamic processes
of a certain type are completely disturbed everywhere. The role of thinking
in reality exactly resides in the introduction of new dynamic possibilities in
activity.
From other documents we should mention the text The significance of the interaction with the ideal form in child development:
(c) the moving forces of development … are the fundamental contradictions
in child development between the ideal and the genetic (developing) forms;
d) in embryonic development the result (the ideal form) is preformed; in evolution it does not exist at all, it is in the end; in child development there is a
genuine cooperation with it; e) which means that the ideal form cooperates
with the genetic one: that the latter relates to the first as part to whole, as cell
to tissue: cf. the child’s one-word utterance forms part of a dialogue with the
ideal speech of the mother, i.e. it is practically part of a whole with grammatization and other characteristics, with which the child’s speech clashes.
Thus: grammar in child speech is not introduced from outside in the 3rd year,
but grammar pulls the child’s first words upward from the very beginning.

The notebooks
The notebook Anomalous development begins with propositions about The collective
as a factor in the development of the anomalous child – an article with the same name, in
two parts, was published in 1931 (Vygotskii, 1931b, 1931c) – their content is much

Vygotsky the unpublished 119

broader than the final variant published in the article. A large part of the notebook
is dedicated to the problem of affect, both normal development of the affective
sphere of personality and its development in pathology.The notebook ends with the
notes of an internal conference in which Vera Schmidt40 took part (the psychoanalytic approach to emotions, its criticism, “the grain of truth” in Freud’s theory, the
observation of ‘psychoanalytic’ cases in Vygotsky’s practice, their assessment from
the viewpoint of his own theory) and the defectologists Dmitrii Azbukin, Antonia
Basova, and Pavel Pochapin41 (the difficult child in the child collective). In addition,
there are propositions for talks about the fundamentals of clinical psychology and
about the state of the theory of emotions in international psychology. A number
of these claims were subsequently elaborated in A theory of the emotions (Vygotsky,
1999) and in Lectures on psychology (Vygotsky, 1998). Finally, Cannon’s experiments
were analyzed, as were the James–Lange theory and the ideas of Pavlov, Freud,
Bleuler, and Kretschmer.
We will provide a quotation from a conference dedicated to the problem of emotions and their study;Vygotsky argued against his critic Zakharii Chuchmarev:42
Galvanometrics
1.

2.

To prove that this registers the emotional state: in the curves.
The study of emotions post mortem:43 first we kill them, then we study
them.
Language was not created for emotions. That is why the expression and
study of emotions is difficult. Does it have to be so??? And naming the
emotion changes the emotion (prise de conscience) not just in the sense of a
“lie” (Tyutchev),44 but also in the sense of changing its course: i.e. behind
this difficulty is the real phenomenon of change

emotion – word!
The notes in the second part of the notebook reflect a transitional moment
between the ‘instrumental psychology’, focused on the sign operation, and the new
theory that aspired to the investigation of meaning and the semantic dynamics
of consciousness. The hypothesis of the semantic structure of consciousness is not
yet clearly described; about some of its aspects (which would take definite form
mid-1932) Vygotsky writes hesitatingly. In the note below the term ‘sense’ appears
for the first time:
Questions about concepts.
1.
2.

Understanding is more than the word.
Exclude all other aspects (expressive aspects).
The change of meanings in language and the development of language
in the child – no psychological laws – weight of this argument.

120

Ekaterina Zavershneva

3.
4.
5.
6.

The concept – what is it? The logical concept of science also in life.
Meaning = sense? Is it enough: meaning = concept?
Various types of meaning: do the roads toward a clear concept
coincide? …
Conclusions? Do the childish forms disappear in adults or ingredients.

Notebook (October, 1932) is replete with very important documents: the plan
for a book about consciousness, the psychophysical problem, a polemic with
Leontiev, propositions for a talk by Solov’ev (the talk about the results of experiments with the modified Karsten method45 is described in a separate document
of the series Library cards). It was published with comments and historical notes in
the journal Novoe Literaturnoe Obozrenie (Vygotskii, 2007; Zavershneva, 2010c; see
also Chapter 6).
Notebook (summer, 1932) contains excerpts from Romain Rolland, Werner
Sombart, Oswald Spengler, Eugen Bleuler, and observations of his daughter Asya
(egocentric speech, child volition, memory, attention, the affective sphere, the
one-year crisis). The document is valuable because it allows us to move the date of
the new period even further back – to July 1932. The following fragment is from
August 17:
The question: what is that? is of a semasiological nature, it is a question about
sense (eo ipso46 also about the word, but not the name, but about the logos) –
its function is – thinking + awareness (prise de conscience). Hence: play with
words: illusion games, their beginning (the solution of child play is in its
semasiological nature) + lie + autism + future time. In particular: the worry
about what will be while Asya is not here (about the immutability of the
situation).
Conferences about research themes, October 27–29, 1933. An exercise book with
propositions for talks and Vygotsky’s comments to them.We will give the themes of
the presentations as indicated by Vygotsky.
1. V. F. Schmidt. The problem of the development of the psychoneurotic child. Contains
twenty-eight propositions with a detailed analysis of Freudian claims and also her
own clinical researches in the traditional psychoanalytic domain. Here is one of the
concluding propositions:
Perspectives: to preserve Freud’s fundamental claim (psychoneuroses are the
result of unsuccessful repression, the conflict between Ego and Id, the higher
and lower layers of mind in the new system: Freud considers consciousness in
light of the theory of the unconscious; we consider the unconscious in light
of the theory about consciousness. The new theory of child psychoneurosis.
[We should] neither discover America anew, nor consider it undiscovered.
He who wants to build a scientific psychology, must follow another
road: Freud did not provide a theory of consciousness; we cannot just

Vygotsky the unpublished 121

complete Freud with a theory of consciousness; we must reorganize his facts
and theories in a new framework.
2. R. A. Averbukh.47 Environment-emotional experience-character. Emotional experience is considered as the unit of the personal and environmental aspects in the
development of character; eight propositions.
3. Ek. Mikh. The analysis of psychomotorics. The name of the speaker could not be
established; six propositions. Here is an excerpt from the text:
1. Recording, architectonics, meaning. Inner speech and motorics … The
unit of psychomotorics: meaningful movement, but not meaning + motor
mechanism … Visible and invisible meaning of movement: visible – the
movement toward the goal (mechanically necessary) – and this meaning,
without which movement is incomprehensible; but we never make only
exact and necessary movements; therefore, movement always has a hidden,
inner meaning of the movement, which always expresses the relation of the
person to the goal, inner obstacle, struggle, hesitation, added goal, a hidden
tendency or motive, ardor, weakness, exaggeration of the goal, to reach the
goal to show off , etc. We do more or less than is required by the situation
and in this more or less resides the key toward the hidden meaning. The
interpretation of motorics.
4. M. S. Pevzner.48 Child development after encephalitis. Twelve propositions about
the structure of consciousness in a post-encephalitic child and about methods to
train these children to become fit persons.
Among the notebooks are exercise books with clinical observations that
Vygotsky carried out until the end of his life (the last note in the exercise book The
Don clinic, written on May 4, 1934, during the examination of the patient Misha
Kotikov, contains an analysis of the disease history; at the end of the note there is a
remark: “show after the 20th”), summaries of other authors, an exercise book with
detailed notes of his conversation with Roman Arrago,49 a person with unusual
numeric skills, who toured the music halls of Europe, South America, and Japan (the
conversation took place in April, 1934), etc.

Scattered notes
From the notes that do not form a series – to date we have found more than 200 of
them – we shall present three documents.
1. Consciousness without word contains propositions – along poetic lines and similar
to the ideas artistically expressed in Osip Mandelstam’s poem “Swallow”, which is
quoted in the last chapter of Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech – about the area where
the word is not yet present, but the thought or its vague core already exists. The
idea of the dynamic of meanings is present, but without clear outlines, the systemic

122

Ekaterina Zavershneva

principle dominates (apparently, the note dates from the beginning of 1932). The
text deserves to be published separately. We shall cite several central fragments.
About the role of the word in the dynamics of consciousness,Vygotsky wrote:
3. The word plays a central role in consciousness but not in its various functions. Cf. attention is a central function of consciousness; its Nichtexistenz50
follows from the Nichtexistenz of consciousness itself; cf. the role of the word
in attention. Syncretism and disintegration are fundamental functions of consciousness: Köhler. If the psychosis of schizophrenia did not exist, we should
call this function schizothymous. Amnesia is a disease of consciousness …
13. The complaints about the inadequacy of the word – but how are they
stated in the word: O, if the soul could express itself without the word.51
A thought once uttered is a lie:52 but this thought is uttered, i.e. false (cf. the
sophism about the Cretan, who lies).53 Where is truth: the word does not
introduce an idyll in consciousness, but drama, even (insoluble) tragedy. In
general, life of consciousness is unlike life of the organism – (this is why consciousness stands outside organic life), is not an idyll, not Spinoza’s peace, but
tragedy: amor fati.54
There is also criticism of his own views (the assessment of Sakharov’s experiments confirms our conjecture that they had the status of a decisive anomaly in the
transition toward the new viewpoint):
7. Like with Sakharov: we unwittingly, spontaneously got what is in
Adolescent.55 He died [Sakharov; EZ] without suspecting it. We should not
proceed that way, but the problem of meaning and system was forced upon
us by this study …
17. Our shortcoming is not a shortage of facts but the inadequacy of the
theory: this is the main difficulty in the analysis of our crisis, not the divergence from the facts.This contra A.N.Thus: salvation is not in the facts, but in
theory.We introduced the system viewpoint too late. Cf. the pictogram, memory in A.N., Morozova’s experiments. Now I have a deeper understanding.
About poetry:
25. Poetry = words without relation to an object, i.e., compositions of pure
meanings (Jakobson, Khlebnikov). It is a remarkable fact that we can think
one and the same thing through different meanings.This fog, this ice, this surface means: she, my beloved.56
2. Propositions for A. R. Luria’s talk consists of the protocol of a symposium with
Leontiev, Bozhovich, Morozova, Levina, and others. Propositions 13 to 61 (end of
the protocol) have been found.The text is remarkable because it may have been the
first symposium where Vygotsky advanced his new idea that we need to study the

Vygotsky the unpublished 123

inner structure of the sign operation – its meaning. Various formulations literally
coincided with the text of the last chapter of Thinking and speech (“the thought is
not expressed in the word, but completed in the word” and so on), other formulations retain their novelty until this day and introduce essential modifications in the
interiorization hypothesis: “55. Inner speech does not come after external speech.
Inner mediation is present in speech from the very beginning, which is the undifferentiated unity of external / internal speech.” Questions from colleagues demonstrated the difficulties Vygotsky met with during the rethinking of the research
program in 1932; Vygotsky moved forward too rapidly and he still had to prove
there was a deep connection between the old and the new view and not a gap:
[On the left part of the page with two exclamation marks; EZ]
38. A.N.: change in meanings, because meaning is not constant? … This is
not the essence of the change of meaning. But this exists as well. Movement
of meanings = their change.
[On the left of the page with a thick vertical line and an exclamation
mark; EZ]
In particular, the change of meanings and movement of the senses is
the experimental method to study systems and their internal structure and
dynamics. The change of meanings is a deeper, more essential, more internal
analysis of the sign operation … It is a new way to experiment with the sign
operation …
42. A.N. Not true that meaning comes from ideology, it leads to the system.
The sign supports the system thanks to the fact that the sign has meaning.The
sign supports the system because it has meaning. …
45. A.N. The word moves from meaning to meaning (no!) or meaning
around the word? Yes … consciousness creates meaning with the help of the
word. Is the word just a carrier or word-centeredness? It all depends on what
you mean by ‘word’ (phoneme or the unity of phoneme and meaning).
“The semasiological method,” which is also discussed at this conference, was
essentially a new version of the indirect method, which Vygotsky already propagated in The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis:
60. The problem of the inner mediation by meanings. What is meaning?
A hypothetical concept about the inner formation, whose exact psychological nature is unknown but whose reality is beyond doubt, whose location
is unknown (cf. the computation of undiscovered planets),57 but whose effect
we can and must study, without it the life of consciousness is impossible, like
the life of an organism is impossible without vitamins or hormones.
3. The result of many years of work. A note of extraordinary importance contains the propositions for a talk in which Vygotsky presented the idea of his book
Thinking and speech for a higher echelon. From the list of contents it is clear that

124

Ekaterina Zavershneva

originally the plan for the book was broader; it also contained a chapter on the freedom of will (in Thinking and speech this theme is discussed in terms of the ideas of
the voluntary and deliberate nature of the higher functions).Vygotsky recorded the
course of the meeting, where he was the main speaker, but which also saw talks by
Kolbanovskii, Shemiakin, and Vedenov.58 Vygotsky’s propositions were subjected to
sharp criticism. The main accusations were idealism, anti-Marxism, methodological
dilettantism, a lack of dialectics, etc. In particular,Vygotsky was rebuked for the fact
that he did not have “phylogenetic material,” such as the work on the material collected during Luria’s expeditions to Central Asia (cf. Chapter 8). Vygotsky made a
sad comment next to the objection about the material: “Where would I get it?”
Vygotsky was also reminded of his duty to take care of his “fellow citizens” and to
study the “laws of the socialist construction.” Individuality and personality were
narrow, limited topics, one of his opponents remarked, and his verdict was: “creeping empiricism + mechanicism.”
The note sheds light on the previously unknown circumstances under which
Vygotsky’s famous work came into being. It shows, in particular, that the book was
already ready for press in 1932.The location and time of the meeting were not indicated. Vygodskaya and Lifanova (1996, p. 136) have published the propositions for
Thinking and speech from the Scientific Archive of the APN SSSR. These were the
propositions for a talk by Vygotsky at the Institute of Scientific Education on April
2, 1932.There is every reason to suspect that the two events were identical, although
we need further evidence, in particular about the participants in the April meeting.
The notes about the discussion of two more of Vygotsky’s talks – on the nature
of schizophrenia and the psychology of emotions – have a similar content: the
opponents characterized Vygotsky’s viewpoint as anti-Marxist and ideologically
harmful (the mildest formulation was: “The talk just outlined approaches but did
not suggest concrete solutions. Theoretical and not connected to the Red Army.
Dissatisfaction of the military workers”).Tired by the irrelevant questions,Vygotsky
noted: “My last opponent is a paranoiac.” One of the archive’s notes, addressed to
the director of the Pedagogical Institute, shows, however, how hard it was for him
to remain cheerful and alert:
To the director of the Herzen Pedagogical Institute in Leningrad
From a copy of the resolution by the committee that purges the
Psychological Institute in Moscow, I learned that all my theoretical views,
which I use in all my work, are qualified in that decision as “idealist and
bourgeois theory” (p. 1) and as an “anti-Marxist conception” (p. 11). Because
the decision of the purging committee was made without any substantial preliminary discussion of my theory and is exclusively based on declarations by
collaborators of the Psychological Institute made during that purge, I assume
that the accusations leveled against my theory are based on misunderstandings
and do not correspond with reality. I have always considered my work to lie
within the system of the Soviet sciences and not outside it. I subjectively felt
that with my work I concretely participated in the construction of a Marxist

Vygotsky the unpublished 125

psychology and until now I continue to think that my work also objectively
had some positive meaning in the struggle against idealist and bourgeois theories [the text stops here].
We thus have Vygotsky’s personal testimony of the complex ideological situation in the scientific institutes during the period of Cultural Revolution in the
USSR. Zeigarnik’s words quoted by Yaroshevskii form external evidence of a similar episode: “I remember he [Vygotsky; EZ] ran back and forth in the room, like
a wounded animal, and said: ‘I cannot live if the Party thinks I am not a Marxist’”
(Yaroshevskii, 1993, p. 16).

Letters
We will cite just one letter from June 8, 1934, which was brought to the dying
Vygotsky in the Sebryanny Bor sanatorium. Its author was Vygotsky’s collaborator
Vera Schmidt, wife of Otto Schmidt, the investigator and navigator:
Dear Lev Semyonovich!
Better than any words these flowers convey our best wishes to you. Otto
Yul’evich asked me to wish you a speedy recovery and would like to challenge you to a match, because you are now colleagues in disease.
I very much wanted to see you already in Moscow, but I was afraid to
bother you. In the autumn, when you and O.Yu. have recovered, we will celebrate together this doubly festive day. Until that time I wish you all the best
(whatever that may be for you). We now do not live in the real world but in a
fairy-tale, amidst enthusiastic people, flowers, greetings, and so on.59 Tomorrow
we will bring O.Yu. to the dacha to recover in peace. Unfortunately, our plan
to travel to the mountains with you did not materialize. The doctors allow
neither you nor him to go there. But never mind! The air of the suburban
pinewoods is no worse than the mountain aroma. Please, do recover, dear Lev
Semyonovich, to the delight of all of us. I firmly shake your hand and send
you my most friendly greetings.
V. Schmidt.

Vygotsky’s last note
It is known that Vygotsky took Hamlet, the work he read all his life, with him to
hospital. Vygotsky always identified with its protagonist and his last words were
those of Hamlet: “I am ready.” In the series Manuscript progress is a note that was presumably his last, written before he left for the hospital.The note The conference (Zaks)
begins by quoting of the words of another person who wonders why Vygotsky
needed a big room and about what he would speak. Then there is a fragmentary

126

Ekaterina Zavershneva

note about a symposium (a discussion of the clinical cases of Kogan and Zaks), and
then the direction of the text changes, it no longer follows the lines but runs across
the page. It is a very personal note that reflects Vygotsky’s mood at the moment he
said farewell to what had been the meaning of his life:
NB! Pro domo suo [sic]60
This is the last thing I did in psychology – and I will die on the summit,
just like Moses who saw the promised land but did not enter it. Forgive me,
dear creations.
The rest is silence.61
These words seem doubly bitter to us, because we know that it would be a long
silence. Fifty years would pass between these words being written and the first publication of Vygotsky’s Collected works – years of censorship, devastating reviews, and
books taken from open access in libraries.62 However, we cannot but note that apart
from sorrow there was something more, which alleviated his despair. It was the happiness to be chosen, to stand on the summit, to know that you did everything you
could or almost everything.

6
“THE WAY TO FREEDOM”
Vygotsky in 1932
Ekaterina Zavershneva

In 2006 we began the first systematic study of a segment of the Vygodsky/Vygotsky
archive that had until then remained unexplored: the scientific diaries and notebooks. Although fragmentary and scrappy, these documents are of enormous value
and not just for the history of psychology. As is known, Vygotsky’s work remained
unfinished, did not lose its heuristic potential, and is open to further elaboration.
Far from all of Vygotsky’s projects were realized and no more than drafts or private notes remained of them; this is why the publication of archival materials may
partially clarify the outlines of the general psychological theory that Vygotsky
attempted to create.
One notebook, dated October 1932, stands out because it is one of the clearest
and most accessible documents in the archive; it is exactly dated and distinguishes
itself from the other texts, whose character reminds us of inner speech that can
barely keep pace with the sudden flashes of insight. All the fundamental problems
that haunted Vygotsky’s thinking in the final years of his life are concentrated in
this notebook. The document was published in Russian in 2007 (Vygotskii, 2007).
The text consists of three parts: (1) the plan for the unwritten book On the
question of the study of consciousness; (2) notes about the psychophysical problem;
and (3) propositions for talks by Vygotsky’s co-workers Ivan Solov’ev and Aleksei
Leontiev, presumably written during an internal conference for close collaborators.
In general we may say that the text marks the turn towards a new psychological
theory – the theory of consciousness as a dynamic semantic system – and also shows
the emerging discrepancy between the views of Vygotsky and Leontiev. In the
autumn of 1932 Vygotsky still hoped to continue the collaboration with Leontiev
and tried to convince him with his ideas (which is confirmed by other notes made
that same year); he criticized and encouraged his colleagues (who could hardly keep
pace with his high speed of development), began a serious revision of his own theory, and also tackled the psychophysical problem.1 Vygotsky was working feverishly,

128

Ekaterina Zavershneva

he was full of plans, and he had less than two years to live. However, these very
two years can be considered the most productive period of Vygotsky’s entire life, a
period that is connected with the discovery of new perspectives for his approach.
Thus, in the notebook dated October 1932 we find the problem of consciousness,
the psychophysical – also known as the mind–body – problem, and the polemics
with Leontiev and the German-American psychologist Kurt Lewin. Let us analyze
each topic in more detail.

The plan for the unwritten book On the question of
the study of consciousness
The plan for the book about the problem of consciousness is one of four similar plans so far discovered in the archive. The analysis of all four plans shows that
Vygotsky’s famous work Thinking and speech formed no more than the prolegomena
to this unwritten book, because it dealt with just one – albeit the most important
one from Vygotsky’s viewpoint – interfunctional link in the system of consciousness. In the preface to this work,Vygotsky wrote that the problem of thinking and
speech is the key problem of all human psychology and directly leads to a new theory of consciousness. However, just a few concluding paragraphs of Thinking and speech
are dedicated to the problem of the theory of consciousness and the study breaks
off “at its very beginning” (Vygotskii, 1934a, p. 3).
That goal – the creation of a general psychological theory of consciousness –
can be traced to the early works of 1924–6 (Veresov, 1999; Zavershneva, 2013,
2014), but only now, in 1932, were the leading principles, and the facts confirming
them, found. In 1930 Vygotsky introduced the principle of the systemic structure
of consciousness into his theory (Vygotsky, 1997b), then towards the end of 1932
gradually the principle of the systemic structure (Vygotsky, 1997d) matured and the
analysis of interfunctional connections lost its leading role to the semic analysis of
consciousness. The accent is shifted from the problem of meaning to the problem
of sense, and – in the last year of Vygotsky’s life – to the problem of “emotional
experience” (Russian word perezhivanie, copied from the term Erlebnis, then widely
popular in German psychology) as the unit of analysis of consciousness and personality. The plan in the notebook is still rather schematic, whereas in later notes
we see more details, connected with, in particular, the role of speech in the genesis
of consciousness as a unified whole. It is remarkable that below the title of the
earliest draft of the plan Vygotsky wrote the names of himself, Leontiev, and Luria.
Apparently, the original idea was to include them as co-authors and Vygotsky later
changed his mind.
In each of the variants of the plan the facts that confirmed Vygotsky’s new theory were discussed. They were all connected with the idea of a leading role for the
semantic plane in the dynamics of consciousness. Four major facts are mentioned in
all variants: Asya, Shereshevskii, schizophrenia, and Khlebnikov (the normal adult is
also mentioned as a comparison).They were examples of developing or disintegrating psychological systems.

Vygotsky in 1932

129

The example “Asya”2 (normal development) dealt with children’s first questions which, according to Vygotsky, were questions about sense, not meaning: “Cf.
Pavlov’s ‘what is it?’ reflex and Asya’s first ‘what’s that?’ questions. It is a semantic
orientation, in consciousness” (Wordless consciousness, Vygotsky archive). Here the
movement in the semantic field is primary; it prepares all actions with the real
object, and the impressions of or guesses about the sense of the event or thing
become connected with a word.
Shereshevskii (“Sh”) was one of Luria’s patients, famous for his phenomenal
memory and the protagonist of his book The mind of a mnemonist (A. R. Luria, 1968).
Vygotsky highly valued this example of anomalous development: “Shereshevskii
must be polished as a diamond; a bright precious one that will cut through the
structure of our problems and solve them like a diamond will cut glass” (Wordless
consciousness, Vygotsky archive). The example of Shereshevskii might show how a
psychological system is built that is centered on memory; what kind of problems
it causes the subject. Shereshevskii “in order to understand must see (he sees me
when we are talking on the telephone),” he must memorize. His thinking was tied
to visual images and did not reach that freedom of movement in the plane of pure
abstraction that is characteristic of the mature thinking of an adult. His world in
many ways resembled that of a preschool child; he represented “consciousness based
on memory. He knows but doesn’t know he knows. Cf. the child who solved a
problem but doesn’t know how” (ibid.).
In schizophrenia speech splits off from the other psychological functions, which
regress to an ontogenetically prior stage. Mature consciousness gives way to more
primitive syntheses that we can also witness in onto- and phylogenesis. The coherence, cognizance, and awareness that speech introduces into consciousness is lost;
emotions that are separated from the word regress to a “primordial state.” The
patient first develops infantile and primitive affects and subsequently the emotional
sphere is completely destroyed (one of the major symptoms of schizophrenia is the
growing indifference to what once was the meaning of one’s life: one’s job, relatives, etc.). The patient’s inner world becomes dim, disjointed, and incomprehensible; the verbal fabric of consciousness is disrupted and the inner dialogue with
oneself and internalized interlocutors stops. The schizophrenic loses the capacity to
verbally interact with himself, according to Vygotsky in his talk “On psychological
systems” (Vygotsky, 1997b). Thus, schizophrenia is a perfect example of the loss of
the semantic structure of consciousness.
The example of Khlebnikov (who also appears in Thinking and speech) was about
supernormal or exceptional development, i.e. how poetry and creative language
could create new perspectives and freedom:
Because thought does not coincide with meanings, because the path from
thought to word goes through meaning, and speech is allegory,3 – for this very
reason: (1) the complaints about the imperfection of speech, the inexpressibility of thought – but the flight of a poor heart ends only with impotent
weariness;4 (2) to overcome these complaints – the attempt to fuse words

130

Ekaterina Zavershneva

by creating new paths from the thought to the word, via new word meanings (Khlebnikov himself compared this work with paving the way from one
valley to another,5 he talked about a (more) direct path from Moscow to
Kiev not via New York, called himself the railwayman of language – “there
is language, the fabric of the unities of mind, the fabric of concepts”); 3) to
overcome these complaints – play on the crookedness of these paths and the
expression of the inexpressible (Pasternak and others).6
(Propositions to A. R. Luria’s talk,Vygotsky archive)
Of special interest is the postscript to the book plan: “Fate becomes a conscious
part of personality. Spinoza’s power of reason and freedom of slavery is not yet the
highest. It is the amor fati: cf. the experiments by Dembo – Koffka.7 ‘For me this
knowledge is enough’.” This remark connected the first and second part of the
text and partially clarified Vygotsky’s attitude towards Spinoza’s ideas. Around the
same period, in his unfinished manuscript A theory of the emotions (Vygotsky, 1999),
Vygotsky presented a wide-ranging critical analysis of contemporary psychology
and returned to the theme of crisis, whose roots he now saw in the Cartesian dualism that was explicitly or implicitly present in all psychological theories. Vygotsky
planned to build the positive part of his theory upon Spinoza’s ideas, where he
hoped to find the monistic point of view so much needed by psychology. The
second part of A theory of the emotions, however, remained unwritten. The fragmentary remarks on Spinoza, and the marginalia to his texts found in the archive, throw
some light on his plan but the quotation given above shows that Spinoza’s theory
was not the ultimate truth for Vygotsky.
Apparently,Vygotsky wanted to go beyond Spinoza to develop a ‘higher’ understanding of the problem of affect and will that was not purely philosophical but
psychological. For Spinoza, Vygotsky wrote, volition is an affect and freedom consists in the mastery of one’s passions which, paradoxically enough, is only possible
by obeying them (Vygotsky, in commenting Spinoza, frequently quoted Francis
Bacon’s aphorism natura parendo vincitur). Another thing is that we are able to choose
what we will obey, e.g. the noble passion, the amor dei intellectualis.8 However, how
is reason able to choose, to strive for something, to make the person purposeful?
Can this freedom be explained by some psychological mechanisms, or is it absolutely incomprehensible? Where does the person’s freedom come from? Spinoza’s
answer – from God – Vygotsky could not accept, if only because it ends the discussion prematurely. A philosopher may, perhaps, find such a logical conclusion acceptable; not so the psychologist.
Below are several propositions from archival documents dedicated to Spinoza (cf.
Vygotsky, 2010):
Freedom: affect in the concept.
A grandiose image of the development of the person: the path to freedom.
To revitalize Spinozian thought in Marxist psychology …
The central problem of all psychology is freedom.

Vygotsky in 1932

131

(NB! Lightnings of Spinozian thought)
The will is a concept that has become an affect; a strong-willed man is a
person, who derives his deeds and way of life from the Ego affect.
(NB! On will)
Vygotsky’s path towards the ‘peak’ explanation of affect and will went through
Marxism. But Vygotsky’s Marxism was not formal. He made serious attempts to
work with the Marxist tenets, to investigate what had to be taken for granted. He
wanted to show how volition develops, how one becomes a mature person. The
Marxist idea of freedom as the appreciation of necessity was in harmony with
Spinoza’s ideas (which is hardly surprising as the roots of Marxism lie in the
new European philosophy), but Vygotsky could not fully agree with Spinoza. We
should not say that we obey something that is ‘naturally’ present in us. Vygotsky
wrote that thought and word, by shedding light on the affect, interfere with it and
change it. The dark natural feeling is first torn from its primordial unity with the
other feelings and then forms new relationships with them through the medium
of words. A new system of feelings develops; a system that would be impossible
in nature. In Thinking and speech Vygotsky provided two criteria for the mature
mind: conscious realization and deliberateness characterize each component of
consciousness. Emotions do not form an exception: transformed by thought and
word, they are liberated from their natural substrate (Vygotsky, 1987), and, as a
result, the ‘higher emotions’ are flexible and finely differentiated. The dynamics of specifically human emotions are no longer determined by a bodily state,
‘from below’, but by meanings, ‘from above’. The tendency that Vygotsky found
in the development of emotions is similar to that found in thinking: we are liberated from natural circumstances and leave them for the domain of the ‘invisible’,
the domain of meanings. From his point of view, man does not obey affects,
even if they are very noble, but himself creates his affects, personality, and destiny.
According to Vygotsky, the ultimate goal of the development of the affect was a
voluntary emotion.
It must be said that this viewpoint is somewhat problematic. Its Marxist base
is evident and it reveals the attitude of expanding one’s possibilities and liberating
oneself from natural lack of freedom with the help of the voluntary act. On the
other hand, we find the idea, nontypical for Marxists, of the transforming role of the
word, which Vygotsky borrowed from the Humboldtian tradition of linguistics and,
in particular, from Aleksandr Potebnia (Bertau, 2014).
The link between the Nietzschean project of the superman and Vygotsky’s
ideas is undisputable. In Vygotsky’s work we trace the new European ideal of
the superman-creator, who reforms nature, including his own. Furthermore, in
Vygotsky’s later works we see the outlines of a new image of man, whose specific
distinguishing characteristic is that he speaks and thinks.
According to Vygotsky one of the major questions of psychology was that
of the specific distinguishing characteristics of man. Behind his psychological
studies the philosophical problem of the nature of man always loomed large.

132

Ekaterina Zavershneva

To an extent his work can be viewed as an attempt to overcome metaphysics,
because he strove to conceptualize the gap between the bodily and spiritual
planes of human existence. The general orientation of Vygotsky’s later works and
his interest in the writings of Khlebnikov and Osip Mandelstam suggest that the
idea of the person as a creative being, as a poet, who “gives names to the things,”
was dear to him.
In contrast to his work from the ‘instrumental period’ of the 1920s, with its
zeal for social ‘reforging’ and the creation of a ‘new man’, Vygotsky’s work from
the 1930s was focused on the study of the subject’s inner world. After all, what
is created in the process of growth and development of the person is his inner
world. Neither did Vygotsky in his later works on developmental psychology deny
spontaneous development and the spontaneous activity of a child confronted with
external conditions. Vygotsky discussed the critical periods in child development,
when the susceptibility to certain environmental factors increases and these factors may radically change the course of development. The spontaneous activity
is formed and guided from outside. Can we model or guide something that is
not there? The transition ‘inwards’ of interpersonal relationships is no mechanistic process and even less a ‘reflection’ (in the sense of Lenin’s so-called theory of
reflection); it is connected with an act of personal choice (cf.Vygotsky, 1997b).The
conviction that man is free was always at the background of the mature Vygotsky’s
thinking. For him the mechanisms of social mediation were not important in
themselves; rather they contained the essence of man: they served as the ladder
towards the new ‘peak’ psychology.
We can see how Vygotsky understood this concept when we have a look at
the abstract of a talk from late 1932, published as The problem of consciousness in
the third volume of the Collected works. First of all, Vygotsky contrasted his ‘peak
psychology’ with ‘depth psychology’ (i.e. Tiefenpsychologie) and the whole psychoanalytic current (Vygotsky, 1997d, p. 137). According to Vygotsky, the unconscious “shines with reflected light,” i.e. in the unconscious we do not find the
essence of man. Our real motives are to be found in the superconscious rather
than in the unconscious. Thus, peak psychology involves a vision of man from the
fullness of his being, the summits of his existence. It is a vision that to some extent
makes Vygotsky’s thinking from the 1930s similar to that of the later humanistic
psychology.
The words about the birth of the ‘new man’ in the process of his ‘social reforging’ – the favorite theme of the young Vygotsky – probably reflected a longing
for a full, holistic, and non-fragmented existence and an understanding of the historical importance of the time in which he was destined to live. The emergence of
scientific psychology meant that people began to study what is most complex and
important: man himself. It meant that the hour had come for the ‘decisive battle’ for
the future; a struggle whose outcome depended upon the possibility of transforming the essence of man. Below we quote the final lines of his work on the psychological crisis, which mixes Marxism, Nietzsche, and themes such as freedom and
necessity in complex ways:

Vygotsky in 1932

133

In the new society our science will be in the center of life. “The leap from
the realm of necessity into the realm of freedom”9 will inevitably give
rise to the question of the mastering of our being, of subordinating it to
