ourselves. In this sense Pavlov was right when he called our science the
last science about man. It will indeed be the last science in the historical
period of mankind or in the prehistory of mankind. The new society will
create a new man … in the future society psychology will indeed be a
science about superman. Marxism and the history of science wouldn’t be
complete without this perspective. But this science about superman will
nevertheless still be psychology; now it’s in our hands to create it. There is
no need that this psychology will resemble the present one as little as – in
Spinoza’s words – the Dog, the heavenly constellation, resembles the dog,
the barking animal.
(quoted from the manuscript, whose text differs from that in
the Collected works; see Zavershneva & Osipov 2012a, 2012b
for a list of differences)
One might accuse Vygotsky of mixing up ideology and science but we should
not forget that Vygotsky echoed the Zeitgeist. It was believed that a new time and
future had come.This belief was not so much Marxist, but rather a belief of the concrete era to which Marxism belonged. Also, when the young Vygotsky echoed or
copied the revolutionary Nietzschean slogans of the Bolshevik leader Lev Trotsky,
he was not so much echoing or copying Trotsky but the spirit of the time that
Trotsky represented: for a discussion of the Nietzschean current in Russian culture
and, particularly, during the revolutionary period, see the works of Bernice Glatzer
Rosenthal (1986, 1994, 2002). It should also be taken into account that in the 1930s
Vygotsky wrote less about the new society and more about man as such. The analogy between the mind and a technical device disappeared and was replaced by
statements about the meaningful word as the microcosm of human consciousness,
as its living cell (Vygotskii, 1934a, p. 318).
Vygotsky’s ideas had yet another feature; it was less obvious than Marxism and
Nietzschean thought but no less interesting: psychological matters were discussed
in a manner that was more characteristic of philosophy than of psychology. The
core of his conception, its main problem (which was not studied directly but represented the philosophical background against which his research proceeded), we can
reconstruct as follows: What is the relation of the word to man’s ‘primary’ nature?
Does the word only reorganize the natural processes, changing nothing but their
order and combination, or does it really transform them in a radical way? What is
the nature of the word itself? – if it is a mediator between nature and culture (or, in
another variant, between body and spirit), then what is it? – a part of culture, a part
of nature, or neither?
The word intervenes in perception and affect, Vygotsky wrote, it shapes destiny. Did these connections, these passions, not exist before the word had elucidated them, or were they there all the time, but we didn’t know about them, and

134

Ekaterina Zavershneva

now we do? In different periods Vygotsky replied differently to these two alternatives: (1) the word changes just the order and connections of ideas, perceptions, and
affects, (2) the word transforms them completely. Vygotsky did not choose one of
these alternatives, but investigated and compared them.
It is remarkable that Vygotsky, who favored monism in explaining the mind,
never resorted to, on the one hand, ‘matter’ (or ‘brain function’), or pure meanings, on the other. Later commentators have drawn the conclusion that Vygotsky
pictured the mind as something purely artificial, something above or even outside
nature. But Vygotsky himself wrote, for example, that:
We should not conceive of artificial (instrumental) acts as supernatural or
meta-natural acts constructed in accordance with some new, special laws.
Artificial acts are natural as well. They can, without remainder, to the very
end, be decomposed and reduced to natural ones … What is artificial is the
combination (construction) and direction, the substitution and utilization of
these natural processes.
(Vygotsky, 1997c, p. 86)
However, on this very same page Vygotsky made a strange remark: “This method
does not negate a single natural-scientific method for the study of behavior and
nowhere intersects with them” (Vygotsky, 1997c, p. 86). So, what do we have?
Special laws, a special method? Or the simple combination of natural functions
about which reflexologists and behaviorists already talked before Vygotsky? The
radical nature of the propositions advanced during the second half of the 1920s,
when the mind was compared to a machine or technical device, was replaced by
more cautious utterances in the later works: “We can only become conscious of
what we have. We can only master an already existing function” (Vygotskii, 1934a,
p. 227). When the focus of Vygotsky’s research program was shifted from the
sign-word to the sign-meaning, the questions about the reformation of nature and
about the proper method became again acute: “By its appearance speech fundamentally changes consciousness” (Vygotsky, 1997d, p. 137).
Indeed, the postulate about the two planes in the development of the higher psychological functions contained an inner conflict. Because of this postulate,Vygotsky
often had to defend himself against critics and at some point he finally recognized
the fallacy of the idea. In the note Symposium, December 4, 1932 he wrote:
The higher and lower functions are not built in 2 floors: their number and
names do not coincide. But neither our previous understanding: the higher
function is the mastery of the lower one (voluntary attention is the subordination of involuntary attention), for this also means 2 floors.
Vygotsky suspected that this conception of lower and higher functions, which was
once considered his main discovery (an assessment that can still be found in textbooks), retained the older Cartesian dualism.

Vygotsky in 1932

135

Remarks about the psychophysical problem
The psychophysical problem arose when it seemed Vygotsky had already got rid
of it. In one of his last talks, about the localization of higher psychological functions (Vygotskii, 1934d), he repeatedly criticized Pavlov because Pavlov identified
thinking with a function of the frontal lobes, and he challenged the researchers
who adhered to the idea of strict localization of psychological functions. Instead, he
proposed the system principle, which says that functions do not have a fixed composition and change structure depending on the concrete task. The organization of
nervous processes and interfunctional links is changed with the help of extracerebral
mechanisms; this reorganization comes about particularly in the social environment,
in the interaction with other people with the help of sign systems. In his virtual
polemic with the neurologists Goldstein and Head,Vygotsky claimed:
We: in naturalistic terms, in the brain and its functions there are no (and
cannot be) structures that correspond with speech, they develop from
above, from the psychological structures (2 brains that interact via the
historical-cultural environment).The word is the source of new brain structures and not all operations with the word are present in the brain’s morphological structure.
(Aphasia, schizophrenia, and other pathological changes,Vygotsky archive)
With the idea of sociocultural mediation Vygotsky reconsidered the psychophysical problem; he suggested replacing the old body–soul dilemma with another problem: “This is terribly important: speech/thinking as a psycho/physical problem”
(Symposium, December 4, 1932; Vygotsky archive). The question again rested upon
the problem of the word and its nature. This theme was so complex that Vygotsky
more than once ended his notes with the same quotation from Aleksandr Pushkin’s
The covetous knight: “for me this knowledge is enough,” i.e. it was enough that the
problem had been formulated (Vygotsky, 1997d, p. 138).
Indeed, the psychophysical problem, which from the viewpoint of ordinary knowledge would seem to be extremely speculative and even curious, had a
fatal influence on the development of scientific psychology. From its origin it was
divided into two branches. The first branch we may call explanatory psychology
(Wilhelm Dilthey’s term), nomothetic psychology (Wilhelm Windelband’s term),
or natural-scientific psychology and subscribed to the principle of complete determinism. The mind was viewed primarily as a function of the nervous substrate and
the problem was to find the link between mental phenomena and neural processes.
The main task was to find universal laws for the development and structure of the
mind and the major research tool was the experiment. The second branch, descriptive or idiographic psychology, followed in the footsteps of hermeneutics and did
not acknowledge the experiment. The focus was on the person as a free, unique,
and unitary being. In Vygotsky’s time, the Soviet psychological literature presented
this opposition as the opposition between materialistic and idealistic approaches to

136

Ekaterina Zavershneva

the study of mind.That was, of course, a gross simplification, because the opposition
between ‘general’ and ‘unique’ evolved long before Soviet philosophers hit upon the
idea to divide the entire world into these two camps.
It is characteristic that the founder of psychology, Wilhelm Wundt, created not
just one psychology, but two: physiological psychology and Völkerpsychologie. These
two psychologies nowhere overlapped and represented totally different sciences.
Within “physiological psychology” evolved the theory of individual consciousness
as a structure composed of elementary sensations and feelings connected by association.With the help of a metronome Wundt measured the volume of consciousness;
later the tachistoscope appeared, which allowed the study of ‘pure’ (i.e. not distorted
by speech) consciousness by the brief presentation of images. Völkerpsychologie was
dedicated to the description of supra-individual processes such as languages, myths,
and customs. Wundt admitted that he was not satisfied with the division, but the
generations after him all repeated him one way or the other. The history of psychology shows that revolutionary shifts in psychology were always connected with the
appearance of two rival theories, one representing the ‘explanatory’ and the other
the ‘descriptive’ side of the old dichotomy. Thus, at the beginning of the twentieth century psychoanalysis and behaviorism appeared almost simultaneously and
remained for a long time the most powerful currents in psychology. Then, in the
1950s and 1960s, cognitive psychology and humanistic psychology appeared and
immediately declared themselves to be opponents.
Vygotsky, however, always strove for a unified view of the mind; he wanted a
theory that would finally combine and synthesize the two branches of psychology.
A large part of his unfinished works The (historical) meaning of the psychological crisis
and A theory of the emotions is dedicated to the analysis of contemporary theories.
Step by step Vygotsky showed that what seemed unified theories were actually mixtures of heterogeneous components. As long as body and soul were considered as
two independent streams, the hypothesis of parallelism worked, although not without problems, but not so in a psychology where the body–soul unity is an empirical
reality. Until this day we have no satisfactory account of this unity, which is no less
than disastrous.To support this view Vygotsky quoted the founder of Gestalt psychology, the great Max Wertheimer:
Only persons belonging to our recent European culture can separate the psychological and the physical as we do. A person is dancing. Do we really have,
on the one hand, the sum of muscular movements and, on the other hand,
joy and inspiration?
(Vygotsky, 1993b, p. 311)10
In the period between 1910 and the 1930s, when Vygotsky wrote his work, there
was much interest in philosophical and methodological matters among psychologists. Many understood the untenability of the theory of parallelism but alternatives
were not yet available. Psychologists and philosophers proposed various solutions
for the psychophysical problem but on further inspection they turned out to be

Vygotsky in 1932

137

no more than new variants of parallelism.Vygotsky believed that hypotheses about
interaction could not solve the problem – after all, one then first tears the unitary
process (“the unitary psychophysical act”) apart and subsequently tries to establish a
link between the parts.Vygotsky’s notebook showed that he saw the hidden parallels
in the solutions proposed.

Propositions for the talks by Vygotsky’s collaborators
The last section of Vygotsky’s notes contains the propositions for two talks by
Solov’ev and Leontiev. It is no coincidence that they are found together. Here we
have two approaches to the same problem: the relationship between the dynamics of
action and the dynamics of meaning. Solov’ev had modified Anitra Karsten’s (1928,
1976) method and conducted a study whose results he generalized in a book chapter, “About psychological saturation” (Solov’ev-Elpidinskii, 1935).11 He managed to
establish the link between the dynamics of saturation in normal children with that
of mentally retarded subjects. It turned out that adults confronted with a meaningless task regress to the level of a seven-year-old child and become field-dependent.
Further experiments showed that the semantically underdeveloped processes of the
mentally retarded children are reflected in rigid action dynamics, that the children
get stuck in concrete details, are dependent on field conditions, on the material, and
so on. A discussion of this and other experiments based on investigations by members
of Kurt Lewin’s research collective, such as those by Karsten and Gita Birenbaum
(1930), can be found in Vygotsky’s article “The problem of mental retardation”
(Vygotskii, 1935c), published in the same volume as Solov’ev’s work.
Lewin was among the most outstanding intellectuals influencingVygotsky: Lewin
was the first to develop a way to study affect and volition; also, just like Vygotsky,
he favored a monistic methodology based on a principle of strict causality. The new
type of experiment proposed by Lewin had no precedent in psychology nor in science at large. Lewin’s studies were some sort of theatrical experiment, where the
roles of subject and experimenter fundamentally changed and the idea of objective method received a new interpretation. These experiments went beyond the
confines of laboratory study and had immediate practical repercussions. It was the
discussions with Lewin that inspired Vygotsky’s thinking in the final years of his life.
This is evident from Vygotsky’s published writings but also from a large number of
notes from 1933 to 1934 found in the archive (See Chapter 9 for discussion of the
personal contacts between Vygotsky and Lewin).
As early as the beginning of the 1930s Vygotsky had underlined the fact that
emotions are the core of personality and that the chapter about emotions must
become the most important one in the psychology of man. Nevertheless, in the
majority of his writings the emotions play the ‘honorable’ role of a subjugated process (the development of emotions is conceived as the intellectualization of innate
affect, as mastering it), and only in Vygotsky’s later works does one see the outlines
of a new integral unit (“emotional experience”; perezhivanie in Russian), where

138

Ekaterina Zavershneva

affect and intellect are no opposites, but form a complex dynamic synthesis. The
cultural-historical theory of emotions that might have grown from these beginnings
has not been developed as yet.
Returning to the polemic between Lewin and Vygotsky, we may remark
that Karsten’s experiment (and the study of Tamara Dembo) dealt not just with
the problem of motivation but also with the problem of will, and it had to be
explained why far from all subjects regressed in a meaningless situation. Lewin
wrote that the ability to stand above the field, to leave the field by creating perspectives for future actions, even when these cannot be realized immediately, is a
sign of maturity (he called this the capacity for volitional behavior).Vygotsky was
looking for his own explanation of this volitional behavior; he criticized Lewin
for his extreme preference for the emotional-volitional domain to the detriment
of the study of thinking. For Vygotsky, it was precisely thinking that formed the
primary lever that allows the person to overcome the forces of the external field
and preserve their independence.Vygotsky also argued that the dynamics of saturation cannot be exhaustively explained unless we deal directly with the domain of
meaning and turn to the semic analysis of consciousness. Subjects continue with a
boring task mainly because they can reappraise the experimental situation and not
because of an energy impulse from other domains of their inner field. As a result
of this reappraisal, both the reorganization of the external and internal domains
of the field and voluntary behavior become possible. In other words, affect can
only be understood in connection with intellect; the dynamics of the situation
can only be understood from the dynamics of meaning. It is important to reach
the highest level of analysis; the level of the whole that determines its parts. Who
should know this better than Lewin, the founder of a theory derived from Gestalt
psychology?
Vygotsky claimed that at the basis of the mature person are tertiary links
between psychological functions (the primary links are the unmediated innate ones;
the secondary links are the mediated interfunctional ones; and the tertiary links are
reformed secondary ones, deliberately constructed by the person to reach a specific vital goal). Hence, conceptual thinking, which makes it possible to understand
oneself, to cast a conceptual net over one’s dimly felt inner world, is of primary
importance to the person. It is the meaningful word, added to the primary affect,
that connects it with other conditions and the world; which elucidates it, makes it
transparent and visible and, therefore, manageable and conscious. Vygotsky understood that his conception verged on intellectualism and he followed Lewin’s studies
with interest – they represented as it were the complement to his own research. It
was only in his last writings on developmental psychology and partly in Thinking
and speech that Vygotsky managed to soften this intellectualism. He searched for
other forms of cognizance and awareness, not exclusively tied to the function of
thinking but based on a complex Gestalt of all psychological functions. However,
he did not manage to complete the revision of his theory.
Vygotsky used similar and quite sharp arguments against Leontiev: in his
view Leontiev underestimated the higher processes and showed a low level of

Vygotsky in 1932

139

theoretical generalization. In the note one senses that Vygotsky’s former enthusiasm about the perspectives of their collaboration gave way to bewilderment.
Leontiev studied the transitions between the cognizing and the practical intellect from the viewpoint of what later would become his activity theory. From
Vygotsky’s viewpoint, however, these transitions had to be studied as part of the
larger – and methodologically primary – problem of consciousness: the dynamics
of the action (such as the phenomenon of saturation) he wished to study solely
within the framework of his “peak psychology,” i.e. in the context of the general
dynamics of consciousness. As soon as we understand the dynamics of meanings, we will understand all the rest as effects that follow from a cause and vice
versa: separating the action from the dimension of meanings, we lose the meaning
of the action, its goal-directed nature, etc.
In his criticism Vygotsky said that Leontiev lacked discipline, lost the focus of
the research, and ignored the systemic principle. To him, the step aside made by
Leontiev was not just a deviation from the main goal – i.e. the creation of a theory
of consciousness – but also a step backwards towards the functional analysis prevalent in Vygotsky’s writings from 1928 to 1930, when each function that formed part
of consciousness was investigated separately. However, now the time had come “to
gather stones together”: to combine all results, to synthesize and rethink them in
order to find a leading, ‘peak’ level of analysis that would permit the explanation of
all components of consciousness, including the dynamics of action, with new laws
characteristic of consciousness as a whole. Leontiev, apparently, was in no hurry to
adapt his studies to the new plan.
The arguments against Leontiev remained within the boundaries of a normal
scientific debate, although it is evident that Vygotsky was losing his patience: “Each
of us made his own independent contribution, starting from the common assumptions. But where is he heading?” (Notebook. October, 1932). It seems that Vygotsky
did not suspect that behind Leontiev’s decision to dissociate himself from their
research project may also have been a wide range of reasons. Leontiev subsequently
developed his “activity theory” and made a career in the complex period from 1930
to 1950, becoming the leader of the activity-theory movement and the founder
of the faculty of psychology at Moscow State University. Without discussing the
differences of opinion between Vygotsky and Leontiev any further, we would like
to point out one further circumstance that indicates that their disagreements were
genuine: if we discount the edition of part of Vygotsky’s (1956, 1960) works during the thaw period, psychologists in the Soviet Union had quite limited exposure
to Vygotsky’s writings until the 1980s, that is, exactly during the period in which
Leontiev occupied leading positions in Soviet science (cf. discussion at the end of
Chapter 3).
However that may be, at the present moment psychology knows an “activity theory” but no general psychological theory of consciousness nor a functioning theory
of emotions. Of course, many things have changed since Vygotsky’s death eighty
years ago and his ideas can and should acquire a new significance, a new meaning.

140

Ekaterina Zavershneva

Just as Vygotsky tried to problematize the psychology of his time, to find its weak
spots and growing points, we must try to find them in Vygotsky’s theory. In this
sense a polemic with Vygotsky may be no less valuable than literally following his
texts. Hopefully, the publication of new historical-biographical studies dedicated to
Vygotsky’s conception and of archival materials will help to resurrect the project for
a general psychology that Vygotsky failed to realize.

PART III

Holism and transnationalism

This page intentionally left blank

7
TRANSLATING VYGOTSKY
Some problems of transnational
Vygotskian science
René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

International interest in what are commonly believed to be the ideas of the
Russian psychologist Lev Vygotsky is enormous. The number of publications
about this historical figure and his scientific legacy is growing almost exponentially. Vygotsky’s international fame and his status of celebrity are widely
acknowledged by scholars and educationists all over the globe, although, it seems,
the reasons for the glorification of Vygotsky in different regions of the world
are different. Also different are the histories of the import and inculcation of
Vygotsky into different regional scholarly communities. Thus, the posthumous
fame of Vygotsky in the Soviet Union and Russia is chiefly due to the administrative success of the clan of his former students and associates who were raised
to power and got control over a considerable segment of Soviet psychology in
the 1960s/1970s. As an instrument for the consolidation of the clan and the
ideology of their informal personal network, the canonic narrative of the archetypical ‘Vygotsky school’ was introduced and developed in numerous propagandist writings in this tradition. This canonic narrative included several foundational
structural components, such as the dogma of the troika of Vygotsky, Leontiev,
and Luria, and the piaterka (the five) of their devoted students and followers.
According to the legend, all these – in the military newspeak of Soviet academia –
‘brethren in arms’ would later constitute the ‘Vygotsky–Leontiev–Luria school’.
Somewhat paradoxically, the rise to administrative power of the ‘Vygotsky students’ clan’ was accompanied by a virtual absence of Vygotsky’s publications
in the Soviet Union. This can be considered as the factual ‘ban on Vygotsky’ in
the Soviet Union in the 1960s–1970s (for an in-depth critical discussion of the
‘archetype of Soviet psychology’, the canonic narrative, and the history of the
alleged ban on Vygotsky in the 1930s–1950s, see Chapters 1, 2, and 3 of this
book, respectively). Although related to the developments in the Soviet Union,
the international history of the ‘exported Vygotsky’ is quite different from the

144

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

domestic one, and is fairly specific to the particular locale, academic culture,
and linguistic tradition. It is hardly possible to understand the issues of translation outside the larger context of the cultural assimilation of ideas circulating
between countries, regions, and local academic and professional communities.
The transnational history of Vygotskian science has not yet been written. And
yet, such a history would be very helpful in our present discussion of “some
problems of transnational Vygotskian science,” and, specifically, the issue of the
translations of Vygotsky’s works. Therefore, we first provide a relatively brief and
superficial sketch of such a future history, to be written some day. Then, we proceed to the discussion of the issues of translation, including the actual and anticipated potential errors, and suggest some practical guidelines for future translators.

A transnational history of Vygotsky in context
A transnational history of science and the practice of the “import” of ideas should
be guided by the sort of questions that Pierre Bourdieu posed in his essay “The
social conditions of the international circulation of ideas”:
A large number of translations can only be understood if they are placed in
the complex network of international exchanges between holders of dominant academic posts, the exchanges of invitations, honorary doctorates, etc.
The question that must then be asked is how it comes about that a certain
writer or editor becomes the importer of a certain thought. Why is writer X
published by publisher Y? For it is obvious that there will always be some sort
of profit involved. Heretical imports are often the work of marginals in the
field, bringing a message, a position of force from a different field, which they
use to try and shore up their own position. Foreign writers are often subject
to such instrumental use, and forced to serve purposes which they would perhaps refuse or reject in their country of origin. One can often use a foreign
thinker to attack domestic thinkers in this way.
(Bourdieu, 1999, p. 223)
The ‘export of Vygotsky’ to North America and, by extension, the Anglo-Saxon
world started as early as in the late 1920s and continued throughout the 1930s,
although the impact of these early efforts was absolutely minimal. The interest in
Vygotsky’s works in the English-speaking world during that period was close to
non-existent. There were, however, two notable exceptions, both directly related to
the activities and relative success of Vygotsky’s associates in the West. First, the book
by Vygotsky’s associate, co-author, and collaborator Alexander Luria, The nature of
human conflicts, which came out in 1932 in the United States and made quite a
stir there (A. R. Luria, 1932c). In that book, Luria did acknowledge the impact
of Vygotsky’s ideas, albeit rather inconspicuously. However, contemporaries were
more inclined to see this volume as a major contribution to the development of the
studies of Carl Jung, Max Wertheimer, or even Kurt Lewin (Leeper, 1943). Second,

Translating Vygotsky 145

the applied work done by Jacob Kasanin and his associate Eugenia Hanfmann in
the field of psychiatry and mental testing, using the revised experimental technique
developed by Vygotsky and his late collaborator Sakharov, was a quite notable contribution to the dissemination of Vygotsky’s ideas in the West – at least some of
these ideas. The effort of Kasanin and Hanfmann deserves a separate mention, for
they not only replicated the Sakharov–Vygotsky studies on North American soil
(Hanfmann & Kasanin, 1937, 1942; Kasanin & Hanfmann, 1938), but also were
instrumental in the actual import of his texts: their first translation came out in
English in 1934 (Vygotsky, 1934). All these efforts did not go entirely unnoticed at
that time, and were acknowledged by a number of scholars, including such names
as Kurt Goldstein, Harry Stack Sullivan, Norman Cameron, John D. Benjamin, S. J.
Beck, E. von Domarus, and Andras Angyal (Kasanin, 1944). Kasanin was also instrumental in the first foreign publication of Vygotsky’s text that would subsequently
evolve into the first English-language publication of Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech
in 1962. The book’s final chapter was published in English as a scholarly paper in
1939, in the journal Psychiatry, edited by the journal’s founder Harry Stack Sullivan.
Eugenia Hanfmann, Kasanin’s collaborator in his clinical research, helped him also
with these translations of the 1930s and later, in the 1950s, became one of the two
translators of the complete book, published under the somewhat erroneous and
misleading title Thought and language (Vygotsky, 1962). The translation, made in
collaboration with Gertrude Vakar, was fairly idiosyncratic. The second edition of
this book came out very soon afterwards, in 1965, by the same publishing house,
the MIT Press.
In the 1930s and 1940s, however, Vygotsky had not yet become a major figure
of international psychology, and fame reached him only through a number of later,
postwar, publications that reveal the agency of a huge transnational informal personal network of scholars, translators, publishers, policy-makers, and the like. This
informal personal network emerged after the relatively brief period, roughly from
1948 to 1955, of lack of contact between the two superpowers, the Soviet Union
and the United States, during the initial phase of the Cold War. This brief period
was characterized by, on the one hand, a dramatic separation of the two superpowers and intense competition in international affairs and, on the other hand, fairly
symmetrical and parallel processes of witch-hunts and politically motivated persecutions in domestic affairs in both the USSR and the USA. Yet, everything was
to gradually change, and the Soviet Union started reopening pretty soon after the
death of Stalin in 1953.The first renewed contacts between Soviet psychologists and
their Western peers took place in 1954 on the occasion of the 14th International
Congress of Psychology in Montreal. Soviet scholars first re-established their connections with francophone, mainly French, psychologists, known for their explicit
Marxist and leftist leanings. Soon thereafter, they renewed their connections with
Anglo-Saxon – North American and British – psychologists as well. In these contacts,Vygotsky and his legacy became one of the main ‘import-export’ commodities
of scientific exchange and interaction. In the history of these exchanges two highly
illustrative episodes are of interest.

146

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

The French connection
The following reconstruction of the first episode, in three acts, is based on the
reminiscences of francophone psychologists, who reflected on their exchanges with
their Soviet peers in 1954–5. The first act of the ‘French connection’ plot took
place in Paris in the spring of 1954. The first two psychologists who made a trip
from the Soviet Union to France in the postwar period were Anatolii Smirnov and
Natal’ia Menchinskaia (respectively, director and deputy director of the Institute of
Psychology of the Academy of Pedagogical Sciences), who attended a conference
on child psychology (Journées de psychologie de l’enfant) held in Paris on April
21–26, 1954. René Zazzo (1910–95), who was one of the organizers of the event,
recalled that just two months later French psychologists realized that this surprise
visit from behind the Iron Curtain was not just coincidental, because another larger
delegation of Soviet scholars (Leontiev, Teplov, Zaporozhets, Kostiuk, Sokolov, and
Asratyan) arrived in Paris in order to greet Henri Wallon (1879–1962) – a highly
influential psychologist and political activist, member of the French Communist
Party and director of the Institute of Psychology in Paris – on their way to the 14th
International Congress of Psychology in Montreal. According to Zazzo’s reminiscences, it was in June, 1954 that Aleksei Leontiev invited Wallon to the luxurious
hotel Lutetia (where the members of the Soviet delegation stayed during their visit
to Paris) and asked Zazzo for help. According to Zazzo, Leontiev was concerned
about the ‘rehabilitation’ of Lev Vygotsky, a brilliant and “the most prestigious” (le
plus prestigieux) Soviet psychologist of the 1930s, who was compromised by his
association with the officially banned discipline of pedology. Leontiev was seeking Zazzo’s assistance in publishing a volume of collected articles from the late
Vygotsky that he and Luria had selected. Such anticipated publication was expected
to make a strong impression on domestic Soviet authorities and speed up the publication of the book at home in the USSR.1 Zazzo’s efforts in France remained
unsuccessful but Luria was able to arrange a deal with publishers in the United
States, and, reportedly, the manuscript was sent to North America in 1955. Finally,
yet another year later, in 1956, the same collection of Vygotsky’s texts came out in
Russian in the Soviet Union before and regardless of the international publication
of Vygotsky’s works in translation.2
The second act of the story took place in Montreal on June 7–12, 1954, where
a Soviet delegation of six arrived, four of whom presented at the thematic symposium Recent advances in conditioning, chaired by Donald G. Marquis (Michigan) and
with Paul Fraisse (Sorbonne), Gregory Razran (Queen’s, Brooklyn), and E. L. Kelly
(Michigan)3 as discussants. The four presentations all seemed grounded in Pavlov’s
teaching on higher nervous activity and abounded with Pavlovian terminology.
Another paper, Development of voluntary movements by A. V. Zaporozhets, was presented at the symposium on Motor skills. Finally, the presentation by Kostiuk on the
development of thinking in children (Du développement de l’intelligence chez l’enfant)
was published in Moscow by the Academy of Pedagogical Sciences of the RSFSR
in a collection of conference papers presented by Soviet scholars.Yet, for unknown

Translating Vygotsky 147

reasons, the presentation by Kostiuk was not listed among the abstracts of the papers
presented at the conference.
The third act of the Soviet-French exchange of 1954–5 was performed back
in the USSR by Soviet scholars and a group of their francophone peers who were
invited to visit the Soviet Union. The group included Henri Piéron (1881–1964),
Paul Fraisse (1911–96), René Zazzo, and Jean Piaget (who was the president of the
International Union of Scientific Psychology at the time). In April/May 1955, three
of them (Piéron could not participate because of ill health) made a trip to the Soviet
Union (mostly to Moscow, but they also visited Leningrad for a couple of days).
Immediately after the trip Piaget published a paper, entitled “Some impressions of
a visit to Soviet psychologists” (Piaget, 1956). In his fairly favorable overview of the
trip and the psychological research conducted in the USSR, Piaget made several
interesting remarks. For example, he noted that, contrary to the superficial impression that one might get from the published sources, psychology seemed to be a
thriving discipline in the Soviet Union: physiologists were numerous indeed, but
there were also large numbers of local psychologists employed by a wide range of
institutions, such as universities, institutes dependent on the Academy of Sciences,
the Psychological Institute of the Academy of Pedagogical Sciences, and the Institute
of Defectology. He also noted that it appeared that the psychologists he met during the trip were surprisingly well read in contemporary scholarly literature: they
“read everything that appears abroad, particularly in French and English.” In the
end Piaget, who was most impressed by the experimental research, the variety of
opinions, and the enthusiasm of his Soviet peers, had just one criticism:
If I may be permitted a critical reflection, the only one that occurred to me
in the course of my visit or in analysing certain studies like those of Kostiouk
[sic] on the psychology of number in children – I am under the impression
that certain writers quote non-Russian sources less than they read them.
(Piaget, 1956, p. 344)
In this article Piaget also mentioned a meeting that their delegation had with the
‘great five’ (Leontiev, Teplov, Rubinstein, Luria, and Smirnov) and a ‘frank discussion’ about a number of questions on their minds, especially with regard to the “real
implications of reflexological explanations in psychology.” In his characterization of
this discussion, he added: “we found not only a full comprehension of our questions, but in addition a critical position more highly nuanced than we would have
thought from some publications on the subject of these essential problems” (Piaget,
1956, p. 343).
Piaget also reported that they “learned inter alia that the publications of
Ivanov-Smolenski [a zealous Pavlovian] by no means carry authority in Moscow,
as is supposed outside the country” (Piaget, 1956, p. 343). In his memoirs of
the 1980s, Zazzo sheds light on this somewhat cryptic reference to the ‘frank
discussion’. He recalled the last meeting of the francophone guests with their
Moscow hosts on May 2, 1955, in informal settings, and quoted their opinions on

148

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

the state of the art in Soviet psychology. In a somewhat provocative and utterly
revealing paper that Zazzo published in 1956 in La Raison, the organ of the
French Communist Party, some of these statements of Soviet psychologists were
rendered verbatim, for instance: “For twenty years Ivanov-Smolenski has done
nothing but verbal work, but no [research on] physiology of verbal [behavior]”
(Leontiev); “I hate someone to explain the complex phenomena (of mental life)
by the scheme of conditioned reflexes” (Teplov); “Within the immense domain
of psychology Pavlov studied just a little bit” (Luria); “Pedagogy can not develop
unless on the basis of paedology” (Smirnov)” (Zazzo, 1989).4 This paper had
been written at the request of Leontiev, who asked Zazzo to help French comrades better understand the complexities of the Soviet situation in psychology
and Pavlovian physiology, and to correct certain of their attitudes toward this
issue (Zazzo, 1982).5 The paper caused quite a stir in Moscow, and, at Leontiev’s
request, another paper signed by ‘the great five’ of Soviet psychology was published in the same edition. This paper was supposed to correct the ‘mistakes’ and
‘misinterpretations’ that might have occurred in translation of their conversation into French. The irony of this excuse was obvious to the participants of the
discussion: everyone but Smirnov spoke French, and Zazzo was meticulously
taking notes. In any case, regardless of the later ‘corrections’ and ‘clarifications’,
the message of the first publication must have reached its intended francophone
audience abroad.

Anglo-Saxon ties: a Cold War story
The second episode also took place in April, 1955. It appears that the group of francophone scholars was not the only delegation to make a trip to the USSR in the
spring of 1955 in order to meet Soviet psychologists. An unidentified “small party of
teachers and educationists” that included Brian Simon, a Communist sympathizer
and a lecturer in Education at the University of Leicester, and, possibly, his wife Joan
Simon, visited the Soviet Union at the invitation of the Academy of Pedagogical
Sciences of the RSFSR. According to Simon, this group of British educationists
arrived in the Soviet Union with the purpose of familiarizing themselves with the
achievements of the Soviets in the field of polytechnical education and in preparation for the raising of the school leaving age to 17. However, as it turned out
during the trip, in order for them to understand the ideas of Soviet educators and
researchers the group of Westerners required some knowledge of developments in
psychology. As a result, the members of the delegation had a series of most productive discussions with Soviet scholars, quite a few of whom are already familiar to
us, including Smirnov, Menchinskaia, Leontiev, and Luria in Moscow, and Ananiev
in Leningrad. The discussions proved so informative and revealing that the idea was
born to prepare a volume of collected works of contemporary Soviet psychologists.
This volume, comprising some twenty translated papers authored by Soviet psychologists, came out in 1957 under Simon’s editorship (B. Simon, 1957). This was

Translating Vygotsky 149

the first major postwar presentation of the achievements of Soviet psychology in the
West: it was the first, but it would definitely not be the last.
The trip of anglophone teachers and educationists to Moscow in April 1955
resulted in a volume, Psychology in the Soviet Union. But this was not the only
outcome of the trip. According to the papers of Brian Simon in the Institute of
Education at the University of London, Simon extensively corresponded with Luria
from the mid-1950s and made several other trips to the Soviet Union. As a result,
Luria’s 1930s studies of twins, published as a book in Russian in 1956, came out in
1959 under the editorship of Joan Simon (translated by J. Simon and O. Kovasc)
from London’s Staples Press (A. R. Luria & Yudovich, 1956, 1959). Apparently, Luria
also traveled to the West in the 1950s,6 and some of these trips were organized and
facilitated by his British colleagues, possibly the Simons. Thus, three lectures given
by Luria at University College London in London in October, 1957 came out as
a book, The role of speech in the regulation of normal and abnormal behaviour in Great
Britain and in the United States in 1961 (A. R. Luria, 1961).7 Luria’s foreign trips
and his foreign contacts were by no means limited to the United Kingdom. In 1960,
during his trip to the United States, Luria met the luminary of American science
and the co-founder of cognitive studies, Jerome Bruner, who later recalled: “Luria
and I became fast friends almost immediately.We were compatible temperamentally
and very much in agreement about psychological matters” (Bruner, 1983, p. 145). It
appears that this meeting was to become one of the major events in the history of
the global spread of Vygotsky’s ideas.
Following his British and North American exchanges, roughly a dozen of Luria’s
books were published in the UK and the USA during the 1960s, which is a strong
indicator of Luria’s rapidly growing reputation as a scholar of international acclaim.8
In turn, Brian and Joan Simon visited the Soviet Union in September, 1961. As a
result of their cooperation with the scholars at the Institute of Psychology of the
Academy of Pedagogical Sciences – once again, Menchinskaia (the Head of the
Department of Educational Psychology and Deputy Director of the Institute) and
Smirnov (Director of the Institute) were mentioned – another comprehensive volume of collected contributions by Soviet psychologists, entitled Education and psychology in the USSR, was published in London by Routledge and Kegan Paul in 1963
(B. Simon & Simon, 1963).
Another British ‘broker’ between the anglophone readership and Soviet psychologists was Neil O’Connor (Medical Research Council psychologist at the Institute
of Psychiatry, the Maudsley Hospital in London), who had been involved in the
Simons’ international projects from the 1950s onwards. O’Connor edited another
collection of contributions by Soviet authors, entitled Recent Soviet psychology, which
was published in the United States by Liveright in 1961 (O’Connor, 1961).This book
identified three main trends in contemporary Soviet psychology, namely: (a) physiological psychology based on Pavlov’s work, (b) the growth of concept formation
and of speech based on Vygotsky’s and Luria’s studies, and (c) the studies on ‘set
theory’ done mostly by the scholars of the Georgian school of Uznadze. Five years
later, in 1966, the same three general trends in Soviet psychology were discussed in

150

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

a volume issued by Pergamon Press.The book, Present-day Russian psychology. A symposium by seven authors, was also edited by O’Connor and included contributions of
scholars from the UK, USA, and Israel (J. A. Gray; B. H. Kirman; P. M. A. Rabbitt;
R. Lynn; D. I. Slobin; L. Rahmani; J. Brožek). The editor proudly boasted that the
book was unique in that it was the first comprehensive survey of Russian psychological literature written by bilingual psychologists (O’Connor, 1966).
American scholars, from the beginning of the 1960s, were increasingly interested in the promise of Soviet psychology, and invested considerable time, funds,
and effort into the exploration of this scientific terra incognita. Perhaps one of the
first American scholars to make a trip to the Soviet Union after the war was
the renowned linguist and literary scholar of Russian origin Roman Jakobson
(1896–1982), who attended the meeting of the International Association of Slavists
in Moscow in the spring of 1956, and later frequently participated in various scientific conventions held in the Soviet Union in the 1950s–1970s.9 A number of
American psychologists visited the Soviet Union in the summer of 1960. Once
again, the trip resulted in the publication of a book. It was edited by Raymond
A. Bauer, who had already authored The new man in Soviet psychology (Bauer, 1952),
which was written a year before Stalin’s death and was largely outdated as of the
beginning of the 1960s. The new book, Some views on Soviet psychology, included
contributions by another group of anglophone scholars that consisted of Alexander
Mintz, Walter R. Reitman, Urie Bronfenbrenner and Otto Klineberg, Tema
S. David,Yvonne Brackbill, Edwin A. Fleishman, Neal E. Miller, Carl Pfeiffmann,
Harold Scholsberg, and Gardner and Lois Murphy (Bauer, 1962). At the beginning
of the 1960s, quite a few more American psychologists made trips to the Soviet
Union, either in large groups and delegations or individually and independently,
like Daniel E. Berlyne, who made his trip to the USSR in 1961, or, for instance,
Michael Cole, whose visit to the Soviet Union for postgraduate studies with
Luria in Moscow in 1962 started a series of continuous and productive exchanges
between these two scholars and, subsequently, Cole’s immense involvement with
the dissemination of Soviet psychology in North America. Also in 1962, Jerome
Bruner paid a visit to Luria in Moscow, and they together even recorded a “discussion program” on national radio (Bruner, 1983, p. 185).
Many of those who traveled to the Soviet Union or otherwise established contacts with Soviet psychologists constituted a network of American brokers between
the East and the West. The members of this network were involved in translations
of works by Russian authors and published Soviet research overviews (most notably,
publications by Gregory Razran, who had been considered as an expert on Russia
from the mid-1930s, Alexander Mintz, Jozef Brožek, Daniel E. Berlyne, and Ivan
London). A number of reviews of books by Soviet psychologists came out on the
initiative of Josef Brožek, who served as the editor of East European section of the
journal Contemporary Psychology. A major breakthrough in bringing Soviet psychological research to the West was the launch of a journal of translations named Soviet
Psychology and Psychiatry in 1962, under the editorship of Dan Slobin (founding
editor of the journal from 1962 to 1969; the journal split into Soviet Neurology &

Translating Vygotsky 151

Psychiatry and Soviet Psychology in 1966; the latter was renamed Journal of Russian
and East European Psychology in 1992). As a result of these numerous efforts to disseminate Soviet psychology, it had already been fairly well presented in the West by
August, 1963, when, at the meeting of the International Union of Psychological
Science (IUPsyS) Executive Committee during the 17th International Congress of
Psychology in Washington, DC, Moscow was nominated as the next psychological
congress venue for 1966.
In Moscow, from August 4 to 11, 1966, the 18th International Congress of
Psychology was held at the invitation of the Soviet government. The Congress
turned out a triumph for international psychology in two main respects. On the
one hand, this Congress appeared to be the most representative congress in the
history of the International Union of Psychological Science, which had organized
such scientific forums ever since the end of World War II. The record attendance
of 3,897 scientists from 43 countries and, in addition, approximately 1,000 accompanying persons was to be surpassed only by the Psychological Congress in Leipzig
in 1980 (Rosenzweig et al., 2000). Thus, the Moscow Congress indicated a major
success for the International Union of Scientific Psychology, the organization that
was created just fifteen years before with the goal and ambition to coordinate psychological research globally. On the other hand, the organization of the triumphal
Moscow Congress coincided with the establishment of the first two – rival and
extremely competitive – Departments of Psychology in the Soviet Union founded
under the aegis of the State Universities in Moscow (Department Dean – A. N.
Leontiev) and Leningrad (Department Dean – B. F. Lomov), both opened in the
fall of 1966. The organizational success of the two – the International Union of
Psychological Science and Soviet psychology as a scientific discipline in the Soviet
Union – was truly remarkable and instrumental in expanding the Union’s activities
over Eastern Europe and the countries of the Soviet bloc and, on the other hand,
the further global dissemination of Soviet achievements in psychology. Vygotsky
and his alleged legacy were a quite essential component of this process – which is
true at least on the rhetorical level and as far as the members of the Leontiev clan’s
diplomacy, politics, and struggle for power are concerned.

The transnational Vygotskian network in action
The enormous global network of many actors from all walks of life and different
theoretical outlooks included a considerable number of individuals who particularly
favored the Vygotskian strand in Soviet psychology and did their best to promote
it outside the Soviet Union. Vygotskian ideas infiltrated into non-Russian science
in many subtle ways: through informal personal exchanges, official collaborative
projects, Soviet research overviews, and original studies conducted chiefly by North
American and Western European scholars who made use of the notions and ideas
that they borrowed from their Russian peers. Still, translations of Vygotsky’s works
constituted the most direct, although, as it eventually turned out, not always the

152

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

most reliable, mode of transmission of his ideas abroad. Vygotsky’s works spread
among speakers of German, Dutch, French, Italian, Portuguese, Spanish, Danish,
Greek, Japanese, Farsi, and many other languages in many different ways. However,
the next part of this chapter is limited to the discussion of English translations only.10
The first major translation of Vygotsky’s work after World War II came out only
in the early 1960s, but a great deal of preparatory work preceded this notable event.
We may speculate as to whether it was during Roman Jakobson’s Moscow trip in
1956 or, more probably, during his trip to Moscow of 1958, or perhaps Luria’s trip
to the United States of 1960, that the work towards an American publication of
Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech began.11
In 1961, a breakthrough took place: the first post-World War II English-language
publication of Vygotsky in the West came out in the United States in the volume
Psycholinguistics: A book of readings, edited by Sol Saporta. This was a republication of
the last chapter of Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech that, under the title Thought and
speech, had previously been published in 1939 in the journal Psychiatry, in an English
translation by Jacob Kasanin, Eugenia Hanfmann, and Helen Kogan, and constituted
one of the chapters in this reader (Vygotsky, 1961).
As mentioned before, in 1962, eventually and after “a great deal of backing and
filing diplomatically to obtain rights” the whole volume was published.12 A truly
international and interdisciplinary team of scholars was involved in this project.They
included Soviet neuro- and cultural psychologist Aleksander Luria, the American
linguist of Russian origin Roman Jakobson, American psychologist Jerome Bruner,
American psychologist of Russian origin Eugenia Hanfmann, American-British
poet and translator Gertrude Vakar, and Swiss psychologist Jean Piaget. This diverse
team was instrumental in bringing to life the first ever English-language book by
Vygotsky: the posthumous collection of Vygotsky’s writings entitled Thinking
and speech, published under the title Thought and language, with an Introduction
authored by Bruner (Vygotsky, 1962). The book also contained a separate leaflet with a rejoinder to Vygotsky’s critique of Piaget’s works of 1923–4, entitled
Comments on Vygotsky’s critical remarks, that Piaget wrote for this occasion (Piaget,
1962).13 Although a major breakthrough in the history of Vygotsky in the West, this
book, however, was not free from serious problems and disadvantages as far as its
authenticity and reliability were concerned: in total, due to considerable editorial
interventions, the book was 168 pages long, which was roughly half of the 324-page
length of the Russian original. Three years later, a paperback version of the book
followed. The publication of this book launched an avalanche of other publications
of Vygotsky’s works, prepared on different occasions and in various editions by a
number of the members of the invisible Vygotskian informal network of scholars.
In 1963, in the United Kingdom, Brian and Joan Simon published an edited
volume of the works of Soviet psychologists: Educational psychology in the U.S.S.R.,
translated by Joan Simon. The first chapter of the book was Vygotsky’s programmatic paper “Learning and mental development at school age” (Vygotsky, 1963).
This chapter presented Vygotsky’s views on the interrelations between learning
and development, and a lengthy exposition of his idea of the ‘zone of potential

Translating Vygotsky 153

development’ – the phrasing that had been approved by Luria (J. Simon, 1987) –
that from other publications is better known as the ‘zone of proximal development’.
In 1965, British journal Neuropsychologia (Pergamon Press, Oxford, UK) published
two articles with the following editorial introduction signed by “The Editors”:
We are publishing in this issue the summary of a paper by the late Professor
LEO S. VYGOTSKY, the outstanding Soviet psychologist and one of the
founders of contemporary neuropsychology. The paper in full was prepared
for the 1st Conference of Psycho-Neurological Sciences held in Kharkov
(USSR) in the late summer of 1934. Professor L. S. VYGOTSKY died on
June 11, 1934 and his premature death prevented him from presenting this
paper. The present summary which he prepared was his last paper and was
published only posthumously. Although it was published in Russian more
than 30 years ago and some contraversions mentioned in this paper are no
longer of urgent importance, the basic ideas of this paper are still of great
interest, and will have considerable influence on the further development of
neuropsychology.
(Editors, 1965)
Apparently it was Luria, a member of the Board of Editors of the journal,
who initiated this publication of Vygotsky’s paper of 1934, “Psychology and the
localization of functions” (Vygotsky, 1965), along with his own paper with the
self-explanatory title “L. S. Vygotsky and the problem of localization of functions”
(A. R. Luria, 1965). According to Luria, the investigation of the development of
higher psychological functions, their changes in conditions of injury, and their disintegration under brain damage, which Vygotsky started in the 1920s, laid the basis
of a new discipline – neuropsychology. Vygotsky’s last, posthumously published
work presented the fullest program of investigation of the functional organization
of the human brain – the organ of human consciousness.
In 1966, the Soviet publishing house Progress Publishers released two volumes
with the works of Soviet psychologists translated into English and French. The volumes were not identical and included different sets of papers by different authors.
For the benefit of an English-speaking audience interested in the work of Vygotsky,
the English volume Psychological research in the USSR opened with Vygotsky’s text
“Development of the higher mental functions” in the translation of D. Myshne
(Vygotsky, 1966). Still, the volumes came out in the USSR, and it is unclear how
they could have reached the targeted wide international audiences had they not
been distributed among the participants in the XVIII International Congress of
Psychology held in Moscow same year, in 1966.
In 1967, the journal Soviet Psychology under the editorship of Dan Slobin republished the wholeVygotskian memorial issue of the Russian journal Voprosy Psikhologii
that had come out in the Soviet Union a year before and included papers by members of the Leontiev clan – A. V. Zaporozhetz, P. Ya. Gal’perin, D. B. El’konin,V. V.
Davydov, and A. R. Luria. All but Davydov (Gal’perin’s and El’konin’s student and

154

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

co-worker) were Vygotsky’s former collaborators and associates.14 It was in Soviet
Psychology that Vygotsky’s paper “Play and its role in the mental development of
the child” first appeared in English (Vygotsky, 1967). The issue contained a preface authored by Jerome Bruner, who argued that Vygotsky’s Thought and language,
which had been published in an “excellent translation” in 1962, had earned him the
“admiration of a discriminating group of American readers, but neither created a
‘boom’, nor produced passionate disciples.” However, in his view the importance of
Vygotsky for North American scholars was enormous:
Vygotsky’s work, completed in the 1930s and extended to our day by his admiring and distinguished students, corresponds to a comparable period in America
when more “cognitive psychologists” were struggling against the orthodoxy
of mechanistic American behaviorism. Each sought to redress the balance that
had tilted psychology toward a simple model of learning based on the conditional reflex – a model given its principal legitimacy by the work of a Russian,
Pavlov. Just as cognitive theorists in America sought to free the organism from
“push-pull” determination of stimulus-response connections … so Vygotsky
sought to break away from the stimulus-substitution model of conditioning by
noting that stimuli took their significance from the linguistic matrix in which
they were imbedded.
Finally, after a brief discussion Bruner concluded that Vygotsky became a “seminal ancestor to contemporary cognitive psychology.” Therefore, “he is, as such, an
important link between Soviet psychology and contemporary American psychology – a link at the cognitive pole, just as Pavlov was at the stimulus-response conditioning pole” (Bruner, 1967, p. 5).
In 1971, the MIT Press published an English translation by Scripta Technica, Inc.
of Vygotsky’s The psychology of art (Vygotsky, 1971) that had been first published in
Russian under the editorship of Vyacheslav Vs. Ivanov shortly before, in 1965 and
a second, revised edition in 1968. Based on a typescript from 1925, which, according to the editor of the book, “Vygotsky himself prepared for publication” (Ivanov,
1968, p. 503), it was a small miracle that this book was published in English.
In 1974, another translation of Vygotsky’s work came out in English, but this
publication was titled Vygotsky’s view about the age periodization of child development and
appeared under the names of the two translators, Mary Ann and Bryce F. Zender.
The latter later served as the editor and translator of the book Computers and education in the Soviet Union (B. F. Zender, 1975) and both disguised Vygotsky’s paper that
had come out in the leading Russian psychological journal Voprosy Psikhologii just
two years before and “smuggled” it into the journal Human Development as their own
paper on age periodization in Vygotsky’s theory (M. A. Zender & Zender, 1974).15
In 1976, Jerome Bruner and his associates republished Vygotsky’s paper “Play and
its role in the mental development of the child” in their thematic reader on play
whose title bore a close resemblance to Vygotsky’s paper title, and directly hinted at

Translating Vygotsky 155

the problems covered in Vygotsky’s original presentation of 1933 – Play: Its role in
development and evolution (Vygotsky, 1976).
In the late 1970s, three other Vygotsky works were published in Soviet Psychology
under the editorship of Michael Cole (journal editor 1969–2002): “The development of higher psychological functions” (1977), “The development of higher
forms of attention in childhood” (1979), and “Consciousness as a problem in the
psychology of behavior” (1979).16 In addition, at the same time, two volumes were
published by M. E. Sharpe, Inc.: Soviet developmental psychology: An anthology, edited
by Michael Cole (1977), and The concept of activity in Soviet psychology (editions of
1979 and 1981), edited by James V. Wertsch, a Ph.D. graduate (in 1975) from the
University of Chicago. Wertsch spent a year as a postdoctoral fellow in Moscow,
where he studied linguistics and neuropsychology (Wertsch, 1981a). The former
volume contained a collection of papers on various topics in developmental psychology published in the journal Soviet Psychology over the fifteen years of its existence,
including a slightly edited text of the 1967 publication of Vygotsky’s “Play and its
role in the mental development of the child,” which became the third publication of
this paper over roughly a decade (Vygotsky, 1977).The volume The concept of activity
in Soviet psychology, in contrast, presented a set of articles previously unpublished in
English, particularly, three newly translated works by Vygotsky: “The instrumental
method in psychology,” “The genesis of higher mental functions,” and “The development of higher forms of attention in childhood,” all three translated on the basis
of the texts that came out in the Soviet volumes of 1956 and 1960. The whole volume was preceded by a preface written by Cole and an introductory chapter, “The
concept of activity in Soviet psychology,” authored by Wertsch. Quite characteristically, the whole set of papers was selected and presented in order to demonstrate
the existence of so-called ‘activity theory’, a phrase that actually never occurred
in Vygotsky’s original writings. Thus, Vygotsky’s three works, as well as his overall
scholarship, were recast from the perspective of ‘activity theory’, which was presented here as the most direct and only development of Vygotsky’s scholarship. In
his introductory chapter Wertsch clarified that
the volume focuses on what is probably the most important concept in Soviet
psychology – the concept of activity [deyatel’nost’]. It attempts to elucidate
the crucial features of one theoretical framework that has been built around
this concept by illustrating how each of these features is used in one or more
contributions selected for translation.
(Wertsch, 1981b, p. 6)
And yet, as in the mid-1960s when Jerome Bruner regretted that notwithstanding the recent publication of Vygotsky’s Thought and speech a ‘Vygotsky boom’
was not apparent, the 1970s did not bring about the large-scale change so much
desired by the “discriminating group” of American admirers of Vygotsky’s work.
A dramatic change in the popularity of Vygotsky only took place by the end of the

156

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

1970s. Two events were instrumental in this truly paradigmatic shift that, ultimately,
led to the ‘boom’ or, in other words, the cult of Vygotsky in the West.
The first event was the publication of yet another volume of Vygotsky’s works.
Generally, the beginning of the work on this publication dates back to the year of
1962–3 when Michael Cole pursued his postdoctoral research in Moscow with Luria,
who asked his American postdoc student to help him with the English-language
publication of the manuscripts of two of Vygotsky’s works. Cole enlisted the help of
his colleagues Vera John-Steiner and Sylvia Scribner, who were to help him with the
translation of Vygotsky’s and some of Luria’s works, including the latter’s autobiography that eventually came out as a book chapter and as an independent book in 1974
and 1979, respectively. As to Vygotsky’s texts, the work on these did not start until
the 1970s. This is how Cole described this project in his recently published memoirs:
I received the Vygotsky manuscripts from Luria in early 1970s. But even
with the expert help of able colleagues and a good translation to work from,
I could not convince the publisher, with whom Luria had made arrangements, that the manuscripts were worth publishing. All of the problems that
I had experienced earlier remained in place. The work seemed dated, the
polemics either opaque or outdated, and the overall product was certain to
produce fiscal disaster, not to say personal embarrassment. Faced with this
seemingly insurmountable barrier and with help from Luria, whom I visited
every year or two and with whom I corresponded regularly, we were able to
produce a reasonable selection of readings from the two manuscripts he had
given me.To these we added several essays of an applied nature so that readers
could see how the abstract theoretical argument played out in practice. The
result, entitled Mind in society, was published in 1978. I heaved a great sigh
of relief: I had finally discharged my obligation to Luria and the publisher,
thanks in good measure to the hard work of my colleagues.
(Cole, 2004, pp. x–xi)
In the Introduction, the editors provided some details about the nature of their
work on the book. According to the editors’ written statement, Luria provided
them with rough translations of two of Vygotsky’s works, the previously unpublished “Tool and symbol in children’s development” and “The history of the development of higher psychological functions” that came out in a Russian volume
of Vygotsky’s selected works in 1960. Then, the editors confessed that they “have
constructed the first four chapters of this volume from ‘Tool and Symbol’”; then
chapter 5 “was taken from section 3” of the other manuscript that they received
from Luria; chapters 6 and 8 were borrowed from Vygotsky’s posthumously published essays of different periods;17 and, finally, chapter 7 was based on a lecture on
play of 1933 that first came out in the Soviet Union in the memorial Vygotskian
issue of the Russian journal Voprosy Psikhologii, which, in fact, became the fourth
publication of this paper in English and made it the most frequently republished
text of Vygotsky’s to date.

Translating Vygotsky 157

Further, the editors – Michael Cole,Vera John-Steiner, Sylvia Scribner, and Ellen
Souberman – in their Introduction to the book confessed that while preparing the
materials for publication they took “significant liberties” with respect to Vygotsky’s
original text:
At several places we have inserted material from additional sources in order
to more fully explicate the meaning of the text. … In a few cases passages
have been taken from the work of Vygotsky’s students or collaborators which
provide concrete examples of experimental procedures or results which the
original text describes with extreme brevity. … The reader will encounter
here not a literal translation of Vygotsky but rather our edited translation of
Vygotsky, from which we have omitted material that seemed redundant and
to which we have added material that seemed to make the points clearer. …
Because proper references were rarely given [in Vygotsky’s original manuscripts], we have supplied our best guess as to the exact sources to which
Vygotsky referred. … We realize that in tampering with the original we may
have distorted history; however, we hope that by stating our procedures and
by adhering as closely as possible to the principles and content of the work,
we have not distorted Vygotsky’s meaning.
(Cole, John-Steiner, Scribner, & Souberman, 1978, p. x)
Thus, as an outcome of all this editorial work the book Mind in society came
out under Vygotsky’s name (Vygotsky, 1978) and, according to Cole’s memoirs of
2004, at the time of its publication it did not promise much of commercial success
or reputational gains.
The second inaugural event that largely contributed to the advent of the
long-awaited ‘Vygotsky boom’ was yet another publication. This was a review of
three books – Mind in society: The development of higher psychological processes (Harvard
University Press, 1978),Vygotsky’s The psychology of art (MIT Press, 1971), and Soviet
developmental psychology: An anthology (M. E. Sharpe, 1979) – by the well-known
British philosopher Stephen Toulmin for the prestigious New York Review of Books,
a magazine written by and for intellectuals (Toulmin, 1978). It is not altogether
surprising that Toulmin dedicated his review to two books that bore the name of
Vygotsky on their covers and another book that included a chapter by Vygotsky. In
earlier reviews for this magazine, published in 1968 and 1971, Toulmin had already
mentioned Lev Vygotsky and, for that matter, his closest associate and collaborator,
Alexander Luria (Toulmin, 1968, 1971). In a footnote to his article of 1978,Toulmin
specifically mentioned that he originally encountered Vygotsky’s and Luria’s work
through neurologist and neuropsychologist Norman Geschwind, a Harvard scholar
like two other Western admirers of Vygotsky, namely Roman Jakobson and Jerome
Bruner.18
But it was in this review of 1978 that Toulmin gave a fairly thorough, lengthy, and
most laudatory exposition of Vygotsky’s and Luria’s ideas and scientific legacy. By
his own admission, “the central figure of this story is Lev Semyonovich Vygotsky,”

158

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

who received from Toulmin the most honorable title of the “Mozart of psychology.”
The other protagonist of this story was Luria, a “Beethoven to Vygotsky’s Mozart”
and “his most distinguished co-worker … whose extraordinary range of interests
and abilities made him possibly the finest all-round psychologist of the century.”Yet,
according to Toulmin, “the wide-ranging intellectual possibilities pursued by Luria,
in his own less theoretical ways, from literature across the board to neurophysiology
by way of linguistics and educational innovation, had all been initially suggested in
discussions with Vygotsky and his associates during the years around 1930.”
The exposition of Vygotsky’s and Luria’s ideas gave Toulmin an opportunity to
reflect on the state of the art in American psychology and, more broadly, American
behavioral sciences. In his review, Toulmin took the position of an external observer and, from this position, he overviewed contemporary psychology in its North
American variation:
If we take the view from Moscow, American psychology appears excessively fragmented, as well as being divided along outdated ideological lines.
A vast amount of busy research has gone on in the US during the last fifty
years, in dozens of different branches of psychology, neurology, linguistics,
and educational innovation. But no common theoretical picture has been
developed capable of integrating all their results. The different branches have,
thus, also been separate branches. Given the positivist conceptions about “scientific method” dominant in American psychology, the behavioral sciences
have proliferated into dozens of highly specialized, and largely noninteracting,
subdisciplines: so behavioral scientists have organized their research on the
principle that the more narrowly and sharply defined a question can be, the
more “scientific” it is.
[Continued in Toulmin’s footnote 11:]Positivism has also impoverished the
practice of experimentation in American psychology. In physics and biology,
many striking experiments aim simply at checking out the qualitative effects
or relationships suggested by some novel theoretical analysis. Behavioral scientists in the United States rarely allow themselves the luxury of such revealing experiments. Instead, they see their empirical task as the pursuit of statistical correlations between the numerical values of “quantifiable” variables.
Toulmin continued his critique and at the same time hinted at a way out of the
lamentable situation in which he believed American psychology was. He suggested
that the solution is in the integrative human science:
Nobody in the American behavioral sciences has, it seems, the breadth of
experience or general standing needed in order to do the integrative thinking
typical of Luria or Vygotsky. Nobody in the West, for instance, experiments
and writes with equal authority – as Luria did – on such diverse topics as the
syndromes of aphasia, cross-cultural differences in reasoning patterns, intellectual development in identical twins, and the performances of calculating

Translating Vygotsky 159

prodigies. Few American psychologists, indeed, would even think it worth
trying to do so.
(Toulmin, 1978)
Toulmin claimed that much of Soviet psychology was of a “cultural-historical”
theoretical orientation and that this consistently socio-cultural and developmental
orientation was from the onset profoundly influenced by Marxian historical thinking, which enabled Soviet scholars to achieve the high level of interdisciplinary
collaboration and intellectual integration that their science demonstrated.19 Thus,
Marxism – dialectical and historical materialism – for Toulmin was primarily a
powerful philosophical movement of enormous potential for the development of
integrative human sciences that Vygotsky was particularly well aware of:
[L]eaving all political issues aside, the general frame provided by a “historical
materialist” philosophy gave him the basis he needed for developing an integrated account of the relations between developmental psychology and clinical neurology, cultural anthropology and the psychology of art – an account
that we in the West can afford to take very seriously today.
(Toulmin, 1978)
In conclusion, Toulmin cited the first editor of Vygotsky’s Psychology of art (1965),
the renowned linguist, Indo-Europeanist, and semiotician Vyacheslav Ivanov, who in
his concluding postscript to the volume, remarked that Vygotsky indicated the way
to unify biological and social studies and proposed that continuation of Vygotsky’s
line of research might be no less significant for science than the deciphering of the
genetic code.20
With these programmatic statements Toulmin concluded his review. It is hard to
evaluate the relative weight of each and every component of the whole that contributed to the ultimate success of Vygotskian psychology in North America, but
what is clear is that following the publication of Mind in society and Toulmin’s highly
favorable book review from the early 1980s Vygotsky’s popularity in the Western
world radically changed. One way or another, two translated, considerably abbreviated, and heavily edited texts, Thought and language and Mind in society – neither of
which he actually wrote in the form they were published in the United States in
1962 and 1978,21 respectively – gradually became Vygotsky’s most-cited publications and eventually turned into international bestsellers. Thus, these two events of
1978 – the release of Mind in society and the publication of Toulmin’s book review –
launched the ‘Vygotsky boom’ that Jerome Bruner already hopefully announced
in 1967.
The first wave of the boom of the early 1980s was instrumental in the launch of
the first major translated edition of Vygotsky’s collected writings – the six-volume
Collected works of L. S. Vygotsky published by Plenum Press (eventually sold to
Springer Publishers) from 1987 to 1999.This edition remains not just the largest, but
arguably the most questionable and unreliable edition of Vygotsky ever published

160

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

in English. The subsequent section discusses the problems of translating Vygotsky
and illustrates them using the materials abundantly supplied by these Collected works.

Typology of Vygotsky’s texts and sources of error
What we learn from the history of the ‘transnational’ and ‘exported Vygotsky’ is that
all the majority knows about this thinker and author is due to available translations
and that these translations and publication were all made in a specific context and
for specific purposes. As we are reminded by Pierre Bourdieu, whose words are
quoted at the beginning of this chapter, as far as translation and transfer of scholarly
works is concerned,
it is obvious that there will always be some sort of profit involved. Heretical
imports are often the work of marginals in the field, bringing a message, a
position of force from a different field, which they use to try and shore up
their own position. Foreign writers are often subject to such instrumental use,
and forced to serve purposes which they would perhaps refuse or reject in
their country of origin.
(Bourdieu, 1999, p. 223)
In other words, a certain amount of ‘intellectual violence’ and distortion of the
author’s original ideas – imported to the new social milieu and assimilated there –
seems to be the norm rather than an exception; it forms part and parcel of the whole
process of transnational and transcultural intellectual exchange. So, distortions of this
kind are related to the social positioning of ideas and should not be blamed too
much, although they must be carefully monitored. However, there is another danger
of intellectual distortion that takes place in the process of knowledge migration, and
it is most directly related to those errors and losses of translation that can hardly be
forgiven or legitimized.These we are going to discuss in some detail below. In what
follows we shall give a brief and incomplete characterization of the currently available translations of Vygotsky’s texts and indicate problems that remain to be solved.
We shall conclude with a proposal as to how to solve these problems – if not in the
presently available, already published texts, then in future translations.
It is important to realize that in translating a historical author there are multiple
sources of error and that in the case of Vygotsky the situation was aggravated for
