ideological or political reasons. The potential sources of error or distortion can be
illustrated as displayed in Table 7.1, which shows that there are multiple sources of
error. As one can see in Table 7.1, first, a publication may be based on an unpublished manuscript – either prepared for publication or unfinished, abandoned and
not considered as publishable by its author – or published articles, or a mixture of
both. Second, this publication may be republished one or more times – during the
author’s lifetime and under her authorization, or posthumously and in a state that
the author would hardly approve of had she survived until the date of publication.
Third, the text may be translated into another language. Last, a translation can serve

Translating Vygotsky 161
TABLE 7.1 Typology of Vygotsky’s texts and translations

Unpublished

Published by Vygotsky

Published posthumously
Translated
Retranslated

Manuscripts, personal notes, paper drafts, future plans,
unfinished manuscripts, typewritten stenograms of
lectures and public oral presentations, students’ notes,
etc.
Scholarly books and articles, book (and theatrical) reviews,
conference proceedings, undergraduate textbooks and
teaching materials, administrative publications, etc.
Edited volumes of collected works, journal publications
published in the USSR from mid-1930s to 1980s
Translations into English
‘Second-degree’ translations

as the source for yet a further translation. All these transitions from one form to
another are potential sources of error. In case something goes wrong in the transition from manuscript to book we are mostly helpless, because manuscripts may no
longer exist or be accessible. Also, it will be difficult to assess whether changes in the
text were part of the normal editorial process or introduced without the consent of
the author. However, when books are based on earlier publications we have in principle a possibility of judging the reliability of the book text. That is, although the
transition from published article to a book volume forms a potential source of error,
it at the same time gives us a means to check the reliability of the final product.
Thus, our ability to verify and potentially correct a translated or republished
text depends largely on the availability of original texts. Some of those have
relatively recently been republished in the original and undistorted form, that is,
allegedly exactly the way they were released – or were planned to be released –
by the author (Vygotskii, 1999, 2008). Even more importantly, the miracle of
the twenty-first century, the contemporary technologies of the Internet and
the so-called Web 2.0, have revolutionized the process of information distribution and scientific publication, and quite a few of the original Vygotsky texts,
including the scans of original publications and facsimile editions of the interwar period (for instance, the original edition of Thinking and speech of 1934
as well as other books and various journal publications of the 1920s–30s), are
nowadays freely available for downloading. We know about several specialized
Russian and international websites with enormous repositories of information
and are confident in claiming that this is a rapidly and steadily growing trend in
twenty-first-century publishing.

Types of error
To illustrate what was said above, a number of examples of errors in the English
translations of Vygotsky’s texts will now be given. These errors were detected as
early as the 1980s and 1990s and were revealed by a number of researchers using

162

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

the means indicated above, that is, comparing publications with the original articles, manuscripts, or books wherever that proved possible. These authors include
Brushlinskii (1996), Etkind (1993), Tkachenko (1983), Tulviste (1987), Mecacci
(1990), Van der Veer (1987, 1992, 1997, 1998), and Van der Veer and Valsiner
(1991). Access to the previously published texts and the ‘archival revolution’ in
Vygotskian studies is a precondition of any progress in this respect (Yasnitsky,
2010a). Fortunately, many exciting new opportunities opened up after the series of recent publications uncovering Vygotsky’s private archives (Zavershneva,
2010a, 2010b, 2010c, 2012a, 2013). Thus, we have most recently witnessed a new
wave of critical studies devoted to the textological analysis of Vygotsky’s publications (Gillen, 2000; Keiler, 2012; Kellogg, 2011a, 2011b; Kellogg & Yasnitsky,
2011; Mecacci & Yasnitsky, 2011; R. Miller, 2011; Yasnitsky, 2011b, 2011d;
Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b) that reveal quite a number of distortions
in the Russian texts of the posthumous Soviet editions of Vygotsky. Many of the
inadequacies of the English translations go back to inadequacies in these Soviet
editions, but translators inevitably added their own share. For clarity’s sake, these
errors have been divided into a number of subcategories, such as:
(1)
(2)
(3)
(4)
(5)
(6)

inaccuracies
suppression of terms or passages
suppression of names
unidentified or suppressed citations
insertions
multiple retranslations.

Inaccuracies
Under the heading of ‘inaccuracies’ are included all those changes of the original
texts that were unintended or intended with the idea of ‘clarifying’Vygotsky’s ideas
or making him more palatable to contemporary taste. As such they differ from deliberate attempts to falsify the texts as discussed below, although the difference is sometimes small (e.g. the case of Kolbanovskii).As examples of inaccurate efforts to present
Vygotsky’s texts, we would first like to mention the abridged versions of Thinking
and speech (Vygotsky, 1962, 1986) and the compilation of texts in Vygotsky 1978.
That Vygotsky 1962 is unacceptable as a translation has been observed by many
and hardly needs arguing. Suffice it to say that the whole book contains a meager 168 pages as compared to the 324 pages of the original. However, as has been
argued before (Van der Veer, 1987),Vygotsky 1986 is also unacceptable. As a whole,
the 1986 translation of Thinking and speech is incomparably better and much more
complete than the 1962 edition, yet the translator has “departed from Vygotsky’s
text when it repeats itself ” and retained large parts of the 1962 translation.The result
is that the book still contains many errors and that various phrases and passages
have not been translated. Vygotsky’s quotation on the final page of his book from
The German ideology, for example, has been left unidentified and is rendered very

Translating Vygotsky 163

incomplete (Van der Veer, 1987). Thus, we argue that both translations (Vygotsky,
1962, 1986) are abridged and as such unfit for genuine scientific study.
We know of only one edition that qualifies as a compilation and that is the previously mentioned Mind in society (Vygotsky, 1978). This book has enjoyed immense
popularity among psychologists interested in cultural-historical theory and it served
a very positive role in introducing American readers to Vygotsky’s thinking. However,
from a historical point of view, it is a very unsatisfactory book. Above we mentioned that the editors “summarized” major theoretical points and “inserted material
from additional sources.” However, on the basis of the editors’ explanation, it proved
impossible to identify the historical texts that lie behind the final result. The editors
merged several texts by Vygotsky and his collaborators into a coherent and readable
whole but failed to do this in a transparent and reproducible way. To consider the
result as Vygotsky’s text would be incorrect. Mind in society thus violates the principle
that states that texts of historical authors should be translated in their entirety or, if
only excerpts are published, that it should be clearly indicated which parts are left
out, where the author’s text ends and the text of others begins, and so on.
Under the heading of inaccuracies, we would further like to discuss the need for
accurate annotations of the translated texts. Here there is no shortage of examples.
The Plenum edition, for example, with the exception of volume 3, is very poorly
annotated. Authors are left unidentified, citations are not traced to the original
source, scientific terms that have gone out fashion are not explained, etc. Of course,
authoritative translations of historical texts become much more useful if they are
properly annotated. That the writing of such notes requires a considerable amount
of work and historical knowledge should not prevent us from trying to reach the
level of work reached in, say, the Standard Edition of Freud’s writings.
Finally, added to the abridged versions, compilations, and inadequate annotations, there are simple translation errors of words, passages, and names.
The fact that Russian often renders foreign names phonetically, as they are
pronounced (by Russians), forms another source of errors. An incomplete list of
inadequately transliterated names is shown in Table 7.2. Of course, even a cursory
consultation of historical psychology books, encyclopedias, and so on, would have
prevented these ludicrous mistakes.
The abridged versions, compilations, and inaccurate translations mentioned
above were made with the best of intentions. However, many of the existing English
translations are based on Soviet editions of Vygotsky’s works that are unreliable for
very different reasons.

Suppression of terms or passages
The problem is that all of Vygotsky’s works were first published in the Soviet
Union, where articles and books were subjected to censorship as a matter of routine. The early and later editors of Vygotsky’s books were all acutely aware of this
system and in their efforts to get Vygotsky’s books published they often preventively
adjusted his texts.

164

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

TABLE 7.2 Examples of inaccurate names in the Plenum edition

given

correct

Charceau
Faininger
Fasler
Gettser
Kompeire
Ronget
Shvabskii
Zelts
Zigvart

Charcot
Vaihinger
Vossler
Hetzer
Compayré
Ronjat
Von Schwaben
Selz
Sigwart

In this respect, the first editor of Thinking and speech (1934a), Kolbanovskii, was
probably no exception. Elsewhere (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, pp. 256–258), it
has been argued that Kolbanovskii may have erased all references to the discipline
of pedology, which by that time had come under attack. Thus Kolbanovskii, who
edited the whole book series, persuaded Vygotsky to give his book the subtitle
Psychological investigations and probably replaced the terms ‘school pedology’ and
‘pedological’ by, respectively, ‘child psychology’ and ‘psychological’ in chapter 6. In
later Soviet editions the subtitle was lost.
In the 1956 and 1982 Soviet editions of Thinking and speech we can also witness the
suppression of unwelcome passages (Mecacci & Yasnitsky, 2011). Thus, in chapter 1
a lengthy reference to and a (not entirely exact) quotation from Sapir’s Language
(Vygotskii, 1934a, p. 12) have been omitted (they should appear on p. 49 of vol. 1):
In the plane of instinctive consciousness, where perception and affect reign,
only contamination is possible, but not understanding and not communication in the proper sense of this word. E. Sapir beautifully explained this in his
works on the psychology of speech. “Elementary language,” he says, “must be
connected with a whole group, with a specific class of our experience. The
world of experience must be extremely simplified and generalized in order to
be symbolized. Only in this way communication becomes possible for singular experience lives in a singular consciousness and cannot strictly speaking be
communicated. In order to become communicable it must be put in a certain
class which by tacit consensus is considered as a unit by the society.” That is
why Sapir considers word meaning not as a symbol of singular perception but
as a symbol of a concept.
We can only speculate why this passage was removed. Perhaps it was an attempt to
make Vygotsky seem more original in the field of linguistics or, alternatively, positive mention of the name of the ‘bourgeois’ or, in Soviet newspeak, ‘reactionary’ (i.e.
as opposed to ‘progressive’) researcher Sapir had become unacceptable.

Translating Vygotsky 165

In chapter 2, on the very first page of Vygotsky’s discussion of Piaget’s theory, a
quite substantial passage containing a citation from Piaget has also been removed.
The quotation from Piaget was as follows:
We thus believe that the day will come when the thinking of the child with
respect to the adult, normal and civilized thinking, will be placed at the same
level as the “primitive mentality” defined by Lévy-Bruhl, as the autistic and
symbolic thinking described by Freud and his followers, and as the “morbid
consciousness”, assuming that this concept that we owe to Blondel will not
one day merge with the preceding one.
(translation by the present authors from the 1924
French edition of Piaget’s Judgement and reasoning in the child)
Vygotsky then continued in a passage approximately twice as long as the quotation
from Piaget by saying that the significance of Piaget’s books is indeed of the same
order as those of Blondel, Lévy-Bruhl, and Freud, and that these authors are by
their philosophical nature intimately connected. (The whole passage including the
extract from Piaget should be inserted on p. 53 of volume 1 of the Plenum edition,
after “this old problem.”)
Finally, in chapter 5 of Thinking and speech, a passage has been removed that ran
as follows:
[A]nd the daily change in different forms of behavior, as has been beautifully
pointed out by P. P. Blonsky, essentially repeats the millennia-old history of
the development of behavior
This passage was most probably removed because it suggested that Vygotsky agreed
with some version of the recapitulation thesis, a thesis that had become unacceptable in the Soviet Union by then. (It should be inserted in paragraph 18, p. 160 of
volume 1 of the Plenum edition, after “the most ancient.”)
This is by no means a complete list of passages that were removed in later Soviet
editions but it suffices to make the point that translations based upon them, such as
Rieber & Carton (1987), are highly unreliable.
However, the problem of suppressed terms or passages is not restricted to
Thinking and speech. Thus, in volume 3 of the Plenum edition, on the penultimate
page of The historical meaning of the crisis in psychology: A methodological investigation
(p. 342), it is said that “The new society will create the new man.” This seems a
rather plausible sentence, but Tkachenko (1983) has convincingly argued that the
original manuscript of The crisis contained the phrase “The new society will create
a superman” (see also Zavershneva, 2012b; Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b).
The term ‘superman’ has, of course, acquired a rather negative connotation since
the Nazi period, which may have been the reason why Soviet editors replaced this
term (but see below).

166

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

Volume 2 of the Plenum edition (Rieber & Carton, 1993) is not free from
problems either. It has been shown, using the original chapter from 1928, that the
chapter “The dynamics of child character” originally contained two positive pages
and three positive passages about Alfred Adler that have been removed in subsequent
Soviet editions of his work (Brushlinskii, 1996). The passages were as follows:
Adler’s theory is often connected – especially in circles of German and
Austrian social-democrats – with Marx’s theory [to be inserted on p. 162 of
volume 2 of the Plenum edition].
A. Adler’s theory, in particular his theory about character is “a truly revolutionary characterological current”, as A. Zalkind rightly points out [to be
inserted in paragraph 2 of the same chapter].
None of the contemporary psychological ideas has such enormous significance for pedagogues, for the theory and practice of education, as this idea of
Adler [to be inserted in paragraph 5 of the same chapter].
These passages show Vygotsky referring positively to Adler’s ideas, which was to
be expected in that period of his career. Elsewhere (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991)
it has been shown that Vygotsky went through a period of deep enthusiasm for
Adlerian theory. Again, we can only guess why these passages were removed. The
fact that Adler was a ‘bourgeois’, the fact that he was a former psychoanalytic theorist, the fact that ‘social democrats’ linked his work to Marx, the fact that Vygotsky
called his theory ‘revolutionary’ (whereas only Russian Marxist ideas could be truly
revolutionary), and an attempt to make Vygotsky seem more original, may all have
played a role (cf.Van der Veer, 2000a).
Vygotsky’s Educational psychology (1997a) is likewise marred by suppressed passages. Elsewhere, it has been shown that, among other things, three lines of a Blonsky
quotation on p. 4, twenty-three lines of a Sherrington quotation on p. 35, and sixty
lines on p. 335 were omitted in the English translation (Van der Veer, 1998).
Again, this list of suppressed terms and passages is far from exhaustive but
hopefully the point has been made. English translations of Soviet re-editions of
Vygotsky’s work are liable to suppressions of substantial passages of the original text.
None of the existing Soviet post-World War II editions can thus play a role in the
serious scientific study of Vygotsky’s writings.

Suppression of names
It will come as no surprise that author names as well have disappeared in Soviet
republications of Vygotsky’s work and its translations into English. Kolbanovskii
may have been the first to use this method: In chapter 5 of the 1934 edition of
Thinking and speech the German psychologist Felix Krueger (cf. Valsiner & Van der
Veer, 2000) has become “one of the contemporary authors.” But there are many
more examples. In the above-mentioned chapter,“The dynamics of child character,”

Translating Vygotsky 167

Adler’s name was deleted various times, as were the names of Lévy-Bruhl, Blondel,
and Freud in the second chapter of later editions of Thinking and speech. In volume
3 of the Plenum edition, chapter 3 ends with a footnote in which Vygotsky claims
that Watson and Lashley had arrived at similar conclusions to him.This footnote was
missing in previous Soviet editions and was reintroduced by the translator. Finally,
a most interesting suppression of a name occurs in that same volume 3. On p. 120,
Vygotsky mentions “several of Freud’s critics who are inclined to …” However, it
has been discovered that the original text from 1930 ran “several of Freud’s critics
(such as Voloshinov) who are inclined to …” This omission is theoretically most
interesting because Voloshinov belonged to the circle of Bakhtin, and theorists have
debated the issue as to whether Vygotsky and Bakhtin knew of each other’s work.

Unidentiﬁed or suppressed citations
We have mentioned above a lengthy passage from Sapir’s Language that was deleted
from Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech. This was a clear case of a citation that was
removed. It has been claimed that Vygotsky’s The psychology of art contained several citations from Trotsky that have been removed as well. Other citations in other
books have become hard to identify because the quotation marks were removed.
We have already mentioned the quotation from The German ideology on the final
page of Thinking and speech. Etkind (1993) has pointed out that Vygotsky ended his
Educational psychology with a long citation from Trotsky. In effect, the entire last page
of his book and part of the penultimate page, that is, pp. 350 and 351 from Vygotsky
(1997a), starting from “Alongside technology,” were borrowed from Trotsky.
Likewise, the final page of The crisis contains a quotation from Trotsky. That implies
that the last four lines on p. 342 of volume 3 of the Plenum edition are Trotsky’s and
that the aforementioned reference to a “superman” was actually Trotsky’s (cf. Rieber
& Wollock, 1997). Recent publications by Zavershneva have confirmed these suspicions (Zavershneva, 2012; Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b).
It is most probable that many more of these hidden citations will be identified in
the future. Again, in many cases one can only guess as to the reasons for removing
the quotation marks or the citations as a whole. The case of Trotsky is simple: political reasons prohibited mentioning his name or quoting his writings, but other cases
are less obvious. One wonders whether editors simply removed quotation marks
because they were unable to identify the author of the citations. That would be one
way to ‘solve’ a time-consuming historical problem.

Insertions
That Soviet or English editors would make cuts in a historical text is deplorable but
to some degree expectable.That they would go as far as to introduce text fragments
(‘foreign bodies’) into Vygotsky’s text seems unlikely.Yet this is precisely what happened on several occasions.

168

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

Relatively inoffensive minor insertions can be seen in Vygotsky (1997a). The
editors or translator have changed, for example, “Lange” into “the Danish psychologist Lange” and “Blonskii” into “Pavel Petrovich Blonskii, the Soviet psychologist and educational reformer.” Such insertions are deplorable, not only because
they introduce an additional source of error (e.g. Lange was a physiologist and not
a psychologist) and confusion (Vygotsky would never have referred to his colleague Blonskii in this way) but also because it becomes impossible to discern where
Vygotsky ends and the editor or translator begins. As has been argued elsewhere
(Van der Veer, 1997), in the restoration of old paintings it has become customary to
ensure that any changes are recognizable as such and are reversible. In dealing with
historical texts we should follow that principle as well.
Simply outrageous insertions have been documented for the above-mentioned
chapter “The dynamics of child character.” This chapter, which was originally published in a 1928 book, not only suffers from numerous suppressions but contains
insertions as well. For example, two text fragments from the same 1928 book, but
authored by Zalkind, have been inserted into Vygotsky’s text. In the Plenum edition
these passages run as follows:
Kretschmer’s scheme does not work for the division of characterological traits
by age. None of this, however, prohibits us from attempting to elucidate the
prevailing predominant specific content of each stage in development. This
specific content, not now taken into consideration by any of the existing
characterological systems, undergoes extraordinary changes under environmental influences. This is why it is dangerous to attach rigid ‘labels’ to any
systems in the given state of science [to be removed from p. 154 of volume 3 of
the Plenum edition].
Adler’s basic philosophical positions are distorted by metaphysical elements.
The characterological interest is limited to Adler’s practice [to be removed
from p. 156 of volume 3 of the Plenum edition].
These insertions hardly need comment. Apparently, anonymous editors found it
necessary to ‘clarify’ the right position as to Adler’s work. That one had to ‘enrich’
Vygotsky’s text to achieve that goal and thus was effectively rewriting history mattered little. The whole process of deleting and inserting passages in psychological
texts is reminiscent of the well-known Soviet practices of removing a persona non
grata (e.g.Trotsky) from repeatedly published photographs and of adding an imaginary glorious military past to the biography of several of its rulers.

Multiple retranslations
It can be argued that providing a definitive translation of an original source is
impossible and that each translation is an act of re-creation of the original text using
the language and concepts available at the time of translating. For this reason the

Translating Vygotsky 169

novels belonging to the canon of the world literature must be translated anew every
twenty or thirty years. After such a period the old translation seems hopelessly obsolete. In a sense, then, translating a text is like remembering an event: the historical
event certainly took place as a concrete reality but is each time reconstructed and
related differently, depending on the condition, mood, and age of the protagonist.
But if the translation of original sources is complex, this certainly holds even more
for translating translations. For this reason the authors share a firm belief in the commandment: thou shalt not translate unless from the original. And yet, we are aware
of several instances of published translations of Vygotsky’s texts that were not made
from the original Russian. Unlike ‘first-degree murder’, which is a premeditated
and deliberate crime, such ‘second-degree translation’ is relatively innocent and
sometimes involuntary, but equally a profound and unforgivable destruction of the
original meaning of the text, fully grounded in the translator’s interpretation of what
another translator believed the original text might have meant. For the sake of illustration, let us consider the most outrageous and at the same time the most hilarious
example of a ‘second-degree translation’ that the history of the alleged Vygotskian
legacy has to offer to date.
Briefly, the story goes like this:
Sometime around 1930, Vygotsky and Luria produced the Russian manuscript
Orudie i znak (half a dozen variations of the title are known) that was to come out
in English translation as Tool and sign in a volume on developmental psychology in
the United States. For some reason this chapter was not included in the book, and
we know nothing about it until the late 1960s/early 1970s, when it re-emerged
from behind the Iron Curtain as a typewritten English text that Luria handed over
to his former postdoc student Michael Cole for publication in the West. Reluctant
to publish the text ‘as is’, Cole and his colleagues made some fragmentary use of it
in the process of creating their cut-and-paste version of Vygotsky that subsequently
came out between the covers of Mind in society in 1978 (see above). In 1984, an
apparently complete version of this text came out in Russian in the six-volume
collection of Vygotsky’s works (Vygotskii, 1984). Subsequently, in 1994, Luria’s
English typescript was fully published as a chapter in The Vygotsky reader (1994)
that came out under the editorship of Van der Veer and Valsiner, who were the
first to compare the Russian version of the text of 1984 and Luria’s typescript. The
comparison revealed an extremely curious feature of the Russian version: first, it
missed several important pieces (such as illustrations) but was nevertheless longer,
and, second, it contained a number of non-verbatim – but semantically identical –
repetitions of text fragments! The editors referred to rumors that the Russian text,
as strange as it might look, was in fact translated from English, which still hardly
explained the repetitions. No plausible rational explanation was offered at that time,
and the whole issue remained a mystery until quite recently.
In 2005, a former student of Alexander Luria’s and a prominent neuroscientist
and medical doctor, Elkhonon Goldberg, published The wisdom paradox: How your
mind can grow stronger as your brain grows older, in which he confessed to having participated in what he referred to as a “benign forgery” of Vygotsky’s text (Goldberg,

170

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

2005). According to Goldberg, it was in the late 1960s that Luria summoned him,
when still a university student, and invited him to participate in the preparation of
the collected works of Vygotsky. He was told that the Russian manuscript of Tool
and sign was not preserved – or had not yet been discovered in the archives – and,
to Goldberg’s astonishment, Luria invited him to make a Russian translation from
the English translation. Apparently, this English translation survived and was subsequently handed over by Luria to Cole, and then, by the latter, to Van der Veer and
Valsiner. So, this account by Goldberg confirmed the old rumors that the 1984
Russian text was based on an English manuscript. However, it could not explain
the curious repetitions of text fragments in slightly different wordings. To explain
that phenomenon one has to know that there actually was a second translator who
partially translated the same passages. We do not yet know with certainty who that
person was but we now understand the repetitions: the work of two (or more)
English–Russian translators was uncritically and very unprofessionally inserted in
different places in the Russian version of the text. Responsible for this tinkering
with a historical text was the editor and censor of the volume, the renowned (some
would say, notorious) Soviet psychologist and historian of psychology, Mikhail
Yaroshevskii (see discussion in a series of thematic publications by Cole, 2012;
Goldberg, 2012;Van der Veer, 2012;Yasnitsky, 2012f).
And yet, the most entertaining and outrageously hilarious – or hilariously outrageous – part of the story, its coda, is still waiting for us. Thus, in 1999, the last
volume that concluded the six-volume collection of Vygotsky’s works in English
was released. This very volume contained the text Tool and sign in the development of
the child, the ‘third-degree translation’ into English of Vygotsky’s and Luria’s work
(see Table 7.3).
In sum, the presumably lost original Russian text was translated into English,
then re-translated into Russian, and then again translated into English, which means
that no fewer than four versions of the same text may exist. For illustrative purposes,
a fragment of this text was previously discussed in one of our earlier studies and
analysed for Russian–English parallels and inconsistencies (Yasnitsky, 2011b, 2011d,
2012g). What we offer here is the comparison of these parallel places in different
versions of the English texts, that is, in ‘first-’ and ‘third-degree translations’ published in 1994 and 1999, respectively (see Table 7.4).
An in-depth comparative analysis of semantic and stylistic characteristics of the
three versions of seemingly the same – but not literally the same – text is beyond
the scope of this discussion. Readers are invited to enjoy the English–English
comparison, and experienced translators and bilingual specialists are welcome to
further explore the similarities and discrepancies in these three variations (two
in English and one in Russian) of Vygotsky’s and Luria’s work. For our present
discussion, though, it suffices to note that despite quite remarkable similarities
between the two variants of the edition of 1999 (see second and third columns of
Table 7.3) – which we attribute to the translator’s (Marie J. Hall) possible familiarity with Van der Veer and Valsiner’s publication of 1994 and their commentaries
on the text – several discrepancies are still quite notable and appear to contribute

to the aura of a systematically “misunderstood author” that is not infrequently
discussed by Vygotsky’s numerous admirers and rare critics.Thus, to give just a few
examples, we would like to point out that “symbolic significance” is not exactly
“sign meaning” and “structural interaction” is not identical with “structural interrelation” (sentence 4); equating a floor with a mirror is quite different from comparing it with a tumbler (sentence 5); similarly, the claims that “a table would
burn,” “one can’t turn on a table,” and “a table cannot be lit” are as different as
they are confusing (sentence 6). Special mention deserves to be made of sentence
2, in which a substantial terminological mix-up takes place. Thus, according to
two versions of the text, a stimulus may be a sign of an object (or a word). In contrast,
the third version suggests that it is a word that might serve as the object’s sign. The

Translating Vygotsky 173

differences between the two ideas might not be considered as crucial by some,
yet they are quite important for our understanding of the author’s idea, especially
so since the phrase in question addresses the most essential issues of Vygotsky’s
theory of sign and meaning. What is even more problematic is the fact that this
confusion between the four basic terms – word, sign, stimulus, and physical object
(“thing”) – occurs in the non-verbatim repetitions of the same text fragment created by the two translators of the text and inserted by the editor in chapters one
and four: these we can see in the publication of 1999 on pages 9 and 52, respectively. This presents an instance of a contradiction and inconsistency between different parts of the same work of Vygotsky, as we know it in the version of the
Plenum edition of 1999.

Conclusion: the “Six Commandments” for the translator
As a summary of the above argument and in order to give an answer to the question
“What needs to be done and how?” (cf.Van der Veer & Yasnitsky, 2011), we would
like to propose guidelines that might be of help to future translators of Vygotsky’s
or, for that matter, other international authors’ works, and not necessarily from
Russian. Thus, translating Vygotsky: how?
(1) Know thy bias.
All people believe in something: in Marxism, Communism or socialism; in feminism, social justice, or equality; in family values, careers, and profit; in holism, transnationalism, or integrative science; in cognitivism, behaviorism, or Gestaltism; in
tradition, culture, or anti-globalism. Know what you believe in. Realize the limits of
your expertise and the extent of your understanding.
Beware of your bias.
(2) Honor thy author.
Under no circumstance can you manipulate the original. The author’s word and
thought is sacred.
Use the most reliable source.
(3) Thou shalt not testify falsely.
Tell people exactly what you did: translated, interpreted, or compiled.
Be transparent. If you make changes, additions or insertions, clearly and unambiguously indicate these.
Leave the reader in no doubt as to where the author’s words end and additions
start. Act like the modern restorer of paintings, who makes sure her additions are
both visible and reversible.

174

René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

(4) Thou shalt learn and educate.
Do not assume that you know everything and the reader is perfectly familiar with the
context: study the topic, understand it thoroughly, and provide generous comments.
Long live footnotes and square brackets!
(5) Thou shalt not translate unless from the original.
Amen.
(6) Liberate thy mind.
Be creative and use thy cerebral hemispheres.
The twenty-first century is the era of new technology: remember that.
Bear in mind the exciting new futuristic opportunities such as e-books (or
e-translation in general): imagine a translation where you can, with simple clicks,
see the original ‘behind’ the translation as a sort of shadow, see elucidating footnotes, see suppressed or added words, see other renderings of the same text, etc., etc.
Colors can, of course, be freely used as well; notes in the margin, clickable references
to other texts. World Wide Web rules! All these possibilities far surpass the limitations of old-fashioned typography, of course.
Be bold, and the future will come today!

8
DID UZBEKS HAVE ILLUSIONS?
The Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932
Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

Alexander Luria: “Uzbeks have no illusions!”
There is a story well known to those psychologists who were educated in the Soviet
tradition and fairly unfamiliar to foreign scholars. According to this story, Alexander
Romanovich Luria organized an ethno-psychological expedition to Central Asia in
the early 1930s. Luria and his research team studied the psychological characteristics of the local population in the context of the rapid and massive socio-economic
changes caused by a ‘cultural revolution’ and the modernization of traditional agriculture. According to Luria and Vygotsky, sensational results were obtained that
confirmed their belief in the social origin of psychological processes. In particular,
a number of experiments supposedly showed that more educated subjects more
often demonstrated abstract thinking based on logical and categorical generalization, whereas the thinking of uneducated subjects was largely tied to concrete situations and personal experiences. This is quite understandable, though, and hardly
counts as an unexpected or counterintuitive finding.
However, in addition to research on thinking, Luria’s research team in Central Asia
also conducted studies of perception.The experiments on optical illusions produced
particularly amazing results: uneducated and ‘uncultured’ subjects – unlike socially
active subjects with at least some formal education – hardly succumbed to optical
illusions. This discovery provoked a telegram that a triumphant Luria allegedly sent
to Vygotsky in Moscow. The legendary telegram literally stated: “Uzbeks have no
illusions!”
It is curious that there is no documentary evidence of any telegram of the kind
whatsoever, and the plausibility of this episode remains highly questionable, especially given that presumably all known correspondence between Vygotsky and
Luria from that period has been published (Vygotsky, 2007). Nevertheless, the story
entered the golden fund of Russian historiography of psychology and is traditionally

176

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

presented in Russia and, to a notably smaller extent, Eastern Europe, as one of the
classic formulations of the essence of the Vygotsky–Luria cultural-historical theory.
This anecdote, however, does not end here. The classic narrative holds that subsequently Luria was heavily criticized for the political mistakes of his research in
Central Asia, which determined the fate of the publication of his ‘sensational’ discoveries: for more than four decades the findings were “brutally suppressed” until
they were produced from Luria’s archive and finally published in the early 1970s.
By then, Luria had already become a living classic and the unquestionable authority
in Soviet psychology.
Thus, the results of these expeditions, according to conventional wisdom, can be
summed up in two statements:
(1) “Uzbeks have no illusions!”
(2) For political reasons, publication was suppressed until the early 1970s.
However, it seems that neither of these statements is exactly true. It is a common view among Russian psychologists that in the early 1930s Luria undertook
an expedition to Central Asia in order to carry out psychological studies. Yet, it is
far less known that there were actually two expeditions, in 1931 and 1932, and that
a scholar of international standing in the field of the psychology of perception,
Kurt Koffka (1886–1941) – a German-American psychologist, one of the leaders of
Gestalt psychology – participated in the second expedition. Even less known is the
fact that a few announcements of the anticipated expedition and brief reports with
preliminary findings of both of these expeditions were published in three languages
in the first half of 1930. Furthermore, it is in one of these publications that Koffka’s
conclusions were publicly and openly presented. According to Koffka, all groups of
native Uzbeks, quite in contrast to Luria’s optimism, were found beyond reasonable
doubt to have optical illusions!

The historiography of the problem
Italian researcher Luciano Mecacci may have been the first researcher to draw attention to the inconsistency between Luria’s published story about the Central Asian
expeditions (cf., e.g., A. R. Luria, 1974, 1976) and Koffka’s account of these events.
This problem was discovered in the mid-1970s during the preparation of a reader
on the history of Soviet psychology of 1917–36, in Italian (Mecacci, 1976). The
discovery of Luria’s English-language publication of 1934 in the Journal of Genetic
Psychology turned out to be quite a disquieting – if not a disturbing – experience. This paper, characteristically titled “The second psychological expedition to
Central Asia,” contained a fragment with a brief report on optical illusion experiments, by Koffka. Although his name was mentioned in a footnote provided by
the translators of Luria’s Russian article, who received Koffka’s contribution directly, his co-authorship of the whole article was not acknowledged (A. R. Luria,

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 177

1934). Mecacci sought comments from Koffka’s correspondent and associate, Molly
Harrower, who kindly provided him with a copy of Koffka’s letters with a more
elaborate, albeit still incomplete, account of the expedition that Koffka sent to
Harrower on September 20, 1934. Also, Harrower pointed Mecacci to the collections of the Archives on the History of American Psychology (AHAP, University
of Akron, OH), where he obtained copies of the available travel notes, research
protocols, and other archival documents – unpublished as they were at that time
(Harrower, 1971). Many of these still unpublished documents were eventually published in Harrower’s book, in which its author conducted a meticulous study of the
manuscript materials that she published along with her extensive correspondence
with Koffka and her personal memories (Harrower, 1983).
Then, in 1975, Mecacci contacted Luria to ask for a comment on this episode. In
his response, Luria confirmed Koffka’s place in the expedition and told Mecacci that
Koffka had indeed participated in that expedition with the goal of confirming the
universality of the laws of perception in different cultures. In other words, Luria in
fact accused Koffka of bias and partisanship that seemingly undermined the scholarly conclusions that he reached in the course of his studies in Central Asia. Besides,
according to Luria, Koffka felt very uncomfortable with Central Asian culture and
communist society and developed psychosomatic symptoms (such as high fever)
and, for these reasons, had to abandon the expedition quite soon. Some of these
archival materials were published with Mecacci’s introduction in the Italian journal
Storia e critica della psicologia (Mecacci, 1980). Mecacci did not specify whether Luria
provided any explanations of the discrepancies between his interpretation of the
studies on optical illusions and that of Koffka as it was presented in the publication
of 1934.
Also in 1980, another pioneering publication of much interest was released.
This was a paper in a special issue of the journal Psychological Research (formerly
Psychologische Forschung, the militant journal of Gestalt psychologists) dedicated to
the memory of Luria. Eckart Scheerer, the journal’s editor, published an article titled
“Gestalt psychology in the Soviet Union” (1980), in which he made a first attempt
to systematically analyze the relationships between Soviet psychologists and the
representatives of the Gestalt psychology movement. In the context of the present
discussion, of particular interest is Scheerer’s statement that Koffka participated in
the expedition of 1932, but – having collected some experimental data – fell ill and
had to quit and return home. Scheerer pointed out that Koffka did not publish the
complete results of his studies of the optical illusions in Central Asia; however, he
acknowledged the partial publication of Koffka’s main conclusions within Luria’s
English article of 1934 and referred to the archival collections of the AHAP at the
University of Akron. The author also observed that in neither version of Luria’s
book, which appeared in two languages – first in Russian, then in English – with
slight, but notable differences (A. R. Luria, 1974, 1976), was Koffka’s participation in
the expedition mentioned. Scheerer completed his discussion of this episode with a
remark that the history of Koffka’s participation in Luria’s Central Asian expedition
required further investigation (Scheerer, 1980, p. 117).

178

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

The next significant attempt to discuss the results of the transnational
Luria–Koffka study was made in the classic and programmatic book by René
van der Veer and Jaan Valsiner, which remains the best biography of Vygotsky to
the present day. The authors devoted an entire chapter to the discussion of the
ups and downs of the Central Asian expeditions (Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991,
pp. 242–255). Using existing published documents (Harrower, 1983) and various
little-known or even unpublished materials – such as Luria’s correspondence with
Vygotsky and Wolfgang Köhler – the authors managed to present a quite detailed
outline of the story of Luria’s two expeditions to Central Asia. Most importantly,
they also discussed Luria’s and Koffka’s conflicting interpretations of the studies of
perception and optical illusions. In this context,Van der Veer and Valsiner wrote:
We thus see that Koffka on the basis of his data flatly denied Luria’s initial
conclusions: the “primitive” Uzbeks did succumb to optical illusions like the
Poggendorf illusion and were able to see perspective in drawings. It is rather
unfortunate – quite apart from the value of both Luria’s and Koffka’s conclusions about the cultural and physiological basis of illusions – that Luria (1974;
1976) ignored these conflicting interpretations in his monograph. Discussion
of Koffka’s failure to replicate his initial results might have started an interesting debate on the various ways of conducting this type of ethnographic
research – Koffka’s remark about the role of the subjects’ attitude toward the
experimenter, for example, was quite relevant … – and would have countered
the effects of the beautiful but oversimplified story about the Uzbeks not having illusions and the like.
(Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, p. 250)
Unfortunately, in their interpretation of the probable reasons for not publishing this study until the early 1970s, the authors relied on the traditional Cold War
Sovietological historiography and hence focused almost exclusively on the negative
effects of collectivization and industrialization in the period of the First Five-Year
Plan. Moreover,Van der Veer and Valsiner notably exaggerated the role of politically
inspired criticism of Luria’s research in publications of that time and the real impact
of this peer critique (such as in Razmyslov, 1934, 2000) on the strategies of administrative decision-making in the Soviet Union under the dominance of the Bolshevik
(i.e. Communist) Party.
Finally, a paper by Victor Nell, “Luria in Uzbekistan: The vicissitudes of
cross-cultural neuropsychology” should also be mentioned in this context (Nell,
1999). The author made an attempt at a first investigation of Luria’s research in
Central Asia and presented a fairly detailed account of the story supported by multiple references to original publications. However, the author added a great deal
of historiographical speculation in the fashion of the ‘oppressed science’ (for some
criticism of narratives of this kind see Chapter 3). Yet, as surprising as it might
appear, the author not only disregarded the preceding publications by Mecacci
(1980), Scheerer (1980), Harrower (1983), and Van der Veer and Valsiner (1991), but

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 179

also apparently “did not notice” the very existence of Luria’s second Central Asian
expedition in 1932 and, therefore, Koffka’s participation in its work. This omission
is particularly amazing, given that Luria’s report that came out in Science under the
self-explanatory title “The second psychological expedition to Central Asia” (A. R.
Luria, 1933c) can be found in the list of Nell’s references. All this, unfortunately,
considerably devalues Nell’s study on the history of Luria’s expeditions to Central
Asia of 1931 and 1932.
A recent series of publications on this topic has considerably advanced our
understanding of this historical episode, its background, and the scientific meaning
of its possible interpretations (Allik, 2013; Goncharov, 2013; Lamdan, 2013; Proctor,
2013; Spiridonov, 2013; Yasnitsky, 2013a, 2013b). The discussion in this chapter is
solidly grounded in this literature.

The expeditions to Central Asia of 1931 and 1932
The idea of studying the influence of cultural changes caused by post-revolutionary
modernization and industrialization on mental processes was likely to have been
born during Vygotsky’s trip to Central Asia in the spring of 1929 at the invitation of
the Central Asian State University of Tashkent, Soviet Uzbekistan. In early April he
left Moscow for Tashkent (Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996, p. 131) and stayed there
for about a month, until early May.1 Nevertheless, during this short stay in Central
Asia Vygotsky conducted pilot experimental work. “I am doing some experiments
and I hope to bring some things” and “We are conducting some experiments, but
I do not know whether they will be successful,” Vygotsky wrote in his letters to
Luria from Tashkent (Vygotsky, 2007, p. 24).
We still know nothing about either the course of these experiments in Central
Asia or their results, but six months after Vygotsky’s trip to Central Asia a continuation of this research project, apparently, took concrete form. Thus, in December
1929, Luria wrote to Kurt Lewin, in Berlin, Germany, that he and Vygotsky had
the idea of conducting a psychological study of “primitive people” in Siberia or
Central Asia, with the proposal that the major part of the expedition could take
place in a little less than two years from then, i.e. in the summer of 1931. However,
it seems that at that time Luria and his colleagues did not have a clear notion of
which organization might be interested in carrying out and, consequently, funding
such an investigation. Luria assumed that the Academy of Sciences of the USSR
or the Institute of Oriental Studies would in principle support such a project, but
only partially fund it. Apparently, the problem of finding organizational support
and funding was one of the most important obstacles in the organization of such
a study and Luria asked Lewin whether he knew of any institution in Germany
that might potentially sponsor this project, or at least one third of it. Thus, in late
1929 Luria considered support from foreign institutions as a possible way out of
the existing difficult financial situation. He hoped to get aid from some German
and American institutions or from the Swiss International Bureau of Education

180

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

(Bureau International d’Éducation), directed by Jean Piaget. Piaget had recently
been appointed to this organization, which eventually became one of the organizational prototypes of UNESCO (founded only after World War II, on November
16, 1945). To facilitate Western funding, Luria pondered which Western scholars
should be invited to participate in the project. He turned for advice to Lewin and
speculated about inviting European anthropologists Erich von Hornbostel, Richard
Thurnwald, or someone from the American team of Franz Boas, for example Otto
Klineberg.2 At the moment we do not know if Luria actually sent invitations to
these scholars, as he planned. What is certain is that Lewin declined the invitation
and that the attempts to attract foreign scholars to participate in this expedition did
not succeed. Thus, in the summer of 1931 the expedition took place with the participation of Soviet researchers from Moscow and Uzbekistan and with the support
of Soviet institutions. In retrospect, this was not really surprising given the stock
market crash of October 1929, which signaled the beginning of the ten-year Great
Depression that affected all Western industrialized (capitalist) countries.
Apparently, the results of the first expedition seemed to Luria and Vygotsky so
interesting and promising that immediately after its end in 1931 they began planning
a second study of the cultural development of ‘primitive peoples’ in the process of
collectivization and Cultural Revolution. Luria’s enthusiasm about the results of the
first expedition is evident from the correspondence between Vygotsky and Luria, to
which we will turn below, but also from his correspondence with two scholars who
promoted the publication of his first book in the USA, the physiologist W. Horsley
Gantt and the philosopher Horace M. Kallen.3 Towards the end of the first expedition, on August 8, 1931, Luria sent a postcard to Kallen, in German. There along
with the greetings from Bukhara, the “magic land” (German: Fabelland) where
“Jews do not speak Jewish,” Luria announced “a new book, with many completely
new thoughts about the primitive mind, with several experimental series, is already
finished in my head.” On August 17, 1931, when Luria was still in Uzbekistan, he
found time to answer a letter from Gantt. Having discussed some matters relating to
the publication of his first book in the United States, Luria wrote about his study
in Central Asia:
As far as I know, this is the first experimental psychological expedition organized in order to study structural characteristics of the psyche at different historical stages and I was very glad when it was possible for me to spend three
months in the most interesting and peculiar places of our Union. During this
time we collected materials of an exceptional interest, forcing us to reconsider
several of the most fundamental psychological views, and in the coming years
I will turn to you for assistance in the publication of a new book in America.
It will be an experimental study of the historically developed structures of
the human mind.4
The second part of the study was to be held the next summer, in 1932. The
preparations for the second expedition to Central Asia were thorough. First, reports

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 181

on the success of the first expedition in 1931, with the announcement of the
upcoming second psychological expedition to Central Asia, were written by Luria
and published in English and German in leading journals such as Science (A. R.
Luria, 1931b), Zeitschrift für angewandte Psychologie (A. R. Luria, 1931c), Journal of
Genetic Psychology (A. R. Luria, 1932a) and Character and Personality (A. R. Luria,
1932b). Luria’s objective was fairly clear: the expedition was originally conceived as
a large-scale international study with the participation of major Western scholars, so
these publications had the purpose of raising publicity and luring foreign experts to
participate in the second expedition. In parallel with the publication of the reports,
Luria continued an active correspondence with Western scientists, hoping to interest them in the new project and to obtain their agreement to participate. Kallen
was the first addressee among those we know of who received a brief summary of
the first Central Asian expedition results. In his letter of October 29, 1931, Luria
informed Kallen about the places where they conducted their studies – Samarkand,
Fergana, the Alay Mountains, and the kolkhozes of Andijan – and the scope of the
work done by Luria and his fourteen co-workers. He described the topics they
studied and again stated his plan to write a comprehensive book about the findings:
The material that we collected (12 themes have been elaborated and appr. 600
exp. protocols have been collected) is really extremely interesting: we studied
how these persons count and understand book texts, how they create experimental myths, how they analyze themselves, judge themselves and others,
what the laws of their perception are, how their active memory is constructed,
how they abstract, compare, what they hold of logic and what the characteristics of their causal thinking are. As you can see, the set of questions was rather
large and many things were investigated; I can only add that each chapter
really reveals totally novel psychological facts. After all, we were in a region
where people measure distances in … teapots (How far is it to that village?
One teapot, i.e., going there requires as much time as needed to finish one
pot of tea), where they have a very peculiar attitude toward logic and practice, where people only understand meaningful action and cannot do things
that do not make sense, where understanding metaphors (better: conceptual
metaphors) is very limited, almost impossible, where I essentially found no
(better: only very few) geometrical-optical illusions, etc., etc. I feel that this
material enriched us all, and when I can still work with this material – and
with the developmental and clinical material – for 1–1.5 years, I hope to
write an interesting book about “Historical Changes in Human Mind”.5
It is obvious that for Luria it was especially important to cooperate with the leading representatives of Gestalt psychology. As we have seen, he invited Kurt Lewin to
participate in the first expedition, but Lewin declined the invitation. Neither could
Lewin take part in the second expedition because of an invitation to spend the
first semester of the 1932/3 academic year at Stanford University (Marrow, 1969,
p. 65). Among Luria’s other candidates to participate in the second Central Asian

182

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

expedition were both Wolfgang Köhler and Kurt Koffka – German-American
scholars and the founders of one of the most influential contemporary psychological theories, Gestalt psychology (Ash, 1995).
In the archival collection of Köhler’s writings just two of Luria’s letters from
that period remain. From these we learn that they got personally acquainted – presumably during the IX Congress of Psychology in 1929 – and were corresponding
more or less regularly. In early December 1931, Luria sent Köhler a letter with a
very detailed report of his first Central Asian expedition and its preliminary results.
Half of the letter was dedicated to the description of the studies of perception
and Luria paid special attention to the findings about optical illusions. According
to Luria (and Vygotsky) the results indicated that, although Uzbeks do have illusions, among ‘primitive’, i.e. illiterate and socially inactive, Uzbek individual peasants
and Uzbek housewives, these are less common than among uneducated kolkhoz
activists and emancipated Uzbek women. The latter had renounced the burqa (also
known as chadri or paranja in Central Asia) just two or three months before the
experiments began and had basic reading and writing skills. Luria also included the
descriptive statistics of his findings.
In the same letter, Luria informed Köhler of his research plans for the near future:
Now I am coming to the practical part of my letter. I would like to make a
suggestion. Would you like to participate in the second psychological expedition to Central Asia, in the summer of 1932? I believe that we will manage
to organize a second expedition and it will have an international character …
If you would like to join us, you can get two (or more) assistants, a Russian
who speaks German (can be Dr. Vygotski or someone from Moscow) and an
Uzbek who speaks Russian and who will always be available. The expedition
will cost you nothing. Our scientific institutes will assume all costs, partly –
the Academy of Sciences of Uzbekistan.You will receive an invitation for 1–2
lectures, for which you will be paid about 1,000 rubles. Then you still have
about 1,000 rubles here (I do not know exactly) as a fee for Gestalt Psychology
(which will be released in the winter) and that will be quite enough for
everything. I am quite convinced that – if this expedition will succeed – we
will be able to do a lot for psychology. I hope that Mr. Lewin and Mr. Koffka
will participate. Perhaps it would also be good to invite Prof. Thurnwald?
However, despite the attractive offer, Köhler, for whatever reason, did not participate in the 1932 expedition. Luria then invited him to participate in the next stage of
the research project, a third expedition planned for the summer of 1933. According
to Luria, the second expedition was to become, on the one hand, a less important
expedition, meant to check the data obtained during the first expedition, and on the
other hand, an expedition that would prepare the next, most ambitious third expedition in the summer of 1933.6 It is difficult to say whether the invitation to Köhler
reflected real plans and arrangements between the scientists and their sponsoring
organizations. What is certain is that a large-scale study in 1933 did not take place

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 183

and that the second expedition to Central Asia was the last. Although neither Lewin
nor Köhler participated, nonetheless the second expedition was truly international
and not just because of the mixed Russian-Uzbek composition of Luria’s team. In
the summer of 1932, Luria’s expedition to Uzbekistan included another world-class
German-American scientist. It was one of the founders of Gestalt psychology and
at that time professor at Smith College in Massachusetts: Kurt Koffka.
Correspondence between Luria and Kurt Koffka prior to the second expedition
would give us an insight into the course of negotiations and preparations for the
journey to Uzbekistan but it has not been preserved in the archival collections of
Koffka’s or Harrower’s papers. From the correspondence between Luria and Lewin
we know only that in the spring of 1932 the issue of the second expedition and
Koffka’s participation had been resolved, although uncertainties about the location
remained. Luria was considering two options, Svaneti in the Caucasus or Central
Asia, and, as we know, eventually preferred the second one.7 Thus, Koffka took part
in the second expedition to Central Asia in 1932.
On his way to Uzbekistan, Koffka stayed in Moscow for a while, where, among
other things, he gave a presentation at the local Institute of Psychology on May
29, 1932. The topic was “Die Überwindung des Mechanismus in der modernen
Psychologie” (The overcoming of mechanism [i.e. mechanistic philosophy] in modern psychology).8 The text of the lecture was published in the journal Psikhologiia,
although slightly abridged and with a different title, as was characteristic of that
period. The new title mentioned another extreme approach in psychology, namely
vitalism, and it now read “Overcoming the mechanistic and vitalistic currents in
modern psychology” (Koffka, 1932). It was then, during his visit to Moscow, that
Koffka, by his own admission, first met Vygotsky. The latter served as an interpreter, because Koffka’s talk was presented in German.9 The meeting with Koffka
clearly made a big impression on Vygotsky and he subsequently mentioned Koffka’s
lecture at least three times in his talks and written works (see Chapter 9). The
correspondence between Koffka and his student Molly Harrower includes rich
material that reflects his impressions from the Central Asian expedition. Harrower
subsequently published these travel notes and memories in a separate chapter, “The
Russian-Uzbekistan expedition,” of her book Kurt Koffka: An unwitting self-portrait
(Harrower, 1983, pp. 143–164). Actually, it is with the help of these rich materials
that we managed to reconstruct the chronology and the route of Luria’s second
expedition to Central Asia in some detail (Yasnitsky, 2013a).10
The expedition was carried out in June and July 1932. It began with the departure from Moscow to Tashkent on June 1, and after a short delay in Tashkent (June
5–9), stayed in Samarkand for a one-week preparatory seminar (June 9–16). The
team consisted of Koffka, three Muscovites – Luria, Shemiakin, and Mordkovich –
and an Uzbek group of researchers from the local Pedagogical Academy directed by
P. I. Leventuev.After the seminar in Samarkand, the extended research team departed
for the remote areas of Eastern Uzbekistan, first to relatively ‘civilized’ and mostly
collectivized Fergana Valley (June 16–July 1) and then to the mountain areas of
Shakhimardan, formerly part of Kirgizstan (July 1 to the end of July). Shakhimardan

184

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

Tashkent
Fergana
Shakhimardan
Samarkand

F I G U R E 8 . 1 Luria’s expedition to Uzbekistan (Tashkent→Samarkand→Fergana→
Shakhimardan, June–July, 1932)

had a predominantly traditional population, which had not been collectivized and
actively resisted the economic and social innovations of the Bolsheviks. The data
collected in these two areas of Eastern Uzbekistan formed the material for the
comparative analysis of the changes in mentality caused by the First Five-Year Plan
(collectivization) and the rapid “economic renascence of Central Asia” (A. R. Luria,
1931b). By late July or early August the members of the expedition had completed
the fieldwork and began to return to their homes.
After his return from Uzbekistan, Luria published several articles in which he
mentioned his Central Asian expeditions. For example, in 1933 in the journal
Sovietskaia Psikhonevrologiia (Soviet Psychoneurology) Luria published an article, entitled “The paths of Soviet psychology in last 15 years,” in which he referred to the
recent expeditions:
The study of higher psychological processes and their structure could not
remain within the confines of psychological laboratories and children’s institutions. The rapid socialist reconstruction of the peripheries of the country –
which determined the transition of those peoples, who preserved the remains
of the long ago surpassed socio-economic types [in Russian: uklad], to the
socialist form of production and social relations – provided new, exceptional
opportunities for psychological research. A number of psychological expeditions (among these it is worthwhile to point out the psychological expeditions to Central Asia of 1931 and 1932) accumulated considerable data on
the historical reconstruction of thinking and psyche and for the first time

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 185

systematically scrutinized the problem of the historical development and
change in logical categories, psychological functions and consciousness.
(A. R. Luria, 1933b, p. 34)
In addition, Luria published two international reports on the second Central
Asian expedition: a brief note in Science (A. R. Luria, 1933c) and a relatively longer
review in the Journal of Genetic Psychology (A. R. Luria, 1934). Neither of these publications mentioned a third expedition project. Despite the author’s assurances that
the findings of both psychological expeditions would be compiled and prepared
for publication in the next year (A. R. Luria, 1933c, p. 192; 1934, p. 259), such
publication of the results of the Central Asian studies was delayed until the 1970s
(A. R. Luria, 1971, 1974, 1976). As mentioned before, this delay is usually explained
by referring to the criticism that these studies received in the Soviet Union (E. A.
Luria, 1994, pp. 66–68;Van der Veer & Valsiner, 1991, pp. 253–255).
However, Luria did publish the announcements of the project and the preliminary reports in Science, Zeitschrift für angewandte Psychologie, Journal of Genetic
Psychology, and Character and Personality in 1931–4, and he continued his foreign
publication activities virtually until the very beginning of World War II; for the
discussion of the last of these prewar publications, a French paper of 1937, see
Lamdan and Yasnitsky, 2013. It is true that in these foreign publications in English
and French, published immediately after the expedition, Luria made no mention
of the expeditions in 1931 and 1932, or of the supposedly sensational discoveries
made in their course. The publications were devoted to entirely different themes,
such as the study of twins in the Institute of Medical Genetics and the work in a
clinic with oligophrenic children (A. R. Luria, 1935c, 1936, 1937). Nevertheless, it
appears safe to assume that, in addition to explicit criticism of Luria’s expedition in
the Soviet Union, there were several other reasons not to publish full results of this
study abroad.

Kurt Koffka: “Uzbeks do have illusions!”
There is another source of knowledge that offers us insights into the course and
preliminary results of the expeditions in 1931 and 1932: the correspondence
between Luria and Vygotsky. Unfortunately, Luria’s letters to Vygotsky of 1931–2
have not yet been found but several enthusiastic letters from Vygotsky to Luria,
written during the first Central Asian expedition in the summer of 1931, have survived.Vygotsky wrote them as a reply to the preliminary results of the field studies
and they leave no doubt that the members of the Vygotsky–Luria circle considered
the first expedition to Central Asia a great success. For example,Vygotsky began his
letter from Moscow to Luria in Central Asia from June 20, 1931 in this way:
Dear Alexander Romanovich, your Report no. 2 was just delivered to me.
I read it with tremendous delight and am hurrying to send a brief response.

186

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

All the things you report are extremely important and interesting; this is now
the best part of our work – and a novel part in the best sense in that it does
not repeat what we already know but moves all the work forward and raises
our older studies (for example, the problem of types of connections in mediated memory and thought) to the highest level.
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 39)
Vygotsky ended his letter as follows:
That is all. Generally speaking, excellent! The best of the year.The road to the
future. It makes up for the six months that were lost. It is our golden reserve
of experiments that are opened directly by the theoretical key. Keep up your
work and write me. I will read your letter to all. It is a marvelous letter.
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 41)
Other reports by Luria on the course of the expedition were also greatly appreciated by Vygotsky. In a letter of August 1, 1931, he wrote:
Earlier I sent you letters to Samarkand and Fergana about the enormous,
totally incomparable impression that your Reports and records have had on
me. This is a huge, decisive, pivotal step in our research toward a new viewpoint. But even in any context of European research, such a survey would be
an event.This study will be your trip to Tenerife.11 I am experiencing a feeling
of elation – in the literal sense of the word – of being on the brink of a major
internal success.
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 42)
As we have seen from the correspondence between Luria and Köhler (Luria’s
letter to Köhler dated December 3, 1931), the study of optical illusions, which
formed the basis of the legendary telegram “Uzbeks have no illusions!” had already
taken place during the first expedition and therefore this hypothetic telegram – if
only it ever existed – is most likely be dated to the summer of 1931, not later.
Above we noted that Köhler did not participate in the expedition of 1932 but
Kurt Koffka did. Moreover, his studies of perception, conducted during this expedition, led him to a diametrically opposed conclusion. Koffka pointed out that his
subjects in Central Asia, with minor and rare deviations, showed the same optical
illusions as the subjects in European studies. At the same time, Koffka noticed that
deviations were occasionally found in the first expedition, more so than in the
second.This phenomenon, Koffka noted, could easily be explained, primarily by the
differences in the technique used.
In Koffka’s view, more open-minded and socially naive subjects who perceived
the experimenter as equal to them and did not consider the experiment as a test of
their abilities, without exception showed optical illusions, such as the Müller-Lyer

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 187

illusion and the Poggendorf illusion. In contrast, suspicious, distrustful, and hesitant
subjects studied the images for a long time before making their verdict and succumbed less to optical illusions, which, according to Koffka, again confirmed previous studies of European psychologists. Koffka formulated his conclusion as follows:
The following results may be considered as proved: With very few exceptions
the men and women examined by us succumbed to the optical illusions – of
which a great variety was shown – just as we do. Quantitative measurements
of the Müller-Lyer and Poggendorf patterns yielded a slightly smaller amount
of these illusions than control experiments with European psychologists. The
exceptions, which were very rare in this expedition, but had been much more
frequent in the first, are easily explained by the attitude of the testees towards
the experimenter. Naïve, social subjects who treated the experimenter on a
footing of equality and did not regard the experiment as a test of their ability
had the illusion without exception. Only when the subjects were suspicious,
staring a long time at the patterns before making their judgments, the illusions
failed to appear with some though by no means with all patterns, in accordance with well-known facts.
Similarly, it could be proved with several very simple figures like Mach’s
book, Necker’s cube, Schroder’s staircase, that plane perspective drawings may
compel the perception of a tridimensional object. If the pattern is more complex and has greater representative value, the intended tridimensional effect, as
a rule, does not appear, although we see these figures as tridimensional. Thus
the opposite report of previous investigators can be explained, according to
which Uzbeks, not reached by modern culture, cannot perceive perspective.
Transformation in the organization of a chessboard pattern was always
performed, though sometimes with difficulty. The Kohts test manifested
great, but not insuperable, difficulties in the abandonment of the originally
perceived form.
(Luria, 1934, p. 257)
This brief report of Koffka’s research in Central Asia was published only two
years after the completion of the expedition, as part of Luria’s article published in
the Journal of Genetic Psychology, and was sent directly to the translators (A. R. Luria,
1934).12 Nevertheless, it is unlikely Luria first learned about Koffka’s conclusions
from the publication of this article. Most likely, judging by Vygotsky’s letter to Luria
of August 17, 1932, Luria and Vygotsky learned about Koffka’s critical conclusions
immediately. In this letter Vygotsky wrote:
I continue to think and will continue to think, until I am persuaded otherwise, that there is now experimental proof … for the phylogenetic existence
of a level of comprehensive thinking, of a different structure, independent of
it, of all the principal systems of the psyche, of all the major types of activity,

188

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

and eventually of consciousness itself. Surely, that is not so little as to be dissatisfied with the outcome of the two trips.
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 45)
In between the lines one can read the awareness of another opinion and an attempt
to overcome the doubts seeded by it. And this meant if not the complete failure of
the Central Asian studies in 1931–2 then at least a serious reason to cast doubt on
the findings of the expedition of 1931 and the need for a critical review of both its
findings and its experimental methods. In any case, such epithets as “golden reserve
of experiments that are opened directly by the theoretical key,” “huge, decisive, pivotal step in our research toward a new viewpoint,” and the claim that “in any context of European research, such a survey would be an event” now seem quite exaggerated. Koffka, one of the greatest and most reputable European (and American)
researchers, the co-founder and leading advocate of one of the most brilliant and
influential psychological theories and one of the most prominent experts on the
psychology of perception of his time, must have thought the same. This circumstance explains the delayed publication of the findings as well as the suggestion of
political censorship (A. R. Luria, 1971). Neither Vygotsky nor Koffka lived until
that time, and for some reason neither Koffka’s conclusions nor even his participation were mentioned in Luria’s book (A. R. Luria, 1974, 1976).13
At a somewhat general, abstract, and philosophical level of discussion, virtually
any phenomenon or event in our lives can be regarded from three distinct – although
closely interrelated – perspectives. First, how other people see us and interpret our
words and actions. Second, how we make sense of our experiences and interpret
the world around us. Three, what we actually see and, therefore, what we fail to see.
The three perspectives are foundational and essential. Likewise, in the context of the
present discussion three questions are most pertinent to the Luria–Koffka controversy as it appears in light of recent historical investigations, findings, and analyses:
•

•
•

First: What was the reception of Luria’s Central Asian research in the domestic context? More specifically: why was the study not published in the Soviet
Union in the 1930s?
Second: Why did Luria interpret the data the way he did?
Third: What did Luria actually see, or rather, what did Luria fail to see in
Central Asia in 1931–2?

Why was Luria’s study not published? The reception of Central
Asian research in the Soviet Union in the 1930s
Luria’s study in Central Asia was not published in the Soviet Union in the 1930s.
One reason may have been that Luria felt reluctant to present the study because
of Koffka’s criticism. After all, his main and most triumphant conclusion was considerably undermined by the findings of Koffka, a scholar of great international

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 189

reputation, specifically in the field of visual perception. This reason for voluntary
non-publication may be termed personal and subjective.Yet, apparently, there were
other, objective reasons not to publish. These were directly related to the wider
social and political context of the time.This was the period of a change in state policy on national and ethnic minorities in the Soviet Union of early Stalinism. Other
major factors to consider were the First Five-Year Plan, the Cultural Revolution,
violent deprivatization of agriculture (so-called collectivization), and the industrialization of the national economy. At the same time, major processes in international
political affairs – most notably, the Nazi Party’s ascent to power in Germany in early
1933 – also played their role in changing attitudes and domestic policies in the
USSR. All these had a considerable impact on the fate of Luria’s expeditions of the
early 1930s – contrary to the original plans, abruptly interrupted in 1932 – and the
non-publication of their scientific results for several decades to come.
The question of the rights of nationalities was central for the Bolsheviks from
the very inception of the Soviet state (at the very end of 1922) and even before.14
Bolsheviks could not avoid the issue because of the multi-ethnic nature of the
Russian Empire in an era of rising nationalism.The tangle of contradictions between
conventional Marxist wisdom on the national question (essentially an internationalist standpoint), the practical needs of governing a multi-ethnic state, the challenge
of modernization, and the attempt to attract the sympathy of the various national
groups necessitated a federative structure of the Soviet state based on nationally
(or ethnically) identified units. Despite the fact that the federative structure was
essentially formal, the official policy was to encourage the development of different
national cultures within the framework of the socialist state, even in localities where
it was impossible to apply the terms ‘nationality’ or ‘national culture’. Although multiple national identities and the ‘national revival of minorities’ throughout the Soviet
Union were encouraged, the identity of the largest ethnic group, the Russians, was
actually hushed up or at least minimized. Russian nationalism was viewed as the
