successor of the Tsarist regime and understood as the greatest danger for the construction of socialism and the stability of a multi-national social structure on the
ruins of the empire.
Hence, the Soviet regime from its inception adopted a policy of affirmative
action for all non-Russian nationalities and ethnic groups or, as they called it, a policy of korenizatsiia (roughly: indigenization). This policy had economic, social, and
cultural aspects. In addition to the attempt to provide territorial autonomy, even
formally, to as many ethnic groups as possible, the central government encouraged
the cultural development of minorities, the official use of local languages, and the
promotion of national or ethnic elites in these new territorial units. In addition, the
state adopted, at least declaratively, a policy of preferential economic development of
the geographical areas populated by ‘delayed’ nationalities, that is, those who lived, in
Marxist parlance, under the conditions of a pre-capitalist, feudal economic system.
Affirmative action policies succeeded relatively well in the Western regions of the
Soviet Union but encountered many difficulties in its Eastern and southern regions.
Justifications for affirmative action policies were different in the West and the East.15

190

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

If in the West the central argument and the rationale for new national policies was
indigenization, in the East ‘socio-economic retardation’ was the preferred argument.
The Great Turn or the Socialist Offensive that began with the First Five-Year Plan
in 1929 led to new changes in the national policy adopted by the Soviet leadership.
In the republics of the “developed West” where significant local elites were relatively
easily established during the period of the 1920s, and where the korenizatsiia focused
especially on expanding the dominance of the local language, the support to local
national elites was stopped or even reversed. To some extent this happened because
of the growing centralization of the economy during the period of forced collectivization and rapid industrialization. Perhaps the most notable example of such reversal of national policy in the developed localities is the case of the Ukraine in the
early 1930s. In contrast, in the “backward East” the affirmative action in education
and employment and the continued attempt to create local elites were well integrated into the process of economic centralization, which determined further support and generous investments in these areas, enhanced with the utopian discourse
of the ideology of the Cultural Revolution as part of a large-scale developmental
project for the economy and society.16 A few examples of this attitude can be found
in the policies implemented in the context of Soviet republics in Central Asia.
Against this background, ethno-psychological studies blossomed in the Soviet
Union. Several of them were conducted within the fairly vague framework of the
discipline of the pedology (developmental child psychology and physiology) of
national minorities. Psychologists and physicians dealing with ethnic issues under
the pedological umbrella profited from the spirit of the time, when social progressivist, transformative, and developmentalist discourse was at its peak, but classical ethnologists found it difficult to integrate Marxist discourse into their work
(Slezkine, 1991).
Pedology as an interdisciplinary science, or science in the making, was very
popular among the scientific elite of the Soviet Union, thanks, mainly, to its essentially Lamarckian standpoint manifested in the promise to influence the development of the next generation, and to a create a new, improved man by changing the
living conditions of children. Therefore, it comes as no surprise that researchers in
the Vygotsky–Luria circle grew acutely interested in this area of studies (Yasnitsky,
2013a).17 Even though Luria’s study in Central Asia was not explicitly pedological
by its nature – in fact, Luria always clearly identified it as a “psychological” expedition – it definitely was a part of the same trend and shared the same optimistic
Lamarckian spirit of the Cultural Revolution.18 Yet, the social processes of the late
1920s/early 1930s developed very rapidly and dramatically and what was endorsed
in an earlier period might turn unacceptable and even forbidden virtually overnight.
Thus, the timing of events is crucial.
By the end of 1932, with the end of the Cultural Revolution, the developmentalist trend in national politics was reversed, or at least silenced. Russian identity
and Russian culture began to go through a process of rehabilitation, culminating in
the second half of the 1930s. From this time, local nationalism was perceived as an
equally great or even greater danger than ‘great-Russian chauvinism’, depending

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 191

on the local political context. The various ethnic groups and cultures were declared
primordial, and local folklore became a kind of trademark that every nationality
should have. The concept of ‘the friendship of nationalities’ became central in the
Soviet discourse, Russians became the first among equals in the family of Soviet
peoples, and the term ‘backward nations’ became politically incorrect.19
These changes in the policy towards nationalities occurred in parallel, and
reflected the changes in the domestic and international situation of the Soviet
Union. Domestically, the change in the policy towards nationalities was part of
the consolidation process of the results of the Stalinist Great Turn. Internationally,
the strengthening of fascism in Europe and the seizure of power by the Nazis in
Germany in early 1933 affected the foreign policy of the Soviet Union. As a result,
‘anti-fascist’ policies and discourse received a great impetus and became particularly
sensitive to anything that looked like advocating the superiority of an ethnic or
national group (Haslam, 2006).
Under these circumstances, an ethno-psychological investigation like Luria’s
would immediately become a target of criticism in the Soviet Union of 1932–3.
Luria’s findings showed – or at least can be interpreted in that manner – that there
were still significant psychological differences between peoples in the Soviet Union,
which reflected a gap between ‘primitive’, i.e. economically and culturally backward, nations and ‘advanced’ ones, i.e., in Marxist terms, those living under the
conditions of higher socio-economic stages. Although Luria attributed the psychological ‘backwardness’ of the local population in Central Asia to the impact of
socio-economic factors, his study and – even more so – his terminology (e.g.‘primitive’, ‘backward’) suggested that he was a nationalist, and this naturally triggered
accusations of chauvinism, nationalism, colonialism, and Western supremacism. In an
era when fascism and, specifically, the Nazis, with their militantly anti-Communist
sentiments, came to power in several Western European countries, accusations of
‘fascist, pro-Nazi attitudes’ equaled charges of ‘political mistakes’ – a phrase that
could lead to fairly grave consequences, especially during periods of social upheaval,
unrest, and increased terror.There is no doubt that Luria fell victim to such criticism
and had to vigorously fight against all political accusations, defending his ‘internationalist’ standpoint and explaining the ‘culturally progressivist’ foundations of
his study. To that end, Luria released a couple of publications in 1933 in which he
explicitly distanced himself from scientific ‘fascism’ of any kind and expressed his
firm belief in the virtually endless possibility of cultural transformation. In other
words, he joined in the rhetoric of that time, which said that the ‘socialist remolding’ of humans into a new type would be within reach in the future socialist state,
despite the limitations of traditional upbringing, biological makeup, and heredity
(A. R. Luria, 1933a, 1933b). In the end, the criticisms of 1932–3 did not have a
notable impact on Luria’s career and might not have precluded him from publishing his Central Asian research results.Yet the situation again changed considerably as
another dramatic turn of events took place in the following year.
In early 1934 the XVII Congress of the Communist Party of the Soviet Union
was held. During the Congress, informally called “The Congress of the Victors,” the

192

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

Party leadership triumphantly summarized the outcomes of the First Five-Year Plan
and declared the overwhelming success of the Soviet state in terms of its economic
growth and cultural development – also in comparison to the declining economies
of the major industrialized countries in the era of the Great Depression.20
In order to facilitate the understanding of the remainder of this chapter a brief
account of some aspects of the Marxist worldview is in order. According to Marxist
social theory, society is based upon modes of production that comprise the dialectic unity
of production forces – that is, human labor power and means of production (such as
tools or technologies) – and social and technical relations of production that encompass
a wide range of social phenomena such as property, power, relations between people
and the objects of their work, and the relations between social classes. The mode of
production is understood as the basis of society that determines the socio-economic
formation of a specific society. Historically, the Marxist model has accounted for five
major progressive socio-economic formations. This hierarchy includes (1) primitive
classless society (also referred to as “primitive communism”), (2) slavery, (3) feudalism, (4) capitalism and, the highest and the most advanced of the five, (5) socialism.
Yet different societies develop unequally, and at any particular period the different
types coexist in different national states or even within the same state, such as the
extremely diverse and multicultural Soviet Union of the 1920s–1930s.
Indeed, as was emphasized during the IX Congress of the Communist Party,
in the early 1920s the economy of the Soviet Union represented the features of
five socio-economic types or structures (in Russian: social’no-ekonomicheskii uklad),
roughly corresponding to the historical socio-economic formations.Yet, due to the
rapid progress made during the First Five-Year Plan only two of these types had
survived in the USSR, which allowed Joseph Stalin to describe the current situation
in his opening report to the Congress of the Party as follows:
At the time when the New Economic Policy was being introduced, Lenin
said that there were elements of five forms of social and economic structure in our country: (1) patriarchal economy (largely natural economy);
(2) small-commodity production (the majority of the peasants who sell grain);
(3) private capitalism; (4) state capitalism; (5) socialism. Lenin considered that,
of all these forms, the socialist form must in the end gain the upper hand.
We can now say that the first, the third and the fourth forms of social and
economic structure no longer exist; the second form has been forced into
a secondary position, while the fifth form – the socialist form of social and
economic structure – now holds undivided sway and is the sole commanding
force in the whole national economy.21
And even that achievement was to be triumphantly overcome by 1937.The Second
Five-Year Plan of 1933–7 that was announced at the Party Congress of 1934 was to
finally eliminate the remnants of capitalism and small-commodity production in the
country and, by the end of the Second Five-Year period (piatiletka) in 1937, lead to
the establishment of a fully socialist economy and even classless society.22

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 193

One may wonder how all this political and socio-economic talk is related to
Luria’s study and the perspective of its publication in the Soviet Union in the 1930s.
The answer is: most directly. Indeed, the propagandist victory of the socialist experiment that was declared at the XVII Congress of the Party in January/February 1934
virtually put an end to Luria’s hopes of publishing his work on psychological development in times of rapid socio-economic change. In order to understand why, we
need to answer the second question posed above, namely:
Why did Luria interpret the data the way he did?

Why did Luria interpret the data the way he did? The case of
Vygotsky’s and Luria’s vulgar Marxism
Much has been written about the influence of Marxism on the work of Vygotsky
and Luria. Theirs is usually described as a creative, sophisticated, complex, or “academic” understanding of Marxism.23 In the context of Luria’s psychological expeditions to Central Asia of 1931–2 this assessment is, in fact, highly disputable. Let
us see how Luria defined the goals of the expedition in his foreign publications of
the time:
The aim of the expedition was to investigate the variations in thought and
other psychological processes of people living in a very primitive economic
and social environment, and to record these changes which develop as a result
of the introduction of higher and more complex forms of economic life and
the raising of the general cultural level.
(A. R. Luria, 1931b, p. 383)
This statement is largely consistent with other sources, including Luria’s private correspondence with his peers abroad. For instance, in his letter to Kallen of October
29, 1931, Luria wrote:
The basic idea of the expedition was as follows: what we assumed to be natural
in the human mind is mostly historical, i.e. developed in the history of mankind; when a person shows other social forms of behavior, he also thinks
differently and the elementary mental functions involved in this thinking also
function in another way. So we should certainly find other laws of perception, memory, abstraction, etc. in the mind of people living under primitive
conditions. When the social reform is as comprehensive and fast as it is right
now here – and particularly in Central Asia – the person will certainly also be
rebuilt psychologically, and certain reforms [unclear in original] of the most
fundamental psychological systems will certainly come the fore. These questions were never dealt with in a real psychological experiment; I do not have
a high opinion of Lévy-Brühl’s material; after all, no single experiment was
done and, hence, no single fact was causally explained.24

194

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

A closer look at the classification of the groups of subjects, which one can
find in Luria’s later book and in his correspondence with Köhler, confirms this
division into two groups. A first category, following Luria’s terminology, can
be described as ‘primitive’ people – ichkari (traditional housewives) and dekkhane (traditional peasants) – living in the pre-modern, traditional Central Asian
socio-economic order. A second category consisted of more ‘advanced’ or ‘progressive’ subjects, those on their way to the civilization that had already reached
the ‘socialist form of social life’: women that had completed pre-school courses,
kolkhoz activists, and women studying at a teachers’ training college. Within each
of these categories, we can see a hierarchy that suggests a five-stage scale used by
Luria in his assessment of the psychological development of the subjects. Thus, in
traditional, ‘primitive society’, men, unlike women, are engaged in production, and
occupy a higher social position than women. Hence, according to this simplistic
Marxist interpretation, men are expected to outperform women during psychological testing. In the second category, the more a person is involved in the
socialist institutions created during collectivization and the Cultural Revolution,
the better his or her psychological performance is supposed to be. To express this
in Marxist terminology, the changes in the socio-economic basis lead directly
and almost instantly to changes in mental life, which, in contrast to the economic
basis, is understood as a part of the superstructure. Thus, it is the mode of production – specifically, the relations of production – rather than the level of education
that, according to Luria’s studies of the early 1930s, determines the psychological
makeup of individuals, specifically, their thinking, perception, personality traits,
and self-observation abilities. Indeed, even those subjects of Luria’s studies who
are included in the most ‘progressive’ categories have a very low level of education. Furthermore, the range of these ‘progressive’ types suggests that Luria in
fact used a bottom-up scale from the less advanced – that is, less socially active –
women with minimal levels of education, through a higher level of uneducated
collective farm activists, to the still higher level of students at the teachers’ training college. Perhaps it is worth mentioning here that in later texts devoted to
the discussion of this historical study, this economic determinism was gradually
replaced by the influence of ‘culture’ and ‘education’, despite the fact that vulgar
Marxist interpretations can still be found in Luria’s publications of the 1970s (see,
e.g., A. R. Luria, 1974, pp. 19–28). Finally, in the traditional, hagiographic, yet
another ‘Great Man’, scientific biography published by a student of Luria’s in the
early 1990s, the economic determinism in the interpretation of Luria’s Central
Asian study design was absolutely absent (Homskaya, 1992).
All in all, Vygotsky’s and Luria’s apparent fascination with economic determinism and their vulgar Marxist simplistic interpretation of psychological development
cost them dear and substantially undermined the chances of immediate publication
of these studies in the Soviet Union at the time of the First and Second Five-Year
Plans. Indeed, as soon as the Communist Party leadership proclaimed the victory
of the socialist mode of production and declared the established fact that, in Stalin’s
dictum, “the socialist form of social and economic structure now holds undivided

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 195

sway and is the sole commanding force in the whole national economy,” it was a
serious political mistake to claim – as Luria did in his foreign publications of 1933
and 1934 – that large groups of the population lagged behind psychologically due
to “primitive economic conditions which are now undergoing tremendous industrial, political and cultural transformation.” Nor was it wise to state that this economic transformation determined “the very dynamics of the transition from the
more elementary psychological laws to the more complex processes” (A. R. Luria,
1933c, p. 191; 1934, p. 255). Such a study, were it published in 1934 or later, would
be considered as not in tune with the times, as hopelessly outdated, and of hardly
any use given the requirement of the “practicality” of scientific research in the
Soviet Union.25

What did Luria actually (fail to) see in Central Asia in 1931–1932?
The cultural-historical psychology of Wertheimer and Koffka
Gestalt psychology has long been perceived worldwide, specifically in the United
States, as the field of psychological studies of perception. Among other things, this
is due to the way these psychologists – primarily the three founders and leaders
of this scientific system of thought, Max Wertheimer, Kurt Koffka, and Wolfgang
Köhler – presented themselves and their scientific system abroad. This is apparent,
for example, from Koffka’s well-known article “Perception: An introduction to the
Gestalt-theorie,” which was the first presentation of the principles of Gestalt psychology in English (Koffka, 1922).
However, a wide range of other topics outside research on visual perception –
including the problem of thinking in a socio-cultural context – played an important
role in the development of Gestalt psychology as such and constituted its theoretical
and axiomatic foundation. This is particularly notable in the work of Gestalt psychology’s founder and ‘godfather’ Max Wertheimer. Thus, Wertheimer’s programmatic
book, Drei Abhandlungen zur Gestalttheorie (1925a), has three chapters, of which only
one deals with the problems of perception.The other two treat the problems of psychological research on productive thinking and sense of number in concrete historical and cultural contexts, specifically in ‘primitive’ peoples (German: Naturvölker).
Together with another of Wertheimer’s foundational writings – his paper on the
music of the Veddas (Wertheimer, 1910) – these studies constitute the solid basis
of what one, in retrospect, might legitimately refer to as ‘cultural-historical Gestalt
psychology’.26 Incidentally, the Veddas were considered as one of the most primitive
tribes of that time, and Max Wertheimer’s biographers D. Brett King and Michael
Wertheimer have suggested that the study of the Veddas was no less important for
the development of Gestalt theory than the foundational work on the psychology
of visual perception (King & Wertheimer, 2005, p. 96). Thus, this complex of ideas
on human mind, value, and culture was at the very foundation of the entire Gestalt
psychology and constituted its – not necessarily explicitly manifested – conceptual
framework and axiomatic basis.

196

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

In light of Wertheimer’s foundational thinking about ethno-psychological issues
that are at the very heart of the entire Gestaltist project we can better understand Koffka’s interpretation of the Central Asian studies as an alternative to Luria’s
simplistic and militant pro-Bolshevik stance and its impact on what he actually
observed – or, for that matter, failed to observe – and understood in the course of
his expeditions. Of particular interest in the context of this discussion is Koffka’s
viewpoint as it is expressed in his book Principles of Gestalt psychology (1935), which
its author conceived and actually started composing during his trip to Central Asia
in the summer of 1932 (see Harrower, 1983, pp. 28–29, cf. also p. 152).
According to Koffka, the situation in psychology had not significantly changed
since the split between experimental, ‘natural scientific’ psychology and the hermeneutic, ‘understanding’ variations were first discussed. Indeed, Koffka argued that
experimental psychology tries to explain scientifically mental phenomena by studying the basic processes and traits of mental life. But it failed to explain the most
complex human phenomena such as Caesar’s decision to cross the Rubicon or the
pleasure one derives from creating art. One cannot explain such phenomena by the
laws of sensation, association, or feelings. Therefore, the understanding of such phenomena is left to scholars from the humanities or Geisteswissenschaften, which further
increases the split between explanation and understanding as the dominant methods
of scientific research in this field of knowledge.
According to Koffka, we can find certain regularities in natural phenomena and
even formulate some general laws regarding them. However, within the framework
of mechanistic and reductionist science these laws allow predictions but will be
devoid of meaning. Only if we see the phenomenon as a whole, and are not merely
concerned with its constituent parts, but also inquire about their meaning, can we
say that we truly attempt to understand it. As Koffka expressed this idea:
[F]ar from being compelled to banish concepts like meaning and value from
psychology and science in general, we must use these concepts for a full
understanding of the mind and the world, which is at the same time a full
explanation.
(Koffka, 1935, p. 21)
The Gestalt ideas on meaning were discussed at considerable length in Willis
D. Ellis’s Gestalt psychology and meaning (1930) and in Köhler’s Harvard lectures of
1934–5 that were later published as a book under the title The place of value in a world
of facts (1938). Another idea, closely interrelated with that of meaning as an object of
psychological research, is the holistic notion of field and its specific variations (for an
introductory discussion, see Yasnitsky, 2014a).Thus, Koffka, unlike Luria, interpreted
the phenomenon of optical illusions in his subjects taking into consideration the
meaning of the entire situation – in other terminology, the meaning of the psychological field or the behavioral environment – for these people.
Koffka’s original report on his part of the experiments that he conducted
in Central Asia was extremely brief but one can understand a little more of his

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 197

impression of these experiments from other publications. Thus, for instance, in his
book of 1935 Koffka briefly discussed the research of Révész, who trained chickens
to peck from the smaller of two simultaneously presented containers. Subsequently,
the chickens were presented with two equal containers under the conditions of
the well-known optical illusion known as the Jastrow illusion – that is, the containers were presented so that one of the two figures appeared smaller. The study
demonstrated that in the vast majority of cases the chicken trained to peck from
the smaller container in the experimental situation pecked from the one which
appeared smaller – apparently, humans and chickens behave alike. Koffka reported
on his replication study of Révész’s experiment, specifically
an experiment which I made in the summer of 1932 in a small village in
Uzbekistan in Central Asia. I had shown the Jastrow illusion, using the pattern
of the “Pseudoptics,” to a young native who was the host of the tea house,
the one meeting place of the male populace of the village. The man behaved
as the hens did, apart from the fact that his reactions consisted not in pecking
but in pointing to the larger of the two pieces of cardboard. I then put one
on top of the other and gave them to him to handle. I wanted to see what
explanation he would give to the curious discrepancy between their previous
inequality and their now manifest equality. He said something like illusion,
but without much conviction; and when I asked, “So you think they do not
really change when you take them apart,” he answered “Oh, yes, I believe they
will change a little.”
(Koffka, 1935, p. 33)
This case seems to illustrate the kind of behavior that both Luria and Koffka
observed in the subjects of the ‘advanced’,‘more developed’ group of collective farm
activists, who succumbed to optical illusions. In contrast, in Koffka’s assessment, this
group was constituted by “naïve, social subjects who treated the experimenter on a
footing of equality and did not regard the experiment as a test of their ability.” From
the perspective of a contemporary observer – neither blinded by Soviet propaganda
nor biased by vulgar Marxist economic determinism – Koffka’s interpretation of the
given case appears far more convincing than Luria’s assertions.
Similarly, Luria and Koffka diverged on the interpretation of those cases when
the subjects did not succumb to optical illusions. According to Luria’s interpretation, this meant that these individuals were at a lower stage of socio-economic and,
therefore, psychological development. Yet again, Koffka’s interpretation was completely different: “Only when the subjects were suspicious, staring a long time at
the patterns before making their judgments, the illusions failed to appear with some
though by no means with all patterns, in accordance with well-known facts.”
Interestingly, these observations and the interpretation of the data by Koffka are
reflected in similar observations that Luria recorded in his own travel diary. Several
fragments of this diary were published in the biography authored by his daughter Elena. In these notes made in Central Asia, Luria pointed out the relative ease

198

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

in establishing contacts with the socially active subjects and the local participants
in collectivization and the building of socialism in Uzbekistan. Quite in contrast,
those people who resided in the ‘uncivilized’ remote and mountainous regions and
preserved the traditional Central Asian lifestyle typically demonstrated distrust and
extreme suspicion to the foreigners:
There was no problem getting into contact with the inhabitants of the kishlaks of the central regions, who worked in sovkhozes [i.e. collective state
farms]: “Sovkhoz in the steppe. Suddenly, in the middle of nowhere – state
bank branch and drinks shop. Conversations about America; they do not
know Germany and Europe here, but they do know socialism. We drink tea
and talk till late night. Pilaf at midnight. We are in one of the most exotic
situations. Namaz, cushions, and socialism …” The inhabitants of distant kishlaks related to the participants of the psychological expedition with apparent distrust: “They definitely do not want to talk with us! Chaikhana and
gloomy outrage …” It was even more problematic to get acquainted with the
inhabitants of the mountainous pasturelands: “They are again afraid of us. At
our approach they all flee into the mountains. A Kirghiz woman and panic
reaction: ‘Do not jinx the baby!’ …” (“From A.R. Luria’s trip notes, 1932”,
preserved in A. R. Luria archive).
(E. A. Luria, 1994, p. 61)
So, the distrustful and hostile subjects from remote mountainous areas of
Shakhimardan were observed by both Luria and Koffka and constituted the very
same group of ‘primitives’ who allegedly succumbed to a relatively smaller number
of optical illusions in the first expedition in the summer of 1931. Luria observed
the fear and distrust of his subjects in the mountainous areas of Uzbekistan and
Kyrgyzstan, but apparently never believed these factors might be of any relevance
in the context of his research. In contrast, Koffka not only observed the fear and
anxiety but also attributed the difference of the performance of the two groups of
subjects primarily to the difference in their emotional state during the experiment.
Koffka’s conclusion should be confirmed by further experimental psychological
studies measuring the correlation between different emotional states with performance. However, from the perspective of our current discussion another question is of
considerable interest: Why would the emotional state of the ‘primitive’ subjects be
so different, or, in other words, what were they scared of? The answer to this question can be found in the social history of Central Asia during the tumultuous period
following Stalin’s Great Break.
Although Luria noticed the great suspicion, fear, and distrust of the local population of the remote areas of Uzbekistan, he definitely did not consider it scientifically
significant. Koffka did not give us a detailed report of the situation there but at least
he paid attention to details that Luria did not want to or could not pay attention
to. In a letter of July 1, 1932, to his former student Molly Harrower, Koffka wrote
the following:

Luria–Koffka controversy of 1932 199

Shaki Mordan [Shakhimardan], our next and final station, must be marvelous
from all I hear. About 5,000 feet above sea level, a true resting place.Today the
president of the Republic, Uzbekistan, and the president of the neighboring
Republic, Kirghizstan, are going to spend a few restful weeks there.The president of the GPU [secret police] here is going with us.
(Harrower, 1983, p. 150)
In other words, on their way to the mountainous regions surrounding the border
between Uzbekistan and Kirgizstan the team of researchers was accompanied by
two presidents of the two Central Asian Soviet republics and by none other than
the local head of the secret police GPU (OGPU as of 1932; subsequently NKVD,
then MGB, but best known as KGB and its heir FSB in the contemporary Russian
Federation). Thus, strangers associated with state authorities and accompanied by
a convoy of security forces visited the remote village to perform some kind of
scientific experiments. This alone would be enough to cause feelings of suspicion
and anxiety in the local population. However, it appears that there was more to it
and that these people were not simply distrustful and suspicious but quite hostile
to the regime. Indeed, the establishment of Soviet rule in Central Asia was a long
and tortuous process. Resistance by part of the local population, the so-called
Basmachi movement, existed long after the official end of the Civil War in the
early 1920s.27 One of the strongholds of the movement had been Fergana Valley,
where Luria and his colleagues performed their ethno-psychological field studies. The Basmachi revolt was overcome only by the middle of the 1920s, though
not completely, by a combination of military actions and concessions to the local
population. At the same time, national delimitation began in Central Asia. In this
process new republics were formed, though not all had originally the status of
independent Soviet Republics within the Soviet Union.28 The national delimitation in the Fergana Valley was especially difficult because of the multi-ethnic
nature of its population.
By the end of the 1920s, the attempt to deepen the Sovietization of the Central
Asian republics, along with the policies of the Cultural Revolution and forced collectivization, led to another outbreak of Basmachi rebellions. Many small groups of
Basmachi were formed in 1928, including in the FerganaValley. In the course of these
rebellions Uzbek poet and Bolshevik activist Hamza Hakimzade Niyazi was killed
in 1929 for his anti-religious, pro-women liberation activity in Shakhimardan –
subsequently, the final destination of Luria’s second expedition of 1932. Two bigger
attempts of Basmachi incursions from neighboring countries into the Soviet Union
were made in the first half of 1931, a couple of months before Luria’s first expedition (Pylev, 2007, pp. 84–88). It is highly likely that – judging against this historical background – the arrival of a large group of Soviet officials and international
researchers accompanied by a convoy of secret police forces was perceived as an
utterly alarming and even potentially life-threatening experience by the local population of the mountainous areas of Southern Uzbekistan, who, by Luria’s admission,
were afraid of the newcomers and immediately fled to the mountains.

200

Eli Lamdan and Anton Yasnitsky

In sum, the Luria–Koffka controversy provides a clear-cut and most curious
example of how Luria and Vygotsky, who are commonly considered to be the
founders of so-called ‘cultural-historical psychology’, totally missed these cultural
and historical components of the psychological research they conducted in Central
Asia. Indeed, their study was ahistorical and non-sensitive to the cultural specificity
of the local population. They also used normative tests designed for mental testing
of subjects in industrially developed countries. Furthermore, the Bolshevik-minded
bias of social activism and progressivism – as manifested by the vulgar Marxist economic determinism in their interpretation of the data – makes this study a spectacular example of the possible impact of political factors on scientific research. By a
strange and somewhat ironic turn of events, it was also political factors that withheld
this research from publication for several decades to come.
In contrast, Koffka was an outsider to the large-scale processes of rapid and
epochal transformation of Soviet society and did not share the social activist enthusiasm of his Russian peers. Thus, he was free from the progressivist and developmental axiomatic basis and the vulgar Marxist bias shown by Vygotsky, Luria, and
their associates and collaborators. Moreover, as a representative of the integrative
and holistic research tradition of Wertheimer’s Gestalt psychology with its requirements of (1) awareness of concrete cultural specificity and idiosyncrasies, (2) holistic
perspective on the entire psychological field of the subjects, and (3) emphasis on the
meaning (alternatively: value, significance) of a specific life situation for the individual, Koffka could clearly see what Luria failed to see during his trips to Central
Asia. First, Koffka was not satisfied with a purely statistical, quantitative approach
to mental testing and was interested in the actual meaning of the study for the participants. This is why he supplemented his experimental tests with interviews that
possibly gave him a better idea of psychological processes hidden from an objectivist approach. Second, from the outset Koffka was alert to the entire psychological
field of the subjects in his experiments, therefore, unlike Luria, he did not dismiss
as irrelevant the social situation of the experiment, but considered it as an utterly
essential factor, which allowed him to realize that the emotional state of the subjects
of the study – their naivety and carelessness, or, in contrast, their fear, distrust, and
anxiety – caused by the larger social context of the study, in a number of instances
notably affected their psychological performance.
Overall, this expedition of 1932 is just one episode in the intriguing and
largely unknown history of the convergence between the Soviet scholars of the
Vygotsky–Luria circle and the German-American researchers who with some reservation can be called ‘cultural-historical Gestalt psychologists’. In the next chapter
we discuss the history and some conceptual issues of this intellectual movement.

9
A TRANSNATIONAL HISTORY OF
“THE BEGINNING OF A BEAUTIFUL
FRIENDSHIP”
The birth of the cultural-historical Gestalt
psychology of Alexander Luria, Kurt Lewin,
Lev Vygotsky, and others
Anton Yasnitsky
The three luminaries and pioneers of contemporary psychology, Kurt Lewin,
Alexander Luria, and Lev Vygotsky, are well known all over the world and are
deservedly credited as highly influential scholars in the fields of social, neuro-, and
cultural and developmental psychology, respectively. Despite the apparent gap and
virtual absence of communication between these subfields of contemporary psychology, as fragmented as it is nowadays, the connections between these three prominent figures have occasionally been pointed out.
Thus, overall, quite a lot has been said about the connections between the Lewin
group and the members of the ‘Vygotsky circle’ (Yasnitsky, 2009b, 2011e) – a lot
and virtually nothing at the same time. The scholarly literature presents on a number of occasions bits and pieces of anecdotal evidence to the effect of some contacts
between the Soviet scholars and their peers in the West, such as Luria’s andVygotsky’s
foreign trips in the summer of 1925, or the personal encounters between Vygotsky
and Lewin in Moscow in 1933, or, for instance, Bluma Zeigarnik’s (the discoverer
of the classical ‘Zeigarnik effect’) successive collaboration with Kurt Lewin (until
spring of 1931, in Berlin) and Lev Vygotsky (from summer of 1931, in Moscow).
However, as surprising as it might seem and despite their apparent importance
for the historiography and theory of psychology, these contacts, exchanges, communications, and even collaborations have never been scrutinized in a rigorous
scholarly study.
Perhaps the earliest observation of the proximity of some of the ideas of the
groups of these Russian and German-American scholars can be found in a book
written by one of their contemporaries before World War II. This is Gestalt psychology. A survey of facts and principles by George W. Hartmann, which appears to be the
first major, book-length work on Gestalt psychology authored by an American and
published in North America. According to Hartmann’s observation, “the Russian

202

Anton Yasnitsky

investigator, Luria, has independently developed a motor and affective theory which
bears an extraordinary resemblance to Lewin’s position” (Hartmann, 1935, p. 226).
And yet, such observations and statements like this were quite rare until relatively
recent years.
Among the studies that most closely approached the topic of the exchanges
and parallels between Soviet and Gestalt psychologies one would first mention the
work of Scheerer, “Gestalt psychology in the Soviet Union,” which traces some
lines along which interactions developed between the Soviet representatives of the
‘Vygotsky circle’ and the German-American Gestalt psychologists. Scheerer covers
the period of the 1920s until the so-called “reactological discussion of 1931” and
generally argues that in the 1930s Gestalt psychology in the Soviet Union was in
decline (Scheerer, 1980), which was elsewhere attributed to Ivan Pavlov’s negative
stance towards Gestaltism (Windholz, 1984). This conclusion is contested in certain other publications, including this study. Thus, in this respect, of certain interest
in this context is recent scholarship that sheds new light on the role of dialectical
materialism in the history of the life sciences in the Soviet Union, specifically, Ivan
Pavlov’s physiology of higher nervous processes that was gradually transformed in
the 1930s as a result of peer critique from Pavlov’s Bolshevik collaborators and associates, who advocated for the unification of Pavlov’s atomism and mechanism with
Gestaltist holism (Todes, 2014; Todes & Krementsov, 2010).
The earlier pioneering paper by Scheerer was complemented by several other
studies conducted by German scholars of the Critical Psychology camp who
critically explored the tenets of Gestalt psychology against the background of
dialectic-materialist psychology, underlined the fundamental affinity between the
principles of Gestalt theory and so-called ‘cultural-historical’ theory, and discussed
how the potential of Soviet psychological research of a ‘Vygotskian’ type might
be instrumental in overcoming certain problems of Gestaltism (Keiler, 1980, 1981;
Stadler, 1975). Cumulatively, this research laid the foundation for subsequent studies
by German researchers who explored some interlinks between the Soviet psychology of the ‘Vygotsky circle’ and the German-American Gestalt psychologists
(Keiler, 1989; Stadler, 1989).
An interesting analysis of Lewin’s theory of his pre-American period (i.e.
before 1933) and the history of Soviet post-World War II developments of
Lewinian ideas and experimental techniques can be found in Bluma Zeigarnik’s
book on Lewin’s theory of personality (Zeigarnik, 1981). In addition, an account
of the history of interwar Lewinian psychology in Germany and in the Soviet
Union was presented in Zeigarnik’s memoir interview publication, released
posthumously (Yaroshevskii & Zeigarnik, 1988). The drawback of these two
publications – a short book and an interview – is that neither represents strictly
historiographical research and, besides, both are currently available in Russian
only. A considerably shorter version of Zeigarnik’s reminiscences on the topic of
Lewinian ideas in the Soviet Union is, however, available in German (Zeigarnik,
1984a) and in English (Zeigarnik, 1984b), too. Finally, of certain interest is an
older discussion about Lewin and Vygotsky as the two “radical and promising

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 203

metatheorists” (Lang, 1994) and a more recent attempt to discuss the ideas of
Lewin and Vygotsky in social research (Chaiklin, 2011), but neither of these is a
historiographical study, so they might only qualify as a mere introduction to the
systematic exploration of the interdependence between the ideas of these two
scholars.
Most recently, however, a great deal of work has been done in order to uncover
the history of communications and collaborations between Soviet and Western
psychologies, specifically, between two large circles of psychologists – Soviet
cultural-developmental Marxists and German-American Gestaltists. Thus, the present chapter is based on a series of studies that discuss the idiosyncratic Soviet
international governmental policies concerning international scientific exchange
and scholars’ communicative strategies and behaviors. These studies in fact reveal a
great deal of international scientific activity between the Soviets and the West that,
contrary to widely accepted views, undermines the common notion of Soviet scientific ‘isolationism’ in the field of psychology and allied sciences during the interwar period (Van der Veer & Zavershneva, 2011, 2012;Yasnitsky, 2010b, 2011a, 2011c,
2012b, 2012c, 2012h). Our research is solidly grounded in the recent studies in
Vygotsky’s archives that demonstrated the growing influence of Kurt Lewin on Lev
Vygotsky’s thought in his last and most mature period of the 1930s (see Chapters 5
and 6 of this book), as well as the findings of studies that have explored the hybrid
of ‘cultural-historical Gestalt psychology’ as it was emerging at the intersection of
the two scientific traditions in the 1930s (Yasnitsky, 2012a, 2012c, 2012d, 2012h,
2012i). This chapter is limited by its focus on the history of the emergence and the
development of the dense “informal personal network” (M. B. Adams, 2001) constituted by the Soviet scholars of the ‘Vygotsky circle’ (see discussion in Chapter 2)
and German-American Gestaltists in the period between the two world wars. The
close interrelations between these two groups of scholars, the numerous contacts,
international trips, and migrations of scholars that led to a range of publishing
and collaborative projects, not to mention intense intellectual exchange – all these
qualify this research as a distinctly “transnational history” (Heilbron, Guilhot, &
Jeanpierre, 2008).

1925–1929: Vygotsky’s and Luria’s trips to Europe
and their consequences
Vygotsky’s first acquaintance with Gestalt psychology took place not later than the
end of 1924: it was on December 22, 1924 that he made a public presentation at the
Moscow Institute of Experimental Psychology titled “About the new psychological
school in Berlin” (A. R. Luria, 1926, p. 251). And yet, Vygotsky’s and Luria’s first
personal encounter with German scholars most likely took place in the summer of
1925 during their trips to Europe. Both Vygotsky and Luria for different reasons
and independently of each other traveled to Western Europe and are known to have
visited Berlin, the capital of Germany’s liberal-democratic Weimar Republic.

204

Anton Yasnitsky

On his European trip Alexander Luria accompanied his father, Roman
Albertovich Luria, who was a noted medical doctor and professor in the city of
Kazan. Luria senior was habitually visiting Western Europe in order to update his
knowledge of the most advanced technologies and practices in his profession and to
sustain contacts with his European colleagues (E. A. Luria, 1994). In turn,Vygotsky
was sent on a state-funded trip to the conference on the education of the deaf that
took place in July 1925 in London (Van der Veer & Zavershneva, 2011). In the
course of their European trips both Vygotsky and Luria spent some time in Berlin
and established personal contacts with local researchers associated with the circle
of Gestaltists. At this point the archival documents that could clarify the details of
these visits to Berlin remain unexplored; thus, one needs to rely on fragmentary and
circumstantial evidence available in various other sources.
In summer 1925 two of the founders and main proponents of Gestalt psychology, Kurt Koffka and Wolfgang Köhler (at that time the former was employed
as a university professor in Giessen and the latter was Director of the Institute of
Psychology in Berlin), were in the United States.There, they had a series of relatively
short-term professional appointments: in 1924/5 Koffka was at Cornell University
as Jacob Schiff Visiting Professor in Education (Ash, 1995, p. 210) and lectured in
a great many other American universities1 (Harrower, 1971), whereas Köhler was
a visiting professor at Clark University in the spring and summer of 1925; he then
taught at Berkeley and Harvard2 and returned to Europe only in early 1926 (Jaeger,
1992).3 Therefore, out of all the leaders of Gestalt psychology in Berlin only two
were likely to be met in Berlin in mid-1925.These were Max Wertheimer, the ‘godfather’ of Gestalt psychology and, in the absence of Köhler, the interim Director of
the Institute, Kurt Lewin, who also taught at the Berlin Institute of Psychology. At
this time we do not have documents that would with certainty confirm Luria’s or
Vygotsky’s acquaintance with the two scholars as of summer 1925, however, there
is indirect evidence that suggests that it was then that this acquaintance might have
taken place.
In all likelihood, Luria first met German Gestalt psychologists – specifically, those of Kurt Lewin’s circle – in 1925 and even took part in Lewin’s student
Tamara Dembo’s experimental study of “anger as a dynamic problem” (Dembo,
1931, 1976). According to the memoirs of another former student of Lewin’s,
Bluma Zeigarnik, Luria as a twenty-three-year-old subject of this experimental
study revealed a highly emotional reaction and somewhat threateningly shouted
at the experimenter: “Hold on, Tamara Vasil’evna, just come to us with a visit, and
we will show you something!” (Yaroshevskii & Zeigarnik, 1988, p. 178). In turn,
in her reminiscences Dembo reportedly characterized Luria as “an interesting, if
somewhat difficult, subject” of her experimental study and, like Zeigarnik, recalled
Luria’s reaction fairly well: “but her clearest memory of the encounter is his invitation to her to visit Moscow, where, he suggested with feigned aggressiveness, ‘We’ll
show you a thing or two about anger!’” (Rosa & Wertsch, 1993, p. 6).4
Vygotsky also left apparent traces of his personal contacts with German Gestaltists
that in all likelihood took place as early as the summer of 1925. Thus, well known

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 205

is Vygotsky’s unfinished methodological manuscript that he titled, with variations,
“(Historical) meaning of the crisis in psychology” and that was posthumously
uncritically published as one of Vygotsky’s central theoretical works – for discussion see (Zavershneva, 2012b; Zavershneva & Osipov, 2012a, 2012b). In this text
Vygotsky made a most curious claim that “the majority of the influential Gestalt
psychologists regard themselves as Marxists in their scientific work” (Vygotsky,
1993b, p. 341) – a statement that can hardly be found in any single work published
by the scholars of this theoretical orientation, therefore, apparently, a conclusion
that Vygotsky reached on the basis of some first-hand personal encounters. Indeed,
Vygotsky refers to a “conversation with one of the most educated psychologists,”
who, according to Vygotsky, said:
What kind of psychology do you have in Russia? That you are Marxists does
not yet tell what kind of psychologists you are. Knowing of Freud’s popularity
in Russia, I at first thought of the Adlerians. After all, these are also Marxists.
But you have a totally different psychology. We are also social-democrats and
Marxists, but at the same time we are Darwinists and followers of Copernicus
as well.
(Vygotsky, 1993b, p. 341)
Vygotsky never left an indication of who his self-proclaimed “social-democrat and
Marxist” interlocutor was, but we have every reason to assume that Vygotsky’s conclusion – whether right or wrong – about the Marxist sympathies of Gestalt psychologists was based on this or a very similar conversation that took place during
his European trip of summer 1925.
There should be no doubt about how different were the relative scholarly
‘weights’, reputations, and international fame of the most notable of Gestalt psychologists in Berlin and their guests from the Soviet Union as of summer 1925.
In 1898, when Max Wertheimer – who would eventually become the founder of
Gestalt psychology – started his university studies in his native city Prague, Luria
was not yet born and Vygotsky was two years old. In turn, by the time Vygotsky
started his university studies in Moscow in 1913, Wertheimer had already defended
his doctoral dissertation (University of Würzburg, 1904), and, then, conducted and
published his first experimental studies that would lay the foundation of the intellectual movement subsequently known worldwide as Gestalt psychology (Wertheimer,
1912a, 1912b). Finally, by 1925 the group of Gestalt psychologists led by the ambitious, innovative scholars Max Wertheimer, Kurt Koffka, and Wolfgang Köhler had
already founded their journal Psychologische Forschung (Psychological Research), a
specialized publication that from its inception in 1921 served as the major publication outlet and the channel for dissemination of the ideas of the new psychological
movement. In addition, by 1925 the group of German scholars not only published a
series of books that presented the new psychological theory’s main tenets and their
experimental findings (Koffka, 1921; Köhler, 1917, 1920, 1921; Wertheimer, 1920,
1925a), but also started their expansion into the English-speaking academic world

206

Anton Yasnitsky

that was manifested by a few notable publications in English (Koffka, 1922, 1924a,
1924b; Köhler, 1925).
In comparison to the wealth of first-rate scholarly studies and influential German
and English publications that came out under the names of internationally renowned
Gestaltist researchers, the academic achievements of Luria and Vygotsky by the time
of their European trips of 1925 were modest and best. Thus, it comes as no surprise
that the scholarship of their Western peers was highly respected by the younger
Soviet researchers and influenced their work. This influence, however, was initially
limited to the experimental studies in the footsteps of Wolfgang Köhler, who conducted his seminal research on the use of tools and problem-solving in apes at the
Primate Station of the Prussian Academy of Sciences on Tenerife (Canary Islands),
located just off the northwest coast of mainland Africa (Köhler, 1917, 1921, 1925).
The members of the Vygotsky–Luria research team replicated Köhler’s studies during their ‘instrumental period’ of the 1920s in order to investigate the so-called
‘practical intellect’ in children.Yet, the influence of Gestaltist work was not limited
to replication studies. Another important aspect of Soviet–Gestaltist convergence
was the beginning of the transnational ‘import’ of Gestaltist ideas in the mid-1920s;
the Vygotsky–Luria group were fairly active advocates of this theoretical movement
in the Soviet Union.
In 1926, a volume of scientific papers authored by the researchers affiliated with
the Moscow Institute of Psychology came out in Leningrad under the editorship of
Konstantin Kornilov, Director of the Institute. This volume, characteristically titled
Problemy sovremennoi psikhologii (The problems of contemporary psychology), contained a section “From new foreign literature” that featured the only paper authored
by a non-member of the Institute, furthermore, a foreigner. This was Kurt Koffka’s
paper “Samonabliudenie i metod psikhologii” (Self-observation and the method
of psychology) (Koffka, 1926) that had been presented two years before at the
University of Cambridge Psychological Laboratory on February 23, 1924, and then,
in October 1924, published as a journal article in the British Journal of Psychology
(Koffka, 1924a).
Vygotsky and Luria were notable and most active participants in the preparation
of this publication. Thus, out of the twelve chapters that constituted the first three
sections of the book (titled I. General and theoretical papers; II. Experimental studies; and III. Overviews, smaller papers) three were authored by Vygotsky and another
one by Luria (in collaboration with A. N. Leontiev). Besides, in his capacity as scientific secretary of the Institute, Luria wrote an overview chapter titled “Moscow
State Institute of Experimental Psychology in 1924” that comprised the whole last
section of the book (i.e. VI. Scientific chronicle). These circumstances allow us to
assume that the publication of Koffka’s paper, translated into Russian, took place in
connection with Vygotsky’s and Luria’s foreign trips of 1925 and upon their initiative. This assumption is partially confirmed by the fact that Koffka’s paper came out
with Vygotsky’s introduction (Vygotskii, 1926; Vygotsky, 1993a). This introduction
gives us an opportunity to assess the intended meaning of this ‘imported publication’ from the perspective of the Soviet editors of the Russian volume.

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 207

In his introduction, Vygotsky presented the author of the paper as “one of the
most prominent representatives” of a relatively new current – and the “most influential and interesting of all currents” – in contemporary Western psychological
theory, “the so-called Gestalt-psychologie,” or “the theory of form, the psychology of form, structural psychology, as it is usually translated into other languages”
(Vygotsky, 1993a, p. 82). The meaning and importance of this new psychological
theory, as Vygotsky claimed in the idiosyncratic Bolshevik newspeak of his time, is
in its contribution to the “most intense and acute war in which everything alive
in psychology is involved” (Vygotsky, 1993a, p. 81). This is the war against vulgar
empiricism, spiritualistic and idealistic subjectivism, vitalism, physiological positivism, and reductionism. This war is characteristic not only of the seemingly marginal
and exotic Marxist psychology in the making in the Soviet Union, but even for the
most advanced psychological theories in the West. In this paper,Vygotsky mentions
American behaviorism as one such advanced theory at the time of its inception
in the 1910s and notes Russian psychologists’ temporary alliance with this theory; however, he argues that the time has virtually come for decisive separation
from these former “fellow-travelers” in the struggle for the truly dialectical and
non-reductionist psychology of biosocial and cultural development:
Being yesterday’s allies in the general war against subjectivism and empiricism,
they may turn out to be our enemies of tomorrow – in the struggle for the
establishment of the foundations of the social psychology of societal man, for
the liberation of the psychology of man from its biological captivity, and for
the return of its meaning as an independent science rather than one of the
chapters of comparative psychology. In other words, as soon as we turn to
the construction of psychology as the science of the behavior of social man,
rather than that of a higher mammal, the dividing line with yesterday’s allies
clearly takes shape.
(Vygotsky, 1993a, p. 81)
This publication of a Western, ‘bourgeois’ author’s paper in a collection of works
by researchers affiliated with the Moscow Institute of Psychology was somewhat
strange, but appears to have performed a quite definite communicative purpose.
Koffka’s paper in all likelihood was supposed to demonstrate to its readers that
psychology as a scientific discipline in the Soviet Union and this discipline in the
West were facing very similar problems and were passing through similar phases in
their historical development in their opposition to various reductionist psychological theories and the extremes of spiritualism, vitalism, and mechanicism. In his
preface Vygotsky argues that the actual problems of Soviet Marxist science were in
fact a part of the more general, global process that also involved the most progressive Western theories. Thus, Marxist psychology in the USSR and German Gestalt
psychology, according to Vygotsky, developed in parallel towards a really new and
revolutionary psychological theory:

208

Anton Yasnitsky

[O]bjective immanent driving forces of the development of psychological science act
in the same direction as the Marxist reform of psychology. To see this we just have
to stop looking at the developing crisis in psychology through the narrow
window of our debate with the empiricists and view it on the scale of international science.
(Vygotsky, 1993a, p. 83)
In his introduction to this publication Vygotsky predicted further divergence
between Soviet psychology and Gestalt theory that, in his assessment of the
mid-1920s, “will grow as both currents develop.” This prediction turned out to be
wrong. It appeared that if not all Soviet psychology, then the ‘Vygotsky–Luria circle’ at least continuously converged with the group of Gestalt psychologists, their
students, and followers during the second half of the 1920s and, particularly, in the
1930s. Alexander Luria played a very important role in this process as one of the
most important and active scientific brokers between Western and Soviet scholars
from the 1920s until the last days of his life.
Therefore, the beginning of the encounters and exchanges between the two
groups of researchers was in 1925. Subsequently, international connections between
Soviet and German-American Gestalt psychologists developed and intensified.Thus,
the translation of a Gestaltist scholar’s work into Russian was followed by the publication of Alexander Luria’s paper, in German, in the Gestaltist journal Psychologische
Forschung (A. R. Luria, 1929a). The European trips of Luria and Vygotsky of 1925
were soon followed by a Gestaltist scholar’s visit to Soviet Russia. This scholar was
Kurt Gottschaldt (1902–91), a former student of Max Wertheimer’s who defended
his doctoral dissertation in Berlin in 1926 (Ash, 1995) and visited the USSR in the
second half of the 1920s, most likely in 1926/7.5 During his visit Gottschaldt made
official scholarly presentations. Vygotsky accompanied Gottschaldt on his Soviet
trip – specifically, in Leningrad – and was so impressed by this meeting with the
German scholar and his research that he recalled Gottschaldt’s work several years
later, in lectures delivered in the spring of 1932 at the Herzen Leningrad State
Pedagogical Institute.6
And yet, it seems that full-scale personal and scholarly contacts and interactions
between the Gestaltists and the Soviet researchers of the Vygotsky–Luria group did
not take place until 1929. However, by the end of the 1920s, the situation dramatically changed, apparently to a large extent due to the impact of the IX International
Congress of Psychology that was held in the United States in September 1929.

1929–1930: IX International Congress of Psychology
and the “holistic revolution”
The IX International Congress of Psychology, the first psychological congress outside Europe, was organized in the United States in 1929, and Luria took part in it.
Thus, his second – and the last prewar – foreign trip lasted several months, from

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 209

August–October 1929, during which he attended the Congress in September, for
a month or so traveled along the East Coast of the United States, and on the way
back to Soviet Russia relatively briefly stayed in Germany, where he met his peers,
Gestalt psychologists (for a reconstruction of Luria’s trip of 1929 see Yasnitsky,
2012h).
The Vygotsky–Luria group prepared two papers to be presented at the
Congress: first, Luria’s presentation on the “new method of expressive motor reactions in studying affective traces” (A. R. Luria, 1930c); second, a co-authored work
by Vygotsky and Luria on “egocentric speech” and their research method of “double stimulation” (Vygotsky & Luria, 1930). For some reason or other Vygotsky did
not attend the congress, so it was Luria who presented both papers in the United
States. The Congress was held between September 1 and 7, 1929, and in order to
get to the Congress for its beginning Luria left the Soviet Union in mid-August.
In principle, he could have met his peers in Germany on the way to America,
yet this is quite unlikely given that Luria described the European part of the trip
as a “Moscow-Paris-Ocean” transit, which was “furthermore, so fast and hectic”
(E. A. Luria, 1994, p. 46). Nevertheless, Luria most certainly met his Gestaltist peers
at the Congress, attended, among others, by Wolfgang Köhler, Kurt Lewin, and
Kurt Koffka. Thus, Luria was bound to meet Lewin on September 4, when his
co-authored paper “The function and fate of egocentric speech” was scheduled
for the same session as Lewin’s paper “Die Auswirkung von Umweltkräften” (The
impact of the forces of environment) (Lewin, 1930).7 Lewin presented in German
and although, possibly, not everyone in the audience could understand and appreciate all the nuances of Lewin’s theorizing, the presentation turned out quite a success
due to Lewin’s demonstration of his film illustrating all key points of his presentation and his theory (Lück & van Elteren, 1990).
We still do not know when and under what circumstances Luria and Kurt Koffka
first met. Thus, from 1924 Koffka mostly worked and resided outside Germany,
chiefly in the United States. At the time of the IX International Congress of
Psychology, in 1929, Koffka held a professorship at Smith College in Northampton,
MA.Yet, it is known that in 1929 they were acquainted, shared accommodation in
Worcester, MA, and even made several short trips around the place.8 At the end
of his North American trip in mid-October Luria left for Europe9 and from late
October he was in the Old World. If his trip developed in accordance with the
earlier plans, then from October 25 to November 5 Luria was in Berlin, where he
anticipated meeting his fellow psychologists and noted Soviet film director Sergei
Eisenstein, who was traveling across Europe at that time.10
The new boost that was given to the interconnections between the Soviet and
the German-American Gestaltist scholars at the Congress contributed to the establishment of the transnational informal network of researchers that fully developed
in the 1930s. The immediate outcome of the activities of this network was a series
of ‘imported’ publications in the Soviet Union that gave considerable exposure to
the ideas of the Gestalt theorists in Russian. Among these Russian publications are
Koffka’s lecture of 1932 that came out in the Soviet journal Psikhologiia in 1932

210

Anton Yasnitsky

(Koffka, 1932) and translations of important Gestaltist books such as The mentality of apes by Wolfgang Köhler (Keler, 1930) and Kurt Koffka’s book on child and
developmental psychology (Koffka, 1934). The former was translated from German
by two of Vygotsky’s close associates at the Defectological Institute: L. V. Zankov
and I. M. Solov’ev.The book came out under Vygotsky’s editorship and was supplemented with his introductory chapter (Vygotskii, 1930a), which is, in fact, virtually
identical to his other paper that had been published as a journal article a year before
(Vygotskii, 1929b). Then, Koffka’s book came out as an “authorized translation by
R. and B. Levin” – the first translator most likely being Roza Levina, yet another
of Vygotsky’s and Luria’s students and collaborators – proofread by Luria11 and
prefaced by Vygotsky (Vygotskii, 1934c). These introductions were complemented
by yet another of Vygotsky’s publications, with a popular exposition of Gestalt
psychology in Russian (Vygotskii, 1930c). This was his book chapter of 1930 titled
“Strukturnaia psikhologiia” (Structural psychology): notably, the Russian word
struktura (structure) was the quite usual translation of the German word Gestalt
in Vygotsky’s writings of that period. Subsequently, Luria briefly negotiated the
Russian translation and publication in the Soviet Union of Kurt Koffka’s and Kurt
Lewin’s later works such as their books of the mid-1930s (Koffka, 1935; Lewin,
1935a, 1936). These projects, though, were never to materialize.
And yet, perhaps the most immediate effect of Luria’s exchanges during the IX
International Congress of Psychology was a relatively short-lived but fairly intensive
research project that Luria most energetically and enthusiastically launched upon
his return home after the Congress. This project had two roots. First, Kurt Lewin’s
convincing demonstration of the promise and perspectives of video-capturing technology in psychological research (Lück & van Elteren, 1990; Van Elteren, 1992).
Second, Luria’s friendship with the Soviet movie director Sergei Eisenstein and
involvement in his quest for a psychological theory in the practice and emerging
theory of cinema production (Bulgakowa, 2001). It is at the intersection of these
two influences that Luria’s new project was founded as a double-sided research on
psychology for the cinema and on the cinema for psychology.The impact of psychology on Sergei Eisenstein and his cinematographic experiments has already been
presented elsewhere (Bulgakowa, 2014) and is beyond the scope of this discussion.
On the other hand, the impact of film production on the development of Soviet
psychology remains far from clear to this day.
Thus, as early as at the end of 1929 Luria initiated the establishment of an international cinema association and international exchange of films produced for the
purposes of psychological research. Luria informed Lewin that he and his associates
had founded a laboratory at the Institute of Cinematography in Moscow. According
to Luria, this meant that apart from the necessity of delivering lectures and supervising student film directors’ projects, they had a valuable opportunity to produce
interesting new films.12 In this context, Luria planned to establish film exchanges
between Soviet and German psychologists and even announced the first series of
films to be sent to Germany that he produced with his collaborators in their studies
of the development of attention and arithmetic thinking in children. In turn, Luria

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 211

was hoping to receive from Lewin the films demonstrating Gestaltist research on
apes – most likely those produced by Köhler in the course of his research on the
Primate Station in Tenerife – and Lewin’s movie that showed the little girl Hanna,
who attempted to sit down on a stone, which would require turning her back to
the object. The two tasks performed simultaneously posed a notable and virtually
insurmountable problem for the little girl, which was a major point in Lewin’s
‘topological’ theorizing. This international exchange of films was a far from trivial matter, apparently, for censorship reasons. Thus, although Luria as of early 1930
did manage to obtain official permission to export video materials from the Soviet
Union we still have no evidence that anything was in fact sent across the border
in either direction.13 In any case, film production appears to have become one of
the important research tools for the Vygotsky–Luria group. Thus, for instance, as
of 1934 a laboratory of film production was a structural unit within the newly
founded Department of Psychology at the Ukrainian Psychoneurological Academy
in Kharkov (Soviet Socialist Republic of Ukraine), led by its Director, Alexander
Luria (Galperin, 1934). No documentary traces of any of these films – reportedly
produced during experimental studies by the Vygotsky–Luria group – are known of
to date. And yet, several still-frame pictures have been published in at least a couple
of sources (A. N. Leontiev, 1931, pp. 85–86, 160, 167;Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996,
p. 158): pictures that might have been taken from such films, possibly, yet to be
discovered.
In the context of this discussion of the possible influences on Luria’s (and,
probably, Vygotsky’s) interest in the potential of film equipment in psychological
research it seems that the name of yet another an innovative researcher and thinker
should be mentioned, besides those of Eisenstein and Lewin. This is Nikolai
Aleksandrovich Bernstein (1896–1966), who considerably enriched both the theory and experimental methods of the Vygotsky–Luria group’s research. Bernstein,
a mathematician and physiologist, investigated human movements in the natural
settings of human functioning in the context of personally meaningful and socially
rewarded activity. Bernstein considerably revised the foundations of psychophysiological research and designed a few original and innovative research methods that
allowed him and his collaborators, using movie production technologies, to capture
human movement with high precision and at extremely short time intervals. We
have certain reasons to assume that Bernstein was also instrumental in Luria’s (and,
presumably,Vygotsky’s) efforts to bring together film production and psychological
research. The evidence in support of this hypothesis is Vygotsky’s photography,
likely taken after an experimental study that involved his wife and elder daughter
Gita. This photograph was published in his daughter’s reminiscences interspersed
with historiographical research by Tamara Lifanova. The caption under the picture reads: “After the conclusion of an experiment. (An image from a film produced by Nikolai Aleksandrovich Bernstein. Study participant – the daughter of
Lev Semenovich [Vygotsky] – on her mother’s lap)” (Vygodskaya & Lifanova,
1996, p. 159). Neither the source of the picture nor the location of the film is
presently known.

212

Anton Yasnitsky

And yet, apart from Luria’s enthusiasm for film production techniques for
the purposes of psychological research, the experience of participation in the IX
International Congress of Psychology had a considerably more profound impact
on the entire course of subsequent empirical research and theorizing in the group
of Vygotsky and Luria. It seems quite appropriate to characterize this impact as a
‘holistic revolution’ of the early 1930s.
Indeed, after his return from the foreign trip of later in 1929 Luria wrote and
published several reports on his experiences abroad, most notable of which are two
publications: a long paper on the current trends in contemporary psychology in
the journal Estestvoznanie i marksizm (Natural sciences and Marxism) (A. R. Luria,
1930b) and a shorter version, titled “International congress of psychologists in
America (September 1–7, 1929)” in the journal Nauchnoe slovo (literally: Scientific
word) (A. R. Luria, 1930a). In the first part of the latter Luria reflected on the
experience of his transatlantic trip and presented a detailed overview of the social
and research practices of psychologists in a number of leading American research
universities, such as Columbia,Yale, Harvard, and Princeton. The second part of this
paper is the author’s analysis of the state of the art in contemporary psychology as
represented at the Congress. Quite in agreement with the contemporary agenda of
scientific and philosophical discussions in the Soviet Union, in his analysis Luria put
the main emphasis on the contrast between the mechanist orientation of behaviorist psychology, dominant in the United States, and the new, holistic theories of
such researchers as Wolfgang Köhler, Karl Lashley – “perhaps, the most prominent
American psychoneurologist” (A. R. Luria, 1930a, p. 94) – George E. Coghill, and
Kurt Lewin:
All these investigations create a new conceptual system that is considerably
different from that habitual to the authors, whose work is grounded in the
mechanistic worldview.The notion of behavior as the result of a combination
of isolated reflectory acts is substituted in these new studies with a different understanding of behavior that is based on the conception of complex
dynamic changes, always more or less integral and structurally different at different developmental stages, distinctly differentiated, but necessarily dynamic.
This worldview that in psychology and physiology is apparently overtaking
the primitive mechanistic worldview has not fully developed yet. However,
the scientific findings that keep coming from various disciplines – from
physiology and neurodynamics, genetic [i.e. developmental] and pathological
psychology – lead us to a conclusion that international psychoneurology is
undergoing a serious crisis and, moreover, that it is starting to overcome this
crisis.
(A. R. Luria, 1930a, pp. 95–96)
Luria concludes his overview paper with a comparative analysis of the IX
International Psychological Congress in the United States in September 1929
and the I All-Union Congress on the Study of Human Behavior that was held in

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 213

Leningrad several months later, in January 1930. Not surprisingly, the author is fully
in favor of the centralized, state-funded, and planned Soviet science of the period
of the First Five-Year Plan.
The ‘holistic revolution’ that Luria discussed in his paper had an enormous
impact on Soviet science, which was undergoing the process of radical revision of
its philosophical, theoretical, and methodological foundations in 1929–32, urged
by the struggle (initiated by the Party leadership) on two fronts: against reductionist mechanism and overly abstract ‘menshevizing idealism’, alienated from everyday
social life (Stalin’s terminological coinage). Thus, the ‘holistic revolution’ that Luria
imported into the Soviet Union after the IX International Congress of Psychology
perfectly suited the current agenda of domestic scientific discussions and suggested
new pathways in the human sciences. Vygotsky’s case was no exception, and a
holistic mindset appeared instrumental in his effort to overcome the numerous
drawbacks and limitations of his theory that he acknowledged and on numerous occasions severely criticized (for a discussion of Vygotsky’s self-critique see
Chapter 4 above).

1930–1931 and later: migrations of German Gestaltists
At the beginning of the 1930s, the students of Kurt Lewin and other Gestaltists
from Berlin started massive international migrations. These migrations have not yet
been subjected to rigorous scholarly study, the exceptions being as rare as they are
laudable (Van der Veer, 2000b; Woodward, 2010). The reasons for these migrations
are numerous, including the Great Depression that to a larger or smaller extent
involved virtually all economically developed countries from 1929 until the outbreak of World War II; the complexities of the job market for women – even more
problematic: Jewish females – in Germany during the Weimar Republic; or the end
of the foreign job appointments for Soviet citizens (in diplomatic, trade, etc. organizations), who were the spouses of some students and collaborators of Kurt Lewin’s.
As a result of all these tribulations, one group of Lewin’s students had finished their
studies in Berlin and moved to the British Mandate of Palestine (in what is today
the State of Israel): thus, for instance, Sara Fajans (alias Sara Fajans-Glück) is known
to have moved to the future Israel as early as the 1930s.14 Under yet unknown circumstances a number of Lewin’s other students made the same life choice, so that by
the mid-1960s a group of Lewinians were settled in Israel. This group included – in
addition to Sara Fajans – Sara Fohrer, Sarah Schlossberg (alias Sliosberg), and Wera
Mahler.15
The group of Lewin’s students in Berlin included a number of female students
of Russian origin who conducted their doctoral research studies in the second
half of the 1920s and published them in Psychologische Forschung in German
(Birenbaum, 1930; Dembo, 1931; Ovsiankina, 1928; Zeigarnik, 1927). None of
the Russian-speaking associates of Lewin moved to Israel.Yet, for different reasons,
all Lewin’s ‘Russian’ students relocated from Germany to other countries: Tamara

214

Anton Yasnitsky

Dembo (in 1929 to the Netherlands, in 1930 to the USA), Gita Birenbaum (in
1930 to the USSR), Maria Ovsiankina (alias Rickers-Ovsiankina: in 1930, USA),
Nina Kaulina (in 1930, USSR), and Bluma Zeigarnik (in summer of 1931, USSR).
Thus, from the beginning of the 1930s the core of the American ‘Russian group’ of
Lewin’s students was formed by Dembo and Rickers-Ovsiankina. They were soon
