joined by another female psychologist of Russian origin and with a German doctoral degree in psychology (from the University of Jena), Eugenia Hanfmann.
Hanfmann was destined to become one of the most notable figures in the
imported versions of Gestalt psychology and the ‘Vygotskiana’ in America.Thus, not
only did she work together with Tamara Dembo in Kurt Koffka’s research laboratory at Smith College upon their arrival in the United States in 1930–32, but also –
as a colleague, friend, and co-author of Lewin’s two ‘Russian’ German-American
students – she collaborated in several their studies and publications in the Gestaltist
vein (Dembo & Hanfmann, 1933, 1934, 1935; Hanfmann, Rickers-Ovsiankina, &
Goldstein, 1944). At the same time, she was instrumental in the dissemination of
Vygotskian ideas. Hanfmann, along with another American scholar of Russian origin, the medical doctor and psychiatrist Jacob Kasanin, published a series of studies
that followed and further developed Vygotsky’s research in the USA (Hanfmann
& Kasanin, 1937, 1942; Kasanin, 1944; Kasanin & Hanfmann, 1938). In addition,
Hanfmann was one of the very first translators of Vygotsky’s works into English
(Vygotsky, 1939, 1962).
Perhaps, another name should be mentioned in this context. This is another
German-American psychologist of Russian origin, Alexander Mintz (his wife, Vera
Efron-Mintz, an instructor in Russian at Smith College, was also of Russian origin).
Alexander Mintz was the last of Kurt Koffka’s students in Germany, and defended his
dissertation in the University of Giessen (Mintz, 1928) and then followed Koffka to
the United States in order to become his collaborator and assistant at Koffka’s newly
founded laboratory at Smith College as early as the winter of 1927/8.16 Koffka and
Mintz’s – as well as Koffka and Hanfmann’s – studies “from the Research Laboratory
of Smith College” were published in English in the Gestaltist journal Psychologische
Forschung in 1930s (Koffka & Hanfmann, 1937; Koffka & Mintz, 1931). Subsequently,
during the Cold War era, Mintz was also known as one of the ‘scientific brokers’
between the East and the West and was apparently considered among the leading
specialists in contemporary Soviet psychology (Mintz, 1958, 1959, 1962).
The group of former Lewinians in the Soviet Union was represented by Gita
Birenbaum, Nina Kaulina, and Bluma Zeigarnik. Birenbaum and Zeigarnik are
relatively well known by their publications. In contrast, Kaulina has remained quite
an obscure figure. Indeed, Kaulina belongs to a group of Lewin’s virtually unknown
students and collaborators who, presumably, pursued their studies under Lewin’s
supervision, but for unclear reasons either did not finish or never published their
works. We know about these individuals from Lewin’s overviews of his research,
most notably the final chapter of his English-language book of 1935, where he
mentions a few researchers (and the topics of their studies), such as Erfurth, Saathop,
and Wöhrmann (and their collaborative study on the “speed of satiation in normal

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 215

and feeble-minded children”); Köpke (“Substitute satisfaction in normal and in
feebleminded children”);17 Lange (“Action unities in the occupations of the kindergarten”); Rosenfeld (“The ontogeny of experiences of success and failure”), Ucko
(“An investigation upon the effect of prohibition”); Wiehe (“The behavior of the
child in strange fields” and “Die Grenzen des Ichs” [The boundaries of the ego])
(Lewin, 1935a).
Unlike many of these collaborators of Lewin’s, Kaulina is mentioned in his book
only once and the topic of her seemingly unfinished study is given only most generally and in relation to somebody else’s research: “Zeigarnik attacks the important
question of the firmness of form of nontense systems. (This question is directly
investigated in an unpublished work of Kaulina and plays an essential part in that of
Schwarz.)” (Lewin, 1935a, p. 244). A little bit more information about Kaulina and
her research can be found in a Russian book on Kurt Lewin’s theory of personality
authored by Bluma Zeigarnik, where in one of her last interviews she gives the full
name of the subject – Nina Nikolaevna Kaulina (Yaroshevskii & Zeigarnik, 1988,
p. 175) – and describes her work as a study, in Lewin’s words, of “nontense systems”
that, according to Zeigarnik, showed that “in those experimental subjects who only
observed the course of experiment on the interrupted task, but did not perform
any task, the phenomenon of better recall of unfinished actions [i.e. the so-called
“Zeigarnik effect”] was not found” (Zeigarnik, 1981, p. 43).
Birenbaum and Kaulina returned to the Soviet Union in 1930 and very soon
joined the Vygotsky–Luria group there, and are known to have led a series of
research group meetings in 1930–1 with a number of thematic presentations and
subsequent group discussions. Thus, Kaulina’s presentation “Psychological charge
and form in the process of remembering” took place on October 29, 1930. Soon
thereafter another ‘Lewinian’ lecture was delivered: Birenbaum presented her work
“On forgetting intentions” on November 9, 1930. Half a year later Zeigarnik
arrived from Berlin and virtually immediately joined this research group: her two
presentations were reportedly given on June 10 and 17, 1931. In these Zeigarnik
presented a range of studies that had been conducted by that time by the members
of Kurt Lewin’s research team in Berlin (Akhutina, 2012).
It appears that the arrival of these former students of Lewin’s made a considerable impact on the work of the Vygotsky–Luria group. A characteristically laudatory
account of the encounter with Lewin’s students and presentations of their research
can be found in Vygotsky and Luria’s correspondence, for instance, in Vygotsky’s
letter to Luria of June 12–16,18 1931:
Zeigarnik has arrived. At the laboratory meeting on the tenth [i.e. June
10, 1931], she presented a report on some new studies (Hoppe’s success and
nonsuccess, Sättigung, the switching of Spannung to other pathways – the
problem of Ersatz in satisfying needs). It was good. Refined. Clever. A bit of a
woman’s needlework.Very much in the style of Lewin. She’s going to present
another report to us tomorrow.19
(Vygotsky, 2007, p. 35)

216

Anton Yasnitsky

Moreover, upon their return to the Soviet Union, former Lewin students –
Birenbaum and Zeigarnik – found jobs at Moscow’s Institute for the Research of
Higher Nervous Activity20 and joined the research activities of the Vygotsky–Luria
group. The employment and future research options for Kaulina were also considered in the context of the discussion of Zeigarnik’s employment. According to
Vygotsky’s same letter of June 12, there were plans to create a large research team
that would include all former students of Lewin’s: “What about her [i.e. Zeigarnik’s]
work? She wants to go (1) to a clinic, (2) to an institute, (3) into pedagogical work …
She wants to work where we are. A true thought: Birnbaum [sic; recte: Birenbaum],
she, Kaulina + us – now that is a force to be reckoned with”21 (Vygotsky, 2007,
p. 35).Yet, there is no evidence of Kaulina’s participation in the research projects of
the Vygotsky–Luria group and their associates until the mid-1930s.
The closest contact and collaboration between Soviet scholars of the
Vygotsky–Luria circle and German-Russian psychologists in the tradition of
Gestalt theory started, thus, in 1930–1. Yet, this was definitely only the beginning
of a much larger process of mutual exchanges and transnational collaborations. In
1932 two events were particularly important from the perspective of the growing
convergence between Soviet and German-American scholars.
First, Kurt Koffka made a foreign trip from the United States, via Germany, to
the Soviet Union in order to take part in Alexander Luria’s second ‘psychological
expedition’ to Central Asia. Koffka’s study of optical illusions in the local population
considerably undermined Luria’s conclusion that “Uzbeks have no illusions” and
launched the Luria–Koffka controversy that first emerged in Luria’s brief published
presentation of the second expedition and Koffka’s report on his findings that came
out within Luria’s paper (Luria, 1934) (for an in-depth discussion of this expedition
and the Luria–Koffka controversy, see Chapter 8).
Second, it was also in 1932 that Kurt Lewin, following an invitation from
Stanford University, made his foreign trip to the United States, where he spent one
semester from mid-1932 until early 1933. On his way back home, Lewin decided
to make a return trip from the western coast of the USA to Germany via Japan
and the Soviet Union, the two countries where his former Berlin students resided
and worked at that time. Thus, Soviet scholars got an opportunity to meet Lewin
in the spring of 1933 when he arrived in Moscow. There is anecdotal evidence of
Lewin’s frequent contacts with a number of Soviet psychologists – Vygotsky, Luria,
and Zeigarnik among them. During his stay in the USSR Lewin allegedly gave
public presentations of his research and even demonstrated his films to the audience
(Vygodskaya & Lifanova, 1996;Yaroshevskii & Zeigarnik, 1988). There is no clarity
as to where Lewin was accommodated during his stay in Moscow, yet, according to
his telegram of April 30, 1933, he suggested return mail should be sent to Vygotsky’s
home address in Moscow (Lück, 2001, p. 174).22
From Lewin’s correspondence with his wife and colleagues it follows that he
was going to emigrate from Germany after the Nazi ascent to power on January
30, 1933 and the introduction of discriminatory anti-Semitic laws prohibiting
Jewish employment in governmental and state-funded positions such as those in

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 217

universities. It seems that in spring 1933 Lewin considered several options for emigration including the United States, the Soviet Union, and, presumably, Austria or
the Netherlands.23 Somewhat later, in the mid-1930s Lewin negotiated his move to
Jerusalem in order to take a position at the Hebrew University of Jerusalem (Bargal,
1992, 1998). Yet, only one of these plans was to materialize: Lewin spent several
weeks in Moscow in April–May, 1933, briefly stayed in Germany to pick up his
family, and left the country forever. From then the new period in his life and academic career is inseparable from the United States, the country where he stayed for
the rest of life (Marrow, 1969).
Like Lewin, after the Nazi rise to power quite a number of other intellectuals
of ‘non-Aryan’ – i.e. of Jewish – origin left Germany. Sooner or later virtually the
whole leadership of German Gestalt psychology moved from Germany, mainly to
the United States.These are Kurt Lewin and Max Wertheimer (in 1933, USA), Kurt
Goldstein (in 1933, the Netherlands, then, in 1935, the USA), Adhémar Gelb (in
1933, the Netherlands, then Sweden), and many others. In 1935 Wolfgang Köhler –
long-term Director of the Institute of Psychology in Berlin, 1922–35, an ethnic
German, and one of the founders of Gestalt psychology – left Germany for the
United States.

1930s: “holistic revolution” in a Lewinian key
The new, holistic paradigm in psychology – so much in demand in the Soviet
Union in the early 1930s – was represented by a number of theories, from which
the work of Kurt Lewin and his group was the important one for Vygotsky and
Luria. In his correspondence of the early 1930s Vygotsky clearly expressed his
excitement about the work of Lewin and the members of his circle.24 Indeed,
the influence of Kurt Lewin’s thought on Vygotsky was profound. On the one
hand, Lewin deeply influenced Vygotsky’s thinking in the sphere of the methodology of scientific research understood in terms of Lewin’s requirement for
the transition from ‘Aristotelian’ to ‘Galileian’ modes of thinking in psychology
(Lewin, 1931), that is, from an ‘instrumental’ to a holistic and systemic mindset in
Vygotsky’s theory. On the other hand, experimental practices in Lewinian studies were another major influence on Vygotsky and his group. These studies were
typically conducted by Lewin’s students in Berlin and were mostly published
in German in the journal Psychologische Forschung in 1927–33, including the
highly influential studies of Bluma Zeigarnik (1927), Maria Ovsiankina (1928),
Anitra Karsten (1928), Gita Birenbaum (1930), Ferdinand Hoppe (1930), Tamara
Dembo (1931), and others. Kurt Lewin succinctly surveyed all these studies of
his Berlin period in the concluding chapter of his first English-language book,
published in the United States in 1935, after his immigration from Germany
(Lewin, 1935a).
A number of scholars and research groups – such as the group of clinical psychologists led by Vladimir Miasischev, in Leningrad25 – demonstrated considerable

218

Anton Yasnitsky

interest in Lewin’s work,Vygotsky’s group being one of the most active and devoted
followers and, at the same time, critics of Lewin. Several lines of research in Lewin’s
footsteps done by the members of Vygotsky and Luria’s group can be discerned.
First, these are the studies by the former Lewin students Birenbaum and
Zeigarnik, who moved to the Soviet Union and joined the Vygotsky–Luria group.
These collaborative studies were conducted in clinical settings in Moscow institutions and a few of them were published in the 1930s in Russian. The studies of this
strand can be characterized as a more or less successful attempt at theoretical synthesis between Vygotskian and Lewinian ideas. It appears that, unfortunately, none
of these publications is available in translation (for an overview see Chapter 2).
A second line of research was pursued at Moscow’s Epstein Experimental
Defectological Institute (EDI; in the mid-1930s reorganized and renamed
Scientific-Practical Institute of Special Schools and Children’s Houses of the
Narkompros of the RSFSR). The only paper dealing with a few replication studies that was eventually published was I. M. Solov’ev’s study of will and emotions
in mentally retarded children (Solov’ev-Elpidinskii, 1935). The study was solidly
grounded in the Lewin group’s research and used the experimental method of
Anitra Karsten’s investigation of the phenomenon of ‘mental satiation’ that emerges
in monotonous, routine, and meaningless activity (Karsten, 1976). Solov’ev’s research
included three relatively independent studies that were conducted in 1930, 1931,
and 1932, respectively. This was the study that was presented during one of the
‘internal conferences’ of the Vygotsky–Luria group that was held in October, 1932
(for discussion of this topic see Chapter 6).
Solov’ev’s study was published as a book chapter in 1935.This book contained two
other chapters, one by L. V. Zankov, the other by Vygotsky.Vygotsky’s contribution
is particularly relevant and important in the context of this discussion. Vygotsky’s
chapter, “The problem of mental retardation” (Vygotskii, 1935c), in all likelihood
was intended as a response to Kurt Lewin’s article written for the memorial volume
for the Belgian pioneer of educational science Ovide Decroly (1871–1932) (Lewin,
1933), later translated and published in English as a book chapter (Lewin, 1935b).
Vygotsky’s chapter is of particular interest as it reports three series of post-Lewinian
experimental studies conducted by the scholars of the Vygotsky–Luria group.This is
how Vygotsky described the themes of these three lines of replication research: “In
the first series of experiments, we studied, as Lewin did, the processes of saturation
in activities for both normal and retarded children” (Vygotsky, 1993c, p. 237); “In
the second series of experiments, we studied, as did Lewin, the tendency to return
to an interrupted activity given unrelieved affective inducement. As he did, we
established that this tendency appears in no less a degree among mentally retarded
children than among normal ones” (Vygotsky, 1993c, p. 238); “Finally, in the third
series of experiments, we tried to study, in both normal and mentally retarded children, the character of the substitutions in affective tendencies which occurred in
response to interruptions of activities” (Vygotsky, 1993c, p. 238).26
This publication of Vygotsky’s is mirrored by Zankov, who authored a book
titled Essays on the psychology of the mentally retarded child (Zankov, 1935) that not

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 219

only provides a discussion of Gestalt theory and, specifically, Lewin’s scholarship,
but also focuses on a study by Lewin’s former student Paul Köpke, who investigated the patterns in resumption of interrupted action in normal and mentally
retarded children (see Zankov, 1935, p. 136). Interestingly, Köpke’s study has never
been published since then. Thus, the author’s familiarity with an unpublished work
by a Lewinian scholar is a clear demonstration of informal and personal contacts
between Soviet scholars of the Vygotsky–Luria group and their peers abroad and
yet another piece of evidence of their direct exchanges of publications and even
unpublished manuscripts.
Finally, the third line of Lewinian studies in Soviet psychological scholarship
was developed in the works of researchers in Kharkov, Soviet Ukraine, at the
Department of Clinical Psychology of the Ukrainian Psychoneurological Academy
under the supervision of Luria and his closest associate, colleague, and co-author
Mark Lebedinskii. The presentation of these studies, published in the second half
of the 1930s, was done in the best traditions of post-World War II, idiosyncratic,
Cold-War-period Soviet science. Indeed, as amazing as it may appear, the editors
of the translated volume 2 of the Collected works of Vygotsky acutely observed that
“there is no direct reference to any of Lewin’s works in any of the six volumes
in Russian and so there may be reason to believe that Vygotsky’s knowledge of
Lewin was obtained through secondary sources” (see editors’ footnote in Vygotsky,
1993c, p. 221). Although the editors were wrong in their assessment of Vygotsky’s
second-hand familiarity with Lewin’s work, their comment on the lack of references to the actual published works of Lewin in Vygotsky’s texts published in the
Collected works is quite correct. This tendency to avoid references to the original
sources is even further aggravated in the published works of the scholars of the
Kharkov group. It turns out that despite apparent borrowings from Western scholarship – for instance, as it follows from the analysis of the research methods of a
few works of Lebedinskii and Zaporozhets of the second half of 1930s that were
definitely based on experimental techniques of Karsten’s study of psychological
‘satiation’ – the authors not only never refer to the original source, but even fail to
mention the names of either Lewin or his student, who actually did the study and
introduced the method (see, e.g., Lebedinskii, 1940).
Interestingly enough, Soviet psychological research had a certain impact on Kurt
Lewin’s associates.Thus, for instance in his correspondence with Luria, Lewin mentioned the great interest that Luria’s, Birenbaum’s, and Zeigarnik’s studies provoked
abroad.27 In particular, Lewin alluded to Tamara Dembo’s presentation, in which
she reported these studies conducted in the Soviet Union.28 As a result of intensive
intellectual exchange between the researchers residing in the Soviet Union and
the United States, a number of references to Soviet research occurred in American
publications in the late 1930s and 1940s. These included references to Vygotsky’s
foreign publications on the topics of thinking, language, and speech that came
out under the supervision and in the English translation of Jacob Kasanin and his
associates (Vygotsky, 1934, 1939). Furthermore, the co-authored work by Roger
Garlock Barker, Tamara Dembo, and Kurt Lewin that was supervised by the latter

220

Anton Yasnitsky

and published in 1941 (Barker, Dembo, & Lewin, 1941) features a reference to
a publication in Russian: Vygotsky’s book Thinking and speech, which had been
published in the Soviet Union in 1934 (Vygotskii, 1934a). There is also evidence
of Vygotsky’s indirect influence on Kurt Lewin’s former students. Thus, according to the testimony of a former colleague of Maria Rickers-Ovsiankina from the
University of Connecticut, Ovsiankina – who was not particularly well trained in
the methods of post-positivist quantitative research of the North American scholarly mainstream – was, nevertheless, as a representative of a European academic tradition, fairly instrumental in introducing a few novel ideas that would later become
quite popular in America. In particular, the source reports that Ovsiankina familiarized her colleagues with the psychology of Vygotsky (Handler, 1995, pp. 174–175).

1934–1936: Vygotsky Festschrift that never was
Vygotsky, the leader of the group of Soviet psychologists and the legendary founder of one of the most remarkable traditions in psychological research in the Soviet
Union, died on June 11, 1934. With some delay Luria published in the West two
obituaries (Luria, 1935a, 1935b) and the provisionally titled Psychological development and decline29 or, according to other sources, Probleme der Entwicklung und Abfall
Psychisches Funktionen (Problems of development and decline of psychical functions).30 The earliest documents covering Luria’s negotiations with potential authors
that have been discovered are dated November 1935,31 but active correspondence
with the contributors is known to have lasted until the fall of 1936. Apparently, this
volume was conceived as an important and prestigious publication of international
significance that seems to have had no precedents among Russian psychological
publications. Indeed, Luria personally invited a number of leading specialists with
a request to contribute chapters written specifically for this occasion. As a result,
by April 1936 Luria appears to have received manuscripts from the renowned psychologists Frederik Buytendijk, Karl Bühler, Kurt Koffka, Arnold Gesell, and Kurt
Goldstein32 and was still waiting for papers from such authors as Adhémar Gelb,33
Kurt Lewin, Karl Lashley, and Jean Piaget.34 Max Wertheimer was also invited to
take part in the project and even sent Luria his paper.35 The whereabouts of this as
well as other papers that Luria presumably did receive for the collected volume is
not known to date, and their contents are of considerable interest to contemporary
psychologists and historians of psychology.
It seems that the last paper that was submitted for the volume was the chapter
from Kurt Lewin, which was originally mailed from the United States to the address
of the library of the Academy of Sciences of Ukraine,36 but eventually – with considerable delays and distractions – was forwarded to Moscow in August/September
1936.37 According to his correspondence with the author, Luria was impatiently
waiting for the paper as early as February 1936. The reason for Luria’s impatience
was obvious: among all the papers submitted for the volume Luria believed Lewin’s
chapter to be the most important, the central chapter of the whole volume.38 Indeed,

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 221

Kurt Lewin’s work was one of the strongest influences on the research of Vygotsky,
Luria, and their associates in the 1930s, strongly affected by Lewin’s holistic ‘topological’ theory in psychology.

1936: “Topologische Meeting, Moskau/Charkow”
Around 1936, Luria was planning to organize Russian translation and publication of
Lewin’s two new books – Dynamic theory of personality (Lewin, 1935a) and Principles
of topological psychology (Lewin, 1936)39 – but for some reason or other these plans did
not materialize.Therefore, of enormous interest is another of Luria’s unfulfilled projects of 1936 that gives us insight into the kind of research that was done within the
remnants of the Vygotsky–Luria circle after Vygotsky’s death in June, 1934. Luria’s
plan was to organize a psychological-topological conference in the Soviet Union
in the summer of 1936. The prehistory of this project begins in 1933 in the USA.
According to Kurt Lewin’s biographer Alfred J. Marrow, very soon after his emigration from Germany to the United States Lewin attracted a few colleagues, students, and friends – old, European, and new, American ones – who constituted an
informal personal network of Lewin’s associates and to a smaller or larger extent
shared his beliefs and ideas about the methods and goals of psychological research
(Lück, 1989, 2001).40 The group formed spontaneously in the course of organizing an informal meeting of like-minded individuals that took place on Christmas
Eve of 1933 at Smith College, where Kurt Koffka taught and headed the psychological research laboratory. Kurt Koffka, Kurt Lewin, Tamara Dembo, and several
others were among those few local and specially invited individuals who came to
Northampton, MA on the occasion of this meeting, which turned out as personally pleasant as it was informative and professionally rewarding for the members of
this small group. Thus, it comes as no surprise that the next year saw the start of
a new tradition of annual meetings of the group, which markedly increased and
included old and new participants (1934, Duke University). The following year was
a real triumph. In 1935 the Topological Group gathered at Bryn Mawr College and
included a constellation of bright scholars such as participants in the first meeting Lewin, Koffka, and Dembo, as well as the ‘newcomers’ Wolfgang Köhler, Erik
Erikson, Margaret Mead,William Stern, David Krech, Edward C. Tolman, and Fritz
and Grace Heider. Since then Topology Meetings – which remained informal gatherings of the members of this group of scholars not exceeding thirty-five–forty
members – became a regular yearly event that was subsequently held under the
aegis of a wide range of American universities and colleges, including Harvard (in
1936) and Cornell (in 1938) (Marrow, 1969, pp. 111–115). Apparently, the news of
the success of the Topological Meetings reached the Soviet Union, and their North
American peers’ example appeared very exciting and tempting for colleagues in
the USSR.
The first reference to the anticipated ‘Lewinian, ‘topological’’ seminar in the
Soviet Union dates back to the beginning of 1936. A postcard, in German, of

222

Anton Yasnitsky

FIGURE 9.1 A postcard in German dated January 25, 1936, signed by Luria,
Birenbaum, and Zeigarnik (all in Moscow), addressed to Kurt Lewin, Tamara Dembo,
and Maria Ovsiankina, and sent to the United States to Lewin’s university address (Kurt
Lewin Archive, at The Drs. Nicholas and Dorothy Cummings Center for the History of
Psychology, The University of Akron)

January 25, 1936 – sent from Moscow to Iowa, OH, USA and addressed to Lewin,
Dembo, and Ovsiankina – was signed by Luria, Birenbaum, and Zeigarnik, who
suggested that the next “Topologische Meeting” be held at their laboratory in
Moscow (Figure 9.1).41 An idea that originally might have looked like mere wishful thinking seems to have evolved and crystallized soon, and took shape as a real
and, presumably, manageable project.Thus, on February 20, 1936 Luria sent another
letter to Lewin that emphasized that their investigations of personality along the
lines of Lewin’s ‘dynamic theory’ were increasingly intensifying year by year and
were represented by a range of research topics and resulted in a series of interesting
findings. Luria mentioned the clinical work of Birenbaum and Zeigarnik and their
forthcoming major study on thinking and affect – and their impact on behavioral
dynamics – in conjunction with neuropsychological research. Luria also mentioned
the studies conducted by Aleksei N. Leontiev and his research team in Kharkov42
that investigated the structure of behavior and action of children and the changing
interrelations between goal, motive, and activity. All these studies gave them a nice
opportunity, argued Luria, to organize a small symposium that would focus on
the discussion of dynamic theory research and to hold a “Lewin-Konferenz” the
forthcoming June, 1936, that would feature presentations by Birenbaum, Zeigarnik,
Solov’ev, and Leontiev and his group.43 For some reason these plans, however, soon

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 223

changed and the decision was made to change the place (but not the time) of the
meeting and organize the small conference – the “Lewin-Symposium” – not in
Moscow, but in Kharkov, in June 1936.44
Among Lewin’s students, even after their return to Soviet Russia, Birenbaum
and Zeigarnik continued fairly consistently using the complex of Gestalt ideas in
their research in Moscow, therefore it is quite clear how their work might have
suited the program of the forthcoming ‘Lewin’ or ‘topological’ conference in the
Soviet Union in 1936. A few of their studies give a pretty clear idea of the kind
of synthesis between Gestaltist, Lewinian, and, on the other hand, Vygotsky’s ideas
they might have planned to present, for instance, such as those they had published
in 1934–5 (Birenbaum & Zeigarnik, 1935; Samukhin et al., 1934; Zeigarnik &
Birenbaum, 1935). The study by Solov’ev in Lewinian fashion is also relatively well
known. This was his replication study that he did in the early 1930s and published
in 1935 (Solov’ev-Elpidinskii, 1935) (see also the discussion of Solov’ev’s study in
Chapter 6). As for Leontiev and his group in Kharkov, there is not much clarity on
what exactly of the work of this research team was scheduled to be presented at
the ‘Lewinian’ conference, although several of those studies that were published
with a certain delay in Soviet Ukraine (in Kharkov) appear quite probable candidates for ‘Soviet Gestaltist’ or ‘Lewinian’ research (e.g. Asnin, 1941; P. I. Zinchenko,
1939). Another possible option for Leontiev and his associates’ presentation at this
Topological Meeting in the Soviet Union was a study that they conducted in
Moscow’s Gorky Central Park of Culture and Leisure, where Leontiev’s research
team included the former Lewin student Nina Kaulina, who seems to have contributed considerably to its distinctly Lewinian flavor. The study of the mid-1930s
was never prepared for publication, yet the preliminary report of the study was published several decades later under the names of the two co-presenters of the paper
(A. N. Leontiev & Rozenblium, 1999).
It appears that Soviet scholars had enough interesting studies to contribute to the
‘Lewinian’ Topological Meeting that was planned for summer 1936 in the Soviet
Union.Yet, it is not quite clear – furthermore, it is fairly questionable – whether all
these studies as a whole were leading to a productive synthesis between Soviet and
German-American traditions. The potential of their theoretical convergence might
be assessed only with the help of first-hand accounts such as Luria’s statement of
his theoretical and empirical research interests around 1936 – the year when the
Lewinian conference was planned to be held in the Soviet Union. One such statement was published by King and Michael Wertheimer in the context of their publication of correspondence of Max Wertheimer and Luria of 1936.Thus, in one of his
letters Max Wertheimer inquired about Luria’s current scientific interests and the
course of his research development. In response, in his letter of May 9, 1936, Luria
identified two major strands of his research (and, by implication, that of his research
group in the Soviet Union):
At the moment I am occupied experimentally with two questions; one
concerns the general laws of psychological development; we are interested

224

Anton Yasnitsky

primarily in how during psychological development the meaningful representation of the world (which we see in the meaning of action and language)
evolves, how in the course of the learning process there occurs a reorganization of the world of meaning and of psychological functions, and how
learning can lead to a reorganization of the interrelation of the psychological
functions.We have succeeded, with artificial intervention in identical twins, in
obtaining very interesting displacements of psychological structures.
The second [issue] in which I am primarily engaged is the destruction
of the world of meaning and of the construction of psychological functions
with brain damage. Here too we succeeded in finding methods of analysis of
the meaning of a word, of the living semantic syntax of language, which have
become a good indicator of the decline of brain capability and which show
a lawful deterioration with brain injuries. The pathological semantics, syntax,
and functional structures of the psyche constitute the second part of the problem field with which we are very concerned here.
(King & Wertheimer, 2005, p. 274)
The document presents quite a few typically ‘Vygotskian’ themes, such as the interest in discovering the “general laws of development,” a wide range of which are so
frequently discussed in a number of Vygotsky’s writings, and the focus on speech
and language, “psychological functions” and interrelations between them, “functional structures” and psychological systems, and the destruction of such composite
“structures” and systems that occurs, for instance, as a result of brain damage. In addition, the letter refers to the theme of “semantics” and “meaning” that seem to have
been equally important for Vygotsky (most conspicuously, in the second half of his
scientific career, i.e. in the 1930s) and the German-American Gestaltists: the latter
were always deeply immersed in the problems of meaning (alternatively referred to
as ‘value’, ‘significance’, ‘sense’, etc.), but for quite different reasons did not discuss
them openly until the second half of the 1930s (e.g. Koffka, 1935; Köhler, 1938).
And yet, no characteristically Lewinian ‘topological’ topics and concepts are
mentioned in this letter of Luria to Wertheimer such as ‘field’, ‘life space’, ‘vectors’,
‘forces’, or the like. Furthermore, no published theoretical work – ‘Vygotskian’ and
equally ‘Lewinian’, ‘topological’ – of a Soviet psychologist has ever come out. In the
absence of a general and, at the same time, sufficiently detailed exposition of the
theoretical meaning of the anticipated “Topologische Meeting” for the remnants of
the former Vygotsky–Luria group after Vygotsky’s death we are left with many open
questions about the actual content of this major theoretical synthesis, although its
potential for future psychological theory is beyond any doubt.

Epilogue: “Stalinist science” in action
What seemed to be “the beginning of a beautiful friendship” was to become its
end. Indeed, the summer of 1936 eventually turned out the dawn, the highest

“The beginning of a beautiful friendship” 225

peak, and, at the same time, the dusk of ‘cultural-historical Gestalt psychology’ in
its ‘topological’ version of Kurt Lewin. The correspondence between Soviet and
German-American peers came to an end in the end of 1936, followed by the radical decrease in publication activity of Soviet scholars for the period of two years,
the notorious Great Terror (or Great Purge) in the USSR. In 1938 a psychological
publications boom started in the Soviet Union, but no traces of the union between
‘Vygotskians’ or ‘Lewinians’ can be found in the publications of this period: both
Lewin and Vygotsky are only occasionally mentioned in the published works of
Soviet psychologists during the several years before World War II (see Chapter 3).
In 1939 World War II started, and the entire world order underwent major changes,
which had profound effects on international communications and the activities of
scholars all over the world.
In addition, the 1930s and 1940s brought about painful losses to Gestalt psychology, dramatically undermined by a series of deaths of its most prominent thinkers and advocates, such as Adhémar Gelb (in 1936), Karl Duncker (in 1940), Kurt
Koffka (in 1941), Max Wertheimer (in 1943), and Kurt Lewin (in 1947).
On March 5, 1946 the British Prime Minister Winston Churchill delivered his
famous Fulton speech reviewing the geopolitical status quo of the time and formulated the new reality of the post-World War II world in terms of the ‘Iron Curtain’.
Another powerful metaphor and related social reality, the ‘Cold War’, was to follow
soon. The era of the Superpowers and xenophobia started.
All these factors and processes considerably diminished opportunities for transnational projects in psychological research such as Luria’s initiatives of the 1930s.Yet
the project could possibly have been developed within the confines of the domestic
‘national science’ in the Soviet Union. One might wonder whether this were in
fact so.
In fact, it appears that the ‘Vygotskian–Lewinian’ trend in Soviet psychology –
although preserved in some very general sense – was considerably mutated and
transformed. Soviet psychological writings and publications of the post-World War
II era notably reduced the number of references to foreign sources and their historical theoretical roots. As a result, a few important ideas, theories, and experimental
practices were used and discussed without giving credit to their original authors, or
simply lost. On the other hand, Soviet postwar scholarship was enriched by occasional very interesting scholarly theories and innovations such as Nikolai Bernstein’s
so-called psychophysiology of activeness (aktivnost’). Generally, the whole psychological discourse in the Soviet Union underwent considerable change in its foundational terminology and fully assimilated the idiosyncratic slang of the quasi-Marxist
newspeak of Soviet science.
The mutant and hybrid that emerged as a result of all these transformations by
the 1960s overtook the entire edifice of psychological science and, under the banner of the so-called psychological ‘activity theory’, started its reign at the summit of
the academic Olympus in the Soviet Union.

This page intentionally left blank

Epilogue

This page intentionally left blank

10
“LOST IN TRANSLATION”
Talking about sense, meaning, and
consciousness
Anton Yasnitsky and René van der Veer

If we could conclude this book with a single paragraph, we would choose a brief
quotation from William Saroyan’s early essay “Myself upon the Earth”:
I love and worship life, living senses, functioning minds. I love consciousness.
I love precision. And life is to be created by every man who has the breath
of God within him; and every man is to create his own consciousness, and
his own precision, for these things do not exist of themselves. Only confusion and error and ugliness exist of themselves. I have said that I am deeply
religious. I am. I believe that I live, and you’ve got to be religious to believe
so miraculous a thing. And I am grateful and I am humble. I do live, so let
the years repeat themselves eternally, for I am sitting in my room, stating
in words the truth of my being, squeezing the fact from meaninglessness
and imprecision. And the living of this moment can never be eff aced. It is
beyond time.
(Saroyan, 1934)
Yet, unfortunately, we cannot do just that. Besides, the quotation would not make
much sense to the reader of this book. That is why a somewhat longer Epilogue
about meaning, sense, consciousness, and other very important issues is needed.
Contemporary psychological and educational discourse is very much overloaded
with mechanist, behaviorist, and cognitivist phraseology concerning ‘knowledge
transfer’ or ‘construction’, ‘conditioning’, ‘enforcement’, ‘storing’, ‘processing’, and
‘retrieving information’, multiple ‘intelligences’, and other related neurospeak jargon that is very much in fashion these days. As a result, it is highly problematic to
talk about ‘consciousness’ as well as other matters widely discussed, for instance,
in the European psychological tradition – or, for that matter, in related oriental
practices – without giving an impression of being ‘unscientific’, or being grossly

230

Anton Yasnitsky and René van der Veer

misunderstood. Some examples of such topics include Russian psikhika (psyche)
as the primary object of psychological research (cf. the fairly special and somewhat
derogatory meaning of the English word ‘psychic’ in its contemporary use, such
as in the case of ‘psychic reader’) or German Seele (soul), which, according to the
translators of a classic German psychological volume, they
had thought to translate [it] by “soul”, in the belief that the time was ripe for
a reintroduction of the latter word into the technical English terminology of
psychology. It seemed impossible that there should be any confusion of the
psychological “soul,” deduced as it is from concrete behavior, with the “soul”
of theology, the properties of which cannot be derived from or tested by concrete behavior. But a sampling of opinion among American psychologists was
against the use of this more accurate translation. It is consequently necessary
to point out that “mind” as here used (“the totality of psychical systems”) is
not to be taken in any narrowly intellectualistic sense.
(D. K. Adams & Zener, 1935, p. vii)
These lines were written almost eighty years ago, but in a certain sense the situation does not seem to have changed much in North American psychology since
then. Indeed, both psyche and soul still have fairly derogatory – or at best somewhat ‘unscientific’– connotations in contemporary English from the perspective of
what a rigorous scientific vocabulary is supposed to be. Unlike these two, ‘meaning’
(or, sometimes, ‘sense’) is often found in the pages of psychological publications in
English nowadays. It was not always so.
Around eighty years ago Kurt Koffka, a prominent German-American scholar
and one of the founders of Gestalt psychology, in his book Principles of Gestalt psychology (Koffka, 1935) reflected on more than a decade of the triumphant spread of
Gestalt psychology over North America. Specifically, Koffka discussed the three
foundational principles of the theory and, it seems, rather to the surprise of his
North American readers, confessed that one of the basic postulates of the theory
had deliberately not been presented to English-speaking audiences until then:
SIGNIFICANCE, VALUE. We turn to the last of our categories: significance. What we mean by that is harder to explain than the two previous
concepts, and yet here lies one of the deepest roots of gestalt theory, one
which has been least openly brought before the English-speaking public.
The reason for this is easy to understand. There is such a thing as an intellectual climate, and the intellectual climate, just as meteorological, varies from
country to country. And just as the growth of a plant depends upon the intellectual climate, so does the growth of an idea depend upon the intellectual
climate.There can be no doubt that the intellectual climates of Germany and
the United States are widely different. The idealistic tradition of Germany
is more than an aff air of philosophic schools; it pervades the German mind
and appears most openly in the writings and teachings of the representatives

“Lost in translation” 231

of “Geistwissenschaften”, the moral sciences. The meaning of a personality prominent in history, art or literature, seems to the German mind more
important than the pure historical facts which make up his life and works;
the historian is more interested in the relation to the events on the plan of
the universe than in his relations to the events on the planet. Contrariwise,
in America, the climate is chiefly practical; the here and now, the immediate present with its needs, holds the centre of the stage, thereby relegating
the problems essential to German mentality to the realm of the useless and
non-existing. In science this attitude makes for positivism, an overvaluation
of mere facts and an undervaluation of very abstract speculations, a high
regard for science, accurate and earthbound, and an aversion, sometimes bordering on contempt, for metaphysics that tries to escape from the welter of
mere facts into a loftier realm of ideas and ideals.
Therefore when the first attempts were made to introduce gestalt theory
to the American public, that side of which would most readily appeal to
the type of German mentality which I have tried to sketch was kept in the
background, and those aspects which had a direct bearing on science were
emphasized. Had the procedure been different, we might have incurred the
danger of biassing our readers against our ideas. Living in a different intellectual climate they might have taken this aspect of gestalt theory for pure
mysticism and decided not to have anything to do with the whole theory
before they had had a chance of becoming acquainted with its scientific
relevance.
At the present moment, however, when gestalt theory has been taken up
as a main source of discussion, it seems only fair to lift the old restriction and
expose all its aspects.
(Koffka, 1935, pp. 17–18)
Thus, it is North American pragmatism, positivism, and – in the field of psychological theory – behaviorism as the predominant mindset characteristic of this culture in general – as seen through the eyes of the German-American scholar – that
prevented the Gestalt theorists from openly discussing such suspiciously ‘idealistic’
topics as meaning, significance, or value. And yet, as of 1935, Koffka believed the
time was ripe to challenge these traditional views:
The positivistic interpretation of the world and our knowledge of it is but
one possibility; there is another one. The question is: Which is really true?
Meaning, significance, value, as data of our total experience give us a hint that
the latter has at least as good a chance of being the true one as the former.
And that means: far from being compelled to banish concepts like meaning
and value from psychology and science in general, we must use these concepts
for a full understanding of the mind and the world, which is at the same time
a full explanation.
(Koffka, 1935, p. 21)

232

Anton Yasnitsky and René van der Veer

Koffka’s attempt to openly use the concepts of ‘meaning’, ‘significance’ and
‘value’ as data of our total experience was concurrent with the work of his
long-time associates and collaborators, the founders of Gestalt psychology. Thus,
for instance, Wolfgang Köhler delivered a series of the William James lectures on
philosophy and psychology at Harvard in 1934–5 that were later released as a
volume, The place of value in a world of facts (Köhler, 1938). In a similar vein, Max
Wertheimer published a series of American essays in 1934–40 on the topics of
values and ethics; some of these were later translated into German and released
in a book with a special introduction by Albert Einstein and under the characteristic title Zur Gestaltpsychologie menschlicher Werte (On the Gestalt psychology of human values) (Wertheimer, 1991). Furthermore, one of Wertheimer’s
New York students later recalled that in his lecture that he called “Being and
doing” Wertheimer explored the ideas of the oriental psychologies of Taoism,
Lao-Tze, and Zen Buddhism, and subsequently “promoted his interest in the
role of values in human experience” (King & Wertheimer, 2005, p. 300): all these
influences seem to have notably contributed to the later work that this former
student of Wertheimer’s eventually published under the title Religions, values and
peak-experiences (Maslow, 1964), a book that is considered by many as highly
inspirational and influential, but hardly as a contribution to rigorously scientific
and experimental psychology.
One might wonder how successful the attempt by Koffka and his associates was
to reintroduce the phraseology – and conceptual framework – of meaning and
related notions into the Anglo-Saxon scientific and experimental psychology of the
time. In order to get a sense of the speed of progress in this direction let us skip a
couple of decades. The success of the Gestaltists in restoring meaning to its rightful place as a subject of psychological research can be judged on the basis of the
statements of pioneers of post-World War II cognitive science such as the legendary
figure in American psychology Jerome Bruner. Thus, in his book Acts of meaning
(Bruner, 1990) the author clearly states:
Now let me tell you first what I and my friends thought the revolution
was about back in the late 1950s. It was, we thought, an all-out effort to
establish meaning as the central concept in psychology – not stimuli and
responses, not overtly observable behavior, not biological drives and their
transformation, but meaning … Its aim was to discover and to describe formally the meanings that human beings created out of their encounters with
the world, and then to propose the hypotheses about what meaning-making
processes where implicated. It focused upon the symbolic activities that
human beings employed in constructing and in making sense not only of
the world, but of themselves. Its aim was to prompt psychology to join
forces with its sister interpretive disciplines in the humanities and the social
sciences.
(Bruner, 1990, p. 2)

“Lost in translation” 233

As this makes clear, meaning had not made it into mainstream North American
psychology by the end of the 1950s. In retrospect, the attempt of the cognitivists of
the late 1950s also seems to have failed.
Bruner hesitated to identify the full set of factors that impacted upon the reception of their theoretical innovation of the 1950s.Yet he mentioned two reasons that
might have predetermined the eventual failure of the project, as he assessed the final
outcome of the ‘cognitive revolution’ of the 1960s. First, the membership in the
new intellectual movement was too inclusive and too ‘tolerant’ from a theoretical
standpoint. As a result, a great many scholars of positivistic and neobehaviorist persuasions were included, which ran contrary to the original revolutionary impulse
of the entire project and in the long run contributed to its taming, domestication,
assimilation, transformation, and thus, consequently, failure. All these processes later
contributed to the perception of the ‘cognitive revolution’ as one of the “mythical
revolutions of American psychology” (Leahey, 1992). Second, Bruner mentions the
characteristic transmutation of the vocabulary used by the new movement. Thus,
somehow swiftly the original proposal of meaning-oriented research was reformulated – and eventually re-conceptualized – so that ‘meaning’ as the foundation of
new-born cognitive science turned into ‘information’, or ‘knowledge’. The whole
revolutionary enterprise – perhaps best understood as a “return to attitudes and
trends that were present prior to the advent of behaviorism and that were alive
and well outside of the United States, where behaviorism had not developed any
coherent support” (Mandler, 2002) – thus boiled down to, in the words of a noted
participant and historian of the cognitivist movement, “a contemporary, empirically
based effort to answer long-standing epistemological questions – particularly those
concerned with the nature of knowledge, its components, its sources, its development, and its deployment” (emphasis added; Gardner, 1986, p. 6). Thus, the mindset
so common to European scholars brought up in the tradition that values scholarly
speculation and puts great emphasis on value, significance, and meaning yet again
did not make it into the North American academic mentality.
This episode brings us back to Koffka’s discussion of cultural traditions in
the pre-World War II human sciences as seen through the eyes of a prominent
German-American scholar. Indeed, “the intellectual climates of Germany and the
United States are widely different.” In a similar vein, Bertrand Russell once famously remarked on the studies of animal intelligence that “one may say broadly that
all the animals that have been carefully observed have behaved so as to confirm
the philosophy in which the observer believed before his observations began,” and,
furthermore, “they have all displayed the national characteristics of the observer”:
Animals studied by Americans rush about frantically, with an incredible display of hustle and pep, and at last achieve the desired result by chance. Animals
observed by Germans sit still and think, and at last evolve the solution out of
their inner consciousness.
(Russell, 1927/2009, p. 34)

234

Anton Yasnitsky and René van der Veer

This remark appears as astute and ironic as it is pessimistic and desperate. Indeed,
the situation as Russell described it makes it look virtually impossible for a scientific
mindset to be changed from outside until the internal processes within a certain
academic culture cause it to change. And even then, there is no certainty that the
change involves deeper conceptual strata rather than represents merely superficial,
cosmetic changes in line with the fashionable phraseology of the day.
Indeed, meaning appears a somewhat vague concept for the empiricist and positivistic mindset that resists any “unscientific idealism” of the kind. The difficulty
of discussing the problems of meaning in the context of North American psychological research is apparent in the book Gestalt psychology and meaning by Willis
Davis Ellis (1930), who seems to have been the first scholar in the Anglo-Saxon
psychological tradition to raise the issue of the Gestaltist attitude to meaning as the
object of psychological research. In an attempt to provide a definition of ‘meaning’
that he made in the book chapter characteristically titled “Meanings of meaning,”
Ellis made an impressive overview of contemporary scholarly literature and put
together a list of such definitions and attributes of ‘meaning’, which occupied three
pages of his book. If Google Scholar is of any use in determining the impact of a
scholarly publication, then the single citation of this publication retrieved by the
Google Scholar search engine is a fairly representative reflection of the resistance to
these ideas and the need for theorizing in these topics as perceived by contemporary
North American psychologists.
Thus, meaning was “lost in translation” – the translation into “cyberspeak,” in the
words of the contemporary historian of cybernetics (Gerovitch, 2002).Yet, this was
not the only loss, and, perhaps, the other loss was even more profound and fatal. In
his recently published memoirs Bruner recalls the history of his encounters with
Luria and remarks:
I realized that one of the things that he was telling me about was a battle for
consciousness in Russia, the parallel of which was starting in the United States
in the Center for Cognitive Studies … But we were somehow wanting to get
back to a notion of mind.
(emphasis added; Cole, Bruner, & Sacks, 2013, p. 41)
Furthermore, even reference to ‘mind’ or any related ‘mental’ processes seems to have
appeared too extreme to some of the founders of the cognitivist movement, therefore, what they would refer to as the “jargon of behaviorism” (G. A. Miller, 2003,
p. 141) was gradually replaced with what eventually turned out to be a “cognitivist
jargon.” Thus, according to one of the leading figures of the new movement, “If
scientific psychology were to succeed, mentalistic concepts would have to integrate
and explain the behavioral data. We were still reluctant to use such terms as ‘mentalism’ to describe what was needed, so we talked about cognition instead” (emphasis
added; G. A. Miller, 2003, p. 142). Against this cultural background Bruner’s reference to Luria’s “battle for consciousness” is quite significant.

“Lost in translation” 235

Luria’s use of ‘consciousness’ can be best understood in the context of the theoretical work of Vygotsky. More specifically, of primary interest in this context is
Vygotsky’s unfinished – yet still apparently quite promising (Zavershneva, 2014) –
theory of consciousness, which he was developing during the last two to three
years of his life until his untimely death in 1934. In this tradition ‘consciousness’ is
quite distinct from ‘mind’ – not to mention ‘cognition’ – as it is often used in the
Anglo-Saxon tradition. The difference can be illustrated by the above-mentioned
comment of the translators of Kurt Lewin’s German works, Adams and Zener, who
warned that although ‘mind’ is used throughout the book, it is “not to be taken in
any narrowly intellectualistic sense” common in its use in scholarly English (D. K.
Adams & Zener, 1935). Consciousness, for Vygotsky, is best understood as a dynamic
phenomenon, an action rather than a state or an entity. Besides, although it is essentially related to language and speech, therefore, to word meaning, for Vygotsky in his
most mature period it is not word meaning, but sense – a special category, broader
than verbal meaning that he introduced in his theory in order to discuss the semantics of objects and actions with them – that constitutes the focus of his work on the
theory of consciousness.Therefore, from Vygotsky’s perspective, there is a very close
interlink between semantic, sense-making processes and the workings of consciousness. Overall, this is a fairly complicated issue to be addressed in English, therefore,
the idiosyncratic use of ‘consciousness’ and its derivatives in the Russian tradition
and its difference from any narrowly intellectualist and static sense will be further
discussed below.
The difference between the two – ‘mind’ and ‘consciousness’ – might appear
merely terminological and insignificant. Indeed, as often happens in science, words
may be virtually unimportant as long as they carry the same meaning. And, at the
same time, the pervasiveness of certain metaphors, phrasal constructions, and verbal
images may be indicative of foundational categories of thinking deeply engrained
in a certain culture and, therefore, resistant to any change. Thus, for instance, both
‘meaning’ and ‘consciousness’ appear to be fairly legitimate topics these days for a
discussion in a mainstream scholarly journal in the human sciences or at a scientific
conference.Yet, the traditional Anglo-Saxon intellectual climate and the intellectual
context of the use of these words and related phraseology suggest that the novelty
of these discursive innovations tends to be superficial rather than profound and
foundational. For an example of such an in essence fairly traditional – mechanist and positivistic – mindset consider the use of ‘consciousness’ in contemporary
‘brainspeak’ academic and media parlance.
Similarly, there is a great deal of ‘meaning talk’ in a range of psychological theories and related publications deeply influenced by semiotics, the science of signs,
for instance, within the so-called socio-cultural theories in psychology (Valsiner &
Rosa, 2007), or inspired by the humanist tradition in psychology and psychotherapy, such as Viktor Frankl’s logotherapy. Notable, however, is the former’s emphasis
on the symbols, signs, and sign systems that are primarily understood as larger social
and cultural entities, chiefly independent of any single individual, which places these
theories considerably closer to sociological thinking than to what we traditionally

236

Anton Yasnitsky and René van der Veer

understand by psychology. On the other hand, humanistic psychological theories
and related practices are mostly concerned with individual human beings as unique
and self-contained, and to a considerable extent independent of larger social superstructures. It is apparent that both positions present a fairly limited and one-sided
view of human psychological functioning as compared with the ideas – however
incomplete and theoretically underdeveloped – of European continental psychologists and the Russian and German-American scholars of the 1930s. Perhaps most
notably and explicitly, this integrative trend was presented in the work of Vygotsky
in the last period of his life and that of his circle, deeply and fundamentally influenced by the cultural and academic tradition of Gestalt psychologists and their
associates (Yasnitsky, 2012a, 2012c, 2012d, 2012i).
Yet another major problem of understanding, intercultural dialogue, and scientific innovation is presented not so much by cultural factors, but for purely linguistic reasons. Thus, the two notions that constitute the core of Vygotsky’s theorizing
during the last two or three years of his life, namely, ‘consciousness’ and ‘sense’,
provide a really impressive lineage of derivative notions – most of them widely
used by Vygotsky and some of his Russian associates – that allow us to effectively
operationalize these vague concepts and see these two ideas not only as presumably
static phenomena, but also as dynamic processes, goal-directed actions, attributes,
or qualities that may be represented, grammatically, by transitive verbs, passive and
active participles, adjectives, adverbs, and, finally, action nouns (roughly corresponding to the gerund in English), and quality nouns (for a list of Russian derivatives see
Table 10.1). Such a multitude of the derivatives of either ‘consciousness’ or ‘sense’ is
simply inconceivable in the English language. English-speakers must be surprised to
realize that a number of words and expressions that they come across in the translations of Vygotsky’s – or some other Russian scholar’s – published works, for instance
‘to make sense’, ‘to realize’, ‘to become aware’, ‘deliberate’ or ‘voluntary’, ‘meaningful’ and the like, in the mind of the Russian-speaker are perfectly connected in the
same semantic field and are closely associated with each other by virtue of all being
derived from either ‘consciousness’ or ‘sense’. For an English-speaker, the implied
interconnections that exist for the Russian speaker are typically obscured by the
language and the cultural traditions of its use, and remain, thus, “lost in translation.”
This poses yet another serious threat to any attempt to meaningfully and productively discuss these matters in English.
Interestingly enough, the two rows of words that derive from Russian soznanie
and smysl are far from parallel in their relation to the respective words in the same
grammatical category.Thus, root nouns no doubt denote quite distinct phenomena;
however, some of their derivatives in the Russian language reveal considerable proximity and may be used in everyday colloquial speech virtually interchangeably. Such
is the case of the Russian verbs osoznat’ and osmyslit’ that in certain contexts both
mean ‘to realize’, ‘to come to terms with something’.The same is true of the adverbs
soznatel’no and osmyslenno that may mean ‘deliberately’ or ‘voluntarily’. Further discussion of these linguistic matters is definitely beyond the scope of this chapter.Yet
it is worth pointing out that Vygotsky’s decision to introduce the category of ‘sense’

(and related phraseology) in his emergent theory of consciousness is not strikingly
novel or sensational for a Russian-speaker and in some sense it is already embedded
in the cultural norms of the use of the language.
In order to remove the linguistic stumbling block posed by the vocabulary and
cultural traditions of its use, a “phraseological toolkit” for the theory of consciousness
in English has been designed and was used elsewhere in the discussion of Vygotsky’s
theory of consciousness in the making (Zavershneva, 2014). Although the English
language resists the effort to fully and consistently reconstruct the amazing lineage
of the Russian derivatives, some progress has been achieved (see Table 10.2).
Some drawbacks are still notable. Thus, for instance, English ‘consciousness’ does
not have any direct derivative verb and keeps suggesting an image of a static entity.
Not much can be done about that from a purely linguistic standpoint. Another
example is ‘semantic analysis’, which might often be associated with semantics: the
subfield of linguistics that deals with linguistic analysis of meaning in human language. Still, in our firm opinion, this choice is considerably truer to the spirit of
Vygotsky’s work of 1932–4 – with its acute interest in the personal psychological
environment and radical departure from the research on external sign-mediated
behaviors of his ‘instrumental’ period of the 1920s – than the alternative of ‘semiotic’ with its direct links to contemporary postmodernist semiotics as the study of
signs and sign processes. Therefore, ‘semantic’ as an attribute of ‘analysis’, ‘structures’,
etc. should be preferred, especially so since in its contemporary use the term is not
narrowly confined to linguistics proper or, for that matter, to any special discipline
and is increasingly used in the sense of ‘of or relating to meaning or the study of
meaning’. Perhaps another problem is related to the pervasiveness of the word ‘sense’
and its ambiguous meaning in the English language as relating to perceptual (e.g.
the sense of smell), emotional (manifested, e.g., in such derivatives as sensual and sensuality), and intellectual (e.g. in making sense of the world) domains at the same time.
In contrast, ‘meaning’ in its actual use in English is in essence quite similar to what
Vygotsky meant by his smysl (sense) (see, e.g., Bruner, 1990), but it can hardly be
used in this function without a reservation due to the terminological difference –
fairly important for Vygotsky in the last two years of his life –between ‘meaning’
understood more or less strictly as a ‘meaning of the word’ in its narrow and special

“Lost in translation” 239

use in linguistics and the larger category of ‘sense’ that applies to a wide range of
phenomena within the psychological field of a person, such as objects and human
goal-directed actions with them. Thus, all these factors played their role in creating
the vocabulary of Vygotsky’s unfinished theory of consciousness in English so that
the links were preserved between the various terms denoting or relating to consciousness and its workings. As an apparently unexpected bonus, English-speakers
have the word ‘cognizable’, which does not find a direct equivalent in the Russian
language, but is a really nice addition to the vocabulary of the future Vygotskian
theory of consciousness in English.
Now, after this ‘linguistic preamble’ and all these terminological clarifications
nothing prevents us from productive discussion of the issues of meaning, sense, and
consciousness in psychological terms following the tradition of the great Russian
and German-American scholars of the past. And the future will show if there is
a need and demand for such discussion. And, even more importantly, whether it
will ever be able to advance further than merely speculative demagoguery, typical
examples of which are manifested in the quasi-Marxist, positivistic psychological
writings of some Soviet authors, or whether this discussion will eventually contribute to the operational, empirical psychological research and practice that is so long
overdue (Galperin, 1969/1995).

This page intentionally left blank

Appendices

This page intentionally left blank

Appendix A
BIBLIOGRAPHY OF VYGOTSKY’S
PUBLISHED WORKS
Vygotsky’s published works: a(n almost)
definitive bibliography
René van der Veer and Anton Yasnitsky

Compiling a bibliography of a scholar’s scientific works is traditionally perceived as an auxiliary and relatively low-prestige genre of academic writing, its scholarly merit and pragmatic meaning notwithstanding.
Also, preparing an authoritative bibliography of scientific works is a very time-consuming and laborious
process, which adds to the relatively low popularity of this type of scholarly research. All this might explain
the fact that despite earlier scholarly contributions of the kind, until now we have not had a reliable, comprehensive, and authoritative list of Vygotsky’s oeuvre. The present appendix is the first attempt to rectify
this situation and to present the rigorous and systematic bibliography of Vygotsky’s published works. In
compiling this bibliography the authors found a number of hitherto unknown publications and greatly
improved the accuracy and reliability of the references mentioned in previous bibliographies.
Several bibliographies of Vygotsky are available in the literature (for discussion of the variety of
Vygotsky’s known bibliographies see Chapter 4 above). The most impressive and comprehensive lists of
Vygotsky’s works can be found in Van der Veer and Valsiner’s book Understanding Vygotsky (1991) and a
series of Tamara Lifanova’s (née Shakhlevich) publications of the 1970s–1990s. Strictly speaking, Van der
Veer and Valsiner’s list as such – regardless of how meticulously and carefully it was prepared – was not a
bibliography per se. Indeed, the list is only part of the references section of the book; therefore, it is not
comprehensive and includes only those sources that the authors found relevant to discuss in their intellectual biography of Vygotsky.Therefore, naturally, quite a few written works do not appear on the list, which
is particularly true of Vygotsky’s earlier,“pre-scientific” newspaper and journal publications on theater and
literature. In contrast, Lifanova’s work gives the impression of an extended properly bibliographic work
that started sometime in the 1970s until it was eventually concluded with the fullest list of Vygotsky’s
works, which came out in two virtually identical versions in 1996. And yet, there are a number of problems with Lifanova’s bibliography – critically discussed at some length in Chapter 4 – that make this work
hardly a reliable source of information. The main problem with Lifanova’s list is the fact that it comprises
actual published works as well as archival materials. Given that comprehensive in-depth philological and
historical work was never done with the archival materials, we still do not know what exactly all these
diverse documents are in terms of their origin, whether they represent distinct scientific works (as opposed
to variations of the same texts, possibly published under different titles, if any), what was the chronology of
their composition and the social context of their generation and intended publication.
For this reason, at this point we excluded all unpublished and archival documents and limited our
bibliography to those works that were actually published in the interwar period, i.e. in the 1920s–1930s.
These were mostly published on Vygotsky’s initiative and with his authorization during his lifetime, but
the list also includes several posthumous publications that were not necessarily finalized by their author,
not to mention their unclear status in terms of authorial approval for publication.

244

Revisionist Revolution in Vygotsky Studies

The whole corpus of Vygotsky’s published works is divided here into several sections, according to
their chronology, themes covered, and the internal logic of a specific period.

Publications of 1916–1923
All of Vygotsky’s publications of the period 1916–23 can be divided into two major groups: those before
the Bolshevik Coup in October, 1917 and post-Revolutionary ones. Thus, the publications of 1916–17
typically came out in the weekly periodical edition of and for the Jewish intelligentsia Novyi Put’ (The
New Path), where Vygotsky served as technical secretary, and in the leftist monthly magazine Letopis’
(Chronicles), edited by the popular Russian writer Maxim Gorky and devoted to the topics of literary,
political, and artistic studies and the like. At the same time, Gorky also edited the newspaper Novaia
Zhizn’ (New Life) and Vygotsky appears to have published at least one article there. This was a paper on
the prominent Russian literary scholar and critic Yulii Aikhenval’d that Vygotsky referred to in his book
manuscript The psychology of art. In a footnote to the text of this manuscript Vygotsky mentioned several
papers that provided the basis for his book. Unfortunately, the posthumous publications of The psychology
of art apparently misrepresented the intended punctuation in this quotation.Thus, on a few occasions the
editors of the book seem to have mistaken colons for semicolons and vice versa. The intended punctuation is restored in our translation in square brackets:
Printed: in Gorky’s “Letopis’” in 1916–17 on new theatre, on the novels of [Andrei] Belyi, on
Merezhkovskii,V. Ivanov, etc.[;] in “Zhizn’ iskusstva” in 1922[:] on Shakespeare[;] in “Novaia
zhizn’” in 1917[:] on Aikhenval’d[;] in “Novyi put’” in 1915–1917.
(Vygotskii, 1968, p. 504; emphasis added)
With very few exceptions, the authors have all the entries of the bibliography in their possession.The
few pieces on the list that they were unable to retrieve (such as the papers “on the new theatre,” “on
Aikhenval’d,” and unknown publications of 1915 in Novyi Put’), are included in the bibliography, but
with tentative titles indicated in square brackets and in English, in agreement with Vygotsky’s statement
of their main topics.
The above-mentioned journals and newspapers were discontinued after the Bolshevik uprising in
1917, which naturally put an end to Vygotsky’s publications in these publications. It is possible – although
by no means sure – that Vygotsky virtually ceased publishing journal and magazine articles for a period of
four years, for we know about only two publications that came out during the post-revolutionary period
of the Civil War (i.e. from 1918 to 1921). First,Vygotsky’s essay “Revolution and theatre”, published in
a small volume of poems and essays on the problems of post-revolutionary art that came out under the
title Stikhi i proza o russkoi revoliutsii (Poems and prose on the Russian Revolution) in Kiev in 1919. We might
assume that this was the paper Vygotsky – possibly, mistakenly – referred to as his unknown publication
“on new theatre” in Gorky’s Letopis’ of 1917, but we are unable to either confirm or disconfirm this
assumption at this point. Second, his essay on Leo Tolstoy’s criticism of Shakespeare’s work printed in
the literary, theatrical, and artistic daily newspaper Zhizn’ iskusstva (The Life of Art) in Petrograd in 1920.
Subsequently, a major outburst of publishing activity took place in 1922 and 1923 with a series of
theatrical and, more rarely, literary reviews and essays on related topics that were published in Vygotsky’s
native town Gomel’. After his graduation in Moscow,Vygotsky moved back to Gomel’ and at some point
became a Bolshevik activist, working in a range of cultural and educational establishments, in particular
in the middle-level position of a governmental employee in charge of theatrical entrepreneurship in
the Gomel’ region. These publications started in the local weekly political and literary newspaper Nash
ponedel’nik (Our Monday) that came out in Gomel’ in 1922–3.It was discontinued in September 1923 in
order to be merged with another local title, Polesskaia Pravda (The Truth of Poles’e), where Vygotsky continued publishing his essays and reviews until his final move from Gomel’ back to Moscow in early 1924.
Another of Vygotsky’s publications is known to have come out in a literary journal, Veresk (Heather),
published on the initiative of Lev Vygotsky, his friends and associates in Gomel’, a new but short-lived
title that seems to have been discontinued after the publication of just two issues, both in 1922.
The authorship of a few of these publications is somewhat questionable. The reason is that many of
them were signed only with the author’s initials, for instance, “L. S.,” “L. V.” or even, in one instance,

“L. –skii.” What further complicates the task of identification of the real author (or authors) of these
essays is the fact that there were at least three people in Gomel’ in the early 1920s with this family name,
who were active in the field of arts and literary criticism and related publishing enterprises. These were
Lev Vygotskii (born Vygodskii) and two of his cousins: the well-known translator and literary critic
David Vygodskii and his brother Lev Vygodskii. Thus, for a while there were two people – furthermore,
of roughly the same age and from the same family – named “Lev Vygodskii” in Gomel’, the only difference between their full names being their patronymics: “our Vygodsky” was Lev Semenovich Vygodskii,
whereas his cousin was Lev Isaakovich Vygodskii. This might explain why “our Vygodsky” was likely to
have often signed his essays with the first letters of his name and patronymic, i.e. “L. S.” And yet, there
is no final certainty about the authorship of publications that were not explicitly signed by their author
with full initials (e.g. L. S.Vygodskii or, later, L. S.Vygotskii) and even less about those that were printed
unsigned. Given the multitude and variety of ways Vygotsky signed his early publications and so as not
to further complicate the issue we made a decision to uniformly preserve the traditional spelling of his
name as it is widely known in post-World War II Russian-language scholarship, namely as “Vygotskii,
L. S.,” which does not necessarily reflect the way a specific piece was originally signed.
The statistics of Vygotsky’s publications in the period 1916–23 are summarized in Table A1, followed
by the complete list.Yet, one needs to be quite cautious in interpreting the total numbers of publications
per specific edition or in a certain year since what these calculations reflect is the author’s agency and
social activism. No information on the length of these publications or their relative historical or theoretical importance, if any, can be found in the table.
