248

Revisionist Revolution in Vygotsky Studies

Publications of 1924–1933
This cluster of Vygotsky’s publications constitutes his main lifetime contribution to psychological science. We have every reason to believe that the vast majority of these texts were finalized and prepared
for publication by their author, and therefore represent his thought virtually as their author intended.
Not all of these publications are of equal importance, however. Thus, quite a few of Vygotsky’s works
were written to earn money to augment his miserably low salary as a university teacher. Those chapters or articles were of a more popular character and intended for a general and, often, barely educated
reader. They were probably quickly written and contain no original ideas. Indeed, on several occasions
Vygotsky extensively borrowed from existing publications by other authors. For that reason, Vygotsky
did not count these publications among his important theoretical works and never included them in
his bibliographies of scholarly publications (for further discussion, see Chapter 4). Another group of his
publications of this decade consists of programmatic or speculative papers, mostly of a political nature,
that arguably also are of minimal relevance to contemporary scholarly research in psychology and allied
sciences. In assessing the total number of Vygotsky’s publications of this period one should also keep
in mind that on a number of occasions Vygotsky used a technique that can be conditionally referred
to as “self-plagiarism,” meaning that the same passages were published over and over again and/or were
included – possibly, with minimal changes – in other, larger publications without ever mentioning the
original source. An example is Vygotsky’s (1930) Introduction to the Russian translation of Köhler’s
book The mentality of apes, which was identical to a journal article in Estestvoznanie i marksizm (Natural
Science and Marxism) published one year before. This idiosyncrasy of Vygotsky’s publication activity has
been noted on a number of occasions, but has never been subjected to thorough investigation. Until
in-depth research of the kind has been done we are unable to identify the number of original contributions published in 1924–33.
All publications of this period are organized chronologically and, further, divided into several categories that we somewhat loosely termed:
(1)
(2)
(3)
(4)
(5)

prefaces, reviews, entries [in dictionaries, encyclopedias, etc.]
proceedings
articles
book chapters
books.

A comment is needed on these five groups of publications as we understand them.
Prefaces, reviews, entries include a wide range of publications that are typically of relatively minor
importance in terms of their theoretical impact and scholarly merit, such as book reviews, information
letters, “responses to journal readers,” encyclopedia entries, an obituary, etc. Similarly, Proceedings include
abstracts of conference presentations, mostly very brief and relatively uninformative. In other words,
these are typically such publications that the authors of this bibliography would not consider worth
translating from Russian and republishing in a psychological volume unless for purely historical reasons.
That said, the group also includes a few fairly important theoretical works of Vygotsky’s that most often
came out as book prefaces. Generally, the length of a publication of this kind is a fairly reliable predictor
of its scholarly weight and importance.
Books as a publication category, perhaps quite counterintuitively, is the most problematic group of
Vygotsky’s publications in terms of its academic value and scholarly integrity. Indeed, judging by mere
numbers,Vygotsky appears to have produced a lot of books, which seems to indicate a high level of scholarly achievement.Yet a closer investigation of the actual publications reveals the striking reality: only a few
of Vygotsky’s books qualify as academic treatises with theoretical merit. Thus, the majority of Vygotsky’s
book publications fall under one of the following categories: (a) edited volumes (including those with no
written contributions by Vygotsky whatsoever); (b) educational materials – basic and introductory textbooks, readers, a dictionary, etc.; or (c) popular books written for the enlightenment of the general reader
in the post-revolutionary Soviet Union of the 1920s–1930s rather than for a scholarly audience. Finally,
the fourth subcategory of Vygotsky’s books is: (d) theoretical treatises, which generously includes collections of Vygotsky’s papers, written at different time periods and on different occasions. For specific titles
that belong to this subcategory see the following section of this bibliography. It is with a certain degree of

Posthumous (i.e. 1934 and after) and foreign publications
This section of the bibliography combines two different sets of Vygotsky’s published works: those that
came out in the Soviet Union around Vygotsky’s death in June, 1934, and his publications abroad. Despite
many differences, the two categories have something very important in common: they were promoted
by a third party (i.e. other than the author and the publisher), without Vygotsky being actively involved
in their publication. The most important names of these “third party” mediators between Vygotsky and
the publishers were Zhozefina Shif, Daniil El’konin, Mira Levina (all in Leningrad), Leonid Zankov,
Viktor Kolbanovskii, and Roza Levina (all in Moscow, after Vygotsky’s death) and Jacob Kasanin, Eugenia
Hanfmann and, in a somewhat limited way, Horace Kallen (in North America). An interesting trend in
Vygotsky’s publications of this period is the first release of his theoretical book-length treatises, published
posthumously in 1934–6 (see Table A4 and compare it with Table A2).
In the case of foreign publications, given that Vygotsky most likely never wrote anything in a language other than Russian, the most direct and evident consequence of the third-party involvement was
that of translating and editing. The terminology of Vygotsky’s works – as inconsistent and unsettled as
it was during his lifetime – is perhaps the most vulnerable component of his discourse, and since we
have no evidence that Vygotsky ever approved any of these translations before their publication abroad,
the third-party activities significantly undermine the reliability of these translated texts as presumably
authentic writings.Yet our bibliography of Vygotsky’s works published abroad before World War II demonstrates a record of eleven publications in five languages (see Table A5) that provides some preliminary data on the social formation of the image – or rather, different national images (cf. Table A6) – of
“Vygotsky” in different cultural environments in Europe and North America.
Furthermore, in the case of posthumous Russian publications the third party’s involvement was even
more profound. Not only is there no certainty as to the phrasing or even, more generally, the contents of
those posthumous publications – not unlikely they were substantially edited by Vygotsky’s former associates in charge of publication of his works after his death – but also the choice of the texts themselves

remains fairly questionable. Thus, there is considerable evidence that Vygotsky did not finalize a number
of texts that were published after his death, and even, in certain instances, did not intend to publish them.
This means that Vygotsky as an author (a) did not actually finish these texts, or, alternatively, (b) never
actually wrote them, which is true of his lectures and public presentations, recorded (i.e. typed up by a
secretary simultaneously with the oral presentation) and later published without the revision required
in accordance with the standards of written scholarly discourse, and (c) failed to enter references and
bibliography.
In sum,Vygotsky’s posthumous and foreign publications are problematic in many respects.Yet they
give us an important insight into the legacy of the Vygotsky–Luria group at the most advanced stage
of theory development.Virtually all of these publications are in our possession, yet in several instances
publications were reported in the secondary literature (including Vygotsky’s published works) and
remained outside our reach. In these cases the authors had to rely on their judgment – rather than on
the results of rigorous scholarly historical, bibliographical, and textological studies, which are virtually non-existent at this point – in making a decision on including specific publications, questionable
in terms of their authorship or actual existence. This implies, of course, that even this bibliography
will necessarily have to be updated as new data become available and that compiling the bibliography
is an ongoing process.
Finally, overall numbers of Vygotsky’s publications were counted and are presented in Table A7.

Appendix C
VYGOTSKY–LURIA CIRCLE
Key protagonists

Note: the list does not include some of Luria’s collaborators of the 1920–30s and those members of
the Kharkov group of researchers – as important as any of them might have been – who did not
work directly with Vygotsky or possibly joined the group after Vygotsky’s death, such as Mirenova, Anna
Nikitichna (1901–45), Rozenblium, Anatolii Il’ich (1900–38), Asnin, Vladimir Il’ich (1904–56), Zinchenko,
Petr Ivanovich (1903–69), Gal’perin, Petr Yakovlevich (1902–88), Yudovich, Faina Yakovlevna (dates of birth
and death unknown), and others.
Averbukh, Roza Abramovna (1883–1940) – medical doctor (Bern, Geneva, Kazan); along with Luria, a
member of Kazan Psychoanalytical Society in early 1920s; when Luria left Kazan in 1923, followed
him to Moscow where they joined Russian Psychoanalytical Society; later joined Vygotsky’s team,
and, in collaboration with Eidinova (see Eidinova, Marina Borisovna), conducted research on “hypoboulic mechanisms” (unpublished study, referred to by Luria in 1960); according to Vygotsky’s notes,
participated in the group’s research meetings in October 1933 with a presentation on the topic of
“Environment – experiencing (perezhivanie) – character.”
Bassin, Filipp Veniaminovich (1905–92) – medical doctor (Kharkov, 1931), along with Luria, Lebedinskii
and others worked at the Clinical Psychology Department of the Ukrainian Psychoneurological
Academy, where, following Vygotsky’s research on thinking in schizophrenia, he conducted a study on
the alteration of word meanings in schizophrenia (unpublished manuscript of 1935); in 1936 moved
to Moscow, worked in All-Union Institute of Experimental Medicine (VIEM); collaborated with
Bernstein (see Bernstein, Nikolai Aleksandrovich).
Bernstein, Nikolai Aleksandrovich (1897–1982) – prominent physiologist and psychotecnic, Vygotsky’s
collaborator at the Institute of Psychology in mid-1920s, with whom Vygotsky co-edited and
co-authored a textbook in 1927, and whose work was one of the main inspirations for most of
Vygotsky’s followers’ research from the end of the 1930s onwards.
Bein, Esfir’ Solomonovna (1906–81) – neurologist, defectologist, under Vygotsky’s supervision conducted
research on pathology of speech and perception.
Birenbaum, Gita Vasil’evna (1903–52) – former Berlin student of Kurt Lewin (see Lewin, Kurt), returned
to Soviet Union around 1930; under the supervision of Vygotsky conducted experimental and theoretical studies in clinical settings in Moscow in early 1930s, after Vygotsky’s death in collaboration with
Zeigarnik (see Zeigarnik, Bluma Vul’fovna) and Samukhin (see Samukhin, Nikolai Vasil’evich) published
several papers that creatively integrate Vygotsky’s and Kurt Lewin’s theoretical work.
Boskis, Rakhil’ Markovna (1902–76) – medical doctor (Kiev), in 1931 moved to Moscow; under
Vygotsky’s supervision conducted research on thinking and speech in deaf children at the
Defectological Institute in Moscow.

264

Revisionist Revolution in Vygotsky Studies

Bozhovich, Lidia Il’inichna (1908–81) – Vygotsky’s student at Pedological Department of the 2nd
Moscow State University (1925–30), member of the piaterka (“the five”) of his students; research on
children’s imitation in late 1920s (in collaboration with Slavina, Lia Solomonovna), various research
projects in Moscow, Kharkov, and Poltava in 1930s.
Daniushevskii, Izrail’ Isaakovich (1890–1950) – Vygotsky’s collaborator of Gomel’ period and in
Defectological Institute in Moscow, later, a relatively high-ranking official in the Narkompros of the
RSFSR and Director of Moscow Defectological Institute; defectologist, co-edited with Vygotsky a
posthumously published book on defectology (1935).
Eidinova, Marina Borisovna (dates of birth and death unknown) – medical doctor, neurologist; around
1930, in Vygotsky’s research team, conducted studies on degradation of mental functions in hysteria
and, in collaboration with Averbukh, on “hypoboulic mechanisms” (unpublished study, referred to by
Luria in 1960); later, research on cerebral palsy in children and its treatment.
Eisenstein, Sergei Mikhailovich (1898–1948) – Soviet film director and film theorist; peripheral participant of Vygotsky–Luria circle from 1925/6 onwards.
El’konin, Daniil Borisovich (1904–84) – former student and associate of prominent psychologist M. Ya.
Basov (Leningrad), Vygotsky’s student and collaborator in Leningrad Herzen State Pedagogical
Institute (in 1931–4); research on children’s play.
Fradkina, Frida Isaakovna or Iosifovna (dates of birth and death unknown) – Leningrad student and collaborator of Vygotsky; research on children’s play and speech development.
Gellerstein, Solomon Grigor’evich (1896–1967) – Vygotsky’s collaborator at the Institute of Psychology
in mid-1920s, with whom Vygotsky co-authored two textbooks in 1927; Vygotsky’s, Luria’s, and
Leontiev’s colleague at All-Union Institute of Experimental Medicine (VIEM) in 1930s.
Geshelina, Lia Solomonovna (1892 – date of death unknown) – medical doctor, member of Russian
Psychoanalytic Society (up to 1930);Vygotsky’s collaborator in 1930s; around 1930 conducted a study
of visual thinking and perception; in 1930s worked with Vygotsky in clinical settings.
Kaulina, Nina Nikolaevna (dates of birth and death unknown) – former Berlin student of Kurt Lewin
(see Lewin, Kurt), returned to Soviet Union around 1930, possibly took part in pathopsychological
studies in Moscow along with Birenbaum (see Birenbaum, Gita Vasil’evna) and Zeigarnik (see Zeigarnik,
Bluma Vul’fovna), after Vygotsky’s death participated in “topological” psychological research in Gorky
Park in Moscow along with A. N. Leontiev (see Leontiev, Aleksei Nikolaevich), A. I. Rozenblium, L. I.
Bozhovich (see Bozhovich, Lidia Il’inichna), and others.
Koffka, Kurt (1886–1941) – German-American Gestalt psychologist, whose research was a major
influence on experimental and theoretical work of the Vygotsky–Luria group; participant in Luria’s
expedition to Central Asia of 1932.
Kolbanovskii, Viktor Nikolaevich (1902–70) – medical doctor (Moscow, 1927) and philosopher by training, Director of the Institute of Psychology in Moscow (1932–7),Vygotsky’s supporter and the editor
of the first edition of his Thinking and speech (1934).
Konnikova, Tatiana Efimovna (1909–75) – Vygotsky’s student and collaborator in Leningrad Herzen
State Pedagogical Institute (1931–4), doctoral research on “The transitional stage in the development
of children’s speech (autonomous speech)” conducted under the supervision of Vygotsky and his
associate Levina (see Levina, Mira Abramovna).
Kotelova, Yulia Vladimirovna (1903–80) – specialist in industrial psychology; in collaboration with
Pashkovskaia (see Pashkovskaia, E. I.) and under supervision of Vygotsky continued studies on concept formation (1927–30) after Sakharov’s death (see Sakharov, Leonid Solomonovich).
Lebedinskii (Lebedinsky), Mark Samuilovich (1895–1980) – medical doctor, neurologist, Vygotsky’s and
Luria’s collaborator in Moscow (from mid-1920s) and in Kharkov (from 1931), where he conducted
and supervised a wide range of developmental, psychogenetic, and clinical psychological studies,
mainly on aphasia and schizophrenia.
Levina, Mira Abramovna (dates of birth and death unknown) – former student and associate of prominent psychologist M. Ya. Basov (Leningrad),Vygotsky’s collaborator (1931–4) in Leningrad Herzen
Pedagogical Institute, where she was Head of Pedological Department; edited Vygotsky’s posthumous
book Foundations of pedology (Leningrad, 1935).
Levina, Roza Evgen’evna (1908–89) – Vygotsky’s student at Pedological Department of the 2nd
Moscow State University (1927–32), member of the piaterka (“the five”) of his students; research on

Vygotsky–Luria circle: key protagonists

265

the planning role of “egocentric speech” (late 1920s), “autonomous speech” (early 1930s), and speech
pathology (from mid-1930s onwards).
Leontiev, Aleksei Nikolaevich (1903–79) – Vygotsky’s close associate, along with Aleksander Luria (see
Luria, Aleksander Romanovich), member of the troika (“the three”); research on the development of
mediated remembering and attention (late 1920s, under the supervision of Vygotsky, book published
in 1931); various research projects of the 1930s.
Lewin, Kurt (1890–1947) – German-American Gestalt psychologist, whose research was a major influence on experimental and theoretical work of the Vygotsky–Luria group; research supervisor of
Birenbaum (see Birenbaum, Gita Vasil’evna), Kaulina (see Kaulina, Nina Nikolaevna), and Zeigarnik (see
Zeigarnik, Bluma Vul’fovna); peripheral participant in Vygotsky–Luria circle from 1925/6 onwards.
Luria, Aleksander Romanovich (1902–77) – Vygotsky’s closest associate and collaborator, the co-creator
of the “theory of the cultural-historical development of higher psychological functions”; along with
Aleksei Leontiev (see Leontiev, Aleksei Nikolaevich), member of the troika (“the three”).
Morozova, Natalia Grigor’evna (1906–89) – Vygotsky’s student at Pedological Department of the 2nd
Moscow State University (1925–30), member of the piaterka (“the five”) of his students; various
research projects under the supervision of Vygotsky and Luria.
Pashkovskaia, E. I.(full name and dates of birth and death unknown) – in collaboration with Kotelova (see
Kotelova,Yulia Vladimirovna) and under supervision of Vygotsky continued studies on concept formation (1927–30) after Sakharov’s death (see Sakharov, Leonid Solomonovich).
Pevzner, Maria Semenovna (1901–89) – medical doctor (in Saratov), psychiatrist and defectologist, in
1931 moved to Moscow in order to study psychopathic disorders in children under Vygotsky’s supervision in Experimental Defectological Institute.
Sakharov, Leonid Solomonovich (1900–28) – Vygotsky’s student, and initiated studies on concept formation using modified method of Narziss Ach (1871–1946), presently known as the method of “double
stimulation” or “Sakharov–Vygotsky method.”
Samukhin, Nikolai Vasil’evich (dates of birth and death unknown) – researcher at All-Union Institute
of Experimental Medicine (VIEM), conducted a series of clinical studies on dementia, published a
research paper in collaboration with Vygotsky and Birenbaum (see Birenbaum, Gita Vasil’evna).
Shein, A. A. (full name and dates of birth and death unknown) – Vygotsky’s collaborator around 1930;
research on the transition from external to internal speech; edited Leontiev’s book Development of
memory (1931).
Shmidt (Schmidt),Vera Fedorovna (1889–1937) – the wife of academician O. Yu. Shmidt, active member
and secretary of Russian Psychoanalytical Society, worked at Psychoanalytical Institute in Moscow
(experimental psychoanalytical boarding school and research laboratory closed down in 1925), provided research data for Vygotsky and Luria’s Studies on the history of behaviour (1930), from around
1930 employee of Defectological Institute and a member of Vygotsky’s defectological research team.
Shif, Zhozefina Il’inichna (1904–78) – Vygotsky’s graduate student and collaborator in Leningrad
Herzen State Pedagogical Institute (1931–3) and Moscow Defectological Institute (1933 onwards),
doctoral research on thinking and scientific concept development in children, defended in Leningrad
in 1933, published as a book in 1935 with Vygotsky’s introduction of February 1934; co-edited (with
Kolbanovskii,Viktor Nikolaevich and Zankov, Leonid Vladimirovich) the posthumous edition of Vygotsky’s
Thinking and speech (1934).
Slavina, Lia Solomonovna (1906–88) – Vygotsky’s student at Pedological Department of the 2nd
Moscow State University (1925–30), member of the piaterka (“the five”) of his students; research on
children’s imitation in late 1920s (in collaboration with Bozhovich).
Solov’ev (alias Solov’ev-El’pidinskii), Ivan Mikhailovich (1902–86) – defectologist, Vygotsky’s graduate student (1925–9) and, along with Zankov (see Zankov, Leonid Vladimirovich), collaborator from
mid-1920s; notably, under Vygotsky’s supervision in early 1930s replicated Anitra Karsten’s study on
satiation (Sättigung) that was originally done by Berlin group of Kurt Lewin (see Lewin, Kurt).
Varshava, Boris Efimovich (1900–27) – Vygotsky’s collaborator in 1920s and co-author of Psychological
dictionary (published in 1931, after Varshava’s death).
Veresotskaia, Ksenia Ivanovna (dates of birth and death unknown) – defectologist, around 1930 conducted research on voluntary attention and voluntary remembering, visual thinking and eidetic
memory; Vygotsky’s collaborator in Defectological Institute in 1930s.

266

Revisionist Revolution in Vygotsky Studies

Zankov, Leonid Vladimirovich (1901–77) – defectologist, Vygotsky’s graduate student and, along with
Solov’ev (see Solov’ev, Ivan Mikhailovich), collaborator from mid-1920s; most notable research conducted under Vygotsky’s supervision is a series of studies on memory and remembering in normal
and mentally retarded children; co-edited (with Shif and Kolbanovskii) the posthumous edition of
Vygotsky’s Thinking and speech (1934).
Zaporozhets, Alexander Vladimirovich (1905–81) – Vygotsky’s student at Pedological Department of the
2nd Moscow State University (1925–30), member of the piaterka (“the five”) of his students; various
research projects under the supervision of Vygotsky and Luria in Moscow, and Luria, Leontiev, and
Lebedinskii in Kharkov; Head of Department of Psychology of the Kharkov State Pedagogical Institute
(from 1938) and the leader of the Kharkov group from mid-1930s.
Zeigarnik, Bluma Vul’fovna (1900–88) – former Berlin student of Kurt Lewin (see Lewin, Kurt), returned
to Soviet Union in 1931; under the supervision of Vygotsky conducted experimental and theoretical studies in clinical settings in Moscow in early 1930s, after Vygotsky’s death in collaboration with
Birenbaum (see Birenbaum, Gita Vasil’evna) and Kaganovskaia published several papers that creatively
integrate Vygotsky’s and Kurt Lewin’s theoretical work.

1 The archetype of Soviet psychology: from the Stalinism of
the 1930s to the “Stalinist science” of our time
1 On Russian utopianism, the Nietzschean tradition in Russian culture, and
medico-biological “revolutionary experiments” in the first third of the twentieth century see Krementsov, 2013; Rosenthal, 1986, 1994, 2002; Stites, 1989.
2 For the history of these disciplines in the West see S. Cohen, 1983, 1999; Depaepe, 1998,
2012; Depaepe, Simon, & Gorp, 2003; Hofstetter, 2004; Nisbet, 2002; Quen, 1977.
3 On Bolshevik modernization in the Soviet Union and its essential similarity with modernization processes in the West see Hoffmann, 2011.
4 This chapter was in fact the printed version of Pavlov’s keynote speech that he had given
at the IX International Congress of Psychology at Yale University in 1929 (Todes, 2014).
5 An overview of the publication activity of Soviet scholars in the interwar period can be
found in Yasnitsky, 2011c.
6 The list of representative publications includes Berman, 1939; Dewey, 1984, 1990;
McFarland, 1935; Schultz & McFarland, 1935; Viteles, 1938; Williams, 1934a, 1934b;
Zacharoff , 1931.

interested in the processes in the Soviet economy or politics during the “Great Break”

often criticized for maintaining large-scale international communication networks (on

that is the whole point: totalitarian censorship makes no distinction between the important and the unimportant, the material and immaterial. It seizes equally on a ‘criminal’,
anti-Soviet text, and on a trivial misprint in a crossword or an odd turn of phrase in a

SPON stood for Sotsial’no Pravovaia Okhrana Nesovershennoletnikh, i.e. the socio-legal

This seems to be an allusion to the New Testament, Matthew 7:7, “Ask, and it shall be
given you; seek, and ye shall find; knock, and it shall be opened unto you.”
It should be mentioned that at approximately the same time Vygotsky wrote several
articles in which he analyzed Adler’s ideas and their significance for modern psychology and defectology. Adler’s notion of supercompensation was a sort of disquieting idea
that required explanation and thereby allowed a progressive shift in Vygotsky’s scientific
research program.
Potebnia, Aleksandr Afanas’evich (1835–91). Philosopher, linguist. Known for his theory
of the inner form of the word, which represented a reformulation and concretization of
Wilhelm von Humboldt’s psychological viewpoint. For discussion see Bertau, 2014.
Shcherba, Lev Vladimirovich (1880–1944). Linguist who exercised a great influence on
the development of psycholinguistics, lexicography, and phonology.
Bakhtin, Mikhail Mikhailovich (1895–1975). Philosopher, linguist, leader of the philosophical group known as the Bakhtin circle. A number of his ideas show affinity with
those of Vygotsky; in particular, the idea of the dialogical nature of consciousness. It
remains unclear whether Vygotsky ever directly quoted Bakhtin or knew him personally – in none of the notes did we find a reference to Bakhtin or his texts. However, in the
reconstruction of the authentic text of “Psyche, consciousness, the unconscious,” it was
found that the editors had deleted a reference to Freidizm, a book written by Bakhtin’s
close colleague Voloshinov. Who exactly wrote this book remains unclear but Bakhtin’s
participation seems beyond doubt.
Political animal (Greek). Classic definition of man in which ‘political’ refers to ‘polis’ as
the rational arrangement of people’s social life.
Published in 2014. See online at: http://gefter.ru/archive/11094; http://assets0.gefter.
ru/Vygotsky_notebook.pdf.
Intellect and will are one and the same (Latin). Cf.: “God’s intellect, God’s will, and God’s
power, are one and the same” (The ethics, Prop. XVII 17, Scholia).

Notes to pages 106–113

273

10 Twice in The psychology of art Vygotsky writes about this principle: (1) “There exist two
positions among psychologists as to whether emotions increase or decrease under the
effect of affective concepts … if we apply to this question the principle of monophasic
energy expenditure as introduced by Kornilov in interpreting rational processes, we will
see that in emotions as well as in thoughts, an increase of the charge at the center leads to
a weakening of the charge in the peripheral organs”; (2) “I feel that this delay and weakening of inner organic and external manifestations of emotions should be regarded as a
particular case of the general law of monophasic energy expenditure due to emotions. As
we have seen, energy flows out from one of two poles, either at the periphery or at the
center; and an increased activity at one pole leads immediately to a decrease at the other.
It seems to me that only from this viewpoint can we approach art which appears to stir
very strong feelings in us, which feelings are not specifically expressed. The enigmatic
difference that exists between artistic feeling and ordinary feeling may be explained as
follows: Artistic feeling is the same as the other, but it is released by extremely intensified
activity of the imagination” (Vygotsky, 1971, pp. 48, 211).
11 Marr, Nikolay Yakovlevich (1864–1934). Russian and Soviet orientalist, philologist, historian. His Japhetic theory of language, created in the early 1920s and accepted as the
only true Marxist theory of language, was officially defended until the late 1940s.

17 Vygotsky is apparently referring to Hamlet’s phrase about his father: “He was a man,

18 Behold (the) man (Latin). Words used by Pontius Pilate in the Vulgate (Latin) translation

19 Semic (or semasiologic) analysis is a method of investigating consciousness as a conscious – i.e. cognized, aware, deliberate – relation to the world. In the literature one
frequently sees a reduced use of this method, which is then called semantic or semiotic.
This is just one of several possible interpretations and it does not exhaust the content of
this method, which, ultimately, was not operationalized in Vygotsky’s work but just proclaimed. For further discussion of sense, meaning, awareness, and consciousness, and their
Russian and English derivatives and possible uses in the discourse of the human sciences,
see Chapter 10 of this book, “Lost in translation.”
20 This brings to mind Wilhelm von Humboldt’s famous maxim that language is not product, ergon, but process, energeia. In his analysis of the interrelation between thinking and
speech,Vygotsky clearly followed Aleksandr Potebnia (1835–91), the author of a highly
influential book Mysl’ i iazyk (Thought and language) (Kharkov, 1862). Thus, Potebnia
insisted that thinking is not merely revealed, reflected in speech, but is objectivized in
verbal discourse, and hence, actually becomes thinking in its highest human forms (e.g. in
generalized concepts). For further discussion of the profound impact of the Humboldtian
tradition on Vygotsky’s legacy see Bertau, 2014.

21 In the Russian original, literally “Oblako – myshlenie?” (Cloud – thinking?). Quite likely,

6 “The way to freedom”: Vygotsky in 1932
1

2
3
4
5
6
7

The psychophysical problem in the broad sense of the term is the problem of the place
of mind in nature; in the narrow sense it is the problem of the relation between psychological and brain (nervous) processes. In the latter case ‘psychophysiological problem’
would be the more exact term.Vygotsky mainly used the first term even when he wrote
about the problem of the localization of psychological processes and their connection
with brain structures. This was because it was important to him to solve the problem at
the highest theoretical level and because he considered the problem of localization as a
special case of this broader understanding. In this analysis we will stick to Vygotsky’s use
of the term.
Asya Vygodskaya (1930–80) was Vygotsky’s youngest daughter.
Potebnya’s words.
A line from Afanasy Fet’s poem Kak trudno povtoriat’ zhivuiu krasotu.
His contemporary Andrei Platonov had similar ideas.
Remarkably enough, the last chapter of Thinking and speech repeats the text of this note
almost verbatim but leaves out the reference to and the quotations from Boris Pasternak.
The reference is to the experiments by Lewin’s student Tamara Dembo (1902–93), who
studied the dynamics of anger (see Dembo, 1931, 1976). Dembo presented the subjects
with a task that had two more or less clear solutions.When they had found them, she suggested there was a third solution.This solution, however, did not exist and the experiment
studied the emotional reactions of subjects in a hopeless situation. The topological interpretation was that the quasi-need to solve the problem was not fulfilled and became transformed into anger, for example, against objects in the field. However, some subjects did not
regress to physical destruction but tried to find imaginary solutions, resorting to clever but
phantastic solutions that could not be realized in real life. Still others ‘left the field’ or liberated themselves from the field: for them the task lost its motivating force and their failure
did not cause anger. Some of them just lost interest but others tried to restructure the task,
engaging in conversations with the experimenter and testing her patience. For Vygotsky
these experiments were interesting, because they showed, first, the connection between
will and affect, and, second, the subject’s capacity for volitional behavior, for the deliberate
change of a meaningless and hopeless situation. That he mentioned Kurt Koffka (1886–
1941) in connection with Dembo is explained in Vygotsky’s Lectures on psychology: “About
Dembo’s experiments with meaningless actions … I recently heard when Koffka was in
Moscow.The subject is given a number of meaningless tasks and it is studied how he reacts
to that. It is interesting to see that [subjects have] … a very strong tendency to attribute
meaning to these tasks and to create a new situation, by changing the psychological field,
in which their action would be meaningful rather than meaningless” (Vygotsky, 1987,

Notes to pages 130–148

277

p. 356). As is known, at the time Koffka gave a lecture in Moscow (Psychological Institute,
May, 1932) and Vygotsky served as his interpreter (see Chapter 9).
8 Intellectual love for God (Latin), a term used by Spinoza.
9 A citation from Friedrich Engels’s Anti-Dühring (1878).
10 The quotation is not verbatim and not complete. See original (Wertheimer, 1925b) and
an English translation: “It becomes rapidly evident that only we Europeans, at a late stage
of culture, have hit upon the idea of separating the physical and psychic of many physical processes in this way. Think of someone dancing. In his dance there is joy and grace.
How is that? Does it represent on the one hand a display of muscles and movement of
the limbs, and on the other hand psychic consciousness? No. But that does not solve our
problem; it only sets the task” (Wertheimer, 1944, p. 96).
11 Anitra Karsten, a student of Lewin’s, investigated the process of psychological satiation
with the following setup. The subjects were presented with a monotonous task (e.g. to
draw circles on a sheet of paper) with the instruction to carry on as long as they wished.
When the subjects after a while asked whether they still had to continue for a long time,
the instruction was changed and the subjects were told it was a test of their stamina. As
a result, subjects who already showed signs of satiation (the need to fulfill the task was
almost satisfied), resumed the task with new vigor. According to Lewin, this meant that
the various need systems communicate with each other and exchange energy: when
they developed a new quasi-need (now it was no longer a boring task but a test of their
endurance), energy was taken from another system. Incidentally, some subjects changed
the rules of the game and tried to wear down the experimenter by continuing the task
endlessly (Karsten, 1928, 1976). From Vygotsky’s point of view, the ‘energetic’ explanation was not sufficient: “Satiation has different functions in meaningful and meaningless
activity: in one case work slows down, in the other it becomes faster (to get rid of it as
soon as possible). In other words, the structures of satiation = subordinate aspects in the
structure of the higher processes” (Notebook. October, 1932) (Vygotskii, 2007).

In the original: “Leontiev se fait un devoir de rompre le silence et me déclare alors que
je peux l’y aider. Le dégel est lent et incertain. Il faut profiter du climat actuel et accélérer
les choses en publiant à l’étranger des oeuvres de Vygotski. Ainsi on peut espérer que les
éditeurs soviétiques devront suivre” (Zazzo, 1989), p. 7.
In the original: “Il s’agissait de trouver un éditeur, en usant des relations que j’avais en
Amérique. Luria prit le relais quelques mois plus tard. Et il réussit où j’avais échoué.
Des textes de Vygotski parurent en 1955 aux Etats-Unis. Un an plus tard, ces mêmes
textes étaient publiés en URSS. C’était l’effet escompté” (Zazzo, 1989, p. 7). See also
Zazzo, 1982.
According to the volume of published conference proceedings, the papers were: “La nature
et la formation des propriétés et des processus psychiques de l’homme” (A. Léontiev),“The

15 It should be noted that the terms “West” and “East” are not geographic concepts. In

educational level of the particular population. Actually, the pair of opposites east/west had
the same meaning as the pairs traditional/modern and delayed/developed. For example,
Crimean Tatars were categorized as an Eastern nationality, while the Volga Germans, who
lived more eastward geographically, were defined as a Western nationality.

project: “The last meeting of the laboratory and tomorrow’s meeting are devoted to

zwar nach den Ukrainischen Akademie für Psychoneurologie, und die Sendung spaziert
Zwei Monate durch das ganze Land (denn ein Teil der Addresse war in Charkow, der
zweite – Frunze 13 – in Moskau).”

vom Jahr zu Jahr intensiver. Sehr gute Arbeit über die Pathologie der Spannungen ist
von Bluma und Gita durchgeführt, ich hoffe, dass sie beide eine grössere Arbeit über
Denken und Affekt [abschließen?]. Der Abfall des Gehirns scheint ein ausgezeichnet
geeigenes [= geeignetes] Material zu sein für das Studium der generellen Schicksal der
Verhaltensdynamik, und ich hoffe Sie werden Freude haben an der Arbeit der Beiden.
Auch Leontiew in Charkow hat eine grosse Reihe Studien ueber die Verhaltens –

von Ziel-Motiv-und Taetigkeit steht hier in Vordergrunde. Das alles machte uns möglich

eine solche kleine Lewin-Konferenz stattfinden (mit Vorträgen: Birenbaum, Zeigarnik,
Solovjev, Leontjew und seine Gruppe).”
44 From Luria’s letter to Lewin of April 4, 1936, AHAP: “Unser Lewin-Symposium wird,

schreiben.”
